# The Prize: The Epic Quest for Oil, Money & Power — Part 1 Notes
## Daniel Yergin (1991)
### Pages 6-619

---

## Prologue (p6-13)

Winston Churchill changed his mind almost overnight about military spending. Until summer 1911, as Home Secretary, Churchill was one of the "economists" in the British Cabinet — critics of increased military spending in the Anglo-German naval race. He argued war with Germany was not inevitable and money would be better spent on domestic social programs than extra battleships.

Then on July 1, 1911, Kaiser Wilhelm sent the German naval vessel *Panther* steaming into the harbor at Agadir, on the Atlantic coast of Morocco, to check French influence in Africa. Though only a gunboat at a secondary port, the arrival ignited a severe international crisis. The buildup of the German Army was already causing unease; now Germany seemed to be directly challenging France and Britain's global positions. War fear gripped Europe for weeks. By late July the tension eased — Churchill declared "the bully is climbing down" — but the crisis transformed his outlook. He was now convinced Germany sought hegemony and would use military muscle. War was virtually inevitable, only a matter of time.

Appointed First Lord of the Admiralty immediately after Agadir, Churchill vowed to prepare Britain militarily. His charge: ensure the Royal Navy was ready to meet the German challenge on the high seas. One of the most important questions he faced was seemingly technical but had vast implications — whether to convert the British Navy from coal to oil. Many thought this pure folly: the Navy would no longer rely on safe, secure Welsh coal, but instead depend on distant and insecure oil supplies from Persia (Iran). "To commit the Navy irrevocably to oil was indeed 'to take arms against a sea of troubles,'" Churchill said. But the strategic benefits — greater speed and more efficient use of manpower — were so obvious he decided Britain must base its "naval supremacy upon oil." He committed himself with all his driving energy to achieving this objective.

"Mastery itself was the prize of the venture," Churchill declared. Yergin argues this captured a fundamental truth applicable not just to WWI but to the many decades ahead. Oil has meant mastery throughout the twentieth century. The quest for mastery is what the book is about.

**The 1990 Iraq-Kuwait crisis as bookend**: On August 2, 1990, Saddam Hussein invaded Kuwait — not just to conquer a sovereign state but to capture its oil riches. If successful, Iraq would become the world's leading oil power, dominating the Arab world and Persian Gulf where the bulk of planetary reserves are concentrated. The world did not accept this as a fait accompli. The UN instituted an embargo; Western and Arab nations mustered military force to defend Saudi Arabia. There was no precedent for either the US-Soviet cooperation or the rapid, massive deployment into the region. Over the previous years, it had become "almost fashionable" to say oil was no longer important — even senior officers of US Central Command had been lectured that oil had lost its strategic significance. The invasion stripped away the illusion.

**Three great themes** underlie the story of oil:

1. **Rise of capitalism and modern business.** Oil is the world's biggest and most pervasive business, the greatest of the great industries of the late nineteenth century. Standard Oil was among the world's very first and largest multinational enterprises. The expansion of the business in the twentieth century — from wildcat drillers to great corporate bureaucracies and state-owned companies — embodies the evolution of business, corporate strategy, technological change, and market development. "Oil is almost like money," as one tycoon said. Of the top twenty Fortune 500 companies, seven are oil companies.

2. **Oil as a commodity intertwined with global politics and power.** WWI established petroleum as an element of national power when the internal combustion engine overtook the horse and coal-powered locomotive. Japan attacked Pearl Harbor to protect its flank while grabbing petroleum resources of the East Indies. Among Hitler's most important strategic objectives was capture of the Caucasus oil fields. America's predominance in oil proved decisive — by war's end, German and Japanese fuel tanks were empty. The Suez Crisis of 1956 truly marked the end of old European imperial powers. "Oil power" loomed large in the 1970s, catapulting peripheral states into positions of great wealth. Oil can also be "fool's gold" — the Shah was granted his fervent wish of oil wealth, and it destroyed him. The Soviet Union squandered its enormous oil earnings on military buildup. The US, once the world's largest producer, must now import half its oil supply.

3. **"Hydrocarbon Society" and "Hydrocarbon Man."** In its first decades, the oil business provided kerosene — "the new light" — which pushed back the night and extended the working day. Rockefeller became the richest man in America from kerosene sales. Gasoline was then a useless by-product, sometimes sold for two cents a gallon or dumped into rivers. But the incandescent light bulb seemed to signal kerosene's obsolescence — then the internal combustion engine opened a new era. Oil toppled King Coal as the power source for the industrial world. Oil became the basis of postwar suburbanization. Today we are so dependent on oil that we hardly stop to comprehend its pervasive significance — it makes possible where we live, how we work, how we travel, even where we conduct courtships. Oil provides essential fertilizer components, transports food to megacities, and provides the plastics and chemicals that are the "bricks and mortar" of contemporary civilization.

The environmental movement now challenges the oil industry — efforts to curtail combustion of all fossil fuels because of smog, acid rain, ozone depletion, and climate change. Yet Hydrocarbon Man shows little inclination to give up his cars. In the 1990s, the world's population is expected to grow by one billion people, all demanding the "right" to consume.

The story's cast of characters includes not just industry tycoons — Rockefeller, Henri Deterding, Calouste Gulbenkian, J. Paul Getty, Armand Hammer, T. Boone Pickens — but also political leaders: Churchill, Hitler, Stalin, Ibn Saud, Mohammed Mossadegh, Eisenhower, Kissinger, George Bush, Saddam Hussein. The twentieth century rightly deserves the title "the century of oil."

---

## Chapter 1: Oil on the Brain — The Beginning (p14-25, continued next batch)

### *The Missing $526.08*

The chapter opens with the story of Benjamin Silliman, Jr., son of a great American chemist and himself a distinguished professor of chemistry at Yale University. In the 1850s, a professor's salary was hardly generous, and Silliman had taken on an outside research project for a fee totaling $526.08. He had been retained in 1854 by a group of promoters and businessmen, but though he completed the project, the promised fee was not forthcoming. His anger was aimed at the leaders of the investor group — George Bissell, a New York lawyer, and James Townsend, president of a bank in New Haven. Townsend had sought to keep a low profile, fearing it would look inappropriate to his depositors if they learned he was involved in so speculative a venture.

What Bissell, Townsend, and the group had in mind was a grandiose vision for "rock oil" — so called to distinguish it from vegetable oils and animal fats. Rock oil bubbled up in springs or seeped into salt wells around Oil Creek, in the isolated wooded hills of northwestern Pennsylvania. A few barrels of this dark, smelly substance were gathered by primitive means — skimming off surfaces of springs and creeks, or wringing out rags or blankets soaked in oily waters. The bulk of this tiny supply was used to make medicine. The group believed rock oil could be exploited in far larger quantities and processed into a fluid burned as an illuminant in lamps — competitive with the "coal-oils" winning markets in the 1850s. They were convinced they could light up towns and farms of North America and Europe, use rock oil to lubricate the moving parts of the dawning mechanical age, and grow very rich. Many scoffed, but they would succeed in laying the basis for an entirely new era — the age of oil.

### *To "Assuage Our Woes"*

George Bissell, more than anybody else, was responsible for the creation of the oil industry. With his long, towering face and broad forehead, he conveyed intellectual force but was also shrewd and open to business opportunity. Self-supporting from age twelve, Bissell worked his way through Dartmouth College by teaching and writing articles. After graduation he was a professor of Latin and Greek, then a journalist in Washington, D.C., then ended up in New Orleans as principal of a high school and then superintendent of public schools. In his spare time he studied law and taught himself several more languages — altogether fluent in French, Irish, Portuguese and could read Hebrew, Sanskrit, ancient and modern Greek, Latin, and German.

Ill health forced Bissell north in 1853, and passing through western Pennsylvania, he saw the primitive oil-gathering industry with its skimmings and oil-soaked rags. Soon after, visiting his alma mater Dartmouth, he spied in a professor's office a bottle of Pennsylvania rock oil brought by another Dartmouth graduate, a physician practicing in western Pennsylvania. Bissell knew rock oil was being used as patent medicine — called "Seneca Oil" after the local Indians and their chief Red Jacket. One purveyor advertised its "wonderful curative powers" in a poem: "The Healthful balm, from Nature's secret spring, / The bloom of health, and life, to man will bring."

Seeing the sample at Dartmouth, Bissell conceived in a flash that rock oil could be used not as medicine but as an illuminant. That intuition became his guiding principle and faith, sorely tested during the next six years as "disappointment consistently overwhelmed hope."

### *The Disappearing Professor*

Bissell aroused investor interest, and in late 1854 the group engaged Yale's Professor Silliman to analyze the oil's properties as illuminant and lubricant. They also wanted his distinguished imprimatur to sell stock and raise capital. Heavyset and vigorous, with a "good, jolly face," Silliman was one of the greatest and most respected names in nineteenth-century science — son of the founder of American chemistry, author of the leading textbooks in physics and chemistry. Yale was the scientific capital of mid-nineteenth-century America.

Silliman was less interested in the abstract than in the practical world of business. Academic salaries were low and he had a growing family; he habitually took on outside consulting jobs. His taste for the practical drew him into speculative business ventures. A brother-in-law was skeptical: "Benjamin Silliman, Jr., he said, is on the constant go in behalf of one thing or another, and alas for Science."

Silliman's analysis promised the result would "meet your expectations." Three months later, nearing the end, he reported "unexpected success in the use of the distillate product of Rock Oil as an illuminator." But the investors owed him $526.08 (equivalent of about $5,000 today) and had insisted on a $100 deposit they never made. Silliman's bill was much higher than expected. The professor was upset and angry — he withheld the study, secretly handed the report to a friend for safekeeping, and took himself off on a tour of the South where he could not be easily reached.

The investors grew desperate. Finally, one of Bissell's partners put up the money "on his own security," complaining "these are the hardest times I ever heard of." The report, dated April 16, 1855, was released and hurried to the printers. Silliman's study was "a turning point in the establishment of the petroleum business." He banished doubts about potential new uses for rock oil — it could be distilled into several fractions, all composed of carbon and hydrogen, one of which was a very high-quality illuminating oil. "Gentlemen, it appears to me that there is much ground for encouragement," Silliman wrote.

Armed with Silliman's report, the group raised funds. Silliman himself took two hundred shares. The enterprise became the Pennsylvania Rock Oil Company. But it took another year and a half before they were ready for the next hazardous step.

### *Price and Innovation*

The hopes pinned on oil arose from pure necessity. Burgeoning populations and the spreading industrial revolution had increased demand for artificial illumination beyond the simple wick dipped into animal grease or vegetable fat. For those with money, sperm whale oil had set the standard for high-quality illumination for hundreds of years; but whale schools of the Atlantic had been decimated, whaling ships forced to sail farther and farther — around Cape Horn and into the distant Pacific. Prices were rising: $2.50 a gallon, a price that seemed sure to go even higher. Cheaper lighting fluids had been developed but were inferior. The most popular was camphene, a derivative of turpentine, which produced a good light but had the drawback of being highly flammable, "compounded by an even more unattractive tendency to explode in people's houses." There was also "town gas" distilled from coal, piped into street lamps and homes of middle-and upper-class families — but it was expensive. There was a sharply growing need for a reliable, relatively cheap illuminant. There was also a second need — lubrication for the advances in mechanical production (power looms, steam printing presses) that created too much friction for common lubricants like lard.

Entrepreneurial innovation had already begun responding in the late 1840s and early 1850s. A court-martialed British admiral, Thomas Cochrane — said to have provided the model for Lord Byron's *Don Juan* — became obsessed with asphalt potential and acquired a huge tar pit in Trinidad. Cochrane collaborated with a Canadian, Dr. Abraham Gesner, who had attempted to start a business exporting horses to the West Indies, was shipwrecked twice, went to Guy's Hospital in London to study medicine, returned to Canada to become provincial geologist of New Brunswick. Gesner developed a process for extracting illuminating oil from asphalt, which he called "kerosene" — from the Greek *Keros* (wax) and *elaion* (oil), altering *elaion* to *ene* to sound like the familiar camphene. In 1854 he applied for a US patent for "a new liquid hydrocarbon, which I denominate Kerosene." By 1859, Gesner's kerosene works in New York City was producing 5,000 gallons a day. The Scottish chemist James Young had pioneered a parallel refining industry in Britain based on cannel coal. By 1859, an estimated thirty-four companies in the US were producing $5 million a year worth of kerosene or "coal-oils."

Oil was hardly unfamiliar to mankind. In the Middle East, bitumen had seeped to the surface through cracks since antiquity — tapped in Mesopotamia back to 3000 B.C. The most famous source was at Hit, on the Euphrates, near Babylon. Bitumen was a traded commodity in the ancient Middle East — used as building mortar, binding walls of Jericho and Babylon, probably caulking Noah's ark and Moses' basket. It was used for road making and lighting. Bitumen served as medicine — Pliny described it checking bleeding, healing wounds, treating cataracts, curing gout, relieving shortness of breath, and being "useful for straightening out eyelashes which inconvenience the eyes."

Oil seepages set aflame also played a role in warfare. Homer recorded Trojans casting unwearied fire upon Greek ships. The Persian King Cyrus used pitch and tow to take Babylon. From the seventh century onward, the Byzantines used *oleum incendiarum* — Greek fire — a mixture of petroleum and lime that, touched with moisture, would catch fire. The recipe was a closely guarded state secret. For centuries it was considered more terrible than gunpowder.

Knowledge of petroleum was lost to the West for many centuries, perhaps because the known major sources lay beyond the Roman empire's boundaries. But oil seepages were observed across Europe — Bavaria, Sicily, the Po Valley, Alsace, Hannover, Galicia. Refining technology was transmitted via the Arabs. Petroleum was mostly used as an all-purpose medicinal remedy. A small oil industry had developed in Eastern Europe — first in Galicia (variously part of Poland, Austria, and Russia) and then in Rumania. Peasants dug shafts by hand. A pharmacist from Lvov, with help from a plumber, invented a cheap lamp suited to burning kerosene. By 1854 kerosene was a staple of commerce in Vienna; by 1859 Galicia had a thriving business with over 150 villages involved in mining for oil. European crude production in 1859 was estimated at thirty-six thousand barrels, primarily from Galicia and Rumania. What the Eastern European industry lacked was the technology for drilling.

In the 1850s, kerosene spread in the US faced two barriers: no substantial supply source, and no cheap lamp suited to burning it. Then a kerosene sales agent in New York learned of a lamp with a glass chimney being produced in Vienna to burn Galician kerosene — based on the pharmacist and plumber's design from Lvov. It overcame problems of smoke and smell. The New York salesman imported it, and though improved many times over, that Vienna lamp became the basis of the kerosene lamp trade in the United States and was later re-exported around the world.

By the time Bissell was launching his venture, cheaper, better-quality kerosene had already been introduced into some homes. The refining techniques required were already commercialized with coal-oils, and an inexpensive lamp had been developed. What Bissell and the Pennsylvania Rock Oil Company needed was to discover a new, cheaper source for the raw material. If they could find rock oil in sufficient abundance, it could be sold cheaply, capturing the illuminating oils market. But how to get it in quantity?

Salt "boring" or drilling had been developed more than 1,500 years earlier in China, with wells going down 3,000 feet. Around 1830 the Chinese method was imported into Europe and copied, stimulating salt well drilling in the United States. In 1856, on a hot day in New York, Bissell took refuge under a druggist's awning on Broadway and spotted an advertisement for a rock oil patent medicine showing several drilling derricks — the kind used to bore for salt. The rock oil for the patent medicine was obtained as a byproduct of salt drilling. With that coincidental glimpse — following his earlier ones in western Pennsylvania and at Dartmouth — the last piece fell into place. Could drilling be applied to the recovery of oil? If yes, here was the means for achieving his fortune.

The essential insight of Bissell and his fellow investors was to adapt the salt-boring technique directly to oil. They were not alone — others in the US and Ontario, Canada, were experimenting. But Bissell's group was ready to move: they had Silliman's report and the capital it attracted. Still, people thought them crazy. When Townsend discussed drilling for oil in New Haven, people derided it: "Oh Townsend, oil coming out of the ground, pumping oil out of the earth as you pump water? Nonsense! You're crazy."

### *The "Colonel"*

Their candidate to carry out the project was Edwin L. Drake, chosen mainly by coincidence. He brought no outstanding qualifications — a jack-of-all-trades and sometime railroad conductor, laid up by bad health, living with his daughter in the old Tontine Hotel in New Haven. James Townsend, the banker, happened to live in the same hotel — a place where men gathered to exchange news and shoot the breeze. The thirty-eight-year-old Drake was friendly, jovial, loquacious, and had nothing else to do. He passed evenings entertaining companions with dramatic, exaggerated tales in which he played the heroic role. Townsend talked frequently about the rock oil venture, persuaded Drake to buy stock, then recruited him to go to Pennsylvania — he was out of work, had a railroad pass and could travel free, and was very tenacious.

Dispatching Drake to Pennsylvania, Townsend gave him a valuable send-off: concerned about the need to impress the "backwoodsmen," the banker sent ahead letters addressed to "Colonel" E. L. Drake. Thus was "Colonel" Drake invented — though a colonel he certainly was not. The stratagem worked. Drake arrived in December 1857 in the tiny, impoverished village of Titusville, population 125, tucked into the hills of northwestern Pennsylvania — a lumber town deeply in debt to the local lumber company's store.

Drake's first job was to perfect the title to the prospective oil land. He returned to New Haven, intent on drilling. "I had made up my mind," he later said, "that oil could be obtained in large quantities by Boreing as for Salt Water. I also determined that I should be the one to do it. But I found that no one with whom I conversed upon the subject agreed with me, all maintaining that oil was the drippings of an extensive Coal field or bed."

Drake was back in Titusville in the spring of 1858. The investors had established the Seneca Oil Company with Drake as general agent. He set up operations about two miles down Oil Creek from Titusville, on a farm with an oil spring producing three to six gallons a day by traditional methods. After several months, he wrote Townsend: "I shall not try to dig by hand any more, as I am satisfied that boring is the cheapest." He begged for more funds: "Money we must have if we are to make anything ... Please let me know at once. Money is very scarce here." Townsend managed to send a thousand dollars, and Drake tried to hire "salt borers" — drillers — needed to proceed. But salt drillers had a reputation for "extreme partiality to whiskey and frequent drunkenness," and he wanted to be careful. He tied compensation to successful completion at one dollar per foot drilled. The first couple of drillers simply disappeared or begged off — they thought he was insane. Drake had nothing to show for his first year in Titusville.

So he devoted himself to erecting the steam engine that would power the drill bit, while the investors back in New Haven fretted and waited.

Finally, in the spring of 1859, Drake found his driller — a blacksmith named William A. Smith, "Uncle Billy" Smith, who came with his two sons. Smith knew something about what was needed, for he made the tools for the salt water drillers. The little team built the derrick and assembled equipment. They assumed they would have to go several hundred feet. The work was slow, and the New Haven investors were becoming more and more restive. Drake stuck to his plan. He would not give up. Eventually, Townsend was the only promoter who still believed in the project. When the venture ran out of money, Townsend began paying bills from his own pocket. In despair, he at last sent Drake a money order as a final remittance, instructing him to pay his bills, close up the operation, and return to New Haven. That was toward the end of August 1859.

Drake had not yet received the letter when, on Saturday afternoon, August 27, 1859, at sixty-nine feet, the drill dropped into a crevice and slid another six inches. Work was called off for the weekend. The next day, Sunday, Uncle Billy came out to see the well. He peered down into the pipe and saw a dark fluid floating on top of the water. He used a tin rain spout to draw up a sample. He was overcome by excitement. On Monday, when Drake arrived, he found Uncle Billy and his boys standing guard over tubs, washbasins, and barrels, all filled with oil. Drake attached a common hand pump and began to do exactly what the scoffers had ridiculed — pump up the liquid. That same day he received the money order from Townsend and the command to close up shop. A week earlier, with the last funds in hand, he would have done so. But not anymore. Drake's single-mindedness had paid off. Just in time.

Farmers along Oil Creek rushed into Titusville shouting, "The Yankee has struck oil." The news spread like wildfire and started a mad rush to acquire sites and drill for oil. The population of tiny Titusville multiplied overnight, and land prices shot up instantaneously.

Success with the drill did not guarantee financial success. What were Drake and Uncle Billy to do with the flow of oil? They got hold of every whiskey barrel they could scrounge in the area. Unfortunately, one night a lantern's flame ignited the petroleum gases, causing the entire storage area to explode and go up in fierce flames. Meanwhile, other wells were drilled, supply far outran demand, and the price plummeted. With the advent of drilling, there was no shortage of rock oil. The only shortage was of whiskey barrels, and they soon cost almost twice as much as the oil inside them.

### *"The Light of the Age"*

Pennsylvania rock oil quickly found its way to market refined as kerosene. Its virtues were immediately clear. "As an illuminator the oil is without a figure: It is the light of the age," wrote the author of America's very first handbook on oil, less than a year after Drake's discovery. "Those that have not seen it burn, may rest assured its light is no moonshine; but something nearer the clear, strong, brilliant light of day, to which darkness is no party ... rock oil emits a dainty light; the brightest and yet the cheapest in the world; a light fit for Kings and Royalists and not unsuitable for Republicans and Democrats."

George Bissell wasted no time getting to Titusville, spending hundreds of thousands of dollars leasing and buying farms around Oil Creek. "We find here an unparalleled excitement," he wrote his wife. "The whole population are crazy almost ... I never saw such excitement. The whole western country are thronging here and fabulous prices are offered for lands in the vicinity where there is a prospect of getting oil." After six years of struggle, he reflected: "I am quite well, but very much worn down. We have had a hard time of it, very. Our prospects are most brilliant that's certain. ... We ought to make an immense fortune."

Bissell did indeed become very wealthy. Among his philanthropies, he donated a gymnasium to Dartmouth with six bowling alleys "in remembrance of disciplinary troubles into which he had fallen as an undergraduate because of his indulgence in this sinful sport." It was said in his later years "that his name and fame is a 'household word' among oil men from end to end of the continent." James Townsend, who had taken the greatest financial risk, was denied the credit he thought he deserved. "The whole plan was suggested by me, and my suggestions were carried out," he later wrote bitterly.

As for Drake, things did not go well at all. He became an oil buyer, then a partner in a Wall Street firm specializing in oil shares. He was improvident, not a good businessman, indeed a gambler. By 1866 he had lost all his money, became a semi-invalid, racked with pain, living in poverty. "If you have any of the milk of human kindness left in your bosom for me or my family, send me some money," he wrote. Finally, in 1873, Pennsylvania granted him a small lifetime pension for his service. Toward the end of his life, Drake sought to stake out his place in history: "I claim that I did invent the driving Pipe and drive it and without that they could not bore on bottom lands when the earth is full of water. And I claim to have bored the first well that ever was bored for Petroleum in America and can show the well."

### *The First Boom*

Drake proved, through drilling, the final requirement for a new industry — the availability of supply. Man was suddenly given the ability to push back the night. But Drake's discovery would also bequeath mobility and power to the world's population, play a central role in the rise and fall of nations and empires, and become a major element in the transformation of human society.

What followed was like a gold rush. The flats in the narrow valley of Oil Creek were quickly leased, and by November 1860, fifteen months after Drake's discovery, about seventy-five wells were producing, with many more dry holes scarring the earth. Titusville became "the rendezvous of strangers eager for speculation." "They barter prices in claims and shares; buy and sell sites, and report the depth, show, or yield of wells, etc. etc. ... Never was a hive of bees in time of swarming more astir, or making a greater buzz."

Down at the bottom of Oil Creek, where it flowed into the Allegheny River, a small town called Cornplanter, named after a Seneca Indian chief, was renamed Oil City and became the major center for what was now known as the Oil Regions. Refineries to turn crude into kerosene were cheap to build — by 1860 at least fifteen were operating in the Oil Regions, with another five in Pittsburgh. A coal-oil refiner visited the fields in 1860 and said, "If this business succeeds, mine is ruined." He was right — by end of 1860, coal-oil refiners were either out of business or had converted to crude-oil refining.

Yet all wells thus far were modest producers and had to be pumped. That changed in April 1861, when drillers struck the first flowing well, which gushed at three thousand barrels per day. When the oil shot into the air, something ignited the escaping gases, setting off a great explosion and wall of fire that killed nineteen people and blazed for three days. Though temporarily lost in the news of Fort Sumter — the opening shots of the Civil War — the explosion announced that ample supplies for the new industry would be available.

Production rose rapidly — from about 450,000 barrels in 1860 to 3 million barrels in 1862. The market could not absorb the swelling volume. Prices, which had been $10 a barrel in January 1861, fell to 50 cents by June and were down to 10 cents by the end of 1861. Many producers were ruined. But cheap prices gave Pennsylvania oil a quick and decisive victory in the marketplace, driving out coal-oils and other illuminants. Demand caught up with supply, and by end of 1862 prices rose to $4 a barrel, then to $7.25 by September 1863. Despite wild price fluctuations, stories of instant wealth continued to draw throngs. One memorable well generated $15,000 of profit for every dollar invested in less than two years.

The Civil War hardly disrupted the boom — it actually gave a major stimulus. The war cut off the shipment of turpentine from the South, creating an acute shortage of camphene, the cheap illuminating oil derived from turpentine. Kerosene from Pennsylvania oil quickly filled the gap. The war had an even more significant impact: when the South seceded, the North lost foreign revenues from cotton, one of America's major exports. The rapid growth of oil exports to Europe compensated for that loss and provided a significant new source of foreign earnings.

After the war, thousands of veterans poured into the Oil Regions seeking their fortunes in a new speculative boom, fueled by prices as high as $13.75 a barrel. The effects of the frenzy were felt up and down the East Coast — hundreds of new oil companies were floated. Office space ran short in the financial district of New York. Shares were sold so rapidly that one new company disposed of its entire issue in just four hours. Washington, D.C., was no more immune — Congressman James Garfield became a substantial investor in oil lands.

Nothing revealed the feverish pitch of speculation better than the story of Pithole, fifteen miles from Titusville. A first well was struck in dense forest in January 1865; by June, four flowing wells were producing two thousand barrels per day — one third of the Oil Regions' total output. People fought their way in on roads already clogged with barrel-laden wagons. "The whole place smells like a corps of soldiers when they have the diarrhea." The land speculation knew no bounds — one farm virtually worthless a few months earlier was sold for $1.3 million in July 1868 and resold for two million dollars in September. By that September, what had once been an unidentifiable spot in the wilderness had become a town of fifteen thousand people, with two banks, two telegraph offices, a newspaper, a waterworks, more than fifty hotels — at least three up to elegant metropolitan standards — and a post office handling more than five thousand letters a day.

But then, a couple of months later, oil production abruptly gave out — just as quickly as it had begun. By January 1866, only a year from the first discovery, thousands had fled. The town was totally deserted. Fires ravaged the buildings. A parcel of land that sold for $2 million in 1868 was auctioned for $4.37 in 1878.

Even as Pithole died, the speculative boom was engulfing neighboring areas. Production in the Oil Regions jumped to 3.6 million barrels in 1866. Oil became part of popular culture — Americans danced to the "American Petroleum Polka" and the "Oil Fever Gallop," and sang songs like "Famous Oil Firms" and "Oil on the Brain."

### *Boom and Bust*

The race to find oil was swiftly followed by another race to produce it as quickly and in as much volume as possible. The drive for "flush production" often damaged the reservoirs, leading to premature exhaustion of gas pressure and less recovery than would otherwise have been the case. Several reasons drove this: lack of geological knowledge, the large and quick rewards to be attained, the nature of leasing terms which put a premium on producing quickly.

But most important in shaping the legal context was the **"rule of capture,"** a doctrine based on English common law. If a game animal or bird migrated from one estate to another, the owner of the latter estate could kill the game on his land. Similarly, owners of land had the right to draw out whatever wealth lay beneath. As applied to oil production, the rule meant that surface owners atop a common pool could take all the oil they could get, even if they disproportionately drained the pool or reduced output of neighboring wells. The owners of adjacent wells were in heated competition to produce as much as they could as swiftly as possible, to avoid having the pool drained by another. The rule of capture contributed to the instability of both production and prices, and led to considerable waste and damage. But it also created room for many more people to enter the industry and helped make possible a wider market.

Fueled by the rule of capture and the race for riches, the Oil Regions became a chaotic scene of shacks, derricks, storage tanks, everyone energized by hope and rumor and the acrid scent of oil. And everywhere — the perennial mud. "Mud, deep, and indescribably disgusting, covered all the main and by-roads in wet weather." A local editor in 1865 captured the scene: "The oil and land excitement in this section has already become a sort of epidemic. It embraces all classes and ages and conditions of men. They neither talk, nor look, nor act as they did six months ago. Land, leases, contracts, refusals, deeds, agreements, interests, and all that sort of talk is all they can comprehend. ... The court is at a standstill; the bar is demoralized; the social circle is broken; the sanctuary is forsaken; and all our habits, and notions, and associations for half a century are turned topsy-turvy in the headlong rush for riches." The editor had a final thought: "The big bubble will burst sooner or later."

The bubble did burst — depression engulfed the industry in 1866-67; the price of oil dropped as low as $2.40 a barrel. Yet while many stopped drilling, new fields were opened beyond Oil Creek. Innovation and organization were being imposed upon the industry.

From the first discoveries, teamsters had clogged the roads with their loads of barrels. They were more than a physical bottleneck — holding a monopoly position, they charged exorbitant rates; it cost more to move a barrel over a few miles of muddy road to a railway stop than to transport it by rail from western Pennsylvania to New York. The teamsters' stranglehold led to the development of pipelines. Between 1863 and 1865, despite much scoffing and ridicule, wooden pipelines proved they could carry oil much more efficiently and cheaply. The teamsters responded with threats, armed attacks, arson, and sabotage. But by 1866, pipelines were hooked up to most wells, feeding into a larger gathering system connected with the railroads.

The refiners needed to acquire oil, and that too was initially chaotic — buyers on horseback rode from well to well. As the industry grew, informal oil exchanges emerged where buyers and sellers could meet and agree on prices — in a hotel in Titusville and at a curbside exchange near the railway tracks in Oil City. Beginning in the early 1870s, more formal oil exchanges emerged. Oil was bought and sold on three bases: "Spot" sales for immediate delivery and payment; "regular" sales completed within ten days; and "futures" — a certain quantity sold at a certain price within a specified future time. Futures prices became the focus for speculation, and oil became "the favorite speculative commodity of the time." Buyers could make a handsome profit — or suffer a devastating loss — without ever taking possession of the oil.

By the time the Titusville Oil Exchange opened in 1871, oil was already on its way to becoming a very big business. The decade of the 1860s had been one of dizzy advance from Drake's lunatic experiment — "the impetuous energy with which the American mind takes up any branch of industry that promises to pay well." Bissell's intuition and Drake's perseverance had opened a turbulent era of ingenuity and innovation, of deals and frauds, of fortunes made, fortunes lost, fortunes never made, of grueling hard work and bitter disappointments, and of astonishing growth.

And what might be expected of oil's future? There were those who saw much greater opportunities ahead, who envisioned the industry on a scale few in the Oil Regions could begin to imagine. Yet at the same time they were repelled and disgusted by the chaos and disorder. They had their own very strong ideas about how the oil business ought to be organized and proceed. And they were already at work, according to their own plans.

---

## Chapter 2: "Our Plan" — John D. Rockefeller and the Combination of American Oil (p40-45, continued next batch)

A curious auction took place one February day in 1865 in Cleveland, Ohio — then a bustling city that had profited from both the Civil War and the oil boom and now stood to prosper from America's industrial expansion. The two senior partners in one of the city's most successful oil refineries had fallen into yet another of their chronic disputes over the speed of expansion. Maurice Clark, the more cautious partner, threatened dissolution. This time, John D. Rockefeller surprised him by accepting. They agreed on a private auction — the highest bidder would get the company, held right there in the office.

The bidding began at $500, climbed quickly. Maurice Clark was soon at $72,000. Rockefeller calmly went to $72,500. Clark threw up his hands: "I'll go no higher, John. The business is yours." On a handshake they parted.

"I ever point to that day," Rockefeller said a half century later, "as the beginning of the success I have made in my life." That handshake also signaled the beginning of the modern oil industry, which would bring order out of the chaos of the wild Pennsylvania boom. The order would take the form of Standard Oil, which, as it sought total dominance and mastery over the world oil trade, grew into a complex global enterprise carrying the "new light" to the farthest corners of the earth — operating according to the merciless methods and unbridled lust of late-nineteenth-century capitalism, yet opening a new era as one of the world's first and biggest multinational corporations.

### *"Methodical to an Extreme"*

The mastermind of Standard Oil was the young man who won that auction in Cleveland in 1865. Even then, at twenty-six, John D. Rockefeller already made a forbidding impression. Tall and thin, he struck others as solitary, taciturn, remote, and ascetic. His unbending quietness — combined with cold, piercing blue eyes set in an angular face with a sharp chin — made people uneasy and fearful. Somehow, they felt, he could look right through them.

Rockefeller was the single most important figure in shaping the oil industry. The same might arguably be said for his place in the history of America's industrial development and the rise of the modern corporation. Admired by some as a genius of management and organization, he also came to rank as the most hated and reviled American businessman — in part because he was so ruthless and in part because he was so successful.

Rockefeller was born in 1839 in rural New York State and lived almost a full century, until 1937. His father, William Rockefeller, traded in lumber and salt, then turned himself into "Dr. William Rockefeller" who sold herbal remedies and patent medicines. The father was often away on long absences; the reason, some suggested, was that he maintained another wife and family in Canada. "I trade with the boys," the father boasted, "and skin 'em and I just beat 'em every time I can. I want to make 'em sharp."

The son's character was already set at a young age — pious, single-minded, persistent, thorough, attentive to detail, with a gift and fascination for numbers, especially those involving money. At age seven he launched his first successful venture — selling turkeys. Mathematics was his best subject in high school, which stressed mental arithmetic — the ability to do calculations quickly in one's head.

Intent on achieving "something big," Rockefeller went to work at age sixteen in Cleveland for a produce-shipping firm. In 1859 he formed his own partnership with Maurice Clark to trade produce. The firm prospered from demand generated by the Civil War and the opening of the West. Clark would later testify that Rockefeller was "methodical to an extreme." As the firm grew, Rockefeller stuck to his habit of holding "intimate conversations" with himself, counseling himself, repeating homilies, warning himself to beware of pitfalls. The firm dealt in Ohio wheat, Michigan salt, and Illinois pork. Within a couple of years of Drake's discovery, Clark and Rockefeller were also dealing in Pennsylvania oil.

Oil had already captured the imagination of entrepreneurial men in Cleveland, when, in 1863, a new railroad link placed Cleveland in a position to compete. Refinery after refinery sprang into existence along the railway tracks. Many were desperately undercapitalized, but this was never true of the one owned by Rockefeller and Clark. Rockefeller initially thought refining would merely be a sideline to the produce business, but within a year, as the refinery became quite profitable, he became convinced otherwise. Now, in 1865, with the auction and Clark out of the way, Rockefeller was master of his own business — the largest of Cleveland's thirty refineries.

### *The Great Game*

Rockefeller won his first victory in refining at a perfect time. The end of the Civil War in 1865 inaugurated an era of massive economic expansion, fierce speculation and competition, and combination and monopoly. Large-scale enterprises rose with technological advances in steel, meat packing, and communication. Heavy immigration and the opening of the West made for rapidly growing markets. Indeed, in the last three and a half decades of the nineteenth century, the business of America was truly business. Young men were irresistibly drawn to what Rockefeller called "the Great Game" — the struggle to accomplish and build, and the drive to make money.

As the oil boom progressed, Rockefeller threw himself wholeheartedly into the Great Game, pouring both profits and borrowed money into his refinery. He built a second one. He needed new markets for his growing capacity, and in 1866 organized another firm in New York to manage both the Atlantic Coast trade and the export of kerosene, putting his brother William in charge. In that year, his sales exceeded two million dollars.

Yet markets for kerosene and lubricants were not growing fast enough to match refinery capacity growth. Too many companies were competing for the same customers. It didn't take much to set oneself up as a refiner. As Rockefeller recalled, "All sorts of people went into it: the butcher, the baker, and the candlestick-maker began to refine oil." Rockefeller and his associates became quite concerned when they learned that a German baker they liked had foolishly traded his bakery for a low-quality refinery. They bought him out in order to get him back to baking.

Rockefeller devoted himself to strengthening his business — expanding facilities, improving quality, and always controlling costs. He took the first steps toward **integration** — bringing supply and distribution inside the organization. His firm acquired its own tracts of land for white oak timber to make barrels; bought its own tank cars, warehouses in New York, and boats on the Hudson River. Rockefeller also established another principle he religiously stuck to — to build up and maintain a strong cash position. Already, before the end of the 1860s, he had built up sufficient financial resources so that his company would not have to depend upon bankers, financiers, and speculators. The cash not only insulated the company from violent busts and depressions, but enabled it to take advantage of such downturns.

One of Rockefeller's great talents was already discernible: he had a vision of where his company and the overall industry were going, and yet at the same time persisted in commanding the critical daily details. "As I began my business life as a bookkeeper, I learned to have great respect for figures and facts, no matter how small they were." He kept an old suit that he would wear to tramp around in the muddy Oil Regions fields, buying oil. By the latter part of the 1860s, Rockefeller owned what was probably the largest refinery in the world.

In 1867, Rockefeller was joined by Henry Flagler, whose influence in the creation of Standard Oil was almost as great as Rockefeller's. Going to work at age fourteen as a clerk in a general store, Flagler had succeeded, by his mid-twenties, in making a small fortune distilling whiskey in Ohio. He sold out in 1858 because of moral scruples about alcohol. He then threw himself into salt manufacturing in Michigan but in circumstances of chaotic competition and over-supply, he went broke — a sobering experience for a man to whom making money had initially come so easily.

Still, Flagler was an eternally buoyant man, determined to rebound, now matured by hard-won lessons. His bankruptcy left him with a deep-seated belief in the value of "cooperation" among producers and a no less deep-seated aversion to "unbridled competition." He had also learned another lesson: "Keep your head above water and bet on the growth of your country."

Flagler became the closest colleague Rockefeller ever had, and one of his closest friends. Their relationship led Flagler to another adage: "A friendship founded on business is better than a business founded on friendship." Energetic and striving, Flagler was well matched to the dour, careful Rockefeller, who was delighted to acquire a partner so "full of vim and push." To a critic, however, Flagler looked somewhat different — "a bold, unscrupulous self-seeker [who] made no bones about conscience. He did whatever was necessary to success." Many years later, after making one great fortune with Rockefeller, Flagler set off on a second conquest — the development of the state of Florida. He would build the railways down the east coast all the way to the Keys, opening up what he called the "American Riviera," and was to found both Miami and West Palm Beach.

In these building years, Rockefeller and Flagler worked in close harness, sitting in the same office with desks back to back, passing drafts of letters to customers and suppliers back and forth until they said exactly what they wanted. "On those walks," Rockefeller said, "when we were away from the office interruptions, we did our thinking, talking, and planning together."

Flagler devised and ran the transportation arrangements, which would prove central to the success of Standard Oil — for they gave the company a decisive power against all competitors. Without Flagler's expertise and aggressiveness in this realm, there might well have been no Standard Oil as the world came to know it.

The size, efficiency, and economies of scale of Rockefeller's organization enabled it to extract **rebates** — discounts on railway freight rates — which lowered its transportation costs below what competitors paid, providing a potent advantage in pricing and profit. These rebates would later be a source of great controversy. Many charged that Standard forced the rebates to undercut competitors unfairly. But competition among railroads for freight was so intense that rebates and discounts of one kind or another became common practice across the nation, especially for anyone who could guarantee large, regular shipments. Flagler was very good at driving the best deal possible.

Standard did not stop with rebates. It also used its prowess to win **"drawbacks."** A competing shipper might pay a dollar a barrel to send his oil by rail to New York. The railroad would turn around and pay twenty-five cents of that dollar back, not to the shipper, but to the shipper's rival — Standard Oil. What this practice really meant was that competitors were, unknowingly, subsidizing Standard Oil. Few business practices did as much to rouse public antipathy toward Standard Oil as these drawbacks.

### *"Now Try Our Plan"*

While the market for oil was growing at an extraordinary rate, the amount of oil seeking markets was growing even more rapidly, resulting in wild price fluctuations and frequent collapses. Toward the end of the 1860s, overproduction caused prices to plummet again, and the new industry went into a depression. Between 1865 and 1870, the retail price of kerosene fell by more than half. It was estimated that refining capacity was three times greater than the market's needs.

The costs of overcapacity were obvious to Rockefeller, and it was in these circumstances that he launched his effort to consolidate the industry in his own grasp. He and Flagler wanted to bring in more capital without jeopardizing control. The technique they used was to turn their partnership into a joint stock company. On **January 10, 1870**, five men, led by Rockefeller and Flagler, established the **Standard Oil Company**. The name was chosen to indicate a "standard quality of product" on which the consumer could depend. At that time, kerosene of widely varying quality was sold — if it contained too much flammable gasoline or naphtha, lighting it could be your last act on earth. Rockefeller held a quarter of the stock, which at that time already controlled a tenth of the American refining industry. "Who would ever have thought it would grow to such a size?"

Newly constituted with more capital, Standard pursued even more vigorously the railroad rebates. But overall business conditions continued to deteriorate, and by 1871 the refining industry was in a complete panic. Profit margins were disappearing, and most refiners were losing money. Rockefeller had married Laura Celestia Spelman in 1864. In her high school graduation essay, "I Can Paddle My Own Canoe," she had written, "The independence of woman in thought, deed, or will is one of the problems of the age." While giving up her dream of paddling her own canoe, she became his closest confidante. Now, in 1872, in the midst of the refinery depression, he reassured her: "You know, we are independently rich outside of investments in oil."

It was at this anxious time that Rockefeller conceived his bold vision of consolidating nearly all oil refining into one giant combination. "It was desirable to do something to save the business," he later said. An actual combination would do what a mere pool or association could not: eliminate excess capacity, suppress wild fluctuations of price — and, indeed, save the business. That was what Rockefeller and his colleagues meant when they talked of **"our plan."** "The idea was mine," he said much later. "The idea was persisted in, too, in spite of the opposition of some who became faint-hearted at the magnitude of the undertaking, as it constantly assumed larger proportions."

Standard geared up for the campaign, increasing its capitalization to facilitate takeovers. But events were moving in another direction as well. In February 1872, a local railway official in Pennsylvania became confused and abruptly doubled the cost of carrying crude from the Oil Regions to New York. Word leaked that the increase was the doing of an unknown entity called the **South Improvement Company**. The independent producers and refiners in the Oil Regions were aroused and alarmed.

The South Improvement Company was the embodiment of another scheme for stabilization of the oil industry and would become the symbol of the effort to achieve monopoly control. Though the idea actually belonged to the railroads, trying to find a way out of bitter rate wars, Rockefeller's name was to be ever more associated with it. Under the scheme, railroads and refiners would band together in cartels and divide markets. The refiners would not only get rebates on their shipments, but also receive those drawbacks — rebates from the full rates paid by nonmember refiners. "Of all the devices for the extinction of competition," one biographer has written, "this was the cruelest and most deadly yet conceived by any group of American industrialists."

Though still cloaked in mystery, the South Improvement Company enraged the Oil Regions. A Pittsburgh newspaper warned it would create "but one buyer of oil in the whole oil region." Three thousand angry men trooped with banners into the Titusville Opera House to denounce it. Thus was launched what became known as the **Oil War**. Producers marched from town to town to denounce "the Monster" and "the Forty Thieves." United against monopoly, they launched a boycott of the refiners and railroads so effective that Standard refineries in Cleveland had only enough crude to occupy seventy of their twelve hundred men. But Rockefeller had absolutely no doubts: "It is easy to write newspaper articles but we have other business," he told his wife. "We will do right and not be nervous or troubled by what the papers say." And: "It is not the business of the public to change our private contracts."

By April 1872, both the railroads and the refiners, including Rockefeller, had decided to disown and scuttle the South Improvement Company. The Oil War was over, apparently won by the producers. Later, Rockefeller would say he had always expected the South Improvement Company to fail, but went along for his own purposes. "When it failed, we would be in a position to say, 'Now try our plan.'" But Rockefeller had not even waited for it to fail. By the spring of 1872, he had already won control over most of Cleveland's refining and some of the most important refiners in New York City — making him the master of the largest refinery group in the world. He was ready to take on the entire oil industry.

The 1870s were marked by ever-rising production. Producers repeatedly tried to restrict production, but to no avail. Storage tanks overflowed. Crude oil was run out into streams and onto farms. At one point, the price dropped to forty-eight cents a barrel — three cents less than housewives in the Oil Regions were paying for drinking water. Efforts to organize shutdown movements always failed. There were far too many producers — estimates ranged as high as sixteen thousand firms. Many were speculators, farmers, highly individualistic and unlikely to take "a long view." Rockefeller, with his passion for order, looked with revulsion at the chaos. "The Oil Regions," he said with acid disdain, "was a mining camp." His target was the refiners.

### *"War or Peace"*

The objective of Rockefeller's audacious battle plan was, in his words, to end "that cut-throat policy of making no profits" and "make the oil business safe and profitable" — under his control. Rockefeller was both strategist and supreme commander, directing his lieutenants to move with stealth and speed. His brother William categorized relations with other refiners in terms of "war or peace."

Standard began, in each area, by attempting to buy out the leading refiners, the dominant firms. Rockefeller and his associates would approach their targets with deference, politeness, and flattery. They would demonstrate how profitable Standard Oil was compared with other refiners struggling through hard times. If all that failed, Standard would bring a tough competitor to heel by making him "feel sick" or giving him "a good sweating." Standard would cut prices in that particular market, forcing the competitor to operate at a loss. At one point, Standard orchestrated a "barrel famine" to put pressure on recalcitrant refiners. In another battle, Henry Flagler instructed: "If you think the perspiration don't roll off freely enough, pile the blankets on him. I would rather lose a great deal of money than to yield a pint to him at this time."

The Standard men, moving in great secrecy, operated through firms that appeared independent to the outside world but had in fact become part of the Standard Group. Many refiners never knew that their local competitors cutting prices were actually part of Rockefeller's growing empire. Through all phases of the campaign, the Standard men communicated in code — Standard Oil itself was "Morose." Rockefeller never wavered in his defense of secrecy. "But I wonder what General of the Allies ever sends out a brass band in advance with orders to notify the enemy that on a certain day he will begin an attack?"

By **1879**, the war was virtually over. Standard Oil was triumphant. It **controlled 90 percent of America's refining capacity.** It also controlled the pipelines and gathering system of the Oil Regions and dominated transportation. Rockefeller was unemotional in victory, bearing no grudge. Some of the conquered were brought into Standard's inner councils to become devoted allies.

### *New Threats*

At the very end of the 1870s, just when Rockefeller thought he had everything tied up, Pennsylvania producers made one last effort to break out of Standard's suffocating embrace — the world's first attempt at a **long-distance pipeline**. The project, named the **Tidewater Pipeline**, had no precedent. The oil would travel eastward from the Oil Regions to a connection with the Pennsylvania and Reading Railroad. Its construction was carried out with both deception and dispatch — fake surveys were taken to throw Standard off. By May 1879, oil was flowing in the pipeline. It was a major technological achievement, comparable to the Brooklyn Bridge four years later, and introduced a new stage in the history of oil — the pipeline would become a major competitor with the railroad for long-distance transportation.

The clear success of Tidewater caught Standard by surprise and meant that its control of the industry was suddenly in jeopardy. Standard sprang into action, building four long-distance pipelines from the Oil Regions to Cleveland, New York, Philadelphia, and Buffalo. Within two years, Standard was a minority stockholder in Tidewater itself and had an arrangement to pool shipments. With the partial exception of Tidewater, Standard controlled almost every inch of pipeline into and out of the Oil Regions.

There remained only one way to hold this giant in check — through the political system and the courts. At the end of the 1870s, producers from the Oil Regions launched a series of legal assaults in Pennsylvania against discriminatory rates. They denounced "the overweening control of the oil business by the Standard Oil Company," castigated it as an "Autocrat" and "this gang of thieves." Legislative hearings in New York focused on Standard Oil's rebate system. The investigations and legal proceedings in the two states marked the first public revelation of the activities of Standard Oil. A Pennsylvania grand jury indicted Rockefeller, Flagler, and several associates for conspiracy to create a monopoly and injure competitors. A vigorous effort was made to extradite Rockefeller to Pennsylvania. He was alarmed enough to exact a promise from the Governor of New York not to approve any extradition order, and the attempt eventually failed.

Still, the cumulative effect on public opinion was devastating and lasting. The charges against Standard were brought together for the first time by **Henry Demarest Lloyd**, in editorials for the *Chicago Tribune* and then in an article entitled "The Story of a Great Monopoly," published in the *Atlantic Monthly* in 1881 — so great was the attention that the issue went through seven printings. Lloyd declared that the Standard Oil Company had done everything to the Pennsylvania State Legislature except refine it. Yet the article had little immediate impact on Standard's business. In the Oil Regions, mothers would warn their children, "Rockefeller will get you if you don't mind."

### *The Trust*

While the courts and public opinion had to be kept at bay, an ingenious internal order and control was created in the vast empire Rockefeller had conquered. There was no clear legal basis for the association of these various refineries around the country. Thus, Rockefeller could say with a straight face and without perjuring himself that Standard Oil itself did not own or control a host of companies that it manifestly did control. The real clue was that it was the *stockholders* of Standard Oil, not Standard Oil itself, who owned shares in the other firms. At that time, corporations themselves could not own stock in other corporations. The shares were held "in trust," not for the Standard Oil Company of Ohio, but on behalf of the stockholders of that corporation.

The legal concept of the "trust" was refined and formalized in the **Standard Oil Trust Agreement**, signed on **January 2, 1882**. It was also a response to judicial and political attacks. There was also a personal reason — Rockefeller and his partners had begun to think about mortality and inheritance, and they concluded that the death of one of them would lead to confusion, controversy, and litigation under the existing system. A trust would get the ownership organized.

In preparing the trust, "every foot of pipeline was measured, every particle of brickwork was estimated." A board of trustees was set up, and the stock of all entities controlled by Standard Oil was placed in the trust. Out of 700,000 total shares, Rockefeller held 191,700 and Flagler, next, had 60,000. The trustees held shares on behalf of the forty-one shareowners and were charged with "general supervision" of fourteen wholly owned and twenty-six partly owned companies. It was the first great "trust," and it was perfectly legal. But this was also why "trust," formerly a device for protecting widows and orphans, became a term of derogation and hatred.

The trust agreement made possible a central office to coordinate and rationalize the activities of the various operating entities. The trust gave Rockefeller and his associates "the shield of legality and the administrative flexibility they needed to operate effectively what had become virtually global properties."

What evolved was a system of management and coordination by committee. There was a Domestic Trade Committee, an Export Trade Committee, a Manufacturing Committee, a Staves and Heading Committee, a Pipe Line Committee, a Case Committee, a Lubricating Committee, and later a Production Committee. Daily reports flowed in from around the country. On top of it all was the **Executive Committee**, composed of the top managers, which set overall policies and directions. The Executive Committee did not issue orders so much as requests, suggestions, and recommendations — but no one doubted its authority or control. "You gentlemen on the ground can judge better than we about the matter, but let us not drift into arrangements where we cannot control the policy."

A basic strategy that had governed Standard in the 1870s became even more explicit in the 1880s — to be the **low-cost producer**. This required efficiency in operations, mastery of costs, a drive for scale and volume, constant attention to technology, and ceaseless striving for ever-larger markets. Refining operations were consolidated; by the middle 1880s, just three Standard refineries — in Cleveland, Philadelphia, and Bayonne, New Jersey — produced upward of a quarter of the world's total supply of kerosene. The focus on costs never wavered. "It has always been my rule in business to make everything count," Rockefeller said. The company also used an extraordinary system of corporate intelligence and espionage to track market conditions and competitors — maintaining a card catalog of practically every buyer of oil in the country, showing where virtually every barrel shipped by independent dealers went.

A central theme underlay Rockefeller's management: he *believed* in oil, and his faith never wavered. Any drop in the price of crude was not a reason for anxiety, but an opportunity to buy. "Hope if crude oil goes down again ... our Executive Committee will not allow any amount of statistics or information ... to prevent their buying," he instructed in 1884. "We must try and not lose our nerve when the market gets to the bottom as some people almost always do." Shortly after, he added, *"We will surely make a great mistake if we do not buy."*

The senior management included Rockefeller, his brother William, Henry Flagler, and two others who altogether controlled four-sevenths of the stock. But it also extended to perhaps a dozen others, virtually all willful, assertive individuals who had been successful entrepreneurs — and, originally, competitors of Rockefeller. "It is not always the easiest of tasks to induce strong, forceful men to agree." The only way such a grouping could work was by consensus. "It is always, I presume, a question in every business just how fast it is wise to go, and we went pretty rapidly on those days, building and expanding in all directions."

The senior managers were frequently found shuttling between Cleveland, New York, Pittsburgh, Buffalo, Baltimore, and Philadelphia. In 1885, the trust moved into new headquarters, a nine-story office building at **26 Broadway**, in lower Manhattan, which soon became a landmark. The senior executives lunched together daily in a private dining room at the top of the building. Over the meal, vital information was exchanged, ideas examined, consensus built. "These men are smarter than I am a great deal," William Vanderbilt of the New York Central Railroad told the legislature. "They are very enterprising and smart men. I never came into contact with any class of men so smart and able as they are in their business."

### *"The Wise Old Owl"*

But the smartest was certainly John D. Rockefeller. At the time the trust was formed, he was in his early forties, already one of the half-dozen richest men in America. He was also strangely, deliberately, inaccessible. Later in life, he recited a little rhyme: "A wise old owl lived in an oak, / The more he saw the less he spoke, / The less he spoke, the more he heard, / Why aren't we all like that old bird?"

He had resolved from the beginning to "expose as little surface as possible." His remoteness and icy, penetrating stare were unnerving. On one occasion, Rockefeller met in Pittsburgh with a group of refiners. Afterward, the talk centered on the taciturn, ungregarious, menacing man from Cleveland. "I've been watching him," one finally said. "He lets everybody else talk, while he sits back and says nothing. But he seems to remember everything, and when he does begin he puts everything in its proper place ... I guess he's 140 years old — for he must have been 100 years old when he was born."

Many years later, one who worked for Rockefeller described him as "the most unemotional man I have ever known." Yet behind the mask, there was a man. The 1870s and 1880s were years when "our plan" reached its fruition, but those years of consolidation and integration, of unexpected political and press attacks, were also years of great strain and tension. "All the fortune that I have made has not served to compensate me for the anxiety of that period," Rockefeller once said. His wife remembered "days of worry," and he himself recalled he "seldom got an unbroken night's sleep."

He sought relaxation in different ways. Late in the day, during business meetings, he would lie down on a couch and participate from there. He kept a primitive muscle extender in his office. He had a special love for horses, fast horses, and would take them out for a carriage ride at the end of the day — "trot, pace, gallop, everything" — after which he would rejuvenate and "take up the evening's mail and get ten letters off."

Outside of business, his life centered on his Baptist church, where he was superintendent of the Sunday school. His Forest Hill estate outside Cleveland absorbed him with the same obsessive attention to detail he brought to business — the building of fireplaces, planting trees, cutting roads through woods. He moved later to a vast estate in the Pocantico hills, north of New York City, where he directed landscaping, constructed views, and worked at laying out new roads himself with stakes and flags.

Even while becoming the richest man in America, he maintained a curious frugality. He wore the same old suits until they became so shiny they had to be replaced. One favorite dish remained bread and milk. Once in Cleveland, he invited a prominent local businessman and his wife to stay at Forest Hill for the summer. They spent a pleasant six weeks — then were surprised to receive a bill of six hundred dollars from Rockefeller for board.

He was not without a sense of humor, though he displayed it only in restricted circles. "Have been in the dentist's chair," he once reported to Flagler. "Think would have preferred to write you, or even read your letters, but could not help myself!" He would entertain his family by singing at dinner, or by putting a cracker on his nose and catching it in his mouth, or balancing a plate on his nose. He loved to sit with his children and play a game called "Buzz" — counting numbers and saying "Buzz" instead of any number with a seven. Somehow, despite his gift for mathematics, he could never get beyond 71. The children found this hilarious.

Rockefeller had begun making small donations to his church as soon as he started earning money. Over time, he devoted increasing efforts to giving away a significant part of his wealth, applying the same methodical investigation he brought to business. Eventually his donations would extend through the sciences, medicine, and education. In the nineteenth century, much of his philanthropy was oriented to the Baptist church.

At the end of the 1880s, he committed himself to the creation of a great Baptist university — providing the endowment and the organizational focus for the establishment of the **University of Chicago**. He continued as by far its largest donor. Though he paid keen attention to its development, he did not interfere in its academic workings, save to insist it stay within its budget. He refused to allow buildings to be named after him. By 1910, he had given to the university $35 million, compared to $7 million from all other sources. Altogether, he was to give away some $550 million.

He carried over his habits of business to his private life. In the Gilded Age of "robber barons," his New York townhouse and Pocantico estate were opulent, but Rockefeller and his family stood apart from the garishness and ostentation of the age. He sought to inculcate values of probity in his children and avoid having them ruined by inherited riches — the children shared only one tricycle so they might learn to share. Young John D. Rockefeller, Jr., in New York City, was made to walk to and from school even as other children of the rich were carried back and forth in rigs accompanied by grooms, and he earned pocket money working on his father's estates for the same wages as the laborers.

In 1888, Rockefeller took his family and two Baptist ministers to Europe for three months. Though he did not know French, he would scrutinize each item on every bill. "Poulets!" he would exclaim. "What are poulets?" Told they were chickens, he would go on to the next item. "Father," John Junior would later recall, "was never willing to pay a bill which he did not know to be correct in all its items. Such care in small things might seem penurious to some people, yet to him it was the working out of a life principle."

### *A Marvel to the Eye*

The company Rockefeller founded continued to expand during the 1880s and into the 1890s. Scientific research was incorporated into the business. Great attention was devoted to quality of product and neatness of operations. The growth of the marketing system — down to the final consumer — was an imperative of the business. "We needed volume." And it surely and steadily moved to ever-higher volumes. The growth in the use of oil, largely as kerosene, was stupendous.

Oil and the kerosene lamp were changing American life — and the clock by which Americans lived. Whether in towns and cities of the East or farms of the Midwest, consumers usually bought their kerosene from their grocer or druggist, supplied by a wholesaler, most of whom were supplied by Standard Oil. As early as 1864, a New York chemist described the impact: "Kerosene has, in one sense, increased the length of life among the agricultural population. Those who, on account of the dearness or inefficiency of whale oil, were accustomed to go to bed soon after the sunset and spend almost half their time in sleep, now occupy a portion of the night in reading and other amusements."

Practical advice on kerosene — showing its quick acceptance — was provided in 1869 by Harriet Beecher Stowe, who assisted her sister with *American Woman's Home or Principles of Domestic Science*. "Good kerosene gives a light which leaves little to be desired," they wrote, but warned against poor quality and impure oils responsible for "those terrible explosions." In the mid-1870s, five to six thousand deaths a year were attributed to such accidents. This was why Rockefeller insisted on consistency and quality control, and why he had chosen the name Standard.

Kerosene was by far the most important product coming out of refineries, but not the only one. Other products included naphtha, used as a solvent or turned into a gas for illuminating buildings; gasoline; fuel oil; lubricants for train engines, railway cars, agricultural implements, cotton spindles, and later bicycles. Other products were petroleum jelly, trademarked as "Vaseline," paraffin for candle making and food preservation, and "paraffin chewing gum," which was "highly recommended for constant use in ladies in sewing circles."

In its effort to reach the consumer, Standard Oil moved to gain control over the marketing side of the business. By the mid-1880s, its control of marketing must have been almost equivalent to its control of refining — in the 80 percent range. Its tactics in acquiring that huge market share were just as ruthless. Its salesmen would "make a fist" and seek to intimidate rivals and retailers who dared to carry competing products. Standard pushed innovations to make marketing more efficient and lower costs. One innovation was the railway tank car, replacing the need to pile barrels into boxcars. Standard also replaced barrels on the streets with horse-drawn tank cars that could disburse anything from a pint to five gallons of kerosene.

### *"Buy All We Can Get"*

But Standard had stayed out of one critical part of the business — the production of oil. It was too risky, too volatile, too speculative. As one Executive Committee member wrote Rockefeller in 1885, "Our business is that of manufacturers, and it is in my judgment, an unfortunate thing for any manufacturer or merchant to allow his mind to have the care and friction which attends speculative ventures."

But a sense of precariousness underlay Standard's great globe-girdling system. There was always the fear that the oil would run out. Flush production quickly exhausted wells. Pennsylvania was the only game, and perhaps what had happened at Pithole could happen to the entire Oil Regions. In 1885, the State Geologist of Pennsylvania warned that "the amazing exhibition of oil" was only "a temporary and vanishing phenomenon — one which young men will live to see come to its natural end."

That same year, John Archbold, a top Standard executive, was told by a company specialist that decline in American production was almost inevitable and chances of finding another large field were "at least one hundred to one against it." These warnings were so persuasive that Archbold sold some of his Standard shares at seventy-five to eighty cents on the dollar. When also told about signs of oil in Oklahoma, he replied, "Are you crazy? Why, I'll drink every gallon produced west of the Mississippi!"

But just at that moment, the industry was about to break out of Pennsylvania — with dramatic suddenness. The scene was northwestern Ohio, where flammable gas springs in the vicinity of Findlay had been known since the earliest settlements. In the mid-1880s, oil was discovered there, igniting a great boom in the region that straddled the border with Indiana — the **Lima-Indiana fields**. By 1890, they accounted for a third of United States oil production.

Rockefeller was poised to make his last great strategic decision — to go directly into oil production. No less than his colleagues, he had great antipathy for oil producers — speculators, unreliable, behaving like greedy miners in a gold rush. Yet here, in Lima, was an opportunity for Standard to gain control of its raw materials on a very large scale. In short, Standard would be able to insulate itself against the fluctuations and volatility of the oil market — and against the disorder of the "mining camp." And that was the direction in which Rockefeller very definitely wanted to go.

But there were two major obstacles. One was the quality of the petroleum — it had very different properties from Pennsylvania crude, including a most unappealing sulfuric odor, like rotten eggs. Some called the Lima crude "skunk juice." There was no known way to remove the odor, and until one was found, the Ohio oil had only a very limited market.

The second obstacle was located at 26 Broadway — the obstinacy of Rockefeller's more cautious colleagues. They thought the risk much too great. Rockefeller argued the company should buy up all the oil and store it in tanks. The oil was flowing in such huge volumes that the price dropped from forty cents a barrel in 1886 to fifteen cents in 1887. But many of Rockefeller's colleagues strongly opposed buying oil for which there was not yet any good use. "Our conservative brethren on the Board," as Rockefeller called them, "held up their hands in holy terror and desperately fought a few of us."
