Eventually, however, Rockefeller prevailed, and Standard Oil put more than 40 million barrels of Lima oil in storage. Then, in 1888 and 1889, **Herman Frasch**, a German chemist employed by Standard, figured out that if the crude oil were refined in the presence of copper oxide, the sulfur could be removed, eliminating the rotten-eggs smell and making Lima oil an acceptable source of kerosene. Rockefeller's Lima gamble proved well worth it — after Frasch's breakthrough, the price of Lima oil immediately doubled from the fifteen cents a barrel at which Standard had acquired it to thirty cents, and continued to climb.

Rockefeller pushed the company toward the final step of buying up producing properties. His colleagues were reluctant, even opposed. Rockefeller was insistent. He carried the day. Of the leases available for purchase he simply ordered **"Buy all we can get."** By 1891, though virtually absent from production a few years earlier, Standard was itself responsible for a quarter of America's total output of crude oil.

Standard committed itself to building the world's largest refinery at a place called **Whiting**, amidst desolate sand dunes on the shore of Lake Michigan in Indiana, to process the Lima crude. There, as everywhere, Standard's cult of secrecy was at work. A reporter from the *Chicago Tribune* found it impossible to get information from Mr. Marshall, the close-mouthed manager: "They might be erecting a $5 million dollar oil refinery or they might be putting up a pork packing establishment. He didn't think it was a pork packing establishment, but he wasn't sure."

Then there was the matter of the price itself. For many years, prices had reflected trading on oil exchanges. Through the 1880s, the **Joseph Seep Agency**, the buying arm of Standard Oil, bought oil on the open market by acquiring "certificates" on these exchanges. When the Seep Agency bought directly at the wellhead, it averaged the day's highest and lowest prices from the exchanges. Increasingly, Seep bought directly from producers, and transactions on the exchanges fell steadily.

Finally, in January 1895, Joseph Seep closed down the era of the oil exchanges with a historic "Notice to Oil Producers." He announced that "dealing" on the exchanges was "no longer a reliable indication of the value of the product." From then on, "the price paid will be as high as the markets of the world will justify." As either purchaser or owner of between 85 and 90 percent of the oil in Pennsylvania and Lima-Indiana, Seep and Standard Oil now effectively determined the purchase price for American crude oil, though always bound by supply and demand.

### *The Upbuilder*

In every dimension, the scale of Standard's operations was awesome, overwhelming competitors. Yet it was not a complete monopoly — somewhere around 15 to 20 percent of oil was sold by competitors, and Standard's directors were willing to live with that. Control of upwards of 85 percent of the market was sufficient to maintain stability. Reflecting upon his landscaping and tree growing, Rockefeller observed in old age, "In nursery stock, as in other things, the advantage of doing things on a large scale reveals itself."

Rockefeller created the **vertically integrated petroleum company**. Many years later, one of his successors at Standard Oil of Ohio mused: "He instinctively realized that orderliness would only proceed from a centralized control of large aggregations of plant and capital, with the one aim of an orderly flow of products from the producer to the consumer. That orderly, economical, efficient flow was what we now, many years later, call 'vertical integration.' I do not know whether Mr. Rockefeller ever used the word 'integration.' I only know he conceived the idea."

The US government's *Mineral Resources* declared in 1882: "There seems to be little doubt that the company has done a great work, and that through its instrumentality oil refining has been reduced to a business, and transportation has been greatly simplified; but as to how much evil has been mixed with this good, it is not practicable to make a definite statement."

For others — Standard's competitors and a good part of the public — the judgment was completely negative. To many producers and independent refiners, Standard Oil was the **Octopus**, out to grasp all competitors, "body and soul." To those who suffered from Rockefeller's machinations, he was a bloodless monster who hypocritically invoked the Lord as he methodically set about destroying people's livelihoods.

Some colleagues were grieved by the criticism. "We have met with a success unparalleled in commercial history, our name is known all over the world, and our public character is not one to be envied," one wrote Rockefeller in 1887. "We are quoted as the representative of all that is evil, hard hearted, oppressive, cruel (we think unjustly)." Rockefeller himself was not so troubled. He even sought to enlist Protestant evangelists and Social Gospel clergy in defense of Standard Oil. He remained confident that Standard Oil was an instrument for human betterment, replacing chaos and volatility with stability, delivering the gift of the "new light" to the world of darkness. "Give the poor man his cheap light, gentlemen," he would tell the Executive Committee. "The day of combination is here to stay," Rockefeller said after he had stepped aside from active management. "Individualism has gone, never to return." Standard Oil was one of the greatest, perhaps even the greatest, of "upbuilders we ever had in this country."

Mark Twain and Charles Dudley Warner, in *The Gilded Age*, grasped the character of the decades after the Civil War — "the manufacture of giant schemes, of speculations of all sorts ... [and of] inflamed desire for sudden wealth." Rockefeller was in some ways the true embodiment of his age. Standard Oil was a merciless competitor that would "cut to kill," and he became the wealthiest of all. Yet, whereas many other robber barons amassed their wealth by speculation, stock and financial manipulation, and outright fraud — cheating their stockholders — Rockefeller built his fortune by taking on a youthful, wild, unpredictable, and unreliable industry, and relentlessly transforming it according to his own logic into a highly organized, far-flung business that satisfied the basic hunger for light around the world.

"Our plan" was to succeed even beyond Rockefeller's boldest visions, but it would ultimately fail. In the United States, public opinion and the political process would revolt against combination and monopoly. At the same time, new individuals and new companies — operating beyond Rockefeller's reach in the US and in faraway places like Baku, Sumatra, Burma, and later Persia — would rise up to prove themselves hardy and persistent competitors. And some would do more than survive; they would flourish.

---

## Chapter 3: Competitive Commerce (p75-85, continued next batch)

Though the rest of the world was waiting for the "new light" from America, it had been no easy thing to get the first shipment of oil off to Europe. Sailors were terrified about explosions and fires. Finally, in 1861, a Philadelphia shipper obtained a crew by getting the potential recruits drunk and virtually shanghai-ing them aboard. That cargo made its way safely to London. The door to global trade was opened, and American oil quickly won markets throughout the world. Virtually from the very beginning, petroleum was an international business. The American oil industry could not have grown to the size it did without its foreign markets. In Europe, rapid demand increase was stimulated by industrialization, economic growth, urbanization, and a shortage of fats and oils. The development of various markets was speeded by United States consuls eager to push "this new Yankee invention," sometimes purchasing oil out of their own pockets to distribute to potential customers.

Consider what the global demand meant: the substance for the popular form of lighting worldwide was provided not merely by one country, but, for the most part, by one state — Pennsylvania. Never again would any single region have such a grasp on supply of the raw material. Almost overnight, the export business became immensely important. In the 1870s and 1880s, kerosene exports accounted for over half of total American oil output. Kerosene was the fourth-largest US export in value; the first among manufactured goods. And Europe was by far the largest market.

By the end of the 1870s, not only was one state dominant, but so was one company — Standard Oil. Eventually, at least 90 percent of exported kerosene passed through Standard's hands. Standard was satisfied with a system in which its role ended in an American port. Rockefeller, indeed, intended to impose "our plan" on the entire world. Petroleum, said Standard Oil's chief foreign representative, had "forced its way into more nooks and corners of civilized and uncivilized countries than any other product in business history emanating from a single source."

There was, of course, a danger — the potential of foreign competition. But the men at 26 Broadway discounted that possibility. The Pennsylvania Geological Report of 1874 proudly commented on how thoroughly the state's oil dominated world markets. It mentioned in passing "the drill in other countries ... would find oil" — but "this was only an issue that some day may interest us." They were already in error.

### *"The Walnut Money"*

Among the most promising markets for the "new light" was the vast Russian empire, which was beginning to industrialize and for which artificial light had special importance. As early as 1862, American kerosene reached Russia and St. Petersburg, quickly winning wide acceptance, with kerosene lamps swiftly replacing the tallow on which the populace had almost entirely depended. But future developments in a distant part of the empire would not only foreclose the Russian market to American oil but spell the undoing of Rockefeller's global plans.

For many centuries, oil seepages had been noted on the arid **Aspheron Peninsula**, an outgrowth of the Caucasus Mountains projecting into the landlocked Caspian Sea. In the thirteenth century, Marco Polo reported hearing of a spring around Baku that produced oil, "good to burn" and useful for cleaning the mange of camels. Baku was the territory of the "eternal pillars of fire" worshiped by the Zoroastrians — the result of flammable gas, associated with petroleum deposits, escaping from fissures in porous limestone.

Baku was part of an independent duchy annexed to the Russian empire early in the nineteenth century. By 1829 there were eighty-two hand-dug oil pits, but output was tiny. The industry was severely restricted by the region's backwardness and remoteness and by the corrupt, heavy-handed, incompetent Czarist administration, which ran the oil industry as a state monopoly. Finally, at the beginning of the 1870s, the Russian government abolished the monopoly system and opened the area to competitive private enterprise. The result was an explosion of entrepreneurship. The first wells were drilled in 1871-72; by 1873, more than twenty small refineries were at work.

Shortly after, a chemist named **Robert Nobel** arrived in Baku. He was the eldest son of Immanuel Nobel, a clever Swedish inventor who had emigrated in 1837 to Russia, where the military establishment excitedly took up his invention of the underwater mine. Immanuel built up a considerable industrial company, only to have it fail when the Russian government swung from domestic to foreign procurement. One son, **Ludwig**, built upon the ruins of his father's business a new company — a great armaments concern; he also developed the "Nobel wheel," uniquely suited to wretched Russian roads. Another son, **Alfred**, gifted in both chemistry and finance, created a worldwide dynamite empire from Paris. But the eldest son, Robert, was unsuccessful in various businesses and finally returned to St. Petersburg to work grudgingly for Ludwig.

Ludwig obtained a huge contract to manufacture rifles for the Russian government. He needed wood for the rifle stocks, and dispatched Robert south to the Caucasus to search for Russian walnut. In March 1873, Robert's journey took him to Baku. Though very much a part of Asia — with minarets and the old mosque of the Persian shahs, populated by Tatars, Persians, and Armenians — the recent oil development had begun to bring great change. Robert was immediately caught up in the fever. Without consulting his brother, he took the twenty-five thousand rubles Ludwig had entrusted to him for buying wood — **"the walnut money"** — and instead bought a small refinery. The Nobels were in the oil business.

### *The Rise of Russian Oil*

Robert quickly set about modernizing the refinery he had bought with Ludwig's money. With additional funds from his brother, he established himself as the most competent refiner in Baku. In October 1876, the first shipment of Nobel's illuminating oil arrived in St. Petersburg. In that same year, Ludwig came to Baku to see for himself. Skilled in dealing with the imperial system, Ludwig won the blessing of the Grand Duke, brother of the Czar and viceroy of the Caucasus. But Ludwig was also a great industrial leader, capable of conceiving a plan on the scale of Rockefeller. He set about analyzing every phase of the oil business, learned everything about the American oil experience, and harnessed science, innovation, and business planning to efficiency and profitability. In a very few years, Russian oil was to take on and even surpass American oil, and Ludwig Nobel would become **"the Oil King of Baku."**

Long-distance transit was a critical problem. Oil was shipped in wooden barrels over an inefficient and lengthy route — by boat on the Caspian Sea to Astrakhan, then transferred to barges for the journey up the Volga River, eventually reaching a rail line. Handling costs were enormous. Even the barrels were costly — no local wood, so wood was imported from America or secondhand American barrels were bought in Western Europe. Ludwig conceived a solution with far-reaching implications: ship the oil in **"bulk"** — in large tanks built into ships.

The idea faced considerable ballast and safety problems. Ludwig figured out the ballast problem and commissioned the first successful **bulk tanker**, the *Zoroaster*, which was put into service in 1878 on the Caspian. By the middle 1880s, Ludwig's concept had proved itself on the Atlantic, launching a major revolution in oil transport. Meanwhile Ludwig was constantly pushing his Baku refinery to be among the most scientifically advanced in the world. His was the first company anywhere in the world to have a permanent staff position for a **professional petroleum geologist**.

The great, highly integrated oil combine built by Ludwig soon dominated the Russian oil trade. The **Nobel Brothers Petroleum Producing Company** had wells, pipelines, refineries, tankers, barges, storage depots, its own railroad, a retail distribution network — and a multinational workforce that was treated better than virtually any other working group in Russia, proudly calling themselves "Nobelites." The rapid development of Ludwig's empire was described as "one of the greatest triumphs of business enterprise in the entire nineteenth century."

Russian crude production, less than 600,000 barrels in 1874, reached **10.8 million** a decade later — equivalent to almost a third of American production. By the early 1880s, almost two hundred refineries were at work in the new industrial suburb of Baku known as **Black Town** — so dense a cloud of dark, smelly oil smoke that life there was compared to "confinement in a chimney-pot." Nobel's company was producing half of all Russian kerosene, triumphantly telling stockholders that "American kerosene has now been completely forced out of the Russian market."

But the company suffered from discord among the Nobel brothers. Robert resented Ludwig's intrusion. Ludwig was a builder, constantly seeking to expand, continuously hungry for new capital. Alfred, remembering how their father had failed through overexpansion, was much more cautious. "The main point of criticism," Alfred scolded Ludwig, "is that you build first and then look around for the wherewithal." Despite their disagreements, Alfred provided crucial assistance — his own money and help arranging loans, including a major borrowing from the Credit Lyonnais. That transaction set a significant precedent: it may have been the first loan for which future petroleum production was used as collateral.

Nobel Brothers dominated distribution of oil within the Russian Empire. Beyond those borders, Russian oil was hardly a factor — geography locked the oil into the empire. To reach a Baltic port meant "2,000 miles, intermittent water and rail transportation through western Russia." Severe winter weather precluded shipping kerosene on the Caspian between October and March, with the result that many refiners shut down for half the year. Even parts of the empire were inaccessible — in the city of Tiflis, it was cheaper to import kerosene from America, 8,000 miles away, than from Baku, 341 miles to the west.

There were also limits to the market within the Russian empire — illumination was far from a necessity for the vast peasantry. Ever-growing production forced the producers of Baku to look hungrily beyond the borders of the empire. Seeking an alternative to the northern route dominated by Nobel, two other producers — Bunge and Palashkovsky — won government approval to begin building a railroad from Baku west over the Caucasus to **Batum**, a port on the Black Sea incorporated into Russia in 1877. But in the midst of construction, the price of oil dropped, and they ran out of money.

Their rescue came from the French branch of a family that had financed many of Europe's wars, governments, and new railroads. They owned a refinery at Fiume on the Adriatic and were interested in acquiring lower-priced Russian crude. They loaned the money to complete the railroad, acquiring mortgages on Russian oil facilities. They also arranged guaranteed shipments of Russian oil to Europe at attractive prices. They were the **Rothschilds**.

This was a time of fervent anti-Semitism in Russia. An 1882 Imperial Decree had forbidden Jews to own or rent any more land within the empire. But in the Rothschilds' case, the decree did not seem to matter. Russian oil was a project of the Paris Rothschilds — that meant **Baron Alphonse**, who had organized France's reparations payments after its defeat by Prussia in 1871, was considered one of the best-informed men in Europe, and was said to own the best pair of moustaches on the Continent — and of his younger brother **Baron Edmond**, who sponsored Jewish settlement in Palestine. The Rothschild loan allowed the railroad from Baku to be completed in 1883, turning Batum almost overnight into one of the world's most important oil ports. In 1886, the Rothschilds formed the **Caspian and Black Sea Petroleum Company**, known ever after by its Russian initials — **"Bnito."** The Baku-Batum railroad opened a door to the West for Russian oil; it also initiated a fierce, thirty-year struggle for the oil markets of the world.

### *The Challenge to Standard Oil*

With the Rothschilds on the scene, the Nobels suddenly faced a major competitor — soon to become the second-largest Russian oil group. Though the two competitive groups discussed amalgamation, they could find no common ground, and their rivalry remained intense. Standard Oil could not afford to ignore the Russian oil industry. Russian kerosene was now competing with American illuminating oils in many countries in Europe. In response, Standard Oil stepped up its intelligence-gathering effort. Reports began to flow into 26 Broadway from all over the world, including some from American consuls who were also on the Standard payroll. The intelligence was disturbing — no longer could Standard complacently count on its overwhelming dominance.

Standard's management figured the Czarist government would never allow it to buy out Ludwig Nobel altogether. But it could try to acquire a substantial number of Nobel shares and retain the invaluable Ludwig in management. In 1885, W. H. Libby, Standard's top business-diplomat, opened talks with the Nobels in St. Petersburg. Ludwig Nobel was not interested. Instead he concentrated on strengthening his own marketing network and building up his sales in Europe. The spectacular increase of Russian oil production forced Nobel and the other Russian oil men to seek new markets beyond the empire. Baku was characterized by astonishing oil "fountains" or gushers, with such names as "Kormilitza" (the Wet Nurse) and Golden Bazaar and Devil's Bazaar. One called "Droozba" (Friendship) gushed for five months at forty-three thousand barrels per day, most of it wasted. By 1886 there were eleven fountains. Altogether Russian oil production rose tenfold between 1879 and 1888, reaching **23 million barrels** — equivalent to more than four-fifths of American production. As the flood of oil rapidly rose in the 1880s, it needed to find its way to markets.

Faced with Nobel's aggressive new sales campaign in Europe and the growing production from Baku, Standard concluded it had to take actions beyond mere discussions. In November 1885, it dropped its prices in Europe — just as it would when attacking a competitor in the US. Its local agents started rumor campaigns in various European countries about the quality and safety of Russian kerosenes. They also resorted to sabotage and bribery. Despite the ferocity of the Standard assault, Nobel and the Rothschilds fought back fiercely and successfully, and Standard's executives watched with dismay as the region of "Russian competition" broadened across the map.

In 1888, the Rothschilds took a new step — establishing their own importing and distributing companies in Britain. Nobel Brothers did likewise. Finally galvanized into action, Standard set up its first foreign "affiliate," the **Anglo-American Oil Company**, just twenty-four days after the official organization of the Rothschilds' new enterprise in Britain. It also established new affiliates on the Continent — joint ventures with leading local distributors. Standard Oil had become a true multinational enterprise.

Still its competitors could not be stayed. The Rothschilds lent money to smaller Russian producers, tying up rights to their production at advantageous prices. The Baku-Batum railroad suffered from a great bottleneck — the seventy-eight-mile stretch over a three-thousand-foot peak was so difficult that only half a dozen cars could be hauled over at any given time. In 1889, the Nobel Brothers completed a forty-two-mile pipeline through the mountain, using four hundred tons of Alfred's dynamite. In this new era of what Libby, Standard's roving ambassador, called **"competitive commerce,"** America's share of the world export trade in illuminating oil fell from 78 percent in 1888 to 71 percent in 1891, while the Russian share rose from 22 percent to 29 percent.

The prolific Baku fields continued to throw up new petroleum fountains and ever more oil. But there had been one dramatic change in the Russian oil industry. While Ludwig Nobel's patience and determination did not abate, physically he was worn out. In 1888 at the age of fifty-seven, the Oil King of Baku **died of a heart attack** while vacationing on the French Riviera.

Some of the European newspapers confused the Nobel brothers and instead reported the death of Alfred. Reading his own premature obituaries, Alfred was distressed to find himself condemned as a munitions maker, the "dynamite king," a merchant of death who had made a huge fortune by finding new ways to maim and kill. He brooded over these obituaries, and eventually rewrote his will, leaving his money for the establishment of the prizes that would perpetuate his name — the **Nobel Prizes** — in a way that would seem to honor the best in human endeavor.

### *The Son of the Shell Merchant*

Still, there was Russian kerosene flowing out of Batum in ever-increasing quantities, in search of markets. The Nobels had a firm grip on the internal Russian market. But for the others, especially the Rothschilds, the problem of "disposal" was growing each year. Somehow, the Rothschilds had to find their way around Standard Oil and into the world market. They looked with special interest to the East, to Asia, where they saw hundreds of millions of potential customers for the "new light." But how to get the oil to them?

The Rothschilds in Paris knew a shipping broker in London named **Fred Lane**, who watched out for their oil interests. Though always a backstage figure, Lane was to be one of the important oil pioneers. He was a big, burly man of great intelligence, a "go-between *par excellence*" eventually known as **"Shady Lane"** — not because he was crooked, but because he sometimes appeared to be representing so many different interested parties simultaneously that it was hard to know for whom he was really working.

Lane was truly expert in shipping, and he had a solution to offer the Rothschilds. He knew a certain merchant of rising prominence — **Marcus Samuel**. He put the Rothschilds in touch with Samuel. The result would be an audacious scheme that might not only solve the problem of Russian oil, but also take the form of a veritable worldwide coup that, if successful, would loosen the iron grip of Rockefeller and Standard Oil on the kerosene trade of the world.

By the end of the 1880s, Marcus Samuel had already gained some prominence in the City of London — no mean achievement for a Jew, and a Jew not from one of the old Sephardic families but from the East End of London, a descendant of immigrants who had come to Britain in 1750 from Holland and Bavaria. Samuel had the same name as his father, Marcus Samuel, most unusual for a professing Jew. The elder Marcus Samuel had begun his business career trading on the East London docks, buying curios from returning sailors. In the census of 1851, he was listed as a "shell merchant"; among his most popular products were little knickknack boxes covered with seashells, known as a "Gift from Brighton," sold to girls and young ladies at English seaside resorts. By the 1860s, the elder Marcus had accumulated some wealth and was importing everything from ostrich feathers to partridge canes to bags of pepper and slabs of tin. He was also exporting manufactures, including the first mechanical looms sent to Japan. Importantly for his son, the elder Samuel had built up a network of trusted relationships with some of the great British trading houses — run mainly by expatriate Scots — in Calcutta, Singapore, Bangkok, Manila, Hong Kong, and other parts of the Far East.

The younger Marcus was born in 1853. In 1869, at age sixteen, he went to work on his father's ledgers. At that very moment, John Rockefeller, fourteen years older, was about to begin his decade-long campaign to consolidate the oil industry. New technology was radically transforming trading and international commerce. In 1869, the Suez Canal was opened, knocking four thousand miles off the journey to the Far East. Steamships were taking over from sail. In 1870, the direct telegraph cable from England to Bombay was completed, and shortly after, Japan, China, Singapore, and Australia were all brought into the telegraph network. These were tools the younger Marcus Samuel would use to build his wealth.

After his father's death, Marcus, in partnership with his brother Samuel Samuel, developed a considerable trading operation. Samuel Samuel was resident in Japan for several years, and the brothers had two firms — M. Samuel & Co. in London and Samuel Samuel & Co. in Yokohama, later Kobe. The brothers played an important role in the industrialization of Japan, and before he was thirty, Marcus had made his first fortune out of the trade with Japan. At the time, Marcus and Samuel Samuel were the only British Jews prominent in the trade with the Orient.

Marcus Samuel was always the trader, the idea man, and Samuel Samuel, always the loyal adherent and sidekick. Marcus was the more complicated — short and stout, with heavy eyebrows, totally unprepossessing in appearance. But he was capable of bold vision, adventurous, ingenious, quick to act, and single-minded when he chose to be. He talked in a very soft voice, sometimes hardly audible, making people strain to hear him and perhaps making himself all the more persuasive. He instilled trust in people, so much so that for two decades he depended for his credit not on bankers but on those Scottish merchants in the Far East. Marcus had more on his agenda than simply accumulating wealth — he had a craving for position. As an outsider, a Jew born in the East End, he would put his energies into seeking acceptance for the name Samuel at the highest levels of British society.

Marcus did not believe in overhead — he profoundly disbelieved in it. He operated out of a small office in Houndsditch in the East End, behind which was his warehouse crammed to the ceiling with Japanese vases, imported furniture and silks, seashells and feathers, and every other kind of knickknack. His operating staff was lean — he had virtually no staff at all. He had little capital, depending on the credit extended by the Far Eastern trading houses. He also used the trading houses as his foreign agents. To charter ships, he used the shipping brokerage firm of Lane and Macandrew, whose senior partner, Fred Lane, could frequently be found in the cramped offices off a narrow alley.

### *The Coup of 1892*

Marcus Samuel's entire business experience had conditioned him to be swift in grasping an opportunity, and here with the Rothschilds was an astonishing one. He moved quickly to lay the groundwork with Lane. The two men made a prospecting trip to the Caucasus in 1890. Samuel observed a primitive bulk tanker and saw in a flash that bulk tankers — the ship as a floating bottle — would be much more efficient. Samuel then traveled out to Japan and back through the Far East, seeking to persuade the Scottish trading houses to sign on with his new venture. Without them, he could not go ahead — he needed their cooperation and they would finance the enterprise. They all agreed.

Samuel carried out a study of the opportunity with a meticulous care uncharacteristic of the normally fast-moving trader. But he knew how large the risks were — and the stakes. He recognized there was no point in trying to break into the market unless he and his partners could undersell Standard Oil — or at least avoid being undersold. The campaign would have to be waged in all markets simultaneously; otherwise, Standard Oil would slash prices in markets where Samuel was competing and subsidize the price cuts by raising prices elsewhere. Speed and secrecy were essential.

But exactly how was Samuel to fight? He needed tankers so kerosene could be shipped in tanks rather than cases — greatly reducing shipping costs per gallon. Like Rockefeller with the railroads, Samuel understood the need to master transportation costs. The type of tanker then in operation simply would not do. Samuel needed a new, larger, technologically more advanced tanker — capable of being steam-cleaned and then filled for the return trip with goods from the Orient. He needed guaranteed supplies of kerosene from Batum in sufficient volume and at prices reflecting savings from not having to tin the kerosene. He needed access to the **Suez Canal**, which would cut the voyage by four thousand miles, increasing his competitive advantage against Standard, whose oil traveled to the Far East on sailing ships around the Cape of Good Hope. But the Suez Canal was closed to tankers on safety grounds — Standard's tankers had already been refused. Samuel also required large storage tanks in all major Asian ports, tank cars or wagons to carry kerosene into the hinterlands, and inland depots where bulk shipments could be broken down into receptacles for local wholesale and retail trade. This demanding enterprise, involving detailed long-distance coordination of markets, engineering, and politics, had to be kept as secret as possible.

Samuel found it difficult to work out the actual deal with the Rothschilds and Bnito — they were of two minds: never quite sure whether they wanted to compete with Standard or reach an accommodation. To M. Aron, the Rothschilds' chief oil man, Standard was always "cette puissante compagnie" ("this powerful company") — not to be trifled with.

But finally, in 1891, after long negotiations and in the face of falling prices, Samuel won his contract with the Rothschilds: exclusive rights for nine years, until 1900, to sell Bnito's kerosene east of Suez. That was what he had always been sure he would get, and he had been proceeding at full speed on the other fronts.

Samuel's tankers represented a significant technological advance — capable of being steam-cleaned and filled with food for the return trip, and designed with new safety features for expansion and contraction of kerosene at different temperatures. Opposition quickly arose to allowing Samuel's tankers into the Suez Canal. By the summer of 1891, the press was darkly reporting rumors of a "powerful group of financiers and merchants" under "Hebrew influence" who were trying to take tankers through the Suez Canal. Then, one of the most eminent firms of solicitors in the City of London, **Russell and Arnholz**, launched a strong lobbying campaign against granting permission — including lengthy correspondence with the Foreign Secretary. They refused to reveal their client, but there was hardly any question that the client was **Standard Oil**. Soon, Russell and Arnholz was hastily alerting the British government that if British merchants were permitted to put tankers into the canal, Russian shipping concerns would also win the same right, and if Russian naval officers and seamen got into the canal, they would "undertake all kinds of mischief," including seeking "to block the navigation of the Canal" and "destroy all the shipping in it."

But Samuel had powerful allies in the Rothschild family, whose English branch had financed Benjamin Disraeli's purchase of the Suez Canal shares in 1875, and in the influential French Banque Worms. Moreover, the Foreign Secretary saw the passage of British tankers through the canal as very much in Britain's interest. Lloyds of London rated Samuel's new tanker design safe.

Meanwhile, M. Samuel & Co. had embarked upon a campaign to build storage tanks throughout Asia. The Samuel brothers sent out their nephews, Mark and Joseph Abrahams, to find sites and supervise construction. Joseph had India and Mark the Far East. Mark was paid five pounds a week and was further rewarded by constant long-range interference, carping, criticism, and insults from his uncles. They hammered at him about keeping costs down and speeding up — two quite contrary objectives. When Mark purchased his own secondhand rickshaw, he could not win his uncles' approval. Yet, through it all, Mark was buying sites and building storage tanks throughout the Far East, including one on Freshwater Island, off Singapore, outside the jurisdiction of an obstructionist harbormaster.

On January 5, 1892, the Suez Canal gave its official approval to passage for tankers built to Samuel's new design. "The new scheme is one of singular boldness and great magnitude," the *Economist* commented four days later.

The first tanker was nearing completion at West Hartlespool. It was called the ***Murex*** — named for a type of seashell, as were all of Samuel's subsequent tankers, a memorial to the elder Marcus, the shell merchant. On July 22, 1892, the *Murex* sailed from West Hartlespool for Batum, where it filled its tanks with Bnito's kerosene. On **August 23**, it passed through the Suez Canal, headed for the East. It discharged part of its cargo at Freshwater Island, Singapore; then sailed on to Mark's new installation in Bangkok. **The coup was launched.**

Taken by surprise, Standard's shocked representatives rushed to assess the dangers. The implications were enormous — as the *Economist* noted, "If the sanguine anticipations of the promoters are realized, the Eastern case-oil trade must needs become obsolete." Standard Oil's agents were too late — Samuel's kerosene was everywhere. Standard could not cut prices in one market and subsidize them by raising prices elsewhere.

The coup was indeed brilliant and the execution superb — with one exception. Samuel and the Far Eastern trading houses had committed a small oversight that almost destroyed their venture. They had assumed that eager customers would line up with their own receptacles to be filled. The customers were expected to use old Standard Oil tin cans. But they did not — throughout the Far East, Standard's blue oil tins had become a prized mainstay of local economies, used to construct everything from roofing to birdcages to opium cups, hibachis, tea strainers, and egg beaters. They were not about to give up such a valuable product. A local crisis was created in each port, as the kerosene went unsold, and despairing telegrams began to flow into Houndsditch.

Marcus proved his entrepreneurial genius. He sent out a chartered ship, filled with tinplate, to the Far East, and simply instructed his partners in Asia to begin manufacturing tin receptacles for the kerosene. "How do *you* stick on the wire handles?" the agent in Singapore wrote to Samuel's representative in Japan. Instructions were sent. "What color do you suggest?" cabled the agent in Shanghai. Mark gave the answer — "Red!" All the trading houses quickly established local factories, and throughout Asia, Samuel's bright and shiny **red** receptacles were soon competing with Standard's blue ones, battered and chipped after the long voyage. Perhaps some customers were buying Samuel's kerosene more for the useful red can than for its contents. Red roofs and red birdcages, as well as red opium cups, hibachis, tea strainers, and egg beaters, began to replace the blue.

And so the day was saved. By end of 1893, Samuel had launched ten more ships, all named for seashells — the *Conch*, the *Clam*, the *Elax*, the *Cowrie*, and so on. By 1895, sixty-nine tanker passages had been made through the Suez Canal, all but four in ships owned or chartered by Samuel. By 1902, of all the oil passing through the Suez Canal, 90 percent belonged to Samuel and his group.

### *The Alderman*

Marcus Samuel was not only on the edge of a great success in business; he was also beginning to achieve some station in British life. In 1891, he had taken time off to stand for and win election as an alderman of the City of London. But then in 1893, all seemed for naught — Samuel became seriously ill; his physician diagnosed cancer and gave him no more than six months to live. The prediction proved slightly off — by some thirty-four years. Still, the threat of imminent death motivated Samuel to create a new entity, the **Tank Syndicate**, composed of the Samuel brothers, Fred Lane, and the trading houses of the Far East, sharing all profits and losses on a global basis — necessary if they were to fight Standard Oil in whatever market it chose.

Samuel's fortune accumulated rapidly, from oil and tankers and from the longer-standing trade with the Far East, principally Japan. The Samuel brothers made money as principal provisioners of weapons and supplies to Japan during its 1894-95 war with China. Within a few years of the *Murex*'s first passage through the Suez Canal, Marcus Samuel had become a very rich man, one who went riding every morning in Hyde Park, who owned a splendid country house in Kent called the Mote, with its own five-hundred-acre deer park.

Samuel had, however, one serious fault as a businessman. Unlike his rival, Rockefeller, he **lacked talent for organization and administration**. Where Rockefeller had an instinct for order, Samuel had an addiction to improvisation. Organization was an afterthought; he ran everything out of his hat. He was operating a large steamship line as part of his oil enterprise, yet had no one in his office with any knowledge or experience of managing such an organization. He simply depended upon Fred Lane. The fleet was run out of a small room in Houndsditch with nothing but a table, two chairs, a small wall map of the world, and two clerks.

Compare Rockefeller's owl-like unfathomability, his masklike face, his quiet deliberation, his drawing out of judgment and consensus from the gentlemen in Room 1400, to the violent quarrels — the combat, anger, and recriminations — by which Marcus and Samuel arrived at decisions. Sometimes a clerk would be summoned and while he waited, "the two brothers would always go to the window, their backs to the room, huddled together close, their arms around each other's shoulders, heads bent, talking in low voices, until suddenly they would burst apart in yet another dispute, Mr. Sam with loud and furious cries, Mr. Marcus speaking softly, but both calling each other fool, idiot, imbecile, until suddenly, for no apparent reason, they were in agreement again."

### *"This Struggle to the Death"*

The rapid rise of Russian production, the towering position of Standard Oil, the struggle for established and new markets at a time of increasing supplies — all were factors in what became known as the **Oil Wars**. In the 1890s, there was a continuing struggle involving four rivals: Standard, the Rothschilds, the Nobels, and the other Russian producers. At one moment they would be battling fiercely for markets, cutting prices; at the next, courting one another, trying to apportion the world's markets among themselves; at still the next, exploring mergers and acquisitions. On many occasions, they would be doing all three at the same time, in an atmosphere of great suspicion and mistrust.

In 1892-93, the Nobels, Rothschilds, and Standard came close to bringing virtually all oil production into one system, dividing the world among them. "In my opinion," noted M. Aron of the Rothschilds, "the crisis has reached its end, for everybody, in America and Russia, is exhausted by this struggle to the death that has gone on so long." Baron Alphonse was keen to get matters settled but mortally afraid of publicity. Finally, Libby of Standard Oil assured Baron Alphonse that with so many foreigners visiting America for the Chicago World's Fair, his arrival would not be much noted. After the meeting at 26 Broadway, a Standard executive reported that the Baron was "very courteous and remarkably fluent in English," adding that the Rothschilds would "immediately begin the steps toward control in Russia." But the Baron insisted that Standard bring the American independents into the contract. Despite great effort, slowed by rivalries and a cholera epidemic in Baku, the Rothschilds, joined by the Nobels, did succeed in getting all Russian producers to agree to a common front as a prelude to a grand negotiation with Standard. But despite its 85 to 90 percent control of American oil, Standard could not deliver the critical missing element — the independent American refiners and producers — and the proposed agreement collapsed.

In autumn 1894, Standard launched another worldwide price-cutting campaign. The Rothschilds regarded Samuel as a tool with which to improve their bargaining position with Standard and were very tough in their interpretation of their contract with him. Suspecting that the dissatisfied Samuel could be the weak link, Standard opened negotiations with him, presenting a proposal to buy him out on a far grander scale than usual — his enterprise would become part of Standard Oil, and he would become a director, though free to pursue his civic interests. Altogether, it was a very attractive offer. But Samuel rejected it. He wanted to keep the independent identity of his enterprise and his fleet, flying the flag of M. Samuel and Company, and he wanted it all to remain British.

Standard Oil immediately returned again to the Russian producers, and on **March 14, 1895**, signed the long-sought grand alliance with the Rothschilds and Nobels "on behalf of the petroleum industry of the U.S." and "on behalf of the petroleum industry of Russia." The Americans were to get 75 percent of world export sales, the Russians 25 percent. But the agreement never came into force — apparently because of opposition of the Russian government. Once again, the would-be grand alliance had collapsed. Standard responded with new price-cutting campaigns.

If Standard Oil could not regain control through a grand alliance, there was an alternative — to beat the Russians at their own game by acquiring access to crude much closer to the Asian market, or indeed in Asia itself. Standard's attention turned to **Sumatra**, in the Dutch East Indies, from which the steaming time to Singapore could be measured in hours. And its eyes fell on a Dutch company that, after years of struggle, had successfully carved out a profitable business from the jungles of Sumatra, now beginning to make a sizeable impact on markets throughout Asia with its own brand, **Crown Oil**, opening up the world's third major producing province. It was called **Royal Dutch**.

### *Royal Dutch*

Seepages had been commented upon in the Dutch East Indies for hundreds of years, and small amounts of "earth oil" had been used for relief of "stiffness in the limbs" and other medicinal purposes. By 1865, no fewer than fifty-two oil seepages had been identified through the archipelago. But matters languished.

One day in 1880, **Aeilko Jans Zijlker**, a manager of the East Sumatra Tobacco Company, happened to be visiting a plantation in the marshy coastal strip of Sumatra. While traipsing around during a powerful storm, he took refuge in a darkened, unused tobacco shed. With him was a *mandur*, or native overseer, who lit a torch. Its bright flame caught Zijlker's attention — he thought it must be unusually resinous wood. How had the *mandur* acquired the torch? He replied the torch had been daubed with a mineral wax. For longer than anyone could remember, the locals had been skimming this wax from the surface of small ponds and putting it to many uses, including caulking boats.

The next morning, Zijlker had the *mandur* take him to one of the ponds. He recognized the smell — imported kerosene had been introduced into the islands. He collected a sample and sent it to Batavia for analysis, which yielded between 59 and 62 percent kerosene. Zijlker threw himself wholeheartedly into the venture. His first step was to win a concession from the local Sultan of Langkat — the concession known as **Telaga Said**, in northeast Sumatra, six miles of jungle from the Balaban River. It was not until 1885 that the first successful well was drilled. Progress was slow and Zijlker was continually strapped for cash. But he finally gained prestigious sponsorship from the former head of the central bank of the East Indies and the former governor general. The Dutch king himself, William III, granted use of the title "Royal" in the company name. The **Royal Dutch** company was launched in 1890, and its first stock flotation was oversubscribed four and a half times.

Zijlker was triumphant: "What won't bend must break." But returning to the Far East in autumn 1890, he stopped at Singapore and there he died suddenly. His grave was marked with an inconspicuous monument.

Leadership of the enterprise passed to **Jean Baptiste August Kessler**, born in 1853. Kessler had been a successful trader in the Dutch East Indies, but business reverses sent him back to Holland, broken and in poor health. Royal Dutch offered him a chance to begin again. When he arrived at the drilling site in 1891, he found total chaos — "I do not feel very cheerful about this business. An enormous amount of money has been lost by precipitate action." Working conditions were awful — men labored in water up to their waists, the site ran out of rice, and Chinese workmen had to swim fifteen miles to a village for provisions. Somehow, working both day and night, often racked with fever, the obsessed Kessler forced the pace.

In 1892, a six-mile pipeline linking the wells in the jungle to the refinery on the Balaban River was completed. On February 28, the entire crew gathered nervously to wait for the oil. They counted the minutes. The moment came and went — there was no oil. Depression settled. Kessler, fearing defeat, turned away. But then suddenly they all froze. A "roar as of a mighty storm" announced the arrival of the oil, and it quickly poured "with incredible driving force" into the first still of the Royal Dutch refinery. The crowd burst into cheers, the Dutch flag was raised, and Kessler and the crew toasted the future prosperity of Royal Dutch.

By April 1892 — while Marcus Samuel was preparing to send the *Murex* through the Suez Canal — Kessler had delivered the first few cases of kerosene, christened **Crown Oil**. But financial resources were quickly strained. Kessler managed to secure capital, returned to Telaga Said in 1893 to find the operation in a deplorable state — "Half-heartedness, ignorance, indifference, dilapidation, disorder, and vexation are everywhere apparent." He pushed the operation hard, summing up the danger: **"To stagnate means to liquidate."**

All sorts of obstacles had to be overcome, including the arrival of almost three hundred marauding pirates from another part of Sumatra. Yet Kessler kept pushing: "If things go wrong, my job and my name are gone and perhaps my sacrifices and my extraordinary exertions will be repaid with censure into the bargain. Heaven preserve me from all that misery."

Kessler persevered and succeeded. Within two years, he had increased production sixfold, and Royal Dutch had finally become profitable, even able to pay a dividend. If Royal Dutch were to survive, it needed to establish its own marketing organization throughout the Far East, independent of middlemen. Royal Dutch also began to use tankers and build its own storage tanks. The immediate danger was that Samuel's Tank Syndicate would move too swiftly ahead and gain a hammerlock on the business. But the Dutch government excluded the Tank Syndicate from the ports of the East Indies, telling its own producers that the Tank Syndicate thus "need not be for the time being an object of terror" to the local industry.

Royal Dutch's business was growing at an astonishing pace; between 1895 and 1897, production increased fivefold. Yet Kessler warned that until Royal Dutch could obtain additional concessions, "we must pretend to be poor." He did not want to draw other European and American interests to the East Indies, or to Royal Dutch. His principal worry was Standard Oil, which if too aroused, would wield its potent weapon — price cutting — and push Royal Dutch to the wall.

### *"Dutch Obstacles"*

Royal Dutch could hardly remain invisible. Its rapid growth, along with other producers in Asia, created new distress for Standard Oil, matching that already created by the Russian producers. Standard investigated all options. Early on, it began negotiating for a concession in Sumatra, but gave that up in the wake of a native revolt. It searched for production from China and Sakhalin to California.

In 1897, Standard dispatched two representatives to Asia to assess what could be done. They met Royal Dutch's local manager, visited installations, called on Dutch government officials, gathered intelligence from homesick American drillers. The representatives warned 26 Broadway against a "promiscuous search through such an enormous expanse" of steaming jungle. Much better to buy existing production and establish a partnership with an authentic Dutch enterprise. Standard's objective, they insisted, should be to "assimilate" the successful companies — and that meant, above all, Royal Dutch.

Standard's agents were impressed by everything about Royal Dutch — Kessler's leadership, the favorable economics, the new marketing system. "In the whole history of the oil business, there has never been anything more phenomenal than the success and rapid growth of the R. D. Co." When the Standard Oil men said good-bye to the Royal Dutch managers in Sumatra, there was something almost wistful: "Would not it be a pity that two such big concerns as you and we own should not go together."

Samuel's syndicate was also hungrily eyeing Royal Dutch. In late 1896 and early 1897, intense discussions took place between the two groups. But their objectives were quite different — Royal Dutch wanted a joint marketing arrangement; Marcus and Samuel Samuel wanted to buy out Royal Dutch. After one visit to the Dutch directors in The Hague, Sam wrote to Marcus: "A Dutchman sits and says nothing till he gets what he wants but of course in this case he won't." Yet Marcus and Kessler maintained a friendly relationship despite their competition.

Standard Oil knew such discussions were going on, and could not be confident that they would not lead to some kind of powerful combination arrayed against it. "Every day makes the situation more serious and dangerous to handle. If we don't get control of the situation soon, the Russians, Rothschilds, or some other party may." In summer 1897, W. H. Libby presented Kessler and Royal Dutch with a formal proposal: the capital would be quadrupled, with Standard taking all additional shares. Standard, Libby stressed, had no intention of getting Royal Dutch "into its power" — it was "only seeking a favorable capital investment." Kessler could hardly believe Libby or the sincerity of his pledge. On Kessler's strong recommendation, Royal Dutch's board rejected the offer.

Standard, disappointed, began discussions about acquiring another concession, but both Dutch government officials and Royal Dutch successfully intervened. "Dutch obstacles are about the most difficult in the World for Americans to remove," a Standard Oil official declared, "for Americans are always in a hurry and Dutchmen never." Still, Royal Dutch did not feel secure. Its directors knew how Standard operated — buying up shares quietly and then putting competitors out of action. To forestall this, the directors of Royal Dutch created a special class of preference stock, the holders of which controlled the board. Admission to this exclusive rank was by invitation only. One of Standard's agents unhappily reported that Royal Dutch would never merge with the American company. It was not merely a "sentimental barrier" on the part of the Dutch; the managers of Royal Dutch greatly enjoyed receiving 15 percent of the company's profits.

### *Samuel as Lord Mayor — Distracted from Oil*

Marcus Samuel achieved his great ambition in November 1902, becoming Lord Mayor of London — the first Jewish Lord Mayor since the 1870s. Deterding attended the inauguration but distanced himself from the event, derisively writing to a colleague: "I certainly should not think it worth a white tie to attend a second time. The Lord Mayor's show was very fine, according to the view here, but in Dutch eyes it was more like the ceremonial parade of [a] circus."

Samuel was consumed by ceremonial duties — receptions, speeches, official trips, interviewing every lunatic to be certified insane at the Mansion House. He spent almost a month before turning back to oil business, and even then was continually pulled away. The strain was immense: ill health, constant headaches, and he had to have all his teeth removed.

On the last Saturday of December 1902, after a whirlwind of funerals, plays, military displays, and city business, Samuel finally turned to private business — only to find a devastating letter from Fred Lane, his old friend and partner, resigning from Shell's board. Lane launched a bitter critique of how Marcus Samuel ran his company: "You are, and have always been, too much occupied to be at the head of such a business. There seems only one idea: sink capital, create a great bluster, and trust to providence. Such a happy-go-lucky frame of mind in business I have never seen before. ... Business like this cannot be conducted by an occasional glance in one's spare time, or by some brilliant *coup* from time to time. It is steady, treadmill work." Unless "some very radical change is made," Lane prophesied, "the bubble will burst." The breach could not be healed; Lane left the board with lasting bitterness on both sides.

### *Asiatic Petroleum — Deterding Triumphs*

Meanwhile, the Asiatic Petroleum Company was still being constructed. Disputes over control and policy continued. Deterding was so intent on getting his way that he was driven into "a state of unreasonable rage and unreasoning venom" that was "close to dementia." He was unwilling to compromise: "I am feeling entirely fit and able to withstand ten Lord Mayors."

By May 1903, ten contracts established Asiatic, owned equally in thirds by Royal Dutch, Shell, and the Rothschilds. It would regulate East Indies production, carry out Far East sales, and control East Indies gasoline and kerosene sales in Europe. The greatest achievement for Deterding: Royal Dutch emerged paramount in every part of the agreement. The managing director of Asiatic would also be the managing director of Royal Dutch — Henri Deterding. Samuel wanted the term limited to three years; Deterding insisted on "Twenty-one years and not a day less," which was another way of saying permanent. He won on that too.

The first Asiatic board meeting took place in July 1903, with Samuel in the chairman's seat. Deterding, speaking without notes, seemed to know where every ship was, its destination, its cargo, and the prices awaiting it in each port. Samuel was much impressed.

### *"The Group" — Samuel Surrenders*

Deterding threw himself into the new enterprise with irrepressible energy. "It so happens that in the oil business one has to seize one's opportunities quickly; otherwise, they escape." He was a calculated risk taker, not a gambler. Royal Dutch quickly assimilated most independent producers in the East Indies, where the oil was particularly suited to gasoline manufacture. Under Deterding's bullwhip, Asiatic won an important share of the growing European gasoline market as automobiles became familiar sights on roads.

Things were getting progressively worse for Shell. Texas supplies from Spindletop had given out. The British Admiralty refused to take seriously Samuel's vision for fuel oil for the Royal Navy. Royal Dutch discovered Borneo crudes suitable for fuel oil, shattering Samuel's hoped monopoly. Standard's price wars continued. Fred Lane, now deputy managing director of Asiatic, used his position to settle scores against Shell. Shell could barely pay 5 percent dividends, while Royal Dutch's were at 65 percent, then 50 percent, then an immensely satisfying 73 percent in 1905.

In winter 1906, Robert Waley Cohen, Samuel's most talented employee, told him the truth: a consolidated marketing company was insufficient — the only way Shell could survive was to amalgamate completely with Royal Dutch on the best terms available. The idea devastated Samuel. He raised the issue with Deterding. On what basis? "Fifty-fifty," said Samuel. "Absolutely not," said Deterding. The days of "British Dutch" were past; the ratio would have to be sixty for Royal Dutch and forty for Shell. "The property and interests of Shell would henceforth be managed by a foreigner!" Samuel protested.

After several months with no improvement in Shell's position, Samuel was forced to return. Deterding offered only one guarantee: Royal Dutch would buy a quarter of Shell's shares, giving it a shareholder's interest in Shell's welfare. Samuel asked for time. Deterding refused: "I am at present in a generous mood. I have made you this offer, but if you leave this room without accepting it, the offer is off." Samuel accepted. His struggle with Deterding had gone on for half a decade. It was over. Deterding had won.

The union was cemented in 1907, creating the **Royal Dutch/Shell Group**, sometimes simply known as "the Group." All oil production and refining assets were lodged in a Dutch company, Bataafsche Petroleum Maatschappij; all transport and storage in an English company, Anglo-Saxon Petroleum Company. Royal Dutch held 60 percent of the stock in operating subsidiaries and Shell 40 percent. There was no Royal Dutch/Shell board and no legal entity called Royal Dutch/Shell. The "Committee of Managing Directors" had no specific legal status but was composed of active members of the boards of the two holding companies.

### *The Three Leaders of Royal Dutch/Shell*

Three men ran all parts of the business. **Henri Deterding** was first — the business side, constantly traveling and negotiating. **Hugo Loudon**, the Dutch engineer who had rescued Royal Dutch with new discoveries when initial wells gave out, focused on the technical side. **Robert Waley Cohen**, young Cambridge chemistry graduate who had gone to work for Marcus Samuel in 1901, was Deterding's de facto commercial deputy — making decisions in Deterding's absences, bucking Deterding when the Dutchman had misgivings.

Samuel initially regarded himself as a failure. "I am a disappointed man," he told newspaper reporters. He treated himself to a 650-ton yacht. But the humiliation healed quickly. Deterding consulted Samuel, made him much richer, and spoke of him as "our chairman." By 1908, Samuel was telling Shell stockholders that Deterding was "nothing less than a genius." Samuel presided for over a decade as chairman, grew even more wealthy, became an engaged philanthropist, and promoted fuel oil for shipping. But there was never any question: Deterding was the boss.

### *"To America!"*

The 1907 amalgamation meant the world oil market was now dominated by two giants: Standard Oil and the Royal Dutch/Shell Group. "If the Standard had tried to wipe us out three years ago, they'd have succeeded," Deterding said in 1910, "Now things are different." He made a pilgrimage to 26 Broadway seeking conciliation, but encountered only an offer to buy Royal Dutch/Shell for $100 million. "I am sorry to have to place on record that my visit to this city ... has been so useless," was his acid response.

Standard replied with a new price-cutting campaign and established a Dutch subsidiary to seek oil concessions in southern Sumatra. The Group had no choice but to counterattack: **"To America!"** became the slogan between 1910 and 1914. If not active in America, the Group would always be vulnerable to Standard's price cutting — Standard could sell surplus at cut-rate prices in Europe while maintaining higher domestic prices.

Deterding moved in two directions: the West Coast (marketing Sumatra gasoline in 1912, then California production in 1913) and the mid-continent. He sent **Mark Abrahams** — Marcus Samuel's nephew, fresh from launching an oil exploration company in Egypt — to Tulsa in July 1912. Abrahams arrived with his own typewriter (in case there were none) and $2,500 in a money belt (in case there were no reputable banks) in the boom town already proclaiming itself "the Oil Capital of the World." He acquired several small oil companies and incorporated them into **Roxana Petroleum**. Deterding sent a jubilant letter to Loudon: "At last we *are* in America!"

### *Russia in Turmoil*

Events soon proved the Royal Dutch/Shell amalgamation wise, given Shell's dependence on Russian oil. Russia's economy had grown under **Count Sergei Witte**, the powerful finance minister from 1892 to 1903, who oversaw rapid industrialization fueled by foreign capital. Conservative critics attacked his program; the Minister of War complained of "too hurried development" by "foreign capitalists, foreign capital, and Jews."

**Czar Nicholas II** was highly vulnerable to flattery, and he and his court descended into mysticism, "imported mediums and home-bred 'idiots' passing as saints." Witte prophetically noted the Czar could "not relinquish his 'Byzantine' habits" and "inasmuch as he does not possess the talents of either Metternich or Talleyrand, he usually lands in a mud puddle — or in a pool of blood."

Nicholas II was contemptuous of non-Russian minorities and sanctioned repression that turned them into rebels. By the early 1900s, the empire was in turmoil. The Caucasus — home of the Russian oil industry — was one of the worst-run parts. Workers in Baku had no families, working fourteen hours with two hours compulsory overtime.

Baku became the "revolutionary hotbed on the Caspian." A secret printing operation called **"Nina"** — hidden in a large cellar under the Tatar quarter — printed Lenin's revolutionary paper *Iskra*, smuggled from Europe via Persia. The oil industry's distribution system provided a perfect vehicle for clandestinely distributing propaganda. Baku's oil fields also provided training ground for future Bolshevik leaders: future Soviet President **Mikhail Kalinin**, future marshal **Klementi Voroshilov**, and most importantly, a young Georgian seminarian, son of a shoemaker, named **Joseph Djugashvili** — who operated underground as "Koba" (Turkish for "Indomitable") and later became **Joseph Stalin**.

In 1901-1902, Stalin became the chief socialist organizer in Batum, masterminding strikes against the Rothschilds' interests. He was arrested eight times, repeatedly escaping exile. In 1903, Baku oil workers went on strike, setting off a wave culminating in the first general strike in the empire. Marcus Samuel, the Rothschilds, and others worried about their dependence on Russia.

### *Russo-Japanese War and Revolution of 1905*

The Czarist regime sought diversion through foreign adventure, choosing war with Japan over Manchuria and Korea. "Russia's internal situation required something drastic," said the Minister of Interior. "We need a little victorious war to stem the tide of revolution."

The Russo-Japanese War began in January 1904 with Japan's surprise attack at Port Arthur. Russia lurched from one disaster to the next, culminating in the burial at sea of the entire fleet at the Battle of Tsushima. In December 1904, Baku oil workers struck again and won their first collective labor agreement. Days later, revolutionaries proclaimed "Workers of the Caucasus, the hour of revenge has struck" — authored by Stalin. The next day, Bloody Sunday: police fired on workers marching to the Winter Palace. This was the beginning of the Revolution of 1905 — what Lenin called the Great Rehearsal.

Government officials, fearful of revolution, provided arms to the Moslem Tatars, who rose up to massacre Christian Armenians, including leaders of the oil industry. One wealthy Armenian, Adamoff, stationed himself on his balcony with his son, held off a siege for three days, until killed when the house was set on fire. In September-October 1905, Tatars attacked the oil industry throughout Baku, setting fire to buildings, pillaging. "The flames from the burning derricks and oil wells leaped up into the awful pall of smoke which hung over the inferno." At two in the afternoon, the smoke was so thick the sun could not be seen. Then, an earthquake shook the entire region.

The impact was profound: for the first time, violent upheaval had interrupted the flow of oil, threatening to make vast investments worthless. Standard Oil quickly recaptured Far East markets formerly lost to Russian kerosene. **Two-thirds of all Russian oil wells had been destroyed** and exports collapsed.

By end of 1905, the revolution was spent. The Russo-Japanese War was mediated by Theodore Roosevelt at Portsmouth. In October 1905, the Czar grudgingly granted a constitutional government with a Parliament (the Duma). The oil region remained in turmoil — Baku elected Bolshevik deputies; Nobel's chief in Batum was murdered. The Bolsheviks sent Stalin back to Baku in 1907, where he fomented "unlimited distrust of the oil industrialists" among workers. In 1910, he was arrested again and exiled. "It was in Baku that he had honed the revolutionary and conspiratorial skills — and the ambition and cynicism — that would help make his future."

### *Return to Russia — Rothschilds Sell Out*

Chaotic drilling and political instability had led to deterioration in Russian production capacity. Operating costs rose sharply. The government unwisely raised transport tariffs, making Russian oil even less competitive. Its price advantage had turned into a disadvantage. Russian oil was increasingly a residual, bought only when other petroleum was unavailable.

A new source emerged in Europe: **Rumania**, where hand-dug pits on the Carpathian slopes had long produced minuscule supply. In the 1890s, Hungarian and Austrian bank investment combined with modern technology dramatically increased production. Standard Oil, the Deutsche Bank, and Royal Dutch entered Rumania, and output grew sevenfold in the first decade of the twentieth century. Deutsche Bank, with its Rumanian production, joined the Nobels and Rothschilds in 1906 to form the **European Petroleum Union (EPU)**, which negotiated market division agreements with Standard Oil — the EPU getting 20-25 percent of various European markets, Standard getting the rest.

The **Rothschilds wanted out** of Russia. Anti-Semitism, political instability, strikes, arson, murder — and profits were now low or nonexistent. All their oil assets depended on Russian production with no international diversification. In 1911, they began negotiating with Royal Dutch/Shell. Fred Lane represented the Rothschilds: "I can assure you to get Deterding to do something is not an easy task. His habit is to allow things to remain as open as possible and he sits like an owl upon it thinking it over." By 1912, the deal was done — the Group paid the Rothschilds in stock of both Royal Dutch and Shell, making them among the largest shareholders. The Rothschilds transformed uncertain Russian assets into substantial holdings in a diversified international company.

Deterding's motive was pure: "his intention was to make money." Overnight, the Group became a major force in Russia, controlling at least a fifth of entire Russian production. The acquisition gave the Group a globally balanced portfolio: **53 percent from the East Indies, 17 percent from Rumania, and 29 percent from Russia.** Between 1904 and 1913, Russia's share of world petroleum exports dropped from 31 to 9 percent. Stalin later reflected: "Three years of revolutionary work among the workers of the oil industry tempered me as a practical fighter... There in Baku I received, thus, my second baptism in revolutionary combat. There I became a journeyman for the revolution."

## Chapter 7: "Beer and Skittles" in Persia

A dapper gentleman from Persia, **Antoine Kitabgi**, carrying the title of general, arrived in Paris toward the end of 1900. Variously said to be of Armenian or Georgian origin, Kitabgi had held several positions in the Persian government, including director general of customs. He was, said a British diplomat, "well versed in Western matters — being able to draw up a concession and initiate commercial movements." The ostensible reason for his visit was the opening of a Persian Exhibition in Paris, but his real purpose was to find an investor willing to assume a petroleum concession in Persia. The Persian government desperately needed money; the reason was "the Shah's prodigality."

### *"A Capitalist of the Highest Order"*

In Paris, Kitabgi sought the aid of a retired British diplomat, who reported back: "Concerning the oil, I have spoken to a capitalist of the highest order, who declares himself disposed to examine the affair." The capitalist was **William Knox D'Arcy**. Born in Devon, England, in 1849, D'Arcy had emigrated to Australia, became a solicitor, developed a passion for horse racing, and took a flyer on an old gold mine. The mine turned out to be very rich in gold, making D'Arcy extremely wealthy. He returned to England, married a prominent actress named Nina Boucicault who entertained lavishly (Enrico Caruso came to sing at their dinner parties), maintained two estates, and had the only private box at the Epsom racing track aside from the royal box. He was an investor and speculator, not a manager, and was looking for a new investment.

Oil seepages had been noted for centuries in Persia. In 1872 and 1889, **Baron Julius de Reuter**, founder of the Reuters news agency, had obtained Persian concessions for oil development, but both ended in failure — generating great protest within Persia and opposition from Imperial Russia. In the 1890s, a French geologist published reports pointing to considerable oil potential. Kitabgi, eager to ensnare D'Arcy, promised "the presence of a source of riches incalculable as to extension."

On March 25, 1901, D'Arcy's representative left Paris, arriving in Tehran via Baku on April 16. Negotiations proceeded slowly; D'Arcy's man passed the time buying rugs and embroidery. Kitabgi "secured in a very thorough manner the support of all the Shah's principal Ministers and courtiers, not even forgetting the personal servant who brings His Majesty his pipe and morning coffee."

### *Russia Versus Britain*

Persia could claim a national identity stretching to the ancient empire of Cyrus the Great and Darius I, which by the fifth century B.C. stretched from India to modern Greece and Libya. The Parthian empire became the redoubtable eastern rival of the Roman empire. Wave after wave of armies — Alexander the Great from the West, Genghis Khan and the Mongols from the East — passed through. At the end of the eighteenth century, the Qajar dynasty won control over a fragmented country of contending principalities and tribal confederacies. The Qajar shahs ruled uneasily for a century and a half, subject to diplomatic and commercial competition between Russia and Britain for dominance.

Lord Curzon, Viceroy of India, described Persia as one of "the pieces on a chessboard upon which is being played out a game for the domination of the world." Beginning in the 1860s, Russia had embarked on a relentless drive of expansion in Central Asia. To Britain, Russia's expansion was a direct threat to India and routes thereto. In 1885, Russia attacked neighboring Afghanistan, nearly precipitating war with Britain.

Russia renewed its pressure on Persia around 1900. The British sought to keep Persia intact as a buffer between Russia and India. The Shah, **Muzaffar al-Din**, was "merely an elderly child" and "the Persian monarchy itself was an old, long-mismanaged estate, ready to be knocked down at once to whatever Foreign Power bid highest." Hardinge feared "the Shah and his ministers were in a state of complete vassalage to Russia, owing to their own reckless extravagance and folly." The Russians wanted political dominion, not trade: "What interest do we have in trading with seven or eight million lazy ragamuffins?"

A British oil concession would assist in righting the balance against Russia. Britain gave its support to D'Arcy's venture. When the Russian minister found out, he angrily sought to block negotiations. But D'Arcy's man threw another five thousand pounds onto the table — "the Shah wanted some ready money and stood out for some on signing the concession." On **May 28, 1901**, Shah Muzaffar al-Din signed the historic agreement: twenty thousand pounds in cash, another twenty thousand pounds' worth of shares, and 16 percent of "annual net profits." D'Arcy received a concession for sixty years covering three-quarters of the country.

D'Arcy had deliberately excluded the five northern provinces, closest to Russia, to "give no umbrage to Russia." But the Russians sought to build a pipeline from Baku to the Persian Gulf to expand kerosene exports and project strategic influence. Hardinge warned the "preposterous" concession for a pipeline, even if never built, would "afford an excuse for covering Southern Persia with surveyors, engineers and protecting detachments of Cossacks, preparing a veiled military occupation." The British opposition succeeded; the pipeline was not built.

### *The D'Arcy Concession — "Wild-Catting on a Colossal Scale"*

What drew D'Arcy to such a risky enterprise — "wild-catting on a colossal scale in a distant unsettled land"? The irresistible lure of immense wealth, the chance to become another Rockefeller. He had gambled before on the Australian gold mine with tremendous success. But this was a vast gamble on a much grander scale, with many more players and a political/social dimension absent in Australia. D'Arcy had been advised it would cost ten thousand pounds to drill two wells. Within four years, he was out of pocket in excess of two hundred thousand pounds.

### *The First Go*

D'Arcy had no organization, only a secretary. He hired **George Reynolds**, a graduate of the Royal Indian Engineering College with drilling experience in Sumatra. The first site was **Chiah Surkh**, an inaccessible plateau in the mountains of northwestern Persia, near what would later become the Iran-Iraq border, three hundred miles from the Persian Gulf. The entire country had barely eight hundred miles of road. Large parts were ruled by warring tribes that hardly recognized Tehran's authority. Persian Army commanders would rent out their soldiers as gardeners to local landowners and pocket the wages.

The population lacked technical skills, and the culture was hostile toward Western ideas. In his memoirs, Hardinge discussed the dominant Shia sect's religious zeal, resistance to political authority, and fierce antagonism toward all outsiders. "The hatred of the Shiahs for the first four Caliphs was, and is still, so strong" that some sought to "hasten their own entrance into Paradise by defiling the tombs of these usurpers." He discussed the doctrine of 'Ketman' or pious dissimulation "which renders it lawful for a good Moslem to appear to dissemble or even lie, for a really pious purpose."

Each piece of equipment had to be shipped to Basra on the Persian Gulf, transshipped three hundred miles up the Tigris to Baghdad, then carried by man and mule over the Mesopotamian plain and through the mountains. Reynolds and his motley crew of Poles, Canadians, and Azeris from Baku struggled to assemble the machinery. To the Azeris, even the introduction of the wheelbarrow was a startling innovation.

D'Arcy telegraphed from London: "Delay serious. Pray expedite." Actual drilling commenced at end of 1902. Equipment kept breaking, insects were incessant, food and parts supply was a constant problem. The "infernal heat" in workers' quarters reached 120 degrees. The work camp had a separate "Mohamedan Kitchen" for local dignitaries who all seemed "very keen on receiving a substantial present from us, especially in the shape of some shares of our Company." Reynolds had to be a diplomat dealing with petty feuds and open warfare between tribes. "The Mullahs in the North are exciting the population as much as they can against the foreigners. The real fight is now between the Shah and the Mullahs for the control of the public affairs."

### *"Every Purse Has Its Limits"*

In October 1903, eleven months after drilling began, there were the first shows of oil. But D'Arcy discovered this was far more difficult and expensive than imagined: "Every purse has its limit, and I can see the limits of my own." He needed to be bailed out or the concession would be lost.
