D'Arcy applied to the British Admiralty for a loan, inspired by **Thomas Boverton Redwood**, the "*eminence grise* of British oil policy before the First World War." Immaculately dressed with an orchid in his buttonhole, often mistaken for a leading actor, Redwood was Britain's premier oil expert — his consulting firm was used by almost every British oil company. He published *A Treatise on Petroleum* (1896), the standard work for the next two decades. He saw the advantages of burning fuel oil rather than coal in the Royal Navy and wanted oil reserves under British control.

Redwood brought D'Arcy's plight to the Admiralty's Fuel Oil Committee. D'Arcy had spent £160,000 on exploration with at least another £120,000 to be expected. He was told to expect in return an Admiralty contract for fuel oil. Both the Admiralty and Foreign Office supported the loan, but the Chancellor of the Exchequer, Austen Chamberlain, thought the House of Commons would never approve it. He turned it down.

D'Arcy was desperate: "It is all I can do to keep the bank quiet and something *must* be done." By end of 1903, he was overdrawn £177,000 at Lloyds Bank and had to put up shares in his Australian gold mine as collateral. In mid-January 1904, the second well at Chiah Surkh turned into a producer. "Glorious news from Persia — the greatest relief to me." But discovery alone was not enough; tens of thousands of pounds more would be required.

D'Arcy tried Joseph Lyons and Company, Standard Oil, and visited Baron Alphonse de Rothschild in Cannes — but the Rothschilds had enough to do with their new links to Shell and Royal Dutch. The flow at Chiah Surkh shrank to a trickle. By April 1904, D'Arcy's overdraft had increased further, Lloyds was demanding the concession itself as security. Less than three years after its inception, the Persian venture was on the verge of collapse.

### *The "Syndicate of Patriots"*

The Treasury's rejection alarmed the Foreign Office. Lord Lansdowne expressed concern that "there is danger of whole petroleum concession in Persia falling thus under Russian control." Hardinge concurred — the Russians might gain control and use it to expand their reach. British majority control should be maintained at all costs.

D'Arcy's visit to the Rothschilds, with the threat the concession might pass under French control, galvanized the Admiralty. The chairman of the Fuel Oil Committee wrote D'Arcy asking that before making any foreign deals, he allow the Admiralty to arrange acquisition by a British syndicate. Lord Lansdowne had risen in the House of Lords in May 1903 to declare: "The British government would regard the establishment of a naval base or of a fortified port in the Persian Gulf by any other power as a very grave menace to British interests, and we should certainly resist it with all the means at our disposal." Lord Curzon called this "our Monroe Doctrine in the Middle East."

**Lord Strathcona**, an eighty-four-year-old self-made millionaire, was asked to become head of a "syndicate of patriots." He agreed not for commercial reasons "but really from an imperial point of view." The partner was **Burmah Oil**, founded by Scottish merchants in 1886 in Glasgow, which had transformed primitive oil gathering by Burmese villagers into a commercial industry with a refinery in Rangoon. The cautious Scottish merchants had very practical questions — could Persia be considered under British protection? The impatient D'Arcy invited Burmah's vice-chairman to watch the Epsom Derby from his private box near the winning post. The rich food and drink so upset the vice-chairman's liver that he was sick four times in the next few weeks and never again accepted an invitation from D'Arcy.

Finally, in 1905, almost exactly four years after the concession was initialed, D'Arcy and Burmah agreed. The **Concession Syndicate** was established; D'Arcy's operation became a subsidiary and D'Arcy became a director. Burmah provided new capital and management expertise. As the historian of Burmah Oil put it, D'Arcy's needs "coincided exactly with those of the Foreign Office, anxious about the route to India, and of the Admiralty, seeking reliable fuel oil supplies." Henceforth profit and politics would be inextricably linked in Persia.

### *To the Fire Temple: Masjid-i-Suleiman*

The Concession Syndicate shifted exploration to southwestern Persia. Under Reynolds's direction, wells at Chiah Surkh were plugged, the camp closed, and some forty tons of equipment dismantled, carried back to Baghdad, shipped down the Tigris to Basra, then transshipped to the Iranian port of Mohammerah. Eventually it would be shipped by river, wagon, and mules (as many as nine hundred) to new sites. Drilling first commenced at Shardin.

But there was another potential site: **Maidan-i-Naftan**, "the Plain of Oil." The specific spot, **Masjid-i-Suleiman**, was named for a nearby fire temple. Reynolds had first visited this roadless spot in late November 1903, feeling dispirited and ready to pack it all in, when he encountered a British official, **Louis Dane**, in Kuwait. Dane was traveling with Lord Curzon to celebrate the Lansdowne Declaration and was compiling a gazetteer of the Gulf. He had come across several references to Maidan-i-Naftan that reminded him of Baku. At Dane's strong urging — "it seems a thousand pities to turn up what may be an immense national benefit" — Reynolds set off for Maidan-i-Naftan. In February 1904, he reported rocks saturated with oil. In 1906, he returned and found even more extensive indications. Boverton Redwood called it the most important and promising information to date.

The operation at Masjid-i-Suleiman was "not all beer and skittles," Reynolds informed Burmah managers in Glasgow. Drinking water was "best described as water with dung in suspension." The nearest dentist was fifteen hundred miles away in Karachi. Workers could find relief closer to home at what was euphemistically called the "dentist" — a mere 150 miles away in Basra.

**George Reynolds** was the man who held it all together. Around fifty when he arrived in Persia in September 1901, he was at one and the same time engineer, geologist, manager, diplomat, linguist, and anthropologist. He had a knack for jerry-rigging machinery when parts broke. He was taciturn, tough, and tenacious. Arnold Wilson described him as "dignified in negotiation, quick in action, and completely single-minded in his determination to find oil. In short, Reynolds was 'solid British oak.'" But his true bane was not the desert nor the tribesmen — it was Burmah Oil in Glasgow, which constantly second-guessed him. Reynolds responded with "searing and impolitic sarcasm": "You really amuse me by instructing me how to run a contumacious Parsee and an alcoholic driller, both suffering from swelled heads."

### *Revolution in Tehran*

The Shah's government was in advanced decay, and foreigners' concessions were a major political sore. Conservative religious opponents led the attack on despotism, joining merchants seeking liberal reforms. In July 1906, riots erupted when the government attempted to arrest a prominent preacher. As many as fourteen thousand Persians took to the streets and sought refuge in the garden of the British legation. The result was a new constitution and a Majlis (Parliament), which put investigation of the concession at the top of its agenda. But the new political system proved unstable.

The new drilling site was in the winter grazing pasture of the **Bakhtiari**, the most powerful tribal confederacy. They were nomads, driving flocks of sheep and goats, living in goat-hair tents. In 1905, Reynolds made an arrangement with some Bakhtiari — in exchange for a fee and profit share, they would provide "guards" for the concession. But among the main things to be guarded against were the Bakhtiaris themselves. Reynolds described one Bakhtiari leader as "a man as full of intrigue as the egg of a nightingale is pregnant with music." D'Arcy could only complain, "Of course Baksheesh is at the root of it all."

The Foreign Office dispatched a guard force — a total of two British officers and twenty Indian cavalry. In 1907, the Anglo-Russian Convention partitioned Persia into spheres of influence: northern Persia under Russian sway, the southeast under British, and the middle a neutral zone — which happened to be the location of the new drilling sites.

### *Racing the Clock*

Masjid-i-Suleiman was "the last throw of the concessionary dice." There was no road; one had to be carved from the desert. A torrential rain wiped out most of a half-year's effort. Finally, in January 1908, drilling began at this last site.

Burmah Oil was most unhappy. Its vice-chairman suggested "the whole thing" might "go smash." In April 1908, the Burmah board told D'Arcy the money was exhausted and unless he put up half of additional funds, work would stop. "Of course, I cannot find £20,000 or anything," D'Arcy complained. But he shrewdly concluded Burmah was too committed to back out, and simply ignored their April 30 deadline.

On May 14, 1908, Burmah sent a letter to Reynolds saying the project was over and he should be prepared to pack up. The letter instructed Reynolds to carry the two wells down to no more than sixteen hundred feet. If no oil was found, he was to "abandon operations, close down, and bring as much of the plant as is possible down to Mohammerah." The end of the Concession Syndicate seemed near. But the letter was not received in Persia for several weeks — just the delay Reynolds needed.

Excitement at the drilling site was mounting. A smell of natural gas could be detected. Then a drill bit came unscrewed and was lost in the hole; several days were spent fishing for it in temperatures reaching no degrees in the shade. Vaporous gas could clearly be seen rising from the hole. On the night of **May 25, 1908**, Arnold Wilson went to sleep outside his tent because of the heat. Shortly after 4:00 a.m. on the twenty-sixth, he was awakened by shouting. A gusher of petroleum, rising perhaps fifty feet above the top of the drilling rig, was smothering the drillers. The accompanying gas was threatening to suffocate the workers.

**Oil had, at last, been struck in Persia.** It was just two days short of seven years since the Shah had signed the concession. According to legend, Wilson sent the news in code: "See Psalm 104 verse 15 third sentence." The Bible reads: "that he may bring out of the earth oil to make a cheerful countenance." D'Arcy was delighted but kept his enthusiasm in check: "I am telling no one about it until I get the news confirmed." Three weeks later, the first well continued to gush, and oil was struck in the second well. Around this time, Reynolds received the May 14 letter from Burmah ordering him to wind down — a striking echo of the letter half a century earlier that had told Colonel Drake to quit at Titusville. Reynolds had already sent a cable back: "The instructions you say you are sending me may be modified by the fact that oil has been struck, so on receipt of them I can hardly act on them."

Reynolds remained in Persia a couple more years but was unceremoniously fired in January 1911 despite D'Arcy trying to protect him. Arnold Wilson offered an epitaph: "He was able to endure heat and cold, disappointment and success, and to get the best out of every Persian, Indian, and European with whom he came in contact, except his Scottish employers, whose short-sighted parsimony had so nearly wrecked a great enterprise."

### *The "Big Company": Anglo-Persian*

On April 19, 1909, the Glasgow branch of the Bank of Scotland was mobbed by fevered investors. The newly incorporated **Anglo-Persian Oil Company** was going public. The public stood ten deep at the counter, clutching application forms. The Admiralty objected to the draft prospectus's "making public" its encouragement of Burmah's stake: "As the Admiralty is our prospective good customer, we cannot afford to stamp on their corns." Mrs. D'Arcy, with theatrical flair, protested the omission of her husband's name from the company's title: "This, I think, a great mistake as far and wide his name is associated with this Persian business. I am making a last bid for fame to you."

D'Arcy came out well — he received shares worth £895,000 (£30 million or $55 million today). But he lamented: "I feel like signing away a child." D'Arcy became a director but his influence faded. He died in 1917; the company merely kept "D'Arcy" for an exploration subsidiary.

Anglo-Persian quickly became a significant company — by end of 1910, it already employed 2,500 people. Arnold Wilson, acting consul in the region, became the de facto adviser on local affairs: "I have spent a fortnight upon Oil Company business, mediating between Englishmen who cannot always say what they mean and Persians who do not always mean what they say."

A 138-mile pipeline, crossing two ranges of hills and a desert plain — its route initially marked by sticks and calico flags — was built in a year and a half using six thousand mules. The refinery site was **Abadan**, a long narrow island of mud flats and palm trees in the Shatt-al-Arab estuary. The laborers were mainly Indians from Burmah's Rangoon refinery, and the construction was badly done. On its first test in July 1912, the refinery immediately broke down, thereafter operating far below capacity with poor-quality kerosene that had a "yellowish tinge and filmed up lamps."

In October 1912, Anglo-Persian made a deal with Asiatic (Royal Dutch/Shell's trading arm) — Anglo-Persian would sell crude, gasoline, and kerosene through Asiatic but reserved the rights to its fuel oil, on which it was basing its strategy for future growth. Shell wanted to contain any new threats: "the situation of these people, apparently with very large supplies, made them rather a serious menace in the East."

But Anglo-Persian was in deep financial trouble again. By end of 1912, working capital was exhausted. John Cargill, chairman of Burmah Oil: "What a hell of a mess Persian things are in. It's all very well to say 'don't worry,' but my name and business reputation are too closely associated with the Anglo-Persian Oil Company to admit of my not being terribly anxious." Millions of pounds were needed, with no obvious way to get new capital. Without an infusion, the effort might grind to a stop or be swallowed by Royal Dutch/Shell. A new savior would have to be found.

## Chapter 8: The Fateful Plunge

In July 1903, during one of his many moments of despair, D'Arcy had taken himself to the spa at Marienbad, in Bohemia. His spirits were lifted by an acquaintance he made — **Admiral John Fisher**, then the Second Sea Lord, already known as the "oil maniac." That chance meeting would eventually push oil to the center of national strategies.

### *"The God-father of Oil"*

**John Arbuthnot Fisher**, memorialized by Marcus Samuel as "the God-father of oil," became First Sea Lord in 1904. For the next six years, "Jacky" Fisher would dominate the Royal Navy as no other man had ever done. Born in Ceylon in 1854 to an impoverished planter family, he went to sea at thirteen as a naval cadet. He had advantages of neither birth nor rank, advancing by sheer intelligence, tenacity, and force of will — "a mixture of Machiavelli and a child." He was "a tornado of energy, enthusiasm, and persuasive power." King Edward VII himself told Fisher, "I wish you would stop shaking your fist in my face."

Fisher had only one consuming passion — the Royal Navy. He dedicated himself to modernizing it, shaking it free of "its ingrained habits, its complacency, its cobwebbed traditions." An officer who served under him said "'Jacky' was never satisfied with anything but 'Full Speed!'" His "Golden rule" was never "to allow ourselves to be out 'classed.'" He championed the submarine, the destroyer, Kelvin's compass, advances in firepower, naval aviation — and petroleum. "Oil fuel," he wrote as early as 1901, "will absolutely revolutionize naval strategy. It's a case of 'Wake up England!'" He wanted to convert the fleet from coal to oil for faster speed and greater efficiency.

While First Sea Lord, Fisher provided much of the impetus for the Admiralty's support of the Persian concession and the pressure on Burmah Oil to rescue D'Arcy. His principal objective was always the same — to bring the Royal Navy into the industrial age and have it prepared for when war came. He was convinced Britain's enemy would be imperial Germany.

### *"Made in Germany"*

The burgeoning naval race between Britain and Germany was the single factor that counted most in their growing antagonism. Germany's *Weltpolitik* — the drive for global political, strategic, and economic prominence — was heavy-handed and aggressive. Kaiser Wilhelm was temperamental, erratic, prejudiced, and mercurial. Germany's aim was to break "England's world domination so as to lay free the necessary colonial possessions for the central European states who need to expand." The Kaiser declared, "Only when we can hold out our mailed fist against his face will the British lion draw back." The Germans launched their naval challenge in 1897.

Naval supremacy was central to England's conception of its world role and to the security of the British Empire. Industrial leadership was slipping to the United States and to Germany. In 1896, *Made in Germany* became a best seller. Britain, moaned a Cabinet minister, was "the weary Titan."

Fisher had no doubt Germany was the future enemy and feared a bolt out of the blue on a long holiday weekend — so his aides were always kept on special duty. By 1904, the naval race was "a runaway technological revolution" in battleship size, speed, firepower, and new weapons.

Britain's ruling Liberal party was torn between "navalists" (big Navy + expanded construction) and "economists" (contain naval spending, fund social welfare). The debate was bitter: "Is Britain going to surrender her maritime supremacy to provide old-age pensions?" The "economists" were led by **David Lloyd George**, Chancellor of the Exchequer, and for a time by **Winston Spencer-Churchill**, "the young man in a hurry."

### *Enter Churchill*

**Winston Churchill** was nephew of the Duke of Marlborough, son of the brilliant but erratic Lord Randolph Churchill and his beautiful American wife, Jennie Jerome. He entered Parliament as a Conservative in 1901 at twenty-six, then bolted to the Liberals over free trade. He became President of the Board of Trade and by 1910, Home Secretary. He lived for politics and grand strategy — on the day of his marriage, waiting in the vestry, he talked and gossiped of politics.

Churchill threw himself into the "economists" campaign against Fisher's expanded naval program, championing an Anglo-German naval agreement to reduce the Navy's budget. He declared belief in the inevitability of war with Germany was "all nonsense."

But in July 1911, the German gunboat *Panther* sailed into the Moroccan port of Agadir. The episode backfired, consolidating anti-German feeling. Churchill's views were instantaneously transformed. Germany meant to make war. At end of September 1911, after the crisis passed, Asquith asked Churchill during a golf game if he would like to become First Lord of the Admiralty. "Indeed I would," Churchill replied.

"The whole fortunes of our race and Empire," Churchill said, "the whole treasure accumulated during so many centuries of sacrifice and achievement, would perish and be swept utterly away if our naval supremacy were to be impaired." His guiding precept: "I intended to prepare for an attack by Germany as if it might come the next day."

### *Fisher and Churchill — Speed!*

Fisher, recently retired, had been entranced by Churchill since their first meeting at Biarritz in 1907. Fisher became Churchill's "dry nurse" — the dominating unofficial adviser. Churchill regarded Fisher as the source of "all the most important steps taken to enlarge, improve, or modernize the Navy." Fisher bombarded him with memos — "a veritable volcano of knowledge and of inspiration."

Fisher set out to educate Churchill about petroleum's importance to naval strategy. To speed Churchill's education, Fisher conspired with Marcus Samuel. Fisher arranged for Samuel to make the case for oil, but Churchill was not impressed with the Shell chairman. Fisher apologized: "He is not as good at exposition but he began as a pedlar selling 'sea' shells! (Hence the name of his Company) and now he has six million sterling of his own private money. 'He's a good teapot though he may be a bad pourer'!"

Fisher lectured Churchill on oil's advantages: "Remember oil like coal don't deteriorate and you can accumulate vast stores of it in submerged tanks so as to be free from destruction by fire or bombardment or incendiaries and *east of Suez oil is cheaper than coal.*" He added: "When a cargo steamer can save 78 percent in fuel and gain 30 percent in cargo space by the adoption of internal combustion propulsion and practically get rid of stokers and engineers — it is obvious what a prodigious change is at our doors with oil!" He warned Churchill about delays: "Your old women will have a nice time of it when the new American Battleships are at sea burning oil alone and a German Motor Battleship is cocking a snook at our 'Tortoises'!"

When Churchill arrived at the Admiralty, the Navy already had fifty-six destroyers and seventy-four submarines solely dependent on oil. But the battleships — the backbone of the Navy — burned coal. Churchill and the Navy wanted a new breed of battleships with bigger guns, stronger armor, and greater speed. Fisher reminded him: "The first of all necessities is SPEED, so as to be able to fight — *When* you like, *Where* you like, and *How* you like." The War College estimated that a "Fast Division" at twenty-five knots could get the better of the emerging German fleet. The Royal Navy wanted an extra four knots — and there was no way to get it without oil.

Churchill's education was complete. Oil offered not only higher speeds but greater rapidity in getting up to speed, a greater radius of action, refueling at sea, reduced stoker manpower by more than half, and less stress and exhaustion from coaling. "As a coal ship used up her coal," Churchill wrote, "increasingly large numbers of men had to be taken, if necessary from the guns, to shovel the coal from remote and inconvenient bunkers... The use of oil made it possible in every type of vessel to have more gun-power and more speed for less size or less cost."

The three naval programs of 1912, 1913, and 1914 constituted the greatest addition in the history of the Royal Navy — all based on oil, not a coal-burning ship among them. The key decision came in April 1912: the **Queen Elizabeth class** — five oil-fired battleships for the Fast Division. With this **"fateful plunge,"** Churchill wrote, "the supreme ships of the Navy, on which our life depended, were fed by oil and could only be fed by oil."

The commitment raised a grave problem — where to find the oil, and would it be militarily secure? Churchill's great gamble was pushing for conversion *before* the supply problem was solved. "To commit the Navy irrevocably to oil was indeed 'to take arms against a sea of troubles.'" Yet "if the difficulties and risks could be surmounted, we should be able to raise the whole power and efficiency of the Navy to a definitely higher level — in a word, 'mastery itself was the prize of the venture.'"

### *The Admiral Cracks the Nut*

Churchill established a committee to study the conversion, then recommended a royal commission headed by the retired Admiral Fisher. Fisher was furious with Churchill over promotions: "You have betrayed the Navy. This must be the last communication with you in any matter at all." Churchill wrote a most forceful letter of cajolery: "This liquid fuel problem has got to be solved... I want you for this, *viz*, to crack the nut. No one else can do it so well. Perhaps no one else can do it at all... You have got to find the oil; to show how it can be stored cheaply... When you have solved the riddle, you will find a vy hushed attentive audience. But the riddle will not be solved unless you are willing — for the glory of God — to expend yourself upon its toils."

Fisher accepted. He sold his Shell shares to avoid conflict of interest. He threw himself into the job, working as hard as he ever had. His urgency increased when he learned the German Navy was going forward with oil propulsion. "*They have killed* 15 *men in experiments with oil engines and we have not killed one!*"

The commission's report (November 1912 and two further sections in 1913) stressed the "overwhelming advantages in favour of oil fuel" over coal for the Royal Navy. It called for much-expanded storage because "Oil don't grow in England."

### *The Shell Menace*

Though Anglo-Persian's creation was the result of D'Arcy, Reynolds, and Burmah Oil, **Charles Greenway** was the man who really fashioned the company. Starting as manager of a Scottish trading house in Bombay, he became managing director and dominated Anglo-Persian for the next two decades. Known later as "Champagne Charlie" and "Old Spats and Monocle," Greenway was tenacious and always ready for a brawl. His central objectives: build Anglo-Persian into a major force in world oil, make it the national champion of Great Britain, resist Royal Dutch/Shell's "unwelcome and suffocating embrace," and ensure his own unquestioned control.

Greenway offered the Admiralty a twenty-year fuel contract and campaigned for a special relationship. His recurrent theme: without government aid, Anglo-Persian would disappear into Shell. If that happened, Shell would have a monopoly and extract monopoly prices from a hapless Royal Navy. He stressed Samuel's "Jewishness" and Deterding's "Dutchness" — Shell was controlled by Royal Dutch and the Dutch government was susceptible to German pressure. Control by Shell would eventually place Anglo-Persian "under the control of the German Government itself."

Greenway said all he and his fellow patriotic Englishmen asked was "some small consideration from the British government — just a guarantee or contract that will at any rate give us a moderate return on our capital." The Foreign Office supported him: the Anglo-Persian concession "embracing as it did the entire oil fields of Persia ... should not pass under the control of a foreign syndicate." Sir Edward Grey, the Foreign Secretary: "what we must do is to secure under British control a sufficient oil field for the British Navy."

Fisher privately insisted: "We must do our d-----st to get control of the Anglo-Persian Company and to keep it for all times as an absolutely 'all-British' Company."

### *Aid for Anglo-Persian*

Initially, the Admiralty feared becoming involved in a "speculative risk." But three factors changed the outlook: (1) growing doubts about reliability of petroleum supplies from other sources; (2) the price of fuel oil doubling between January and July 1913 in response to rising maritime demand; (3) Churchill pushing decisions and forcing analysis.

In June 1913, Churchill presented the Cabinet with a key memorandum on "Oil Fuel Supply for His Majesty's Navy," calling for long-term contracts, "keeping alive independent competitive sources of supplies," and frustrating "the formation of a universal oil monopoly." The Cabinet agreed the government should "acquire a controlling interest in trustworthy sources of supply." The idea emerged that the government itself become a shareholder in Anglo-Persian.

On **July 17, 1913**, Churchill made a landmark statement to Parliament. "If we cannot get oil," he warned, "we cannot get corn, we cannot get cotton and we cannot get a thousand and one commodities necessary for the preservation of the economic energies of Great Britain." The Admiralty should become "the owners or, at any rate, the controllers at the source" of a substantial part of the oil it required. "On no one quality, on no one process, on no one country, on no one route and on no one field must we be dependent. Safety and certainty in oil lie in variety and variety alone."

The Cabinet sent a commission to Persia led by **Admiral Edmond Slade**, former Director of Naval Intelligence. The company hastily introduced cosmetic improvements at Abadan, rushing in a new refinery manager from Rangoon. Slade privately informed Churchill: "It seems to be a thoroughly sound concession, which may be developed to a gigantic extent with a large expenditure of capital. It would put us into a perfectly safe position as regards the supply of oil for naval purposes *if we had the control of the company.*" In his official report (January 1914), he added it would be "a national disaster if the concession were allowed to pass into foreign hands."

### *A Victory for Oil*

On **May 20, 1914**, the deal was signed: the British government would invest **£2.2 million** in Anglo-Persian, acquiring 51 percent of the stock and placing two directors on the board with a veto on Admiralty fuel contracts and major political matters, but not commercial activities. A separate secret contract gave the Admiralty a twenty-year fuel oil supply with a rebate from profits.

On **June 17, 1914**, Churchill rose in the House of Commons to introduce the historic bill. Charles Greenway sat in the official box. Also present was Samuel Samuel (Marcus's brother), who became increasingly fidgety as Churchill spoke. Churchill described the world oil landscape: "Two gigantic corporations — one in either hemisphere — stand out predominantly. In the New World there is the Standard Oil. ... In the Old World the great combination of the Shell and the Royal Dutch... has practically covered the whole ground."

Samuel Samuel popped up three times to object to Churchill's characterizations of Royal Dutch/Shell and was ruled out of order. "He had better hear the case for the prosecution," Churchill acidly said, "before he offers an argument for the defense."

Churchill's oratory was full of sarcasm. If the bill failed, Anglo-Persian would become part of Shell. "We have no quarrel with the 'Shell.' We have always found them courteous, considerate, ready to oblige, anxious to serve the Admiralty... The only difficulty has been price." With the leverage of Persian oil, "we do not think we shall be treated with less courtesy, or less consideration."

Samuel finally got his chance: "I do protest most strongly on behalf of one of the greatest British commercial industrial companies, that the attacks that have been made are wholly unjustifiable." Another M.P., Watson Rutherford, criticized Churchill for raising the specter of monopoly and for "Jew-baiting," declaring rising fuel oil prices resulted not from a trust but from the recent emergence of an international fuel oil market.

Churchill's proposal for government ownership was unprecedented, save for Disraeli's purchase of shares in the Suez Canal half a century earlier. Despite strong criticism, the oil bill passed by an overwhelming vote — **254 to 18**. After the vote, Greenway asked Churchill, "How did you manage to carry the House with you so successfully?" "It was," Churchill replied, "the attack on monopolies and trusts that did it."

Churchill had been more than a little cynical. There was no evidence Shell had ever served the Admiralty poorly — indeed, years before, Marcus Samuel had actually asked the government to place a director on Shell's board. And while Churchill had taken a dislike to Samuel, he had developed a most favorable opinion of Deterding — the foreigner. Fisher wrote to Churchill that Deterding "is Napoleon and Cromwell rolled into one. *He is the greatest man I ever met.* ... Napoleonic in his audacity: Cromwellian in his thoroughness! ... Placate him, don't threaten him! Make a contract with him for his fleet of 64 oil tankers in case of war. Don't abuse the Shell Company. ... [Deterding] has a son at Rugby or Eton and has bought a big property in Norfolk and [is] building a castle! Bind him to the land of his adoption!" Churchill did exactly that.

Deterding was practical and understood the rationale for the Anglo-Persian arrangement. Fisher wrote on July 31, 1914: "I have just received a most patriotic letter from Deterding... to say he means you shan't want for oil or tankers in case of war — *Good Old Deterding!* How these Dutchmen do hate the Germans! Knight him when you get the chance."

### *The Coming of War*

Critics were perplexed by the government's purchase. The Viceroy of India declared it "altogether unwise to become dependent upon a most insecure foreign source of oil when Britain was blessed with an abundance of secure coal." The Secretary of State for India likened it to "the owners of the *premier cru* vineyards in the Gironde went about preaching the virtues of Scotch whisky as a beverage."

But the decision was driven by the technological imperatives of the Anglo-German naval race. Oil offered a vital edge in speed and flexibility. The deal assured the British government a large supply of oil, provided Anglo-Persian with desperately needed capital, and spoke to the survival of the empire. **By the summer of 1914, the British Navy was fully committed to oil and the British government had assumed the role of Anglo-Persian's majority stockholder. Oil, for the first time, but certainly not the last, had become an instrument of national policy, a strategic commodity second to none.**

During the weeks leading up to the June 17 debate, Europe had seemed more at peace than in years. British naval units were making courtesy visits to German ports. Many would later look back on the early summer of 1914 with nostalgia, as the dusk of an era. On June 28, 1914, eleven days after Parliament approved Churchill's bill, Archduke Franz Ferdinand of Austria was assassinated at Sarajevo. It was not until August 10 that the Anglo-Persian Oil Convention received its Royal Assent. By then, Russia had mobilized, Germany had declared war, and at 11:00 p.m. on August 4, Churchill flashed a message to all of His Majesty's ships: "commence hostilities against Germany." The First World War had begun.

## Chapter 9: The Blood of Victory — World War I

It was supposed to be a short war, over in a few weeks or months. Instead, it sank into stalemate and dragged on. An estimated 13 million people died, many millions more were wounded and displaced. It was a catastrophe for the political systems of much of Europe. One of the great historians of international relations would recall the war as "the well-spring of our discontents."

It was a war fought between men and machines — and these machines were powered by oil, just as Admiral Fisher and Winston Churchill had foreseen, but to a much greater extent than even they expected. Oil and the internal combustion engine changed every dimension of warfare, even the very meaning of mobility on land, sea, and in the air.

### *The Taxi Armada*

The French government had already evacuated Paris with 100,000 civilians. The fall of the capital seemed imminent. General Joffre, Commander in Chief, considered ordering his troops to drop back. But the military governor of Paris, **General Joseph Gallieni**, had other ideas. Aerial reconnaissance convinced him an opportunity existed to hit the German lines. The British Army would not take him seriously — "No British officer would be seen speaking to such a comedian." But in an emotional angry nighttime phone call — what Gallieni called his "*coups de telephone*" — he finally persuaded General Joffre to launch a counterattack.

On September 6, 1914, the French went on the offensive. But when the Germans brought up reinforcements, the French were in a precarious position. Desperately needed reinforcements were in Paris but could not be moved — railway was disrupted, marching would be too slow.

Gallieni had his inspiration at 8:00 p.m., sitting at a lycee on the boulevard des Invalides: an armada of Paris taxicabs. He ordered every one of the three thousand available taxis commandeered. Policemen stopped cabs, demanded passengers disgorge, and directed them to the Invalides. "How will we be paid?" one driver asked the lieutenant who flagged him down. "By the meter or on a flat rate?" "By the meter," the lieutenant said. "All right, let's go," replied the driver, making sure to put down his flag before starting off.

Within two hours, scores of taxis were converging. They took off in great convoys up the Champs-Elysees, along the rue Royale and the rue Lafayette. "Today destiny will deliver a great decision," Helmuth von Moltke wrote to his wife. "What torrents of blood have flowed!" Once night fell, each taxi crammed with soldiers, their meter flags down, set off in convoys of twenty-five to fifty toward the battlefield — "this forerunner of the future motorized column."

Thousands of troops were rushed to the critical point. They made the difference. On September 9, the Germans fell back. "Things are going badly, the battles east of Paris will not be decided in our favor," Moltke wrote. "Our campaign is a cruel disillusion." The taxicab drivers returned to Paris after two days without sleep, were paid their fares, and were besieged by the curious. They had helped save Paris and demonstrated what motorized transport would mean in the future.

### *Internal Combustion at War*

The First Battle of the Marne (September 6-8, 1914) was the turning point, ending the much-planned German offensive. When the Germans halted their retreat, both sides dug trenches, settling into a long, bloody, senseless war of attrition — the static war of defense. For more than two years, the lines on the western front moved no more than ten miles in either direction. The machine gun, combined with trenches and barbed wire, gave primacy to defense. Lord Kitchener, British War Secretary: "I don't know what is to be done. This isn't war."

The only way to break the stalemate was mechanical innovation. The first military man to diagnose the disease and conceive the antidote was British colonel **Ernest Swinton**, who, as an "official eyewitness" in France, came up with the idea of an armored vehicle powered by the internal combustion engine, impervious to machine gun bullets and barbed wire. The high command did not take the idea seriously. It might have died altogether had it not been championed by Churchill, who told the PM in January 1915: "The present war has revolutionized all military theories about the field of fire." Churchill doled out Navy funds for development. The new machine was known as the "land cruiser" or "landship" — Churchill called it the "caterpillar." For secrecy, it needed a code name; various names including "cistern" and "reservoir" were considered. But finally it became known as the **"tank."**

The tank was first used prematurely at the Battle of the Somme (1916), played a more important role at Cambrai (November 1917), but had its most decisive impact on **August 8, 1918**, at the **Battle of Amiens**, when 456 tanks broke through the German line, resulting in what General Erich Ludendorff called the "black day of the German Army in the history of the war." The "primacy of the defense" was over. When the German High Command declared in October 1918 that victory was no longer possible, the first reason was the introduction of the tank.

Another oil-powered revolution was mechanized transport. The British Expeditionary Force in August 1914 had just 827 motor cars (747 requisitioned) and 15 motorcycles. By the last months of the war, British Army vehicles included **56,000 trucks, 23,000 motorcars, and 34,000 motorcycles**. The U.S., entering in April 1917, brought another 50,000 gasoline-driven vehicles. The victory of the Allies over Germany was "in some ways the victory of the truck over the locomotive."

### *The War in the Air and at Sea*

The airplane had an even more dramatic impact. The Wright brothers flew in 1903, but General Foch dismissed aviation as "good sport, but for the Army the aeroplane is worthless." At the outbreak of war, the British aviation industry barely numbered a thousand people and had produced just 250 planes (sixty experimental). Yet by July 1915, every machine in the air at the war's outbreak had become obsolete. Air combat evolved from pilots shooting with handguns to machine guns synchronized with propellers. By 1916, planes flew in formation with developed tactics. Tactical bombing and strategic bombing emerged — Germany launched zeppelin and bomber assaults against England in what became "the first Battle of Britain." By the war's last months, aircraft speeds had more than doubled to over 120 mph, with a ceiling of 27,000 feet. Wartime production: Britain 55,000 planes; France 68,000; Italy 20,000; Germany 48,000; U.S. (in a year and a half) 15,000.

The prewar naval race produced a stalemate. Britain's Grand Fleet was superior but the two fleets met only once in major engagement — the **Battle of Jutland** (May 31, 1916). The German fleet was tactically victorious but strategically the British won, dominating the North Sea for the rest of the war. Events proved Churchill and Fisher right: the conversion to oil gave the British fleet greater range, speed, and faster refueling. The German High Seas Fleet was primarily coal-burning with no stations outside Germany — its reliance on coal made its name a misnomer.

### *Anglo-Persian Versus Shell*

Anglo-Persian in 1914 accounted for just under one percent of total world oil output. Churchill himself, less than a month after war began, despaired of Britain's ability to defend the Persian oil fields: "There is little likelihood of any troops being available for this purpose. We shall have to buy our oil from elsewhere."

The forces of the **Ottoman Empire** were the chief threat. Immediately after Turkey entered the war on Germany's side in autumn 1914, its troops threatened the Abadan refinery. British soldiers repulsed them and went on to capture **Basra** — critical because it guarded approaches to Persian oil. The British extended their line toward Baghdad; one of the major considerations was to secure oil fields and counteract German subversion. In 1917, after a degrading defeat, the British finally captured Baghdad. Oil production in Persia grew more than **tenfold between 1912 and 1918 — from 1,600 barrels per day to 18,000**. By late 1916, Anglo-Persian was meeting a fifth of the British Navy's entire oil needs.

**Charles Greenway** pursued a clear strategy to transform Anglo-Persian from a crude producer into an integrated oil company — "to build up an absolutely self-contained organization." His most important step was purchasing **British Petroleum**, one of the largest petroleum distribution networks in the UK. Despite its name, it had belonged to the Deutsche Bank for its Rumanian oil; the British government had seized it at the outbreak of war. Anglo-Persian acquired not only a major marketing system but a most useful name. Up until 1916-17, over 80 percent of Anglo-Persian's fixed assets were in Persia; the very next year, only half were, with the rest in tankers and distribution. It had become an integrated company.

Greenway revived the "Shell menace," attacking "the schemes of Sir Marcus and his associates for securing a worldwide monopoly of the oil trade." But these charges were unfair. Deterding had naturalized himself British and spent the war years in London, identifying with the Allies. Marcus Samuel was simply a fierce British patriot — one of his two sons was killed in France leading his platoon. Of his two sons-in-law, one was killed in action, the other died after the war from trench warfare effects.

Samuel himself masterminded an audacious scheme of critical importance: extracting **toluol** — the essential ingredient for the explosive TNT — from Shell's Borneo crude. By end of 1914, coal-based toluol production was inadequate and Britain was perilously close to running out of explosives. Shell's toluol-extracting factory had been built in Rotterdam by the Dutch arm of the Group, and German companies were using its output to make TNT. In the middle of the night at the end of January 1915, the plant was disassembled, each piece numbered and camouflaged, loaded onto a Dutch freighter that rendezvoused at sea with British destroyers. A cover story leaked to Germans said the evacuation would occur a day later — that following night, a similar Dutch freighter was torpedoed at the mouth of Rotterdam's harbor. The plant was re-erected in Somerset within weeks and, along with a second plant Shell built, **provided 80 percent of the British military's TNT**. Samuel was awarded a peerage for this achievement.

Shell acted as the **quartermaster general for oil**, acquiring and organizing supplies around the world for the British forces — delivering products from Borneo, Sumatra, and the United States to the railheads and airfields in France. Government officials, concerned about alienating Shell when it was most needed, began to react negatively to Greenway's continuing attacks. Greenway so overplayed his hand that he eventually turned many in the government against Anglo-Persian.

### *"A Dearth of Petrol"*

As late as 1915, oil supply raised little anxiety. But by early 1916, the *Times* of London reported "a dearth of petrol." The following May, the *Times* called for a sharp definition of where "joy-riding" ended and war demands began. The reasons were twofold: (1) the growing shortage of shipping tonnage owing to the **German submarine campaign**, which constricted oil along with all other raw materials; (2) the rapidly growing demand for oil on the battlefield and home front. The government instituted rationing.

Pressure returned at the beginning of 1917 when Germany unleashed **unrestricted submarine warfare** against Allied shipping — a blunder of immense proportions that led the United States to enter the war. Tonnage lost in the first half of 1917 was twice that in the comparable period of 1916. Standard Oil of New Jersey lost six tankers, including the brand new *John D. Archbold*. Among Shell's losses was the *Murex* — the first vessel Marcus Samuel had dispatched through the Suez Canal a quarter century earlier.

By end of May 1917, Admiralty oil stocks were less than half the six-month target, and it was even suggested the Royal Navy stop building oil-driven ships and go back to coal. "Desperate" telegrams were dispatched to America declaring the Royal Navy would be immobilized, "fleet out of action," unless more tonnage was available. "The Germans are succeeding," the American ambassador in London despairingly wrote in July 1917. "They have lately sunk so many fuel oil ships, that this country may very soon be in a perilous condition — even the Grand Fleet may not have enough fuel." In October, Walter Long, Secretary of State for the Colonies, warned the House of Commons: "You may have men, munitions, and money, but if you do not have oil, which is today the greatest motive power that you use, all your other advantages would be of comparatively little value." Pleasure driving in Britain was summarily and completely banned.

France's oil position was also degenerating. In December 1917, Senator Berenger warned Clemenceau that France would run out of oil by March 1918. Clemenceau urgently appealed to President Wilson on December 15, 1917, that an additional hundred thousand tons of tanker capacity be made immediately available, declaring that gasoline was "as vital as blood in the coming battles" and "a failure in the supply of gasoline would cause the immediate paralysis of our armies." He ominously added that a shortage might even "compel us to a peace unfavorable to the Allies." Wilson responded quickly with the necessary tonnage.

An **Inter-Allied Petroleum Conference** was established in February 1918 to pool, coordinate, and control all oil supplies and tanker shipping — members were the U.S., Britain, France, and Italy. Standard Oil of New Jersey and Royal Dutch/Shell really made the system work, though they continually argued about who was making the larger contribution. That joint system — along with the introduction of convoys as an antidote to U-boats — solved the Allies' oil supply problems for the rest of the war.

### *The Energy Czar*

American oil had become essential to the European war. In 1914, the U.S. had produced **266 million barrels — 65 percent of total world output**. By 1917, output had risen to 335 million barrels — 67 percent. Exports accounted for a quarter of total U.S. production, with the bulk going to Europe. With access to Russian oil closed off by war and revolution, the U.S. was to **satisfy 80 percent of the Allies' wartime requirement for petroleum**.

America's entry into the war complicated the picture — supplies needed for the American military, the Allies, American war industries, and normal civilian use. The **Fuel Administration**, established by President Wilson in August 1917, was part of overall economic mobilization. The head of the Oil Division was **Mark Requa**, a California petroleum engineer who became America's first energy czar. His Oil Division worked with the **National Petroleum War Service Committee**, chaired by **Alfred Bedford**, president of Standard Oil of New Jersey. It was this committee that organized the supply of American oil for the war in Europe. This new pattern of close cooperation between business and government stood in marked contrast to the battle between government and Standard Oil just a decade earlier. Trust busting seemed far away, as the industry was now pushed to run itself as a single body, under the leadership of the once-hated Standard Oil of New Jersey.

In 1917, surging demand hit the limit of available supplies. The bitterly cold winter of 1917-18 created a severe coal shortage. In January 1918, the Fuel Administration ordered almost all industrial plants east of the Mississippi to close for a week to free fuel for Europe-bound ships immobilized in East Coast harbors. Factories were ordered to remain closed on Mondays. "Bedlam broke loose," observed Colonel Edward House, Wilson's political confidant.

The coal shortage stimulated demand for oil; by early 1918, average crude prices were double 1914 levels. On May 17, 1918, Requa warned there was "no justification" for further price advances and called for "voluntary" price controls. Standard Oil of New Jersey agreed; but when independent producers did not, Requa bluntly told them in Tulsa there would be direct government controls. In August 1918, maximum prices were set. An appeal was made for **"Gasolineless Sundays"** — the only exemptions were for freight, doctors, police, emergency vehicles, and hearses. "I suppose," declared President Wilson, "I must walk to church."

### *The Man with the Sledgehammer*

Despite periodic alarms, the Allies never suffered a protracted oil crunch. **Germany did.** The Allied blockade succeeded in choking off supplies from overseas, leaving only one source — **Rumania**. Germany was heavily dependent on it. For the first two years, Rumania remained neutral, waiting to see which side was winning. In August 1916, Rumania declared war against Austria-Hungary. Victory in this Eastern theater was essential for Germany. "As I now saw quite clearly, we should not have been able to exist, much less to carry on the war, without Rumania's corn and oil," said Ludendorff.

German and Austrian troops advanced on Rumania in September 1916. They captured a vast amount of petroleum products, including a large cache of Allied gasoline on the Black Sea. The British Cabinet War Committee discussed the matter urgently on October 31, 1916: "No efforts should be spared to ensure, in case of necessity, the destruction of the supplies of grain and oil, as well as of the oil wells." But the Rumanian government was reluctant to destroy its national treasure.

The British recruited **Colonel John Norton-Griffiths, M.P.**, to organize the destruction. A larger-than-life figure known as "Hell-fire Jack," "the Monkey Man" (for having eaten a monkey in Africa), and most treasured, **"Empire Jack"** — handsome, physically imposing, a charming swashbuckler. His first great engineering feat in WWI had been adapting Manchester sewer-tunneling techniques to mine beneath German trenches. He arrived in Bucharest on November 18, 1916, via Russia, accompanied only by his manservant. As the Germans continued their advance, the Rumanian government finally agreed to the policy of destruction.

The destruction teams swung into action with "Empire Jack" at the forefront. Fields went up in flames on November 26-27. Explosives were placed in refineries, petroleum products in storage were allowed to flow in, creating lakes of oil several inches deep, then everything was set afire. Those who challenged Norton-Griffiths were overwhelmed by sheer force of personality — or he would deliver a powerful kick or pull out his revolver shouting, "I don't speak your blasted language." Apparatus was smashed; derricks dynamited; wells plugged with stone, spikes, mud, broken chains, drillbits. The last field was Ploesti itself. On December 5, only a few hours after the facilities went up in flames, the Germans entered the town. Norton-Griffiths barely escaped by car, just ahead of the German cavalry.

After the war, Ludendorff admitted Norton-Griffiths's efforts "did materially reduce the oil supplies of our army and the home country. We must attribute our shortages in part to him." Altogether, some **seventy refineries and an estimated eight hundred thousand tons of crude oil and petroleum products** had been destroyed. It took the Germans five months to restart production, and for all of 1917, production was only a third of 1914 levels. By 1918, they had pushed it back to 80 percent. Germany's timely capture of the Rumanian oil, along with Rumanian grain, "made just the difference between shortage and collapse" for the German side — but only for a time.

### *Baku*

Even as the Germans were getting Rumanian fields back, Ludendorff set his sights on a greater prize — **Baku**, on the Caspian Sea. The collapse of the Czarist regime in early 1917, the rise of the Bolsheviks, and the fragmentation of the Russian empire held out hope. Germany sought access to Baku petroleum in March 1918 with the Treaty of Brest-Litovsk. But the Turks, Germany's ally, had already begun advancing toward Baku. Fearing their ally would destroy the oil fields, the Germans promised the Bolsheviks they would try to restrain the Turks in exchange for oil. "Of course, we agreed," said Lenin. Stalin telegraphed the Bolshevik Baku Commune ordering it to comply. But the local Bolsheviks refused: "Neither in victory nor in defeat will we give the German plunderers one drop of oil produced by our labor."

The Turks continued their advance. By end of July, they were laying siege to the city. In mid-August 1918, the British intervened with a small force through Persia, charged with saving Baku and keeping oil from the enemy. If need be, they were to follow the Rumanian plan and "destroy the Baku pumping plant, pipeline and oil reservoirs." The British stayed only a month — but long enough to deny Baku oil to the Germans at the critical moment. "A serious blow for us," Ludendorff said. Then the Turks captured the city, and the local Moslems, abetted by the Turks, began again to pillage and destroy, killing every Armenian they could find. Twenty-six Bolshevik commissars were taken to the desert, 140 miles east of the Caspian, and executed. One of the few to escape was a young Armenian named **Anastas Mikoyan**, who eventually got to Moscow to tell Lenin what had happened. But by the time the Turks took Baku, it was too late for the Germans.

### *Floating to Victory*

The denial of Baku was a decisive blow for Germany. By October 1918, the picture was grim. The German Army had all but exhausted its reserves. The German High Command was anticipating a grave petroleum crisis. **Air and mechanized land warfare would cease absolutely within two months.** War industries operating on oil would run out in two months; lubricants exhausted in six months. Limited land operations could continue on a strictly rationed basis.

The estimates were never tested. Within a month, Germany surrendered. The armistice was signed at five in the morning, November 11, 1918, in Marshal Foch's railway car in the Forest of Compiegne. Six hours later, the war was over.

Ten days after the Armistice, the British government hosted a dinner for the Inter-Allied Petroleum Conference at Lancaster House, with **Lord Curzon** as chairman. He had been the great Persian expert, Viceroy of India, member of the War Cabinet, and was shortly to become Foreign Secretary. He told the guests that "one of the most astonishing things" he had seen in France and Flanders "was the tremendous army of motor lorries." Then he resoundingly declared, **"The Allied cause had floated to victory upon a wave of oil."**

Senator Berenger was even more eloquent, declaring oil was **"the blood of the earth — the blood of victory."** Germany had boasted of its superiority in iron and coal, "but it had not taken sufficient account of our superiority of oil." He prophesied: "As oil had been the blood of war, so it would be the blood of the peace. At this hour, at the beginning of the peace, our civilian populations, our industries, our commerce, our farmers are all calling for more oil, always more oil, for more gasoline, always more gasoline." Then he broke into English: **"More oil, ever more oil!"**

## Chapter 10: Opening the Door on the Middle East — The Turkish Petroleum Company

Ten days after the "blood of victory" toasts, French Premier **Georges Clemenceau** visited British Prime Minister **David Lloyd George** in London. Oil was now inextricably linked to postwar politics. Britain wanted to assert influence over Mesopotamia (later Iraq), thought to be highly prospective of oil. France had a claim to one part — Mosul, northwest of Baghdad.

What did Britain want? Clemenceau asked. Would France give up its claim to Mosul, Lloyd George responded, in exchange for British recognition of French control over neighboring Syria? France would, Clemenceau replied — so long as it received a share of the oil production from Mosul. Lloyd George assented. Neither Prime Minister bothered to inform his respective foreign minister. Their casual verbal agreement was not a settlement — it was the beginning of the great postwar struggle for new oil sources that would pit French against English and draw in the Americans.

The Great War had made abundantly clear that petroleum had become an essential element in the strategy of nations. Politicians and bureaucrats would now rush headlong into the competition, drawn by the common perception that the postwar world would require ever-greater quantities of oil for economic prosperity and national power.

### *Mr. Five Percent*

**Calouste Gulbenkian** was the second generation of his family in the oil business. His father was a wealthy Armenian oil man and banker who had built his fortune importing Russian kerosene into the Ottoman Empire and had been rewarded by the Sultan with the governorship of a Black Sea port. The family lived in Constantinople. At age seven, Calouste's first recorded transaction: he took a Turkish silver piece to the bazaar, not to buy candy, but to exchange it for an antique coin. (He later created one of the world's great collections of gold coins, acquiring J. P. Morgan's superb collection of Greek gold coins.) Unpopular as a schoolboy, the young Calouste spent after-school hours in the bazaar, listening to deals and learning the arts of Oriental negotiation.

He studied mining engineering at King's College, London, graduating in 1887 at nineteen with a first-class degree. He wrote a thesis on petroleum. His father sent him to Baku, where the young man was fascinated by the oil industry and was drenched by a gusher — "fine and consistent" oil, though he found the experience unpleasant and never visited oil country again. He published highly regarded articles on Russian oil in a leading French magazine in 1889 and turned them into a book in 1891, making himself a world oil expert at twenty-one.

Turkish officials immediately asked him to investigate Mesopotamian oil possibilities. He put together a competent report based on others' writings and talks with German railway engineers — the region had very great petroleum potential. Thus began his lifelong devotion to Mesopotamian oil, pursued with extraordinary dedication and tenacity over six decades.

In Constantinople, Gulbenkian tried several commercial ventures, mastering the bazaar arts: trading, dealing, intrigue, baksheesh, and information-gathering. He developed his lifelong passion for hard work, his capacity for vision, and his great skills as a negotiator. His favorite Arab proverb: "The hand you dare not bite, kiss it." He was not prone to budge: "It would have been easier to squeeze granite than Mr. Gulbenkian." He was totally and completely untrusting — Sir Kenneth Clark of the National Gallery said, "I have never known anybody so suspicious. He always had people spying for him." He employed two different sets of doctors so he could check one against the other.

In 1896, during one of the periodic Turkish massacres of Armenians, Gulbenkian fled to Egypt. He made himself invaluable to two powerful Armenians — an oil millionaire from Baku and Nubar Pasha, who helped rule Egypt. These connections opened the doors of both oil and international finance. In London, he allied with the Samuel brothers and Henri Deterding. His son Nubar later wrote that Gulbenkian "and Deterding were very close for over twenty years. One never knows whether in the end it was Deterding who used my father or my father who used Deterding." To Shell, Gulbenkian brought deals, especially acquisitions, and arranged financing.

One of his earliest transactions was the Persian concession that went to D'Arcy. Gulbenkian and Deterding had looked at the prospectus but rejected it: "a very wild cat, and it looked so speculative that we thought it was a business for a gambler." Thereafter, he framed a motto — **"Never give up an oil concession"** — that would be his guiding principle for life. In 1907, he persuaded the Samuels to open a Constantinople office. He was financial adviser to the Turkish government and a major stockholder in the **Turkish National Bank** — from this base he brought the rival British and German interests, and then Royal Dutch/Shell, into the **Turkish Petroleum Company** (TPC). The Deutsche Bank and Royal Dutch/Shell each held a quarter, the Turkish National Bank held half (of which Gulbenkian was the silent owner of 30 percent, giving him a 15 percent stake in TPC).

### *The Foreign Office Agreement and the Self-Denying Clause*

From 1912 onward, the British government forced the TPC to amalgamate with D'Arcy's Anglo-Persian syndicate and jointly pursue a concession. Under the **Foreign Office Agreement of March 19, 1914**, British interests predominated: Anglo-Persian held 50 percent, Deutsche Bank and Shell each 25 percent. Gulbenkian was still to contend with: Anglo-Persian and Shell each gave up "beneficiary interests" of 2.5 percent to the Armenian. He could not vote the shares but would enjoy all financial benefits. Thus **Mr. Five Percent** was born.

The signatories agreed to the **"self-denying clause"**: none would be involved in oil production anywhere in the Ottoman Empire save jointly through the Turkish Petroleum Company. The only exceptions were Egypt, Kuwait, and "transferred territories" on the Turco-Persian border. That clause would establish the foundation for oil development in the Middle East — and for titanic struggles — for many years.

On June 28, 1914 — the very day the Grand Vizier promised the concession would be formally granted — Archduke Franz Ferdinand was assassinated. Had the concession actually been granted, or only promised? The answer would hang much argument. The war interred the TPC — apparently.

### *"A First-Class War Aim"*

The **Sykes-Picot Agreement** (1915-16) casually assigned Mosul — one of the most promising oil regions — to a future French sphere. This "surrender" outraged many British officials. The issue became more urgent when British forces captured Baghdad in 1917. Sir Maurice Hankey, secretary of the War Cabinet, wrote to Foreign Secretary Balfour that "oil in the next war will occupy the place of coal in the present war. The only big potential supply that we can get under British control is the Persian and Mesopotamian supply." Therefore, Hankey said, **"control over these oil supplies becomes a first-class British war aim."**

Wilson's Fourteen Points and the call for self-determination complicated British designs. Balfour worried that explicitly pronouncing Mesopotamia a war aim would seem "too old-fashionably imperialistic." Instead, in August 1918, he told the Prime Ministers of the Dominions that Britain must be the "guiding spirit" in Mesopotamia. "I do not care under what system we keep the oil," he said, "but I am quite clear it is all-important for us that this oil should be available." British forces captured Mosul after the armistice with Turkey.

### *Clemenceau and His Grocer*

Before WWI, Clemenceau had supposedly said, "When I want some oil, I'll find it at my grocer's." The war changed his mind. On December 1, 1918, following his drive through London's cheering crowds with Lloyd George, Clemenceau apparently surrendered France's claim to Mosul in exchange for British support for France in Syria, plus a guarantee of a share of any Mosul oil.

But the exchange settled nothing. It initiated protracted, acrimonious negotiations. During the Paris Peace Conference in spring 1919, the two leaders rancorously disagreed and accused each other of bad faith. The discussion nearly became an actual fistfight.

Finally, in April 1920, Lloyd George and France's new premier, Alexandre Millerand, hammered out the **San Remo Agreement**: France would get 25 percent of the oil from Mesopotamia (which would become a British mandate under the League of Nations). The vehicle remained the Turkish Petroleum Company; the French acquired the former German share. France gave up its territorial claim to Mosul. Britain ensured any private company developing Mesopotamian oil would be under its control.

The French also created their own national oil champion: the **Compagnie Francaise des Petroles (CFP)**, established in 1924 under **Colonel Ernest Mercier**, a *Polytechnicien*, war hero, and technocrat devoted to modernizing France. He had already built a modern electric industry. French companies and banks were reluctant to invest — Mesopotamia looked "so full of international difficulties." "None of the initial investors begged for the favor of being admitted into the CFP." Mercier found ninety banks and companies. By 1928, France established a new "constitution" for oil: a quota system to curtail "Anglo-Saxon oil trusts," build a domestic refining industry, and develop France's Mesopotamian share. The state acquired 25 percent ownership of CFP.

### *Amalgamation? — Shell vs. Anglo-Persian*

Britain continued efforts to upset the 60-40 Dutch-British split in Royal Dutch/Shell and bring the Group under British control. Deterding and Shell were interested in amalgamating with Anglo-Persian to gain control before it became a fearsome competitor. But Deterding would not accept British government interference in commercial operations. He also saw disadvantages in too close an association with the British government — Latin American countries were banning concessions to government-controlled companies, specifically targeting Anglo-Persian.

The strongest opposition to amalgamation came from the Admiralty, which was antagonistic to Shell and deeply attached to its right to obtain fuel oil from Anglo-Persian at a substantial discount. Anglo-Persian itself vehemently opposed: Charles Greenway had not fought so hard to turn it into an integrated oil company just to become "merely an addendum to the hated Shell."

### *Reenter Churchill*

Robert Waley Cohen had a brain wave: at a carefully orchestrated dinner, he approached **Winston Churchill** — then out of a job, defeated at Dundee East and writing at Chartwell — with a proposition. Would Churchill lobby for Shell's amalgamation with Anglo-Persian, whereby Shell might purchase the government's shares? Churchill wanted ten thousand pounds if the deal didn't go through and fifty thousand if it did. "We couldn't very well haggle or bargain with Churchill," Burmah's chairman remarked. The sum was split between Shell and Burmah via a secret account.

Churchill, the same man who as First Lord had castigated Shell's voraciousness and insisted the government buy shares to guarantee Anglo-Persian's independence, was now prepared to undo all that. In August 1923, he called on PM Stanley Baldwin, who was "thoroughly in favor of the Oil settlement" and mentioned twenty million pounds as "a very good price" for the government's shares — almost ten times what the government had paid.

But Baldwin called a snap election; Churchill returned his fee and charged back into politics. Britain's first Labour government rejected the amalgamation. In autumn 1924, the Conservatives returned — and the minister now responsible for the government's shares was none other than the new Chancellor of the Exchequer, Winston Churchill, who now opposed selling.

### *Oil Shortage and the Open Door*

American companies were embarking on a worldwide campaign for new oil. A fear of imminent depletion — a virtual obsession — gripped the American oil industry and government at the end of WWI and into the early 1920s. President Wilson agreed: "There seemed to be no method by which we could assure ourselves of the necessary supply at home and abroad." American oil consumption had increased 90 percent between 1911 and 1918. Registered motor vehicles in the U.S. jumped from **1.8 to 9.2 million between 1914 and 1920**. One Senator called on the Navy to reconvert from oil to coal.

The Bureau of Mines director predicted American oil fields would reach maximum production in two to five years. George Otis Smith of the U.S. Geological Survey warned of a possible **"gasoline famine"** and said known American reserves would be exhausted in exactly nine years and three months. There was much discussion of shale oil in Colorado, Utah, and Nevada — *National Geographic* declared "no man who owns a motor-car will fail to rejoice" — but development costs were woefully underestimated. Britain was even cultivating Jerusalem artichokes in Dorset hoping to produce automobile fuel.

Crude prices jumped 50 percent between 1918 and 1920. Both the oil industry and the American government firmly believed Britain was pressing an aggressive policy to preempt the rest of the world's oil. Washington rallied to support American companies' quest for foreign supplies under the principle of the **"Open Door"** — equal access for American capital and business.

The news of the **San Remo Agreement** stunned Washington. Standard Oil of New Jersey protested to the State Department; the American press denounced it as old-fashioned imperialism violating the Open Door. Congress passed the **Mineral Leasing Act of 1920**, denying drilling rights on public lands to foreign interests whose governments denied similar access to Americans — aimed specifically at the Dutch in the East Indies and the British in Mesopotamia.

The Wilson Administration, once the embodiment of progressivism, now gave full support to the oil companies — particularly Jersey Standard, the most prominent heir to the dragon slain by the Supreme Court a decade earlier. The British ambassador marveled at the "rapprochement between the Wilson Administration and the Standard Oil interest" that "completely reversed the prewar relationships." The Harding Administration, which came in 1921, proved even stronger in defending American oil interests.

### *"The Boss": Walter Teagle*

Commerce Secretary **Herbert Hoover** suggested a syndicate of American companies for Mesopotamia. At a meeting in May 1921, Hoover and Secretary of State Hughes explained the U.S. could not swing the door open on behalf of one company alone. Jersey Standard's **Walter Teagle** put together a consortium of several leading companies — what would recently have been attacked as restraint of trade was now supported as a national champion for the Open Door.

**Teagle** was second-generation oil — on his mother's side, actually third: his maternal grandfather was **Maurice Clark**, the partner Rockefeller had bought out at the critical 1865 auction. Teagle's father was a successful independent Cleveland refiner who had resisted Standard Oil for years, was one of Ida Tarbell's heroes, and eventually sold out to Standard. Young Teagle went to Cornell, wrote a thesis on de-sulfurization of crude scoring a perfect one hundred, and was told by his professor to study for an advanced degree. His father responded with a terse telegram: "Come home at once." At nineteen cents an hour, Teagle fired a still in the family refinery, then proved himself a formidable salesman on the road — "He haggled over everything. He'd trade and trade. If it was company money, he'd think he was paying too much for a five cent cigar and try to get it for four."

By 1908, he was head of Standard Oil's Foreign Export Committee. He promoted conciliation with Deterding — once settling a Far East competitive dispute by spending two days shooting grouse in Scotland with Deterding (both excellent wing shots), two days playing poker, then working out the matter. Yet their mutual respect could not overcome fundamental suspicion. Deterding, Teagle once said, "frequently changes his mind and usually forgets to tell you that such is the case."

In **1917**, at thirty-nine, Teagle became president of Standard Oil of New Jersey. He was a new style leader — a professional manager, not a substantial stockholder. He installed Rockefeller's old rolltop desk — number 44 — in his office and set about reinvigorating the moribund company. He created an in-house magazine, *The Lamp*, instituted an "open door" for the press, and was apparently candid and forthright with reporters (though carefully controlled).

Teagle's big problem was crude supply — Standard Oil's production was only 16 percent of its refinery output. At the company's fiftieth anniversary in 1920, he declared: "The present policy of the Standard Oil Company is to be interested in every producing area no matter in what country it is situated." Wherever oil might be found, Standard Oil of New Jersey intended to be there.

### *The Crowded Table — Negotiations for Iraq*

In summer 1922, Teagle went to London to begin negotiations on American participation in the Turkish Petroleum Company. The discussions were fruitless. Arrayed against him were Deterding, Greenway, Mercier of CFP, and hovering nearby, Gulbenkian. The participants argued over shares, whether to maintain the self-denying clause, and the acrimonious matter of revenues. Teagle and Greenway wanted oil sold to stockholders at cost (avoiding Iraqi taxes); this did not please Iraq, which wanted a direct share of earnings, nor Gulbenkian, who was "most interested in receiving his dividends in money — not oil."

The new Turkey was challenging the border with Iraq and trying to undermine the TPC's legal basis. The British government, using its League of Nations mandate, put pressure on Iraq to grant a new concession.

### *Faisal of Iraq*

During the war, London had encouraged **Hussein**, Sharif of Mecca, to raise an Arab revolt against Turkey, aided by **T. E. Lawrence** ("Lawrence of Arabia"). Hussein's sons were to become rulers of the new Arab states. **Faisal**, the third son, was considered the most able — Lawrence described him as "an absolute ripper." Faisal cut a romantic figure at the Versailles Conference; Secretary of State Lansing wrote that Faisal's "voice seemed to breathe the perfume of frankincense."

The British put Faisal on the throne of Syria. But when France took control under the postwar understandings, Faisal was abruptly deposed and turned out of Damascus. He showed up at a railway station in Palestine, sat on his luggage, and waited for his connection. But his career as a king was not over. The British needed a monarch for Iraq — a new state formed from three former Ottoman provinces. Political stability was needed for oil, for defense of the Persian Gulf, and for the imperial air route to India, Singapore, and Australia.

Churchill, then head of the Colonial Office, chose the out-of-work Faisal as his candidate. Summoned from exile, he was crowned King of Iraq in Baghdad in August 1921. His brother Abdullah was installed as king "of the vacant lot which the British christened the Amirate of Transjordan." Faisal had not inherited a well-defined nation but rather a collection of diverse groups — Shia Arabs, Sunni Arabs, Jews, Kurds, and Yazidis. The minority Sunni Arabs held political power while the Shia were most numerous. The Jews were the largest single group in Baghdad.

Oil exploration could not begin without a new, sounder concession. Washington consistently refused to recognize the validity of the 1914 grant. **Allen Dulles**, chief of Near Eastern Affairs at the State Department, told Teagle the TPC's claim was "invalid." Iraqi cabinets — fearful of nationalistic sentiments and assassination — were reluctant to sign. But finally, on **March 14, 1925**, a new concession agreement was signed. Gulbenkian later noted the Open Door was mere "eyewash."

### *The Architect — Gulbenkian's Triumph*

Everything seemed settled except for one stumbling block — Gulbenkian and his 5 percent. Throughout the negotiations, he had remained strange and solitary, going to great lengths to avoid meetings but scrutinizing every word of memoranda. "Oil friendships are very slippery," he once said. His formerly close relationship with Deterding had ruptured in the mid-1920s — partly over a dispute about a Venezuelan oil company Gulbenkian had brought to Royal Dutch/Shell, partly over the affections of a White Russian lady, **Lydia Pavlova**, former wife of a Czarist general. When Deterding couldn't pay $300,000 for emeralds from Cartier's, Gulbenkian arranged a bridging credit. But Lydia became the second Mrs. Deterding, leading to bad blood between the two men. Deterding resented Gulbenkian's "persnickety interference"; Gulbenkian could not stand Deterding's "overbearing grandeur."

Gulbenkian insisted on his 5 percent of the TPC to be paid in cash, which the Americans opposed. His break with Deterding only strengthened his obstinacy. Teagle was driven to say Gulbenkian was "most difficult in a difficult situation." Gulbenkian was convinced "the oil groups headed by the American had only one aim, that is, by hook or by crook to wipe out his rights."

At a famous luncheon at the Carlton Hotel, Teagle tried an appealing line: "Surely, Mr. Gulbenkian, you're too good an oil merchant not to know that the property won't stand any such rate as that." Gulbenkian's face went red: "Young man! Don't you ever call me an oil merchant! I'm not an oil merchant and I'll have you distinctly understand that!" Teagle apologized.
