"I'll tell you how I classify myself," the Armenian replied hotly. **"I classify myself as a business architect. I design this company and that company. I designed this Turkish Petroleum Company and I made a room for Deterding and I made a room for the French and I made a room for you. Now, the three of you are trying to throw me out on my ass."**

### *Toward the Red Line — Baba Gurgur*

In 1925, a joint geological expedition from Anglo-Persian, Royal Dutch, and the American companies arrived in Iraq and reported with rising excitement. One American reported back that he knew of no other region where the promise of drilling was greater.

Drilling began in April 1927. At 3:00 a.m. on **October 15, 1927**, from a well at **Baba Gurgur** — about six miles northwest of Kirkuk, where for thousands of years two dozen holes had been venting natural gas (always alight, thought to be the "burning fiery furnace" into which Nebuchadnezzar had cast the Jews) — the drill bit had barely passed fifteen hundred feet when a great roar was heard reverberating across the desert. A powerful gusher reached fifty feet above the derrick, carrying rocks from the bottom. The countryside was drenched with oil, hollows filled with poisonous gas. Whole villages were threatened. Seven hundred tribesmen were recruited to build dikes. After eight and a half days, the well was brought under control. It had flowed at **ninety-five thousand barrels per day**.

The leading question had been answered — there *were* petroleum resources in Iraq, potentially bountiful. On **July 31, 1928**, nine months after the discovery and almost six years since Teagle had first sailed for London, the full contract was signed. Royal Dutch/Shell, Anglo-Persian, and the French each received **23.75 percent**, as did the **Near East Development Company** (the American consortium). Gulbenkian received his **5 percent interest in oil**, which he could immediately sell to the French at market prices, transmuting crude into his beloved cash.

The critical **self-denying clause** was reborn. Gulbenkian called for a large map of the Middle East, took a thick red pencil, and drew a line along the boundaries of the old Ottoman Empire. **"That was the old Ottoman Empire which I knew in 1914. And I ought to know. I was born in it, lived in it, and served in it."** This far-reaching oil settlement was thereafter called **"The Red Line Agreement."** Within the red line were eventually to be found all the major oil-producing fields of the Middle East, save those of Persia and Kuwait. The partners were bound not to engage in any oil operations within that vast territory except in cooperation with the other members of the Turkish Petroleum Company. It set the framework for future Middle Eastern oil development — and became the focus for decades of bitter conflict.

Teagle later looked back: "It was a damn bad move! Should have gone in by ourselves three years earlier." But for Gulbenkian, it was a great victory — the culmination of thirty-seven years of perseverance and tenacity. To mark the event, he chartered a boat for a Mediterranean cruise with his daughter Rita. Off the coast of Morocco, he caught sight of a strange ship with its funnel jutting up at the extreme stern. He asked what it was. An oil tanker, Rita told him. He was fifty-nine years old, had just made one of the greatest oil deals of the century, and **he had never before seen an oil tanker**.

## Chapter 11: From Shortage to Surplus — The Age of Gasoline

In 1919, U.S. Army Captain **Dwight D. Eisenhower**, depressed by the tedium of peacetime military life, volunteered for a cross-country motor caravan organized to demonstrate motor transportation's potential and the need for better highways. "A coast-to-coast convoy," he said, "was, under the circumstances of the time, a genuine adventure." He called it "Through Darkest America with Truck and Tank."

The journey started on July 7, 1919, from just south of the White House, with forty-two trucks, staff cars, ambulances, tank trucks, mobile field kitchens, and repair shops. The drivers seemed more familiar with teams of horses than the internal combustion engine. The first three days, they managed five and two-thirds miles an hour — "not quite so good as even the slowest troop train." Roads varied "from average to nonexistent." In some places, trucks broke through the road surface and had to be towed out one by one. Some days when they had counted on sixty or seventy miles, they would do three or four. They left Washington July 7 and did not arrive in San Francisco until September 6. "The old convoy had started me thinking about good, two lane highways." Three and a half decades later, as President, Eisenhower championed the interstate highway system. But in 1919, his mission "Through Darkest America" signified the dawn of a new era — **the motorization of the American people**.

### *"A Century of Travel"*

Deterding had written in 1916: "This is a century of travel, and the restlessness which has been created by the war will make the desire for travel still greater." By 1929, **23.1 million** autos were registered in the United States, up from 3.4 million in 1916. Each was being driven farther — from 4,500 miles per car in 1919 to 7,500 in 1929. Frederick Lewis Allen captured the transformation in *Only Yesterday*: villages on the railroad languished while villages on Route 61 "bloomed with garages, filling stations, hot-dog stands, chicken-dinner restaurants." The interurban trolley perished. Railroad after railroad gave up branch lines. "The age of steam was yielding to the gasoline age."

By 1929, **78 percent of the world's autos were in America** — five people per motor vehicle, compared to 30 in England, 33 in France, 102 in Germany, 702 in Japan, and 6,130 in the Soviet Union. Total U.S. oil demand rose from 1.03 million barrels a day in 1919 to 2.58 million in 1929. Oil's share of total energy consumption rose from 10 to 25 percent. Gasoline and fuel oil together accounted for 85 percent of total oil consumption in 1929. As for kerosene, it was negligible. **The "new light" had given way to the "new fuel."**

### *"The Magic of Gasoline"*

The transformation was accompanied by the emergence of the drive-in gasoline station. Before the 1920s, most gasoline was sold by storekeepers in cans under the counter, often adulterated with cheaper naphtha or kerosene. The first drive-in station is attributed to the Automobile Gasoline Company in St. Louis in 1907 — a small tin shack with a couple of barrels and two old hot-water tanks on high brackets with garden hoses to drain gasoline by gravity. By 1920, no more than 100,000 establishments sold gasoline (half were grocery and hardware stores). By 1929, the number had grown to **300,000**, almost all gas stations or garages. Drive-in stations specifically grew from about 12,000 in 1921 to **143,000** in 1929.

California, specifically Los Angeles, was the true incubator of the modern service station — standard structures with huge signs, restroom facilities, canopies, landscaped grounds, and paved entrances. Shell pioneered the standardized "cracker box" station. Stations made money not only from gasoline but from "TBA" — tires, batteries, and accessories. Standard of Indiana turned stations into grand emporiums selling everything from motor oil to furniture polishes to oil for sewing machines. A glass bowl atop the pump, where gasoline was forced up to be seen by the customer, reassured him of the product's purity.

Competition heated up, and the stations hoisted the signs and symbols of the new age: Texaco's star, Shell's scallop shell, Sun's radiant diamond, Union's "76," Phillips's "66" (suggested by Heinz's "57 Varieties"), Socony's flying horse, Gulf's orange disc, Standard of Indiana's red crown, Sinclair's brontosaurus, and Jersey Standard's patriotic red, white, and blue. Oil companies also made "uniquely American contributions to cartography" — the oil company road map. Gulf started the practice in 1914, handing out free maps at its first Pittsburgh station.

Shell of California required free uniforms for attendants, forbade magazine reading on duty, and insisted "Air and water service is a gratuity which you are expected to render the public, showing no distinction as to whether the individual is a Shell customer or not." By 1927, "service station salesmen" were expected to ask, "Can I check the tires for you?" and told: "Salesmen should be careful in their attendance upon Oriental and Latin classes of customers and refrain from using broken English in conversation with them."

**Bruce Barton**, the advertising man who had written *The Man Nobody Knows* (proving Jesus was "the greatest advertiser of his day"), called upon oil men in 1928 to reflect upon "the magic of gasoline": "My friends, it is the juice of the fountain of eternal youth that you are selling. It is health. It is comfort. It is success. And you have sold merely a bad smelling liquid at so many cents per gallon."

### *The Tempest in the Teapot*

Gasoline prices had become a political issue by the 1920s — whenever they rose, it was reported by press, discussed by governors, senators, and even presidents, and investigated by government branches. In 1923, Senator **Robert "Fighting Bob" La Follette** of Wisconsin conducted highly charged hearings on gasoline prices, warning that "if a few great oil companies" continued to manipulate prices, Americans would soon pay $1 a gallon. His warning lost punch as surplus mounted and prices plummeted — by April 1927, retail gasoline fell to **13 cents a gallon** in San Francisco and 10.5 cents in Los Angeles.

But La Follette was on target in another drama — he led the Senate crusade that uncovered one of America's most famous scandals: **Teapot Dome**. The field in Wyoming, named for the shape of a geological structure, was one of three oil fields (two others in California) set aside as "naval oil reserves" by the Taft and Wilson administrations. These reserves were meant to be "a supply laid up for some unexpected emergency" in time of war. A long battle had raged in Washington over whether private interests could lease them.

When **Warren G. Harding** won the presidency in 1920, he appointed Senator **Albert B. Fall** of New Mexico as Secretary of the Interior — a rough-and-ready rancher, lawyer, and miner described as "the frontiersman, the rough and ready, two-fisted fighter" who looked like "an old-time Texas sheriff." Fall's "belief in the unrestrained disposition of the public lands was as typically Western as his black, broad-brimmed Stetson hat and his love of fine horses." Conservationists called him a member of the "exploitation gang."

Fall wrested control of the naval oil reserves from the Navy Department and placed them in Interior. In spring 1922, just before leases were signed, **Walter Teagle** of Standard Oil visited advertising man **Albert Lasker** (who had directed Harding's campaign publicity) and warned: "the Interior Department is about to close a contract to lease Teapot Dome, and all through the industry it smells." Lasker reluctantly told the President, who paced behind his desk: "This isn't the first time that this rumor has come to me, but if Albert Fall isn't an honest man, I'm not fit to be President of the United States."

Fall leased Teapot Dome to **Harry Sinclair** and the more bountiful California reserve, Elk Hill, to **Edward Doheny**. Both were "new men" who had risen outside the Standard Oil inheritance. Doheny was a legendary prospector who once fought off a mountain lion with a knife; by the 1920s his company Pan American was a larger crude producer than any Standard Oil successor. Sinclair, son of a small-town Kansas druggist, had lost the family drugstore at age twenty, then built tiny oil companies one per lease in southeast Kansas and Osage Indian territory of Oklahoma. "Where he sat, there was the head of the table." By WWI he was the largest independent producer in the midcontinent; in 1916 he raised $50 million and assembled one of the ten largest integrated oil companies.

The Interior Department signed contracts with Doheny and Sinclair in April 1922 amid swirling rumors about Fall's friendships with "interests of an oleaginous nature." La Follette investigated and found naval officers who opposed the transfer had been reassigned to distant posts. In March 1923, Fall resigned, still respected but increasingly controversial.

The Harding Administration was sinking in scandal. Harding himself lamented: "I have no trouble with my enemies... It is my friends that are giving me my trouble." He died suddenly in San Francisco — "an embolism" said a doctor, but a newspaper editor called it "an illness that was part terror, part shame, and part utter confusion." **Calvin Coolidge** succeeded him.

The Senate's Public Lands Committee took up Teapot Dome. Fall had made expensive renovations to his New Mexico ranch and bought a neighboring ranch with hundred-dollar bills lifted from a small tin box. He claimed a $100,000 loan from **Ned McLean**, publisher of the *Washington Post*, who initially confirmed but then said Fall had returned the checks uncashed. On January 24, 1924, **Doheny** testified he had provided the $100,000, carried "in a little black bag" by his son to Fall's office — "just a loan to an old friend." He produced a mutilated promissory note with the signature ripped off (his wife held it "so as not to embarrass Fall").

By 1924, *The New Republic* said all of Washington was "wading shoulder-deep in oil... the sole subject of discussion is oil. Congress has abandoned all other business." Coolidge distanced himself through a "campaign of silence" and won the 1924 election handily. In 1928, it emerged that Sinclair had channeled several hundred thousand dollars more through a bogus company, Continental Trading Company — Fall had received at least **$409,000** total. In 1931, Fall became **the first Cabinet officer convicted and imprisoned for a felony committed while in office**. Sinclair got six and a half months for contempt. On his way to jail, Sinclair stopped at a Sinclair Consolidated board meeting where directors gave him "a public vote of confidence." Doheny was judged innocent, leading one Senator to complain, "You can't convict a million dollars in the United States."

### *The Colonel and the Liberty Bonds*

The scandal widened when investigators discovered the Continental Trading Company was really a kickback mechanism — prominent oil men received Liberty Bonds on oil purchases by their own companies. Sinclair had used part of his kickback to pay Fall and gave some bonds to the Republican National Committee. Among the recipients was **Colonel Robert Stewart**, chairman of Standard of Indiana — a broad-faced, bulky man who had ridden with Teddy Roosevelt's Rough Riders and risen through the company as a lawyer, not an oil man. Autocratic and commanding, he made Standard of Indiana the nation's number-one gasoline marketer. After years of evasion, Stewart admitted receiving about **$760,000** in bonds.

**John D. Rockefeller, Jr.** — the largest stockholder in Standard of Indiana — was appalled. Short, shy, serious, and reclusive, "Junior" had been schooled in "duty" and "responsibility." At Brown University he had hemmed his own dish towels. His vocation was philanthropy, not oil. He had even courted **Ida Tarbell**, his father's nemesis, delivering materials to her Gramercy Park apartment for review of his father's interviews. After Tarbell judged the elder Rockefeller's comments "self-serving," Junior abandoned the biography project.

Now Junior launched a proxy fight to oust Stewart. The colonel counterattacked: "If the Rockefellers want to fight, I'll show them how to fight." He had quadrupled the company's net assets, declared an extra dividend and stock split. Some saw it as East vs. West for control of oil. But the Rockefeller forces organized relentlessly, and in March 1929, with **60 percent** of stockholder votes, Stewart was out. Junior had intervened "not for mere profits but in the name of decency and high standards." A college professor wrote that "no endowment of a college nor support of a piece of research could have done more... to educate the public toward honest business." But the whole Teapot Dome scandal — from Fall, Doheny, and Sinclair to Stewart — had "picked up where the Standard Oil Trust had left off in ingraining in the public mind a nefarious image of the power and corruption of 'oil money.'"

### *Geophysics and Luck*

At the dawn of the automotive age, many worried supplies of the "new fuel" were running out. The years 1917-20 had been disappointing for discoveries. Leading geologists prophesied U.S. production limits were near. Some refineries ran at only 50 percent capacity for lack of crude. Walter Teagle remarked that "pessimism over crude supplies had become a chronic malady in the oil business." Oklahoma crude rose from $1.20/barrel in 1916 to **$3.36** by 1920; a record number of wells were drilled.

The technology for finding oil was about to transform. By 1920, "surface geology" — mapping visible landscapes — had gone as far as it could. The emerging science of **geophysics** provided new ways to "see" underground. Key innovations adapted from WWI military technology included:
- **Torsion balance** — measured gravity changes point to point, revealing subsurface structures. Developed by a Hungarian physicist, used by Germans in WWI to restore Rumanian fields.
- **Magnetometer** — measured vertical components of Earth's magnetic field.
- **Seismograph** — most powerful of all. Originally developed mid-19th century for earthquakes, Germans used it in WWI to locate enemy artillery. Refraction seismology was introduced to U.S. oil ~1923-24 by a German company: dynamite charges sent energy waves through underground structures, picked up by "geophones" on the surface, identifying salt domes where oil might be found. The reflection seismograph soon supplanted it, recording waves bouncing off rock interfaces to plot underground structures at all depths.
- **Aerial surveillance** — adapted from wartime troop spotting. Union Oil hired two former Army lieutenants in 1919 to photograph California terrain.
- **Micropaleontology** — analysis of microscopic fossils from drilling depths provided clues to sediment ages.
- Drilling itself improved: deepest wells went from 6,000 feet in 1918 to **10,000 feet** by 1930.

And always, luck. One of the most dramatic discoveries was at **Signal Hill**, rising 365 feet behind Long Beach, south of Los Angeles. Once used by local Indians to signal brethren on Catalina Island, later coveted by real estate developers. In June 1921, during subdivision into residential lots, Shell's **Alamitos Number 1** blew in, creating a stampede. Parcels were so small that wooden derricks' legs interlaced. Property owners got 50 percent royalties. Even the next-of-kin of persons buried in Sunnyside Cemetery received royalty checks for oil drawn from beneath grave plots. Signal Hill made California the **number-one producing state in 1923**, source of one quarter of the world's oil output. Yet that same year, the Federal Trade Commission still warned crude was "being rapidly depleted." In fact, American crude production exceeded domestic demand for the first time in a decade.

## Chapter 12: The Fight for New Production

### *The Tycoon*

**Harry Doherty** was an anomaly in oil — with oversized glasses and Van Dyke beard, he looked like "the professor" rather than a businessman. But he was among the great entrepreneurs of the 1920s, controlling a host of companies including **Cities Service**, earning the title "the nearest approach on Wall Street to Ned the Newsboy of the Horatio Alger stories." He began working at age nine selling newspapers in Columbus, Ohio, dropped out of school at twelve ("I had not been in school more than ten days before I grew to hate school worse than Satan"), rose through night school and engineering training to become director of 150 companies — gas and electric utilities composing "Cities Service." When one company struck oil in Kansas drilling for gas, Doherty became an oil man too. Eccentric and prolific with epigrams: "Never give orders — give instructions... Make a game out of your work... The greatest dividend in human life is happiness." His favorite relaxation was driving through New York City traffic.

Doherty was an intellectual gadfly of oil, insistent on one theme: the **"rule of capture"** had to be eliminated. This doctrine, sanctioned by English common law regarding migratory wild beasts, meant every operator drilled as fast as possible, draining oil from under neighbors' property. The courts offered "scant solace": "Only go and do likewise." The result was chaotic flush production and wildly fluctuating prices with every discovery.

Doherty argued the multiplication of wells and rapid production from the rule of capture exhausted underground pressure prematurely. Oil that might otherwise be produced was left underground, unrecoverable — gas and water pressure provided the "lift" to bring oil to the surface. His solution: fields should be **"unitized"** — tapped as single units with output apportioned among owners, producing at controlled rates to maintain underground pressure and maximize ultimate recovery. "Conservation" meant measured production for the largest recoverable resource, not reduced consumption.

The radical part: Doherty insisted the **Federal government** would have to lead, or at least sanction industry cooperation, with public enforcement of superior production practices. Most of the industry was hostile. Larger companies would discuss voluntary cooperation but no more. Independents wanted nothing to do with unitization — "They wanted their chance to get rich too." Doherty was a "diabolical needier" — barred three times from the API board and three times from presenting at API meetings, he hired his own hall. Others called him "that crazy man" and "an oil man is a barbarian with a suit on."

But Doherty had a friend in **President Coolidge**. In August 1924, he wrote: "If the public some day in the near future awakens to the fact that we have become a bankrupt nation as far as oil is concerned... they will blame both the men of the oil industry and the men who held public offices." Coolidge established the **Federal Oil Conservation Board** after winning the 1924 election, declaring: "The supremacy of nations may be determined by the possession of available petroleum and its products." The board stimulated research showing natural gas was not just "a commodity of smaller commercial value" but provided the essential underground pressure — dissipating it through helter-skelter production left vast amounts of petroleum unrecoverable.

**William Farish**, president of Humble (Jersey's Texas affiliate), who had scorned Doherty in 1925, was thanking him by 1928 for "making the industry see the virtues of better production methods." Farish became a strong advocate of unitization. But Doherty grossly underestimated the possibilities for finding new oil. **J. Howard Pew** of Sun Oil, a bitter opponent of government involvement, sarcastically predicted that "the nitrates in the soil would disappear, timber reserves be depleted, and the rivers of the world change their course before petroleum reserves were exhausted."

### *The Rising Tide*

It was Pew, not Doherty, who proved the more accurate prophet. Spring 1926 saw the first major discoveries in what became the **Greater Seminole field** in Oklahoma — one of the most rapid developments the world had seen. A breakneck, wasteful drilling competition driven by the rule of capture, with traditional boomtown chaos — "streets clogged with equipment, workers, gamblers, hucksters, and drunks; hastily built wooden structures; the stifling odor of escaping gas and the acrid smell of burning oil." By July 30, 1927, just sixteen months after the first major finds, the single field produced **527,000 barrels per day**. Other major discoveries followed in Oklahoma. In Texas, the late 1920s established the **Permian Basin** — the vast, sun-scorched region of West Texas and New Mexico — as one of the world's great oil concentrations.

Technology also altered the demand side. **Cracking** techniques increased gasoline yield per barrel — one barrel of cracked oil produced as much gasoline as two barrels uncracked, and cracked gasoline had superior antiknock properties. So though gasoline demand grew, crude demand didn't grow at the same rate, adding to surplus.

By decade's end, the gloomy predictions of the early 1920s had been washed away by floods of oil. Flush production — "too many straws in a tub" — damaged reservoirs and reduced ultimate recoverable resources. The massive oversupply disrupted markets and caused sudden price collapses. Yet as glut mounted, opinion shifted toward Doherty's conservation and production control ideas — not to forestall shortage (proof of surplus was everywhere) but to prevent ruinous flush production that shook pricing structures.

The debate over who should control production — voluntarily or under government aegis, Federal or state — was fierce. Within Jersey Standard, **Teagle** favored voluntary control while **Farish** at Humble insisted: "The industry is powerless to help itself... We must have government help, permission to do things we cannot do today." Farish added: "I have come to the conclusion that there are more individual fools in the petroleum industry than in any other business." Independent producers opposed government regulation: "No state corporation commission will tell me how to run my business," thundered **Tom Slick** to cheering Oklahoma producers. The independents formed the **Independent Petroleum Association of America** and campaigned for a tariff on imported oil (mainly Venezuelan oil imported by majors), but failed to get it into the Smoot-Hawley Act of 1930.

### *Emerging Competition*

The oil industry had always confronted chronic supply-demand imbalances, responding with consolidation and integration. In the 1920s, the same strategies reemerged among Standard Oil successor companies and others, turning them into vigorous competitors. A new dimension appeared: oil companies were becoming **marketers**, selling automotive fuel directly to motorists at brand-name stations springing up across America. Oil wars were being fought not only for foreign supply and markets but also on America's main streets. In its drive toward consolidation and integration, the American oil industry began to take on its modern and familiar outline.

The 1911 dissolution had left **Standard Oil of New Jersey** a huge refining company with virtually no oil of its own, highly dependent on other companies. Walter Teagle sought secure crude sources both domestic and foreign. As early as 1919, Jersey bought just over half of **Humble Oil**, a leading Texas producer that badly needed capital. By 1921 Humble was the largest producer in Texas. **Standard of Indiana** moved aggressively to assure its own crude supply from the Southwest and Wyoming, and also purchased **Pan American Petroleum** (Doheny's company), one of the leading American companies in Mexico. Meanwhile, major crude producers went downstream to assure markets: **Ohio Oil Company** (later Marathon) had been the largest Standard Oil producing company before dissolution; now it moved into refining and marketing, eventually controlling half of the immensely prolific Yates field in Texas.

**Phillips Petroleum** was created by **Frank Phillips**, an ex-barber and ex-bond salesman with considerable flair for oil deals. Also a banker, he was adept at overcoming investor skepticism and raising money in New York, Chicago, and other cities. By the mid-1920s, Phillips and his brother had built the company into one of the major independents, in the same league as Gulf and the Texas Company. In November 1927, Phillips opened its first refinery in the Texas Panhandle and its first service station in Wichita, Kansas. To start things off, officials planned to offer coupons for ten free gallons of gas. "Sure, go ahead," Phillips replied. "It isn't worth as much as water anyway. Give 'em all you want to." By 1930, within three years of its first station, Phillips had built or acquired **6,750 retail outlets** in twelve states.

Competitive pressures pushed other companies to break through the wholesale wall into retail by acquiring gas stations and marketing facilities. Between 1926 and 1928, **Gulf** expanded retail rapidly into the North Central states. The Texas Company and Shell were marketing in all **forty-eight states** by decade's end. Established retailers had to expand into new areas to protect profitability as new competitors invaded their territories.

These invasions finished the work of the Supreme Court. A kind of shadow Standard Oil Trust had persisted for a decade after dissolution — successor companies remained tied by contracts, habits, personal relationships, old loyalties, and shared dominant stockholders. Each successor refining company had been based in a specific geographical region, and for a decade they more or less respected one another's borders. But in the 1920s, they began invading one another's territories. **Atlantic Refining** entered the markets of both Standard of New Jersey and Standard of New York — "as a matter of protection, rather than of desire." Jersey and other Eastern successors got into a bitter, highly publicized price war with Midwestern successors including Standard of Indiana. **Ida Tarbell** wrote with astonishment: "It certainly looks very much as if the Standard Oil Company might be crumbling — crumbling within."

By the mid-1920s, the concept of the "Standard Oil Group" exerting total control was obsolete. The successor companies were turning into large, fully integrated companies that, along with "independents" like the Texas Company and Gulf, dominated the industry. A 1927 FTC study found the separated Standard companies controlled **45 percent** of refined products output, down from 80 percent two decades earlier. The cozy relationship had dissipated: "There is no longer unity of control of these companies through community of interest," the FTC found. On price control, the FTC was skeptical that Standard companies could manipulate prices: "The price movements for the longer periods are substantially controlled by supply and demand conditions."

### *"Those Sunkist Sons of Bitches"*

The breakup of the Standard Oil Trust into aggressive competitors, combined with the appearance of new companies based on crude discoveries or gasoline refining and marketing, spurred a powerful wave of **mergers**. Rockefeller's impulse toward acquisition and consolidation lived on — not to exert total control (no longer possible) but to protect and improve competitive position. Standard of New York bought a major California producer and refiner, then merged with the Vacuum Oil Company to form **Socony-Vacuum** and develop the brand name **Mobil**. Standard of California acquired one of the other major California producers.

**Shell** grew rapidly through aggressive acquisition, but continued to involve American investors per Deterding's 1916 dictum: "It is, of course, always galling (apart from political considerations) in any country to see an enterprise doing well without local people being interested." Deterding was put off by American investment bankers: "Of all the grasping individuals I have ever met... the American bankers absolutely take the cake." In 1924, Shell came close to buying a production company called **Belridge** near Bakersfield, California for $8 million but decided it was too expensive. Fifty-five years later, in 1979, Shell finally bought Belridge — for **$3.6 billion**. Shell also acquired a quarter of **Union Oil of California**, but California stockholders invoked patriotism against "parties foreign to California and entirely unknown to us," embroiling the Senate and FTC, and eventually forced Shell to sell its holdings (though Shell made a 50 percent return on a two-year investment).

The Texas Company and Phillips came close to merging. So did Gulf and Standard of Indiana. Between 1929 and 1933, **Standard of New Jersey and Standard of California** devoted much managerial time to merger negotiations. To keep conversations secret, Walter Teagle traveled to one Lake Tahoe rendezvous in a private railroad car under an assumed name. Talks collapsed partly because of Standard of California's tough negotiating stance — its president **Kenneth Kingsbury** and associates were known to Jersey people as **"King Rex" and "those sunkist sons of bitches."** A more important reason for failure was Jersey's accounting system, which could not satisfactorily establish either book value or true profitability.

One thing united virtually the whole industry by decade's end: though scientific understanding of oil production had advanced, opposition to direct Federal regulation was overwhelming. Harry Doherty, outraged, predicted: "The oil industry is in for a long period of trouble... I do not know how long it will take, but I will stake the last shred of my reputation that the day will come when every oil man will wish we had sought Federal legislation." His own health broken from the strain, he wrote in 1929: "If a man has ever gotten a dirtier deal from an Industry than I have gotten from the Oil Industry, I would certainly like to meet him. I often wish to God I had never gone into the oil business."

No one paid attention to his prophecy. The new corporate giants were preoccupied with sorting out competitive positions, and the prospects for stabilization without government intervention looked reasonable. But then the stock market crashed in October 1929, heralding the Great Depression. And in autumn 1930, a throw of the dice led to the discovery of the largest oil field ever found in the forty-eight states — **the Black Giant** — one that could have met a very substantial part of the entire American demand. Harry Doherty would turn out to have been right on the mark.

## Chapter 12: "The Fight for New Production" (continued — foreign oil)

The equation — oil equals power — had already been proven on WWI battlefields, and from that conflict emerged a new era in relations between oil companies and nation-states. If oil was power, it was also a symbol of sovereignty. That inevitably meant a collision between oil companies' objectives and nation-states' interests — a clash that would become a lasting characteristic of international politics.

### *Mexico's Golden Lane*

In the early twentieth century, Western Hemisphere exploration outside the U.S. centered on **Mexico**. The two dominating companies were **Pan American Petroleum** (led by Edward Doheny) and **Mexican Eagle** (led by Englishman **Sir Weetman Pearson**, later Lord Cowdray). Doheny, already successful in California, had gone to Mexico in 1900 at the invitation of the head of Mexican State Railways, who needed fuel.

Pearson's interests were far more sweeping — he was one of the greatest nineteenth-century engineer contractors. Talented, innovative, and a daring entrepreneur, "born to engineering" with gifts in mathematics — "slow, steady, meticulous, and persevering." The "**Pearson touch**" — his knack for grand-scale success — was much admired. To his daughter: "Dame Fortune is very elusive; the only way is to sketch a fortune which you think you can realize and then go for it baldheaded." To his son: "Do not hesitate for one second to be in opposition to your colleagues or in overriding their decisions. *No business can be a permanent success unless its head is an autocrat.*" He built Blackwall Tunnel under the Thames, four tunnels under the East River in New York for the Pennsylvania Railroad, and Dover Harbor. His eventual empire included the *Financial Times*, the *Economist*, Penguin Books, and the investment bank Lazard's in London.

President **Porfirio Diaz**, Mexico's dictator, invited Pearson to undertake major projects — the Grand Canal draining Mexico City, Vera Cruz Harbor, and the Tehuantepec Railway connecting Atlantic and Pacific. Pearson cultivated Diaz with European objets d'art and £100,000 to found a hospital. "Poor Mexico," Diaz supposedly remarked, "so far from God and so close to the United States" — he invited a world-famous British engineer to balance American dominance.

In 1901, Pearson missed a rail connection in the Texas border town of Laredo, discovered the town was "wild with the oil craze" from Spindletop three months earlier. He examined every oil prospectus, then cabled his manager to "move sharply" to acquire Mexican oil lands — all accomplished during a nine-hour stopover. He hired **Captain Anthony Lucas** (of Spindletop fame) to assist in Mexico. After a full decade of large expenditures with little to show, Pearson was despondent: "I entered lightly on this enterprise, not realizing its many problems." To his wife: "I cannot help but think what a craven adventurer I am compared to men of old... I am slothful and horribly afraid of two things — first that my pride in my judgment and administration should be scattered to the winds and secondly that I should have to begin life again."

Finally in 1910, Pearson (now **Lord Cowdray**) made major strikes, beginning with the fabulous **Potrero del Llano 4**, which flowed at **110,000 barrels per day** — considered the biggest oil well in the world. Production centered along the "**Golden Lane**" near Tampico, where 70,000-100,000-barrel wells were not uncommon. By 1913, Mexican oil was even used on Russian railroads. During WWI, Mexico became critical for the U.S., meeting **20 percent** of domestic American demand by 1920. By 1921, Mexico was the **second-largest oil producer in the world** with annual output of **193 million barrels**.

But the political environment had changed dramatically. In 1911, the 81-year-old Diaz was overthrown ("distracted by a toothache that had gone septic"), inaugurating the **Mexican Revolution**. Violence reduced foreign investment appetite. E. J. Sadler, head of Jersey's Mexican operations, was captured by bandits, beaten savagely, and left for dead — thereafter he never carried more than $25 in cash and always wore a cheap gold watch. Oil camps belonging to Mexican Eagle were overrun by rebels and employees killed.

In October 1918, Cowdray was approached by **Calouste Gulbenkian** on behalf of Deterding — Royal Dutch/Shell wanted to buy a substantial part of Mexican Eagle. Cowdray, weary and wary of further risk, accepted. Shell's acquisition proved well-timed: almost immediately, salt water intruded into the big producing wells, beginning Mexico's decline.

The emerging conflict established a lasting battle line between governments and oil companies. In Mexico, the issue was sovereignty and ownership — to whom did the benefits of oil belong? Until 1884, subsoil resources had belonged to the crown/nation. Diaz altered this, giving subsoil ownership to surface landowners who welcomed foreign capital (which eventually controlled 90 percent of all oil properties). The revolution's **Article 27 of the 1917 Constitution** restored national ownership of subsoil resources, becoming the center of battle. Mexico had recaptured the oil but couldn't develop it without foreign capital. Various Mexican regimes imposed regulations and tax hikes, fueling continuous conflicts. Doheny and other American oil men whipped up sentiment in Washington for military intervention to protect "vital" American-owned reserves.

Relations between Mexico and the U.S. became continually turbulent — Washington withheld diplomatic recognition, and the countries seemed close to war more than once. When Washington looked south, it saw "instability, insecurity, banditry, anarchy, a dangerous threat to the flow of a strategic resource, and welching on contracts." When Mexico looked north, it saw "foreign exploitation, humiliation, the violation of sovereignty, and the enormous weight, pressure, and power of 'Yankee imperialism.'" Oil company investment declined, output plummeted, and Mexico soon ceased being a world oil power.

### *General Gomez's Venezuelan "Hacienda"*

The fear of shortage, oil's new role in national power, and the profits to be made fueled what Royal Dutch/Shell called "the fight for new production" in its 1920 annual report: "We must not be outstripped in this struggle to obtain new territory... our geologists are everywhere where any chance of success exists." **Venezuela** was at the top of the list. The political turmoil in Mexico was stimulating a wholesale migration of oil men to Venezuela, where early Spanish explorers had observed Indians using oil seepages to caulk canoes.

Venezuela offered a friendly political climate — the handiwork of **General Juan Vicente Gomez**, the cruel, cunning, and avaricious dictator who ruled for twenty-seven years for his personal enrichment. Venezuela was an underpopulated, impoverished agricultural nation. Of 184 legislature members in the mid-1890s, at least **112 managed to claim the rank of general**. Gomez seized power in 1908, turning the country into a personal hacienda. Barely literate, he fathered **97 illegitimate children** (by one count), installed his brother as vice-president (until the brother was murdered by Gomez's son), affected Teddy Roosevelt big game hunter garb, was pro-German during WWI (dressing up in imitation of the Kaiser), and was called "scoundrel" by Woodrow Wilson. The British minister described Gomez as "an 'Absolute Monarch' in the most medieval sense of the word."

Gomez shrewdly recognized that luring foreign investment required political predictability and fiscal stability — in sharp contrast to Mexico. His government even sought help from the American minister in Caracas and American companies in drafting the **Petroleum Law**, setting terms for concessions, taxes, and royalties. By 1913, Royal Dutch/Shell was already at work near **Lake Maracaibo**, with minor commercial production beginning in 1914.

In 1919, Jersey Standard sent scouts to Venezuela, including a geologist who recommended skipping the Maracaibo Basin altogether: "Anyone who stays there a few weeks is almost certain to become infected with malaria or liver and intestinal disorders." But a Jersey manager disagreed — what counted was Royal Dutch/Shell's commitment there: "The fact that they have spent millions there leads us to suspect that there is considerable oil in this country. Failure to develop production in Latin America could endanger Standard Oil's ability to remain predominant."

Getting concessions on Gomez's "hacienda" wasn't easy. Standard's representative arranged to see the general directly, but on the same day, a concession was bid on by **Julio Mendez** — Gomez's son-in-law — who won and immediately flipped it to another company. Eventually, Jersey acquired acreage including **4,200 acres under Lake Maracaibo** — considered a joke. A Jersey official suggested buying a boat so that "if the 4,200 underwater acres proved valueless as an oil concession, the company could go into the fishing business."

Exploration was difficult and hazardous — virtually no roads passable by auto, geologists traveled by canoe or mule, rivers on maps didn't exist or were tributaries of different systems. "Mosquitoes were the worst and largest of any place I ever saw." One Jersey driller was killed by an arrow from hostile Indians; Shell protected tractor cabins with special cloth dense enough to stop arrows.

In December 1922, despite skepticism, Shell's **Barroso well** in the **La Rosa field** (Maracaibo Basin) blew out with an uncontrolled flow estimated at **100,000 barrels per day**. The field had been selected and staked by **George Reynolds** — the same "solid British oak" who had guided Anglo-Persian to its first discoveries in Persia a decade and a half earlier, then been let go with only a minuscule bonus. Now his persistence had opened Venezuela.

The Barroso blowout announced Venezuela to the world. The scramble intensified — Jersey, Shell, Gulf, and others competed fiercely for concessions. Gomez played them against each other, extracting maximum personal benefit. His family members and cronies served as intermediaries, taking cuts on every deal. Standard Oil of Indiana also entered Venezuela, buying concessions from the **Creole Syndicate** — a group of Venezuelan insiders close to Gomez. Indiana Standard paid $33 million for its Venezuelan properties, one of the largest foreign investments of the era.

### *The Russian Question*

While the fight for new production raged in Venezuela and elsewhere, the question of Russian oil loomed large. The **Bolshevik Revolution** of 1917 had nationalized all oil properties, wiping out the investments of the Nobels, the Rothschilds, and Royal Dutch/Shell. The question was whether to deal with the Soviets or boycott them. Anglo-Soviet discussions proceeded with great difficulty. Lenin passed a secret message to **Leonid Krasin** in London: "That swine Lloyd George has no scruples or shame in the way he deceives; don't believe a word he says and gull him three times as much." Krasin proved singularly adept at stimulating British businessmen eager for trade.

The Soviet Union was headed toward economic disaster — industrial underproduction, inflation, severe capital shortage, and widespread famine. It desperately needed foreign capital. In November 1920, Moscow announced a new policy of offering concessions to foreign investors. In March 1921, Lenin went further with the **New Economic Policy (NEP)** — expanded domestic markets, return of private enterprise, broadened foreign trade concessions. "We cannot by our own strength restore our shattered economy without equipment and technical assistance from abroad," Lenin declared. His first two examples of concessions were oil — "a quarter of Baku, a quarter of Grozny." A Bolshevik newspaper called petroleum "liquid gold."

Lenin's rapprochement with the West met opposition from comrades including **Stalin**, who warned the businessmen would include "the best spies of the world bourgeoisie." Nevertheless, Krasin signed the **Anglo-Soviet Trade Agreement** in London, then proceeded to dangle oil concessions to companies while playing them off against each other.

Deterding claimed not to be disappointed about the Nobel deal. He believed Standard Oil's entrance into Russia brought insurance to all foreign investors. "We have already several good seats and a very great part of the food on the Russian table," he lectured Gulbenkian. "Dining is very much better in company with other people who have also got a very big interest in the dinner." But Deterding had no intention of accepting the Bolsheviks' sell-off of what he regarded as his properties. Nor did Walter Teagle.

### *In Search of a United Front*

In 1922, Jersey, Royal Dutch/Shell, and the Nobels formed the **Front Uni** — a common bloc against the Soviet threat. A dozen other companies joined, pledging to fight the Soviet Union together and not be picked off individually. They agreed to seek compensation for nationalized property and refrain from dealing with Russia independently. But "the brethren of oil merchants" hardly trusted one another, let alone the Soviets. The Front Uni stood on unsteady legs, and Krasin continued to play the companies off against each other "with a master's skill."

The Soviet oil industry, virtually dormant 1920-1923, revived quickly with Western technology imports, and the USSR reentered the world market as an exporter. Jersey executives faced a dilemma — buy cheaper Russian oil or stand back on moral/business grounds? Teagle regretted the Nobel investment: "I am convinced that instead of sitting up with a sick child of this character and nursing it along for years, we could have taken the same amount of money and invested it elsewhere in the oil business in such a way that the investment would have immediately become productive."

**Heinrich Riedemann**, head of Standard's German operations, saw it differently — private companies could not easily defend their rights against confiscation and nationalization. "The participation of a government in industrial and business enterprise as in Russia is new and unheard of in the history of business." Other Western groups were already seeking concessions across the Soviet Union — from Baku to the Caucasus to Sakhalin off Siberia. The Soviets were selling oil from those properties as if they were their own.

Teagle reluctantly agreed to form a joint Jersey-Shell buying organization in November 1924 to explore options with the Soviets. "I know I am old fashioned in feeling this way," he said, "but somehow or other the idea of trying to be on friendly terms with the man who burglarizes your house or steals your property has never appealed to me." He wrote to Riedemann lamenting the hasty handling of the matter. Cooperation with Royal Dutch/Shell was one thing, but cooperation with the Soviets remained repugnant. "Affording the Soviet a market for petroleum not only is actually becoming a receiver of stolen goods, but operates to encourage the thief to persist in his evil courses by making theft readily profitable."

A Jersey-Shell agreement for joint Soviet purchase seemed imminent — even providing that 5% of the purchase price would compensate former owners. But both Teagle and Deterding remained personally skeptical. When the agreement fell apart in early 1927, Deterding was "almost gleeful": "I am so glad that nothing came of these Soviet deals... I feel that everybody will regret at some time that he had anything to do with these robbers, whose only aim is the destruction of all civilization and the re-establishment of brute force."

Deterding's anti-communism had intensified after his marriage to **Lydia Pavlova**, a White Russian emigre. He even telegraphed John D. Rockefeller Jr., beseeching him to block Standard companies from buying Russian oil, calling the Soviet regime "anti-Christ" and begging "for humanity's sake" that "all decent people" abstain from helping the Soviets.

### *Price War*

Despite Deterding's entreaties, **Standard Oil of New York (Socony)** and **Vacuum** went their own way, dealing directly with the Soviets. Socony built a kerosene plant for the Russians at Batum and leased it back. Both companies contracted to buy large amounts of Russian kerosene, particularly for India and Asian markets. Socony needed Russian oil for India; Shell had other sources to substitute, but Socony did not.

Deterding went into "an awful rage," denouncing Socony's president **C. F. Meyer** as "a man who has neither honor nor intelligence." In 1927, in retaliation, Deterding launched a **brutal price war** in India that spread to other markets worldwide. Socony counterattacked, cutting prices elsewhere. Deterding orchestrated a press campaign against Standard of New York for buying "communist" oil. Jersey was dragged in too — accused of buying communist oil. Deterding expected Jersey to bring Socony "sharply to heel," saying "the minor New York Cy. should be made to understand that she is the servant of the Jersey Cy., and not her boss."

Jersey's executives suspected Deterding had withdrawn from the joint Soviet deal due to British government pressure, but a senior British official denied this: "Sir Henri Deterding always got himself into hot water because of his tactlessness." When Russians questioned the compensation arrangement, "Sir Henri completely lost his head and told them that he would prevent any one from buying Soviet oil.... This was an utterly stupid and at the same time thoroughly characteristic thing for him to do."

Vacuum's president countered accusations of betraying American principles by noting American farmers were busily selling cotton and other products to Russia: "Is it more unrighteous to buy from Russia than to sell to it?" By the late 1920s, the major companies were weary of the whole Russian matter. The gusher at **Baba Gurgur in Iraq** turned attention to new Middle East sources. Jersey adopted a neutral stance — neither seeking Soviet contracts nor boycotting. Riedemann summed up in autumn 1927: "Personally, I have buried Russia." But Soviet oil proved "a lively corpse," as growing volumes entered the world market, and Deterding's price war had far larger consequences for all players in international oil.

## Chapter 13: The Flood

### *Dad Joiner and the East Texas Discovery*

**Columbus "Dad" Joiner** was seventy years old in 1930, walked bent from rheumatic fever, and was the caricature of a down-on-his-luck wildcat promoter — always optimistic, silver-tongued, ever-persuasive. He had a silky-smooth complexion (attributed to eating carrots), only seven weeks of formal schooling, had been tutored at home with the Bible as his text, and learned to write by copying the Book of Genesis. He absorbed the King James language and could spin an enticing web of promised wealth. He also wrote love letters to widows whose names he found in newspaper obituaries — "his interest, to be sure, was not in their lonely hearts; it was in their purses."

Joiner was one of many in the big oil promotion frenzy of the 1920s. Oil stocks and deals were madly enticing in the speculative climate. One promoter, **Dr. Frederick Cook** (who claimed to have beaten Admiral Perry to the North Pole), mailed up to 300,000 letters a month, pulling in some $2 million in one year before being apprehended. The **General Lee Development Company** found a Robert A. Lee, descendant of the Confederate general, and had him tell investors "I would rather lead you and a thousand others to financial independence than to have won Fredericksburg or Chancellorsville."

Dad Joiner was a small-time operator who actually wanted to wildcat for oil, not just fleece the gullible. He worked out of Dallas, hanging around the Adolphus Hotel lobby. His eyes eventually fell on **East Texas** — drought-ridden, dirt-poor, piney woods, sandy soil, still mired in agricultural depression. Neither Overton nor Henderson had one paved road. Joiner told the locals that under their barren soil lay "an ocean of oil, a treasure trove all the kings of earth might covet."

Geologists scoffed — there was no oil in East Texas. But Joiner was convinced by **"Doc Lloyd"** — a mysterious, 300-pound, self-proclaimed geologist who wore sombreros and riding boots, was variously said to be a veterinarian, pharmacist, and patent medicine showman. Lloyd's geology description was totally fabricated — he was a "trendologist" who drew trend lines from all major U.S. oil fields intersecting in East Texas. But he did one memorable thing: he told Joiner exactly where to drill.

Joiner mailed prospectuses with Doc Lloyd's fictitious geology to his "sucker list" and scraped together money to drill on **Daisy Bradford's** farm in Rusk County. He paid workers in "royalty rights" and "certificates" against possibilities of discovery — sold at deep discounts, they became local currency. A Texaco geologist visited and uttered the famous taunt: "I'll drink every barrel of oil you get out of that hole." For three years (1927-1930), Joiner and his motley crew drilled with rusted, third-hand equipment amid constant breakdowns.

In early September 1930, **Daisy Bradford Number 3** tested positively. A shantytown called **"Joinerville"** sprang up overnight on the road to the well. Thousands gathered for the vigil — it felt like a religious event. Hamburgers cost a quarter (vs. sixteen cents elsewhere). On **October 3, 1930**, at 8 PM, a gurgling was heard, the driller shouted "Put out the fires! Put out your cigarettes! *Quick!*" The earth trembled, oil and water shot high above the derrick. The crowd went mad. A crewman pulled a pistol and fired into the oil spray — three men tackled him and wrestled the gun away, as one spark could have caused the well to explode.

### *The Black Giant*

"Joiner's Wildcat a Gusher" headlined the *Henderson Daily News*. Industry leaders initially responded with skepticism. But over the next three months, two other wildcat wells blew in. The **East Texas reservoir** proved to be **forty-five miles long, five to ten miles wide, 140,000 acres** — the **Black Giant**, the largest oil field ever discovered in America. The boom dwarfed everything before it — Spindletop, Cushing, Greater Seminole, Oklahoma City, Signal Hill.

In early 1931, while the Great Depression gripped the nation, East Texas was exuberant and going crazy. People poured in, crowding into tent cities and shanty towns. This fundamentalist region of stern values suddenly became home to honky-tonk districts catering to every vice. By end of April 1931, the area produced **340,000 barrels per day**, with a new well spudded every hour.

Prices collapsed catastrophically. From **$1.85/barrel in Texas in 1926**, to about a dollar in 1930, to as low as **fifteen cents** by end of May 1931 — some oil sold for **six cents**, even **two cents** a barrel. By early June 1931, one thousand wells had been completed and East Texas was producing **500,000 barrels per day**. Cheap, pint-sized "teakettle" refineries sprang up producing "Eastex gasoline" sold at discount stations offering premiums — a crate of tomatoes or a free chicken dinner with each fill-up.

### *Dad Joiner's Deal with H. L. Hunt*

Dad Joiner had been "very cavalier" in his promotion — selling more "interests" than actually existed, some leases sold multiple times (one at least eleven times). Of his 5,000 acres of leases, he had clear title to just two. A local newspaper editor cast him as "the second Moses to be led to the Promised Land, permitted to gaze upon its 'milk and honey,' and then denied the privilege of entering."

**Haroldson Lafayette "H. L." Hunt** — stout, wearing a straw boater and string tie, called "Boy" by Joiner — was a failed cotton farmer with two formidable talents: gambling and complex mental arithmetic (like Rockefeller and Deterding). He had opened a gambling hall in El Dorado, Arkansas, switched to oil when the Ku Klux Klan threatened to burn it down, and was already maintaining **two wives, each with a growing family**.

Hunt befriended Joiner before the well blew in. After the discovery, Hunt holed up with Joiner in **Room 1553 at the Baker Hotel** for an interminable negotiation. Crucially, Hunt was being fed secret reports on the nearby **Deep Rock well** — showing a second major discovery. After 36+ nonstop hours, between midnight and 2 AM on **Thanksgiving, November 27, 1930**, Joiner signed all rights to "Boy." Hunt ordered cheese and crackers to celebrate.

Hunt paid Joiner **$1.33 million** ($30,000 up front, rest from production). When Joiner later learned Hunt had paid $20,000 to the Deep Rock driller for secret advance information, he sued for fraud but then withdrew. Hunt became the largest independent in East Texas and went on to amass an immense fortune. He later achieved notoriety as a right-wing patron, health food promoter, and enemy of white flour and sugar. Dad Joiner spent the money wildcatting and romancing his "secretary" — died at 87, still wildcatting, net worth barely more than his car and house.

### *Anarchy in the Oil Field*

The East Texas flood brought prices down nationwide, threatening ruin for even the largest producers. The rush of competitive production meant "competitive suicide" for the entire industry. Controlling the East Texas field was complicated by fragmented ownership — independents owned much of it and resisted any surrender of freedom that might reduce their advantage over the hated big companies.

The **Texas Railroad Commission** — originally created in 1891 by Governor Jim Hogg for railroad regulation — had been given some mandate over oil. However, it was specifically forbidden from "economic waste" regulation (market proration). It could only prevent "physical waste." The commission argued that flush production would cause stripper wells to shut down (physical waste), but federal courts repeatedly blocked its proration efforts. The commission was even held in contempt of court.

**Frederick Godber**, a Shell director from London, visited the U.S. in spring 1931 and found "unmitigated gloom." The chairman of Standard of Indiana was "very depressed, almost panicky." Teagle at Jersey was "very pessimistic, feels there is nothing to do but sit back and rely on lowering prices." Godber reported: "much of the industry's troubles are due to known causes over which individuals have little or no control and which cannot be remedied until laws are passed in the various producing states."

In Oklahoma, Governor **"Alfalfa Bill" Murray** proclaimed a state of emergency, declared martial law, and ordered the state militia to take control of major oil fields, keeping them closed until "price hits one dollar." **"A dollar a barrel"** became the rallying cry throughout the oil states.

By August 1931, both East Texas and the petroleum market were in total anarchy. Production exceeded **one million barrels per day** — almost half of total American demand. Crude prices had plunged to **thirteen cents a barrel**. Texas oil was even underselling Russian petroleum in Europe. Production costs averaged around eighty cents. Violence was in the air — talk of dynamiting wells and pipelines. The Texas economy was on the verge of collapse.

**Texas Governor Ross Sterling** — a founder and former chairman of Humble Oil — declared war on East Texas. On **August 17, 1931**, he announced a "state of insurrection" and "open rebellion," sending in several thousand **National Guardsmen** and **Texas Rangers** (arriving on horseback because rains made roads impassable). They set up on **"Proration Hill"** and shut down production within days. An eerie quiet settled over East Texas. Even the chickens, which had feasted on insects drawn by gas flares, were forced to "return to the prosaic ante-petroleum practice of scratching for worms."

The National Guard commander banned "beach pajamas" — a garb favored by the busy prostitutes — and ancillary businesses went into a sharp slump. The shutdown actually worked: prices rose from thirteen cents. The Railroad Commission continued issuing proration orders enforced by state troopers. By **April 1932**, prices were nearly back to the magic dollar — **ninety-eight cents**. In 1932, the commission issued **nineteen separate proration orders**, each declared invalid by the judiciary. But the market held firm, and stronger prices finally persuaded many independents of the value of prorationing.

In November, Governor Sterling gave the commission the specific power to counter "economic waste" — calling a special legislative session and ramming through a bill allowing **market prorationing**. The new law was facilitated by understanding that the East Texas reservoir was driven by **"water drive"** (not gas pressure as elsewhere) — rapid, chaotic production would damage the water drive and prematurely stunt overall output.

### *Hot Oil and the Crisis of 1933*

Despite the Railroad Commission's new powers, spring 1933 looked as bad as or worse than summer 1931. The commission had set the East Texas quota far too high — twice what "bottom hole" pressure engineering suggested. Hundreds of thousands of barrels were being illegally produced above quotas. This excess became known as **"hot oil"** — a term coined in East Texas. Origin story: a state militiaman shivering on a chilly night was told by a suspected operator to lean against a tank of the suspect oil. "It's hot enough," said the operator, "to keep you warm."

Hot oil was being smuggled out of Texas and across state borders. The Texas Company slashed its posted price from seventy-five cents to **ten cents a barrel**. So vast was the flood that "hot oil runners" had trouble finding markets at even **two cents a barrel**. Pipelines were mysteriously dynamited. **William Farish**, president of Humble, wrote to Teagle that only the shock and pain of very low prices could convince independents their long-term interests lay in production control and unitization. "The law of the tooth and claw" was the only recourse. Some Texas producers urgently petitioned for Federal supervision.

### *The Reformer: Harold Ickes*

**Franklin Roosevelt's New Deal** brought a new activist administration willing to intervene. Roosevelt appointed **Harold L. Ickes** as Secretary of the Interior — a post still tainted by memories of Albert B. Fall and the Teapot Dome scandal. Ickes was described as "a plump, blond, bespectacled gentleman" — a Chicago lawyer who had been a leading figure in progressive Republican politics. He had managed Theodore Roosevelt's Chicago campaign in 1912 and chaired the Western Committee of the National Progressive League for FDR. He was a man of "powerful liberal convictions, strong passions, stinging polemics, pervasive suspicions, oversensitivity to any slight (real or imagined), immense self-righteousness, great dedication to duty, and a deeply ingrained moral conscience."

Ickes had been raised in a poor household by a stern Calvinist mother — not even permitted to whistle on Sunday. He was so good in high school that when the Latin teacher fell ill, he took over. As class president, he first practiced what became an art: the impulsive resignation on grounds of high principle, only to have it rejected. Decades later, Roosevelt would respond to one of his several resignations: "You are needed.... Resignation *not* accepted!"

As a young attorney with "restless reforming energy," Ickes joined campaigns against corruption, monopoly, and social injustice in Chicago — civil rights, women's trade unions, the ten-hour day. He joked about his "uncanny ability to pick losers!" In addition to Interior, Ickes assumed the role of **Oil Administrator** and held the key New Deal position of **Public Works Administrator**.

Ickes threw himself into intricate administrative details, haunted by Teapot Dome. "With the spotty record of Interior always in mind," he wrote, "I slaved away over endless mountains of documents, contracts, and letters, refusing to sign anything that I had not personally read." After Harry Sinclair (one of Fall's paymasters) visited in 1933, Ickes wrote in his diary: "I kept wondering whether the ghost of Albert B. Fall, carrying a little black satchel, might not emerge from one of the gloomy corners of this office." He set up an internal investigating unit using wiretaps as standard operating technique.

Ickes learned quickly how "thorny" the petroleum situation was. On **May 1, 1933**, he wrote Roosevelt about "utter demoralization" and "impending collapse" of the oil industry. On **May 5**, entering a Cabinet meeting, he received a telegram: East Texas prices had fallen to **four cents a barrel**. The Governor of Texas wired that "the situation is beyond the control of the state authorities." Ickes warned: "the oil business has broken down and... to continue to do nothing will result in the utter collapse of the industry."

### *The Government Acts*

Ickes started with prices — too low, like other raw materials during the Depression. Ten cents a barrel would prolong the Depression. For prices to rise, production had to be controlled. Ickes launched an all-out campaign against the "hot oiler" with "sly animal cunning." **Hot oil** production in 1933 was estimated at up to **half a million barrels per day**. This bootleg oil was secretly siphoned from pipelines, hidden in camouflaged tanks covered with weeds, moved through secret pipeline networks and by trucks, smuggled across state borders at night — every step smoothed by graft and payoffs. Any firming of prices only incentivized more hot oil production.

The Federal government's power to regulate interstate commerce provided the answer. Legislation in 1933 gave the President explicit power to ban **"hot oil" — petroleum produced in excess of state-mandated levels — from interstate commerce**. Roosevelt was appalled by "the wretched conditions" in the oil industry. On **July 14, 1933**, he signed an executive order. Ickes wrote in his diary: "stop the carrying into interstate or foreign commerce of any petroleum... produced in violation of the law of the state of their origin." Ickes dispatched Federal investigators to East Texas to examine refinery records, test oil gauges, inspect tanks, even dig up pipelines. Federal **"certificates of clearance"** were required to move any oil out of East Texas.

The **Oil Code** under the National Industrial Recovery Act gave Ickes extraordinary power to set **monthly quotas for each state**. On **September 2, 1933**, aiming to reduce production by 300,000 barrels per day, Ickes sent telegrams to governors telling them their state quotas. It was a historic act — the days of flush production were over. With prorationing, **the rule of capture was overturned**. "What had made sense for deer and game birds on estates in medieval England would no longer do when the very structure of the American oil industry seemed likely to be washed away."

Some in the industry wanted the government to go further and fix prices directly. "If you do not give us price regulations," said a Standard of California representative, "you can make codes from now to doomsday and you will get nowhere." But others feared government price-setting would make oil a public utility with regulated profits. Regulation of production — not prices — was chosen as the preferred method, with the actual job kept at the state level where it was less controversial and closer to actual production.

### *The System Stabilizes*

By end of 1934, the Federal-state partnership was gaining headway. But in **January 1935**, the Supreme Court struck down the NIRA subsection banning hot oil — a potential death blow. Without hot oil controls, the whole system would collapse. New legislation was speedily drafted and passed: the **Connally Hot Oil Act** (named for Senator Tom Connally of Texas). Then in **June 1935**, the Supreme Court declared much of the NIRA unconstitutional (the "sick chickens" case — a poultry jobber in New York). The voiding of the NIRA stripped Ickes of his power to set mandatory state quotas.

Yet the system survived. By this time, a framework for regulation was in place and a consensus established. The **Connally Hot Oil Act** provided police powers against contraband oil. The **Bureau of Mines** prepared demand estimates and "assigned" each state a suggested voluntary "quota." States were not required to accept these quotas — but they essentially did, adopting Federal estimates and prorating to fill their share of projected demand. The memory of ten cents a barrel was powerful motivation.

A state that greatly exceeded its quota would risk reprisal from the Federal government and other states, plus the peril of another glut and price collapse. The **Interstate Oil Compact** was established in 1935, formalizing cooperation among oil-producing states — a forum for exchanging information, standardizing legislation, and coordinating prorationing and conservation. Oklahoma wanted it to be an enforceable cartel; Texas refused to surrender sovereignty. Texas won, and the Compact became much less than a cartel but still useful.

The system also required an **oil tariff** to check cheap foreign imports. In 1932, a duty of **twenty-one cents a barrel** on crude and fuel oil and **$1.05 on gasoline** was passed. This tariff provided the protective barrier behind which the prorationing system could work. The duties were supported by a voluntary agreement on import volumes between Ickes and the main importing companies. Imports fell from **9-12% of domestic demand** in the late 1920s and early 1930s to **only 5%** after the tariff. Venezuela was hardest hit — it had supplied over half of U.S. crude imports, with 55% of production going to the U.S. Ships filled with expatriate oil men sailed home. Venezuelan companies reoriented exports toward Europe.

### *Stability*

The regulatory system emerged incrementally, in nasty and disordered circumstances — bitter debates, rancorous and desperate. It took East Texas and ten cents a barrel to shock the industry. It was devised by an unlikely alliance of Texas and Oklahoma oil men, patronage politicians in Austin and Oklahoma City, and Ickes and New Deal liberals in Washington. Though innately suspicious, they worked together to bring stability to an industry prone to boom and bust.

The system possessed a powerful underlying logic: it rewrote the book on production and "ownership" of oil reserves. Two working assumptions were central: (1) demand for oil was not particularly responsive to price movements — oil at ten cents would not mean far greater demand than at a dollar; (2) each state had its "natural" share of the market. When discoveries in **Illinois** in the late 1930s made it the nation's fourth-largest producer (and it didn't belong to the Interstate Oil Compact), Texas and Oklahoma cut back to make way. There were recriminations and calls to scrap the system, but it withstood even this challenge.

Prices were not fixed by the government — its advocates insisted on this point. But setting production to match demand did establish a level of crude that could be marketed at a stable price. From **1934 through 1940**, the average price of oil in the U.S. varied between **$1.00 and $1.18 per barrel**. The magical "dollar a barrel" rallying cry had been realized. The flood had been stayed, and the relationship between oil companies and the government had been forever changed.

## Chapter 14: "Friends" — and Enemies

### *Achnacarry Castle and the "As Is" Agreement*

**Malcolm and Hillcart**, a real estate agency in Fort William on the west coast of Scotland, had prepared "particulars" for a property called **Achna-carry Castle** — a dozen miles away in Inverness-shire, with fifty thousand acres of shooting, fishing for 90 stags, 160 brace of grouse, and 2,000 fish. In August 1928, **Henri Deterding** rented the property for the month. Joining him were **Walter Teagle** (Jersey), **Heinrich Riedemann** (Jersey's German chief), **Sir John Cadman** (Anglo-Persian), **William Mellon** (Gulf), and **Colonel Robert Stewart** (Standard of Indiana). Their holiday entourage included secretaries, typists, and advisers housed in a secured cottage seven miles away.

Despite great efforts at secrecy, the London press galloped northward, only to be told the oil men had gathered merely for grouse shooting and fishing. "The grouse were to have no warning," the *Daily Express* hypothesized. Deterding's two teenage nieces — "hellions" in Teagle's phrase — poured molasses into Riedemann's bed and tied his pajamas in knots. As for the shooting, "it was lousy," Teagle later said. But it did not matter — they were not after grouse. They were searching for a solution to overproduction and overcapacity. More than a truce to the oil wars, they wanted **a formal treaty for Europe and Asia** — one that would bring order, divide markets, stabilize the industry, and defend profitability. Achnacarry was a **peace conference**.

This was a year before the 1929 crash and two years before East Texas. But oil was already surging from the U.S., Venezuela, Romania, and the Soviet Union, weakening prices and threatening "ruinous competition." The flow of Russian oil, and the vicious price war Deterding launched in retaliation, had carried the oil men to Achnacarry. The battle had spread to many markets worldwide — bitter global warfare with collapsing prices.

Achnacarry reflected the temper of the times: industrial rationalization, efficiency, elimination of duplication were values celebrated by businessmen and government officials alike. Mergers, cartels, marketing agreements, and associations were the pattern. As in Rockefeller and Flagler's day, "unbridled competition" was the danger. But total monopoly was no longer possible — no one firm was powerful enough, and political realities would not allow it. A **concordat, rather than conquest**, was the objective.

### *The Hand of the British Government*

The meeting was not only the oil companies' work. Behind the scenes, the **British government** was prodding and pushing the companies toward collaboration. At the juncture stood **Sir John Cadman**, successor to Greenway as chairman of Anglo-Persian. By 1928, Cadman was operating on the same plane as Deterding and Teagle, but with unparalleled credibility with the British government. He grew up in a mining family, began as a coal mine manager (winning awards for saving miners during disasters), became professor of mining at Birmingham University, where he introduced "petroleum engineering" — so novel that an academic opponent denounced it as "flagrantly advertised" and "a blind alley" with "a freak title." During WWI, as head of the Petroleum Executive, Cadman demonstrated considerable political and managerial skills. In 1921 he became Anglo-Persian's technical adviser; six years later, the government's candidate, he became chairman.

Anglo-Persian's output from Persia and Iraq was poised to increase **fourfold**. "It was essential that new markets should be found," Cadman said. The choice: fight into new markets with large investment and unavoidable competition, or set up joint ventures with established companies and divide markets. Cadman chose the second path — pooling markets and facilities in India with Shell and Burmah, then proposing an "alliance" with Royal Dutch/Shell in **Africa**, splitting markets fifty-fifty.

For this African venture, Anglo-Persian needed permission from its majority stockholder — the British government. The Admiralty feared Anglo-Persian might be absorbed by Shell, violating basic government policy. The Foreign Office and Treasury worried about alienating the United States — that such a combination might be seen as " 'war' on American interests represented by the Standard Oil Company," extending blame to the British government through its Anglo-Persian ownership. **Winston Churchill**, now Chancellor of the Exchequer, initially doubted the combination: "The moment when Sir Henri Deterding is at 'war' with the Standard seems a singularly inopportune one for the British government to be drawn into the quarrel." But upon reflection, Churchill concluded combination was the best and cheapest policy.

The alternative, Churchill told the Committee on Imperial Defense, was for Anglo-Persian to fight for the African market alone — requiring much more money and possibly requiring him to approach Parliament again for funds (as in 1914). The government gave firm support to Cadman's African alliance with Shell. A joint Treasury-Admiralty memorandum in February 1928 declared: "Such a policy will in the long run be more in the interest of the consumer than cutthroat competition." The memorandum went further — it might promote "similar alliances elsewhere — especially with Standard Oil of New Jersey."

This was the most important outcome: the government had given Anglo-Persian a **mandate to talk with Standard Oil** and seek similar market arrangements with the Americans "to allay their jealousies and show that we are not out to quarrel." Unhindered by American antitrust tradition, the British government was partial to combination. "Our experience has broadly been that the amalgamation of oil interests has not resulted in the consumer suffering."
