Cadman, now representing government policy as well as Anglo-Persian, pursued a concordat with the Americans. He wrote to Teagle proposing "a small 'clearinghouse' for matters of the very highest policy." These developments were among the major influences leading to Deterding's invitation to Achnacarry.

### *"The Problem of the Oil Industry"*

The two weeks of discussion on the banks of the River Arkaig resulted in a seventeen-page document, agreed to but not signed, called the **"Pool Association"** — better known as the **Achnacarry or "As-Is" Agreement**. The document summarized "the problem of the oil industry" — overproduction, "the effect of which has been destructive rather than constructive competition, resulting in much higher operating costs.... Recognizing this, economies must be effected, waste must be eliminated, the expensive duplication of facilities curtailed."

The heart of the "As-Is" understanding: **each company was allocated a quota — a percentage share of total sales in various markets, based upon its 1928 share**. A company could only increase actual volumes as total demand grew, but always keeping the same percentage share. Beyond this, companies would share facilities, be cautious about building new refineries, and increase efficiency by supplying markets from the nearest geographical source. The sales price would be based on the traditional formula — **American Gulf Coast price plus freight** — even if the oil came from a closer location. This established a uniform selling price and eliminated price competition among adherents.

A few months later, the leaders agreed to control production as well. Excess production above market quotas would be sold to other pool members. An **"Association"** with a representative from each company would handle statistical analysis and allocate quotas.

The **Soviet Union** was notably absent. By 1928, **Russian Oil Products** was the fourth-largest importer into the UK. In **February 1929**, despite Deterding's and Teagle's distaste, the major companies reached an understanding giving the Soviet Union a guaranteed share of the British market. With Russia roped in, there was only one major exception to this amicable division: the **domestic U.S. market was explicitly excluded** to avoid violating American antitrust laws.

The Achnacarry Agreement harked back to Rockefeller and Archbold, Deterding and Samuel, the Nobels and the Rothschilds — all had sought a grand concord and failed. This time was no more successful. While the "As-Is" companies were by far dominant, **"fringe" players** who did not belong could price just below the majors and win market share. When forced out of one market, they moved to another.

It was critical to control **American oil exports** (about a third of all oil consumed outside the U.S.). Seventeen American companies formed the **Export Petroleum Association** under the **Webb-Pomerene Act of 1918** (which allowed U.S. companies to do abroad what antitrust banned at home). But negotiations with the "European Group" fell apart over allocation, and the association never controlled more than **45% of American exports** — far too few companies to agree on prices and quotas. This failure further undermined Achnacarry.

**J. B. Kessler**, a Royal Dutch/Shell director, warned Teagle: "the figures we had before us showed that, of the potential world production, a large part is controlled by companies which are not controlled either by you or us or any of the few other large oil companies." Discoveries and output were building toward the great crescendo of East Texas. In the surge of uncontrollable production, the Achnacarry Agreement was washed away.

### *Discord Within "Private Walls"*

The Big Three — Jersey, Shell, and Anglo-Persian — tried to reformulate an alliance in 1930, less grandiose this time. They revised the "As-Is" into a new **Memorandum for European Markets**, with operating companies making "local arrangements" and dividing shares with "Outsiders." Yet again, the system proved ineffective against rising American, Russian, and Romanian oil. The Soviets never hesitated to cut prices to earn more foreign currency for Russia's industrialization drive.

By 1931, Jersey had grown disenchanted. **E. J. Sadler**, Jersey's head of production, advocated junking cooperation and making war on Shell: "Now is the best time to fight the Royal Dutch, as they are most vulnerable in the Far East.... In this region we have never made any profit, and a price war would cost us almost nothing." In March 1932, **M. Weill**, overseeing the Rothschilds' large interest in Royal Dutch/Shell, reported to Baron Rothschild: "Prices are bad everywhere, and except for a few rare places, money is not being made."

In November 1932, **Sir John Cadman** addressed the American Petroleum Institute, extolling "cooperation" — "strictly, of course, within the laws of every country." He publicly declared that "the principle of 'As-Is' has become the keystone of cooperation in international petroleum trading outside the United States." He warned: "It is still raining outside," and the companies could not give up seeking shelter from the storm.

The companies came up with a new version: the **Heads of Agreement for Distribution (December 1932)**, a guide for drawing up local cartels and agreements. Adherents included Royal Dutch/Shell, Jersey, Anglo-Persian, Socony, Gulf, Atlantic, Texas, and Sinclair. Two "As-Is" committees (New York and London) managed the arrangement, with a central secretariat in London. Internal "As-Is" departments were established at some companies. But friction persisted — chronic cheating and disputes over **"virginal markets"** (markets where participants had not previously traded but now sought to enter).

The **1934 Draft Memorandum of Principles** called for "economy in competitive expenditure" — reducing road signs, billboards, newspaper advertising, "premiums to racing drivers," promotional gifts (cigarette lighters, pens, calendars). Even the number and type of gas station signs were to be standardized.

Within each company, management referred to other companies as **"friends"**: "Friends in London say..." or "Friends not yet made up mind." But it was not friendship — it was desperation in a depressed world economy. They were aggressive competitors who never forgot it. Collaboration was paralleled by pervasive distrust and deep-seated rivalry. Just months after Achnacarry, Shell entered the U.S. East Coast market and expanded rapidly. Jersey was infuriated. In 1936, when Deterding learned Jersey was discussing selling its entire Mexican operation to **William Davis** (a "fringe" player), he wrote angrily: "We are engaged together in resistance to Davis' activities... and surely it is hopelessly inconsistent with sound warfare to sell the enemy a complete set of much needed munitions."

A Standard-Vacuum executive objected to outside auditors reviewing trade numbers: "Not only is the thought of having outside auditors go over our books objectionable for obvious reasons, but it seems to us that the As-Is would be on a very weak foundation if the parties concerned cannot trust each other." In December 1934, Shell director Godber in the Far East reported: "Figures trade clearly show Texas Co. unnecessarily aggressive and will end year with larger share of trade than that to which they are entitled." He wanted "sterner measures" against Texas Company.

The "As-Is" agreements became much more effective from 1934 onward due to three factors: (1) in the U.S., Ickes finally brought production under control; (2) in the Soviet Union, quickening industrialization consumed domestic oil, constricting exports; (3) large companies gained some control over Romanian output. But in **early 1938**, Jersey gave verbal notice of termination. Any surviving "As-Is" activities came to an end in **September 1939** with the outbreak of World War II.

### *Nationalism*

The "As-Is" agreements were meant to defend against both the oil glut and the Depression, and against **powerful political forces** in Europe and elsewhere. "Throughout the European continent, government policies confronted those of private foreign oil companies — and the scope of the confrontation was unprecedented." During the 1930s, governments imposed import quotas, set prices, placed restrictions on foreign exchange, forced companies to blend alcohol from surplus crops into motor fuel, levied new taxes, and controlled export/import trade to match bilateral agreements. They blocked profit remittances, forced investment in domestic facilities, and insisted companies maintain extra inventories.

It became standard practice for European governments to compel foreign companies to participate in **national cartels** and divide markets between foreign and local companies. In France, under 1928 legislation, the government allocated specific market shares to each company. A Jersey executive warned: "failure to cooperate with a national commercial endeavor — whatever the sacrifice in dollars or in principles — invariably provoked retaliatory legislation which was more costly." In **Nazi Germany**, regulations and manipulations of all kinds mounted as that government geared up for war.

**Orville Harden**, Jersey vice-president, summed up in 1935: "We are now confronted with nationalistic policies in almost all foreign countries, as well as decidedly socialistic tendencies in many.... These problems are between the Government, on the one hand, and industry as a unit, on the other." An observer of the oil industry that same year noted: "Operations in the oil business in Europe are **90 percent political and 10 percent oil**."

### *The Shah's New Terms*

At the very bottom of the Depression, **Shah Reza Pahlavi** of Persia was infuriated to discover that "oil is not gold in these days." Petroleum royalties from Anglo-Persian provided **two-thirds** of Persia's export earnings and a substantial part of government revenues. But with the Depression, royalties had plummeted to the lowest level since 1917. On **November 16, 1932**, the Shah abruptly announced at a Cabinet meeting that he was **unilaterally canceling Anglo-Persian's concession**. It was a thunderbolt that threatened the company's very existence.

The cancellation culminated four years of negotiation. In 1928, Cadman had observed "that concessionaries can regard their future as safeguarded against the rising tide of economic nationalism in proportion to the extent in which the national interests and their own approach identity." In 1929, Cadman thought he had worked out a deal with the Shah's Minister of Court, **Abdul Husayn Timurtash** — higher payments, 25% of the company's shares, board representation, and a share of global profits. But the deal could never be closed. The Shah's greatest fault, said Timurtash, was "his suspicion of everybody and everyone. There was really nobody in the whole country whom His Majesty trusted."

**Reza Shah** — formerly Reza Khan — was tough, domineering, brutal, and blunt. He had used his command of the Cossack Brigade to become War Minister in 1921, then Prime Minister in 1923, then crowned himself Reza Shah Pahlavi in 1925, founding the new Pahlavi dynasty. He told visitors the Persian people were "bigoted and ignorant." He was bent on consolidating power and reducing all competing centers — including the mullahs (religious clerics). He personally beat an ayatollah who questioned his family's dress at a shrine. "It has often been said that the Shah's greatest achievement is his victory over the Mullahs."

In the Shah's opinion, Anglo-Persian was like the mullahs — an independent power center to be reduced. The local press, responding to the Shah, attacked everything from D'Arcy's original concession to the refrigeration of food at the Abadan refinery (considered irreligious). The Shah was also angry with Britain over Bahrain (which Persia claimed) and the British recognition of Iraq. Anglo-Persian's claim to operate independently of the British government was never believed: Persians "could only imagine that duplicity was being compounded with complicity."

The cancellation was a direct challenge to the British government, whose military security had been tied to Persian oil since Churchill in 1914. The issue was sent to the **League of Nations**, which put it into abeyance. Five months later, in **April 1933**, Cadman flew to Tehran himself. He noted: "There is no doubt that H.M. [His Majesty] is after money." Negotiations deadlocked. Cadman, frustrated, returned to the Shah's palace and to emphasize that a breakdown was imminent and his patience at an end, **instructed his pilot to carry out a trial flight and taxi the plane visibly from the windows of the Shah's palace** during the meeting.

The Shah retreated. By end of April 1933, a new agreement was forged: the concession area was **reduced by three-quarters**; Persia was guaranteed a fixed royalty of **four shillings per ton** (protecting against price fluctuations); it would receive **20% of worldwide profits** distributed to shareholders above a minimum; a minimum annual payment of **£750,000** was guaranteed; royalties for 1931-32 were recalculated; and the "Persianization" of the workforce was to be accelerated. The concession was extended from **1961 to 1993**. "I felt that we had been pretty well plucked," Cadman noted afterward. But Anglo-Persian's essential position was preserved.

### *The Mexican Battle*

Of all the nationalistic challenges, the greatest came in **Mexico** — the focal point being **paragraph 4 of Article 27** of the 1917 Constitution, which declared subsoil resources belonged to the Mexican state, not the surface property owner. The companies fought hard against Article 27, invoking U.S. and British government support, maintaining that property rights acquired before the revolution could not be retroactively seized. Mexico insisted it had owned the subsoil all along. The result was a standoff — an agreement to disagree.

The modus vivendi was hardly easy — punctuated by sharp acrimony. In 1927, tensions rose so high that a rupture seemed imminent, with possible U.S. military intervention. President **Plutarco Elias Calles** ordered **General Lazaro Cardenas** (then military commander in the oil zone) to prepare to **set the oil fields on fire** in the event of a U.S. invasion. From 1927 onward, greater stability emerged, but by the mid-1930s, the detente was unraveling. Mexico was losing competitiveness — higher production costs, increasing taxation, field exhaustion. Venezuelan oil was being landed at Tampico for refining because it was cheaper than Mexican oil. Mexican production had fallen from **499,000 barrels per day to 104,000** — an 80% drop in a decade.

**General Lazaro Cardenas** became President at the end of 1934. A man of "striking appearance" — "the long, mask-like face and inscrutable, obsidian eyes of the Indian" — son of an herbalist, educated only to age eleven but a voracious reader of history. By age eighteen he had been a tax collector, printer's devil, and jailkeeper before enlisting in the revolution. A general by twenty-five, protege of Calles, he was sober, puritanical, a supporter of prohibition and opponent of casinos. As president, he packed Calles into exile and demonstrated independence. He aggressively pushed land reform, education, and public works. Labor unions became powerful under his presidency.

Cardenas viewed the foreign oil industry as a painful presence — he resented what he saw as their arrogant treatment of Mexico as "conquered territory." Mexican Eagle was caught between local management trying to adjust to radicalism and Deterding in London, who "was incapable of conceiving of Mexico as anything but a Colonial Government to which you simply dictated orders." The local manager tried to "disillusion" Deterding but failed and was accused of being "half a Bolshevik."

**Everette DeGolyer**, the prominent American geologist, privately urged Jersey's **Eugene Holman** to "work out a partnership deal with the Mexican Government which would satisfy their national aspirations." Holman adamantly rejected: "The matter was so important as a precedent in other areas that the company would prefer to lose everything that it had in Mexico rather than acquiesce in a partnership which might be regarded as a partial expropriation."

In 1937, **Bolivia** confiscated Standard Oil's local subsidiary, winning great applause throughout Latin America. In Mexico, wages had supplanted taxes and concessions as the main point of contention. The oil workers' union struck in **May 1937**. Cardenas set up a review commission under **Professor Jesus Silva Herzog**, who described company officials as "men without respect who were unaccustomed to speaking the truth." The commission declared the companies had been "making lucrative profits while raping the Mexican economy" and recommended much higher wages totaling **26 million pesos** annually, plus a forty-hour week, six weeks' vacation, and retirement pensions at 85% of wages at age fifty.

The companies claimed their total combined profit 1935-37 was only **23 million pesos** — less than the demanded wage increase. They said compliance would force them to close down, gambling Mexico would not take that chance. The government confirmed the commission's recommendations and added retroactive penalties. Mexican Eagle evacuated wives and children.

On **March 8, 1938**, Cardenas met privately with oil company representatives — a further stalemate on wages. That same night, he made up his mind to expropriate if needed. On **March 16**, the companies were declared "in rebellion." They finally accepted the 26 million peso wage hike but refused to transfer management decision-making and administrative control to the unions.

On the night of **March 18, 1938**, Cardenas met with his Cabinet and told them he intended to take over the oil industry. It was better to destroy the oil fields, he said, than let them be an obstacle to national development. At **9:45 PM**, he signed the **expropriation order** and broadcast it from the Yellow Room. His words were greeted with a **six-hour parade** through Mexico City. For Mexico, it was a great symbolic act of resistance to foreign control. For the companies, it was absolutely illegitimate — a violation of clear agreements and formal commitments.

The companies joined in a united front to negotiate — not about compensation (in which they had no confidence) but to get their properties back. One Shell director warned: "a precedent is established throughout the world, particularly in Latin America, which would jeopardize the whole structure of international trade and the security of foreign investment." Companies organized embargoes against Mexican oil around the world. The company hardest hit was **Mexican Eagle** (Shell-controlled, largely British stockholders). The British government took a very strong stand against Mexico.

Britain insisted the properties be returned; Mexico instead severed diplomatic relations. A break with the United States was barely averted. Washington tried to exert economic pressure, but half-heartedly. In the era of Roosevelt's **Good Neighbor Policy** and criticism of "economic royalists," the American government could hardly act harshly against Mexico or oppose the sovereign right of expropriation so long as "fair compensation" was offered. With the rapidly deteriorating international situation, Roosevelt did not want to aggravate relations with any hemispheric country, as that could benefit the Axis powers.

The British-led embargo had unsettling effects: **Nazi Germany** became Mexico's number-one petroleum customer (at discount or barter terms), with Fascist Italy next and Japan a major customer as well. Japanese companies were exploring for oil in Mexico and discussing a pipeline to the Pacific. The Roosevelt Administration concluded that additional American pressure would only enable the Axis powers to strengthen their footholds. The British position was driven by strategic concerns: just eight countries accounted for 94% of world production, and U.S. neutrality legislation might foreclose American petroleum to Britain in a crisis. Latin American oil was essential — "defense requirements" and access to wartime oil were "the paramount consideration."

### *"As Dead as Julius Caesar"*

After war broke out in September 1939, national security trumped restitution for the Roosevelt Administration. Washington did not want Nazi submarines refueling in Mexican ports or German "geologists" wandering near the U.S. border and Panama Canal. U.S. Ambassador **Josephus Daniels** told Roosevelt in 1941 there was no sense defending a status "as dead as Julius Caesar."

By autumn 1941, Washington pushed for settlement — compensation, not restoration. Valuations ranged wildly: Mexico said **$7 million**, the companies claimed **$408 million**. A joint U.S.-Mexican commission found a creative solution: it judged that **90% of all subsoil reserves** had already been produced by the time of expropriation. Under this formulation, there was no point arguing about ownership of the reserves. The commission proposed **$30 million** in compensation, spread over several years. The companies reacted with bitter outrage, arguing they had sought foreign oil partly at the U.S. government's behest. **Secretary of State Cordell Hull** made clear the companies had no obligation to accept but should expect no further government support. In **October 1943**, the American companies took the deal.

There was a price for Mexico too. **Petroleos Mexicanos (Pemex)** was established as the national oil company. But the oil business was no longer export-oriented; it shifted to cheap domestic fuel for Mexico's own development. Mexican exports became a small factor. The industry was hamstrung by shortage of capital and lack of technology and skills. The promised 26-million-peso wage hike — the *casus belli* of the expropriation — was not only indefinitely postponed but in fact cut. Britain did not settle with Mexico until **1947** — Mexican Eagle/Shell won **$130 million**, proportionately much better than the Americans.

Both British and American companies agreed: the Mexican expropriation was the biggest trauma since the Bolshevik Revolution, perhaps since the 1911 Standard Oil dissolution. For Mexico, the 1938 nationalization was one of the greatest triumphs of the revolution. **Pemex** would emerge as one of the first and most important state-owned oil companies in the world. Mexico had established a model for the future.

## Chapter 15: The Arabian Concessions — The World That Frank Holmes Made

### *Major Frank Holmes*

Among the millions displaced by World War I was **Major Frank Holmes** — born on a New Zealand farm in 1874, he had worked in South African gold mines and then spent two decades as an itinerant mining engineer specializing in gold and tin, from Australia and Malaya to Mexico, Uruguay, Russia, and Nigeria. He was "robust and sturdy in stature, assertive and headstrong in manner." A competitor described him as "a man of considerable personal charm, with a bluff, breezy, blustering, buccaneering way about him."

During WWI, as a quartermaster in the British Army on a **beef-buying expedition to Addis Ababa, Ethiopia, in 1918**, Holmes first heard from an Arab trader about **oil seepages on the Arabian coast of the Persian Gulf**. Later, stationed in Basra, he took further interest in Anglo-Persian's activities across the border and reports of petroleum seepages along the Arabian coast.

After the war, Holmes helped set up the **Eastern and General Syndicate** to develop business opportunities in the Middle East. Its first venture was a drugstore in Aden. But Holmes's passion was oil — he was convinced the Arabian coast would be a fabulous source. He traveled up and down the Gulf, from one impoverished ruler to the next, spinning his vision, "promising them wealth where they saw only poverty, seeking always to put another concession into his kit."

British officials regarded Holmes with suspicion — an unscrupulous troublemaker with "a capacity for mischief," trying to undermine British influence. One official called him "nothing more than a rover in the world of oil." But to the Arabs, Holmes was something altogether different — **"Abu al-Naft," the "Father of Oil."**

Holmes set up headquarters on **Bahrain**, attracted by reports of oil seepages. The Sheikh had no interest in oil but desperately wanted fresh water. Holmes drilled for water, struck it, and made a nice profit. In exchange, the grateful ruler rewarded him with an **oil concession in 1925**. Holmes also won options to concessions in **al-Hasa** (eastern Saudi Arabia) in 1923, the **Neutral Zone** between Saudi Arabia and Kuwait in 1924, and tried for Kuwait proper.

Anglo-Persian was alarmed — it did not want anyone else operating within its "sphere of influence." The company was convinced there was no oil in Arabia. Cadman said geological reports "leave little room for optimism," and one director declared in 1926 that Saudi Arabia was "devoid of all prospects" for oil. (Albania, the director added, was the promising play.) A Swiss geologist retained by the syndicate produced a damning report declaring the region did "*not present any decided promise for drilling on oil*" and exploration "would have to be classified as pure gamble." This report leaked to the City of London, making it impossible to raise money.

By 1926, the syndicate was in deep financial trouble. Holmes tried to sell all concessions to Anglo-Persian, which said no. In the City of London, he got a frigid reception. "Holmes was the worst nuisance in London," one businessman recalled. "People ran when they saw him coming."

### *Bahrain and the New York Sheikhs*

With no luck in Britain, Holmes sailed for New York, hoping for better luck with "the really big New York Sheikhs." Jersey turned him down — Bahrain was "too far away and too small" (no larger than a pencil point on the map). Other companies were focused on the Turkish Petroleum Company. But **Gulf Oil** showed a flicker of interest — committed to diversified global production as a hedge. Holmes provided Gulf with rock samples and a "greasy substance" along with reports of oil traces in Bahrain water wells. In **November 1927**, Gulf took overall rights claimed by Eastern and General to Arabian concessions and agreed to work with Holmes's group to secure a Kuwait concession.

But a problem arose: in **1928**, Gulf joined the Turkish Petroleum Company and became bound by the **Red Line Agreement**, which precluded independent operations within the Red Line — clearly ruling out Saudi Arabia and Bahrain. The TPC board would not take up Holmes's entire Arabian package. So while Gulf could pursue Kuwait (outside the Red Line), it had to surrender its Bahrain interest.

Gulf brought the Bahrain concession to **Standard of California (Socal)**, which was aggressively committed to developing foreign oil but had nothing to show for very large expenditures. Unlike Gulf, Socal was not part of the TPC and not bound by the Red Line. Socal set up a Canadian subsidiary, the **Bahrain Petroleum Company**, to hold the concession.

Both Socal (in Bahrain) and Gulf (in Kuwait) then ran into **British government opposition** — the "British nationality clause" in Gulf concession agreements required that oil development be carried out by "British interests" only, excluding Americans. A nasty series of negotiations followed, backed by the U.S. government on one side and Britain on the other. The Americans saw it as a cunningly constructed barrier; the British felt harried and defensive before greater American power. In **1929**, Britain reconsidered — American capital would encourage rapid development, benefiting local rulers (who might otherwise need British subsidies) and the Royal Navy. Britain backed off on Bahrain, allowing Socal to exercise its option under conditions guaranteeing British political primacy (e.g., all company communications to the Amir had to pass through the British political agent).

The Bahrain Petroleum Company began drilling in **October 1931**. On **May 31, 1932**, it hit oil — petroleum had been discovered in the Arab world. Though only modest in production, the discovery had far wider implications. Over the course of a decade, Major Holmes with his oil obsession had been a figure of condescension and ridicule. Now his instincts had been vindicated. Was he to be proved right on a much grander scale? After all, Bahrain was only twenty miles from the Arabian mainland, where the geology was exactly the same.

### *Ibn Saud*

Britain's political agent in Kuwait spoke of the ruler of neighboring Saudi Arabia as "the astute Bin Saud, who always takes the 'long view.'" But **Ibn Saud** did not have the luxury of a long view — he needed money for his treasury urgently. He was skeptical about oil prospects and uneasy about what foreign oil development would mean — foreign capital and technicians could disturb traditional values. But granting a concession to *explore* was another matter, so long as it came with appropriate financial considerations.

**Abdul Aziz bin Abdul Rahman bin Faisal al Saud** was just over fifty years old. At six feet three inches with a barrel chest, he had an imposing physical presence. He combined "his qualities as a soldier that grasp of statecraft which is yet more highly prized by the tribesmen." His later amassing of immense wealth was remarkable for a ruler who, in his early days, could carry his entire national treasury in the saddlebags of a camel.

The **Saudi dynasty** had been established by **Muhammad bin Saud**, emir of Dariya in the Nejd, in the early 1700s. He allied with **Muhammad bin Abdul Wahab**, who espoused a stern puritanical version of Islam — the **Wahabi** branch that would become the religious mortar of the dynasty and state. The Saudis' rapid expansion alarmed the Ottoman Turks, who defeated them in 1818. Muhammad's great-grandson Abdullah was beheaded in Constantinople. The family eventually reestablished itself in Riyadh, but fell apart due to power struggles. By 1891, Abdul Rahman (Ibn Saud's father) fled into exile — the future Ibn Saud made part of the journey in a bag hung from a camel saddle. The family eventually settled in Kuwait under the protection of the Sabah family.

**Mubarak**, the Amir of Kuwait, gave the young prince an education in *realpolitik*. When the Turks incited the Rashids (the Saudis' enemies) to attack Kuwait, Mubarak dispatched Ibn Saud, then just twenty years old, to retake Riyadh. He led a small band across the desert, was repulsed, then on a second attempt entered the city by night and at dawn slew the Rashids' governor. In **January 1902**, his father proclaimed him Governor of Nejd and Imam of the Wahabis — the second al-Saud restoration had begun.

Over the next years, Ibn Saud made himself the acknowledged ruler of central Arabia. He became leader of the **Ikhwan (Brotherhood)** — intensely religious warriors. During 1913-14, he brought eastern Arabia including the Shia-populated **al-Hasa** under his control. By December 1925, the Ikhwan had captured the **Hejaz** (the holy land of Islam with Mecca and Medina). In **January 1926**, at the Great Mosque of Mecca, Ibn Saud was proclaimed King of the Hejaz, keeper of the holy places. By age forty-five, he had reestablished Saudi ascendancy over nine-tenths of the Arabian Peninsula.

But then the Ikhwan began criticizing Ibn Saud for backsliding from Wahabism — the telephone, telegraph, radio, and motorcar were "tools of the devil." In 1927 they rose in rebellion. By 1930 he had destroyed the Ikhwan movement. In **1932**, the realm was renamed **Saudi Arabia**.

Just as Ibn Saud had consolidated power, the **Great Depression** hit. The flow of **pilgrims to Mecca** — the kingdom's major revenue source — slowed to a trickle. Finances fell into desperate straits; civil servants were six to eight months in arrears. Ibn Saud tried to collect taxes a year in advance, sent his son **Faisal** to Europe to seek aid (unsuccessfully). As his financial problems grew, the King did not know where to turn.

### *The Sorcerer's Apprentice: Jack Philby*

Perhaps valuable resources were hidden under the soil. This idea was suggested to Ibn Saud during an automobile ride, probably autumn 1930, by his companion — an Englishman, former Indian Civil Service official turned Jidda merchant, who had recently converted to Islam. The King had given him the Islamic name Abdullah. His real name was **Harry St. John Bridger Philby** — known as Jack. He is now remembered best as the eccentric father of **Harold "Kim" Philby**, the notorious double agent who headed anti-Soviet counterespionage in British intelligence while spying for the Soviets. On reading Kim's memoir, Ibn Saud's retired court interpreter marveled that Kim was "a true replica of his father."

Jack Philby was a relentless contrarian, an inveterate rebel against authority. In Jidda he once paraded his pet baboons to demonstrate he could do without the European community. Raised in Ceylon, a Trinity College Cambridge graduate, he began in the Indian Civil Service and was posted to Baghdad and Basra during WWI, where he became a gifted Arabist. He first met Ibn Saud on a mission to Riyadh in 1917 — thirty-four hours of personal interviews that set the course of the rest of Philby's life.

In 1925, angered by British policy, Philby quit the civil service and returned to Saudi Arabia to establish a trading company in Jidda. He became an informal adviser to the King, joining his traveling party and evening Privy Council deliberations. On the eve of his conversion to Islam in 1930, the King told him "how nice it would be for me when I became a Muslim and could have four wives." The conversion enabled Philby to pursue his famous explorations — the Rub al-Khali (Empty Quarter), northwestern Arabia — for which he received the Founder's Medal of the Royal Geographical Society. He kept up five o'clock tea and cricket scores from Lord's even in the desert, yet remained at odds with Britain, which he saw as exercising "traditional western dominance."

The British found Philby "most troublesome" — "His methods have been as unscrupulous as they have been violent. He is a public nuisance." Another official denounced him as "an arch-humbug." But Philby knew very well Ibn Saud's severe financial problems. During the automobile ride in autumn 1930, noting the King was particularly despondent, Philby said cheerfully that the King and his government were "like folk asleep on buried treasure." Development required prospecting, foreign expertise, and foreign capital. "Oh, Philby," the King replied, "if anyone would offer me a million pounds, I would give him all the concessions he wanted."

Philby suggested **Charles Crane** — an American plumbing tycoon and philanthropist with a special interest in the Arab world. Crane arrived on **February 25, 1931**, was received with great pomp, mesmerizing sword dances, and gifts of rugs, daggers, swords, and two pedigreed Arabian horses. At his own expense, Crane made available an American mining engineer named **Karl Twitchell** to investigate the kingdom's water potential. After a 1,500-mile journey, Twitchell arrived in Jidda in April 1931 with bad news: no prospects of artesian wells.

In **March 1932**, the Amir of Kuwait, **Sheikh Ahmad**, visited Ibn Saud in Riyadh — traveling 300 miles by motorcar. He was struck by the King's ill health and strain. Sheikh Ahmad urged Ibn Saud to reduce his cars by three-quarters and "standardize by concentrating on Fords and Chevrolets." Having delivered this advice, he drove back across the desert in a gift from Ibn Saud — a large eight-cylinder Cadillac limousine.

The two rulers discussed oil exploration. Ibn Saud was "not anxious in the least to grant concessions to foreigners," but given his financial difficulties, did he have a choice? Twitchell had found some promising oil prospects in **al-Hasa**. Then, on **May 31, 1932**, Socal's discovery on Bahrain significantly increased the attractiveness of al-Hasa. Twitchell agreed at the King's behest to try to locate interest and capital in the United States.

### *The Negotiation*

Socal was delighted and retained Twitchell as a negotiator. He returned to Saudi Arabia in **February 1933** with **Lloyd Hamilton**, a Socal lawyer, to negotiate with Ibn Saud's minister of finance, **Abdullah Suleiman** — "a cunning and masterly opponent." The brother of the King's private secretary, a Nadji by birth (most senior administrators were Syrians, Egyptians, or Libyans), Suleiman had been an assistant to an Arab merchant in Bombay. The King called him "my support." Ibn Saud's interpreter considered him "the ultimate eminence grise" with "power and influence so monumental that I often thought of him as the uncrowned King of Arabia."

Suleiman knew exactly what he wanted — a very large sum of money, as soon as possible. Whether oil was there could be left to later. The **Iraq Petroleum Company** (IPC) dispatched **Stephen Longrigg** as its representative — also in effect representing Anglo-Persian. But IPC's efforts were more "prophylactic than prospective" — they wanted to keep out competitors, not make a large commitment. Longrigg's brief was "certainly not to buy a pig in a poke by paying big money at this stage for the right of extracting problematic oil."

Philby was glorying in his multiple roles — paid agent for Socal, adviser to the Saudis, coaching IPC through his contact with Longrigg (who regarded him as a confidant), and casually dropping in conversation what the King had said. Socal paid Philby **$1,000 a month for six months** plus bonuses on signing and on discovery of oil — money he used to pay his son Kim's Cambridge University fees, where Kim was taking "the first steps on the way to becoming a Soviet spy."

Socal was offering only about a fifth of what the Saudis wanted. IPC/Anglo-Persian continued to be skeptical — they didn't need more oil and were unwilling to pay much. Finally Philby advised Longrigg: "You might just as well pack up; the Americans are far and away higher than that." Longrigg departed abruptly, leaving the field to Socal. Philby coaxed Socal and Suleiman toward what he called "this detente" — a much higher Socal offer.

By May 1933, the final draft was ready. Ibn Saud told Suleiman: "Put your trust in God, and sign." The agreement provided for **£35,000 ($175,000)** in gold up front (£30,000 as a loan, £5,000 as the first year's royalty in advance). After eighteen months, a second loan of **£20,000 ($100,000)**. An additional loan of **£100,000 ($500,000)** in gold on discovery of oil. The concession was good for **sixty years** and covered about **360,000 square miles**. On **May 29, 1933**, the agreement was signed.

Getting the gold proved tricky — America had just gone off the gold standard. Socal's effort to dispatch gold directly was turned down by Assistant Secretary of the Treasury **Dean Acheson**. The Guaranty Trust's London office obtained **35,000 gold sovereigns** from the Royal Mint, transported in seven boxes on a P&O ship to Saudi Arabia. Care was taken that all coins bore the likeness of a **male English monarch, not Queen Victoria** — feared to be devalued in Saudi Arabia's male-dominated society.

When Philby told **Sir Andrew Ryan**, the British minister, that Socal had won the concession, Ryan was "thunderstruck, and his face darkened with anger and disappointment" — which delighted Philby no end. Washington was slow to realize the significance; the Roosevelt Administration refused to establish diplomatic representation, repeating there was no need. Not until **1939** was the U.S. minister to Egypt also accredited to Saudi Arabia, and only in **1942** did the U.S. establish a permanent one-man legation.

Anglo-Persian and the IPC realized they had blundered — "too timid and too miserly." In **1936**, the IPC group obtained a concession for the **Hejaz** (western Saudi Arabia, extending to Yemen) on much higher terms. The only drawback: the IPC never found oil in its concession.

### *Kuwait*

**Sheikh Ahmad** of Kuwait had been courting concessionaires for a decade. The Bahrain exploration "was a stab to my heart," he told Holmes in 1931, "when I observed the oil work at Bahrein and nothing here." The jovial, thickset Sheikh (Amir since 1921) prided himself on modernity — dress slacks and patent leather shoes beneath his robes, an enthusiast of the British Navy with photographs of officers and war sloops on his drawing room walls. He played a dangerous balancing act — "trying to play His Majesty's Government, the Iraqi government and King Ibn Saud off against each other."

Both **Anglo-Persian** and **Gulf** courted Sheikh Ahmad. Gulf was working through Holmes and his Eastern and General Syndicate (which the Foreign Office called Gulf's "jackal"). Anglo-Persian was skeptical about Kuwait oil prospects and mainly interested defensively — to prevent anyone from moving in on its "flank" and threatening its position in Persia and Iraq. Kuwait's economy had been devastated by **Japanese cultured pearls** — **Kokichi Mikimoto**, a Japanese noodle vendor turned pearl cultivator, had developed artificial pearl cultivation, and by 1930 large volumes were destroying the natural pearl market. Kuwait's export earnings plummeted; merchants went bankrupt; boats were laid up and divers returned to the desert.

Sheikh Ahmad believed American oil company entry would bring American political interest, bolstering his position against regional rivals. Yet he could not alienate Britain, on which Kuwait depended for security against Saudi Arabia, Iraq, and Persia (which did not even acknowledge Kuwait's existence). Britain insisted on the **"British nationality clause"** for Kuwait even after dropping it for Bahrain. The Admiralty worried about "American warships intervening in Gulf affairs." But by **April 1932**, key British departments — Foreign Office, Colonial Office, Petroleum Department — were prepared to jettison the clause. "The last thing we want," said one Foreign Office official, "is an oil war with the United States." Cadman told the Foreign Office any Kuwait oil would not "be of interest to the Anglo-Persian Oil Company. The Americans are welcome to what they can find there!"

Holmes attributed the "wonderful victory" partly to Ambassador **Andrew Mellon** — former Treasury Secretary, patriarch of the family that controlled Gulf Oil. He arrived in London in 1932 at seventy-seven, under a cloud (allegations of special treatment for Mellon companies during his Treasury tenure). He personally intervened to facilitate State Department help for Gulf in Kuwait, referring to Gulf as "my company." The State Department had to delicately manage the appearance of acting "merely for the personal benefit of Mr. Mellon."

After Socal's Bahrain discovery in May 1932, **Anglo-Persian abruptly changed its mind** — Cadman hurriedly disavowed his "no interest" statement and declared Anglo-Persian now very much wanted to bid for Kuwait. The Sheikh was delighted: "Yes, I have now two bidders, and from the point of view of a seller that is all to the good." A bidding war ensued — each company impressed by the other's determination. **Cadman** raised the possibility of amalgamation with Mellon. But both sides concluded that a **fifty-fifty joint venture** was the only way to prevent being played off against each other.

In **December 1933**, they came to final terms: the **Kuwait Oil Company** — fifty-fifty Anglo-Persian and Gulf. Holmes and **Archibald Chisholm** (for Anglo-Persian) were the "heavenly twins" sent to negotiate with the Sheikh. At a tiny church service in Kuwait, when the lesson on "blessed are the pure of heart" was read, Holmes jabbed Chisholm: "At last, you and I are pure in heart about each other."

The Sheikh proved a tough negotiator, well-informed about concession terms in Iraq, Persia, and Saudi Arabia. On **December 23, 1934**, Sheikh Ahmad signed a **seventy-five-year concession** with the Kuwait Oil Company. He received an upfront payment of **£35,700 ($179,000)**, minimum annual payments of **£7,150 ($36,000)** before oil discovery, and **£18,800 ($94,000)** or more after discovery. The Sheikh appointed Major Frank Holmes as his representative to the Kuwait Oil Company — a position Holmes held until his death in 1947.

### *The "Sure Shot"? — Saudi Exploration*

Socal had set up **Casoc** (California-Arabian Standard Oil Company) to hold the Saudi concession. Administrative headquarters were established in Jidda — in a building owned by Jack Philby. In **September 1933**, the first two American geologists arrived at Jubail on a motor launch. They had grown beards and donned Arab headdresses and robes. A few days later, they identified a promising geological structure — the **Dammam Dome** — a desolate expanse of sand and naked rock, only twenty-five miles from the Bahrain discovery. It was a "sure shot," they believed.

Drilling commenced in summer 1934, but the first half-dozen wells were all failures. More geologists arrived, traveling by camel with escorts of ten guards. Conditions were extreme — 115-degree daytime temperatures, bitter cold nights. Distances were measured in "camel days." Three weeks from Jubail, they hunted gazelle and Qatar birds, or bought sheep from passing Bedouin. They used seismography and aerial surveys (a Fairchild 71 with a hole cut in the bottom, using specially heat-resistant Kodak film).

Socal's management in San Francisco grew increasingly anxious — "sometimes there was an open question whether the venture should be abandoned and the approximately $10 million spent written off as a total loss." There was also the alarming possibility that Socal might find oil where it had no distribution facilities in a depressed world market.

### *The Blue Line Agreement — Caltex*

Socal was already facing this problem with Bahrain (capacity 13,000 barrels/day, potential 30,000). In early 1935, it choked production to just 2,500 barrels/day for lack of markets. A proposed deal with Jersey, Shell, and Anglo-Persian fell apart. In early 1936, discouraged **K. R. Kingsbury**, Socal's president, went to New York. **James Forrestal** of investment bank Dillon, Read brought Kingsbury together with **Texaco**'s management. Forrestal (assisted by vice-president **Paul Nitze**) worked out a scheme: Socal and Texaco would pool all assets **"East of Suez"** in a fifty-fifty joint venture. Socal threw in its Bahrain and Saudi concessions plus an East Indies concession; the joint venture took over Texaco's widespread marketing system in Africa and Asia. They delineated their territory with what they called the **"Blue Line"** — and the joint venture became known as **Caltex** (California-Texas company). The established companies were relieved — the merger meant Bahrain and any Saudi oil would be managed and would not destroy prices.

### *Discovery*

Exploration in Kuwait began in 1935; seismic work in 1936 pinpointed the **Burgan field** in southeastern Kuwait. On **February 23, 1938**, petroleum was struck with a surprisingly large flow. To gauge the discovery, crude was allowed to flow into an adjoining sand reservoir, then ignited — the heat was so intense that the sand walls were transformed into **sheets of glass**. Anglo-Persian and Gulf directors heaved a sigh of relief. Holmes was jubilant; Sheikh Ahmad needed no longer worry about cultured pearls.

In Saudi Arabia, Socal's board grew increasingly restive. In **November 1937**, Socal's foreign production manager cabled Arabia with a firm order: no more projects without detailed proposals first. Then, in **March 1938** — a few weeks after the Kuwait discovery — stunning news: at **4,727 feet**, large quantities of oil were tapped in **Well Number 7** in the Dammam Zone. Discovery at last — almost three years after drilling began on Dammam Number 1.

The Saudi discovery set off fevered efforts by the IPC and, more ominously, by **German, Japanese, and Italian interests** seeking drilling rights. The Japanese established diplomatic representation in Saudi Arabia, offered "astronomical" sums for concessions, and presented Ibn Saud with a gift of classic samurai military armor (too small for the large monarch). The Germans accredited their Baghdad minister and pursued an arms deal. But Casoc had a secret annex to the 1933 agreement giving it preference rights to Saudi territory. On **May 31, 1939**, Casoc expanded its exclusive concession to **440,000 square miles** — about one-sixth the size of the continental United States. Socal repeatedly made loans totaling several million dollars to the kingdom.

The Well Number 7 discovery had opened a new era. Work sped up at **Dhahran** — which would become an American middle-class suburb in the desert. A pipeline linked the oil field to **Ras Tanura**, a coastal marine terminal. In **April 1939**, a great procession of **four hundred cars** carrying the King and his huge retinue crossed the desert to Dhahran, where they encamped in 350 tents.

The occasion was the arrival of the Socal tanker ***D. G. Scofield*** at Ras Tanura to pick up the first cargo of oil. With appropriate pomp, **King Ibn Saud himself turned the valve** through which the first trickle of oil flowed out of Saudi Arabia.

### *War Intrudes and the Fates of Philby and Holmes*

Socal hastened to spread exploration. A wildcat well drilled to ten thousand feet indicated very large deposits. Production in 1940 reached **twenty thousand barrels per day**. But then World War II intruded. In **October 1940**, the Italians bombed Dhahran (apparently aiming at Bahrain). A small refinery started at Ras Tanura in January 1941 was closed down the following June. In neighboring Kuwait, on orders of the Allied governments, all wells were **plugged with cement** for fear they would fall into German hands.

In Saudi Arabia, operations were mostly shut down — a skeleton crew kept production of 12,000-15,000 barrels per day going as feedstock for the Bahrain refinery. Further development was postponed; the enterprise entered "a state of suspended animation." But elsewhere, people were beginning to assimilate what Saudi Arabia's oil potential might mean — the country's petroleum reserves would become "the object of political power plays more intricate and intense than anything that might have been imagined."

**Jack Philby** prospered in Saudi Arabia through the 1930s with his explorations and trading. After WWII began, he tried to broker a deal between Ibn Saud and **Chaim Weizmann** (Zionist leader) for a partition of Palestine — it came to nothing. His anti-British sentiments did not abate. He was arrested on a trip to India, sent back to Britain, and **jailed for half a year**. He spent the war writing pamphlets, poetry, and unpublishable books. Returning to Saudi Arabia after the war, he again became adviser to the King. With a young woman presented to him by the King, he became a father again at sixty-five. After Ibn Saud's death, he criticized the spendthrift ways of King Saud and was expelled, though later allowed to return. On a trip to Beirut in 1960 to visit his son Kim, he fell ill and was rushed to the hospital. The man "whose life had been so eventful and panoramic, so daring and theatrical, now lay unconscious." He awoke briefly, murmured to his son "I am so bored," and expired. His gravestone in a Moslem cemetery in Lebanon bore the simple inscription: **"Greatest of Arabian Explorers."**

And **Major Frank Holmes** — **"Abu al-Naft," the Father of Oil**? He was the one who imagined, conceived, and promoted the entire Arabian oil venture. In the mid-1940s, as the extent of Arabian oil riches became clear, Holmes — by then Kuwait's oil representative in London — was asked an obvious question. What made him so certain of Arabian oil prospects, in the face of the virtually unanimous verdict of the world's leading geologists that Arabia would be "oil dry"? His practical experience as a mining engineer had taught him that theoretical advice, however expert, could be quite wrong. But Holmes offered a simpler answer. He tapped his finger to his nose. **"This," he said, "was my geologist."**

## Chapter 16: Japan's Road to War

### *The Manchurian Affair*

On the night of **September 18, 1931**, soldiers of the Japanese Imperial Army in the semi-autonomous Chinese province of Manchuria carried out a bomb attack against the **South Manchurian Railway**. The actual damage was scant — about thirty-one inches of track, so negligible that a speeding express train passed over the spot minutes later. But this was by intention: the Japanese controlled the railroad and wanted to keep damage minimal while blaming the Chinese. The Japanese Army now had its desired pretext to launch an attack on Chinese forces, which it did without delay. The **Manchurian Affair** had begun, marking the entry into what the Japanese would call **the Valley of Darkness**.

Japan had gained economic and political prerogatives in Manchuria through its victories over China (1895) and Russia (1905). By the late 1920s, there was strong support to take complete control — Manchuria was "Japan's life line," as one Prime Minister described it. It would supply raw materials and "living space." Moreover, the Japanese Army feared the dual threats of Soviet communism and Chinese nationalism.

### *"Shall We Trust Japan?"*

In 1923, **Franklin Roosevelt** (then a private citizen, formerly Assistant Navy Secretary) wrote an article entitled **"Shall We Trust Japan?"** He observed that "long before the events of 1914 centered attention elsewhere, an American-Japanese war was the best bet of prophets." Yet Roosevelt answered affirmatively — Japan had changed. Through the 1920s, his analysis proved correct. The 1921 **Washington Naval Conference** defused a potential naval race. Japan based its security on cooperation with Anglo-American powers.

But this cooperation did not survive the decade. The Japanese military, particularly the Army, came to dominate the government. The Great Depression brought economic hardship, heightening vulnerability from lack of raw materials and shrinking international markets. The Army and segments of society were gripped by extreme nationalism, arrogance, and a mystical belief in "The Imperial Way." Prime Minister **Osachi Hamaguchi**, who favored naval treaty cooperation, was **shot at a railway station** by an enraged youth in 1930 and died in 1931. A cult of ultranationalism — "government by assassination" — took hold. Japan organized the puppet state of **Manchukuo** with the deposed Chinese emperor **Pu Yi** as figurehead. When the League of Nations condemned Japan, it stalked out of the League.

### *The New Order in Asia*

Japanese politics seethed with conspiracies, ideological movements, and secret societies rejecting liberalism, capitalism, and democracy. By the mid-1930s, elements of the military focused on total war doctrine — a **"national defense state"** harnessing all industrial and military resources. Officers who studied Germany's WWI failure attributed it to economic vulnerability — lack of raw materials and inability to withstand naval blockade. Japan, they recognized, was far less well-endowed. It was **almost bare of oil**. While petroleum was only about **7% of total energy consumption**, its strategic importance was paramount — most was consumed by the military and shipping. By the late 1930s, Japan produced only about **7% of the oil it consumed**. The rest was imported — **80% from the United States**, another 10% from the Dutch East Indies.

An acrimonious split between **Army** and **Navy** shaped policy. The Army focused on Manchuria, North China, Inner Mongolia, and the Soviet threat. The Navy, under the doctrine of ***hokushu nanshin*** — "defend in the north, advance to the south" — set its sights on the **Dutch East Indies, Malaya, Indochina**, and Pacific islands to secure access to natural resources, especially oil.

In the early 1930s, the Japanese government asserted domination over the oil industry. **60%** of the internal market was held by two Western companies — **Rising Sun** (Shell's Japanese affiliate) and **Standard-Vacuum (Stanvac)** (the Jersey/Socony amalgam for the Far East). In 1934, the **Petroleum Industry Law** gave the government power to control imports, set quotas, fix prices, and make compulsory purchases. Foreign companies were required to maintain **six months of inventories** beyond normal levels — to build up the Japanese-owned refining industry, reduce foreign companies' role, and prepare for war. Japan also established a petroleum monopoly in Manchukuo.

In **August 1934**, Deterding and Teagle went to Washington to suggest "frightening" Japan into moderation by hinting at an embargo. In **November 1934**, the British Cabinet endorsed "the stiffest possible resistance" to Japanese oil policies, including government support for a privately organized embargo. But **Secretary of State Cordell Hull** refused, and the embargo talk died.

### *"Quarantine"*

On the night of **July 7-8, 1937**, clashes between Japanese and Chinese troops at the **Marco Polo Bridge** near Beijing escalated into full-scale war. **Chiang Kai-shek** declared: "If we allow one more inch of our territory to be lost, we shall be guilty of an unpardonable crime against our race." Japan decided the Chinese needed "a thoroughgoing blow" and went to war with China.

Japan accelerated its war economy. A special session of the Diet passed the **Synthetic Oil Industry Law** — a seven-year plan to produce synthetic fuels from coal by 1943, in volumes equivalent to half of Japan's 1937 consumption. The goal was extremely unrealistic. Roosevelt, frustrated by isolationist constraints, obliquely broached a **"quarantine"** in an October 1937 speech to check the "epidemic of world lawlessness." After a Japanese air attack on four American ships in the Yangtze, he privately told his Cabinet he meant "such a thing as using economic sanctions without declaring war." But prevailing isolationism prevented action.

In 1938, after Japanese bombing of Canton, polls found a **large majority** of the American public opposed continued export of military materiel to Japan. "Japan furnishes the pilot, America furnishes the airplane, gasoline, oil, and bombs for the ravaging of undefended Chinese cities." A **Gallup Poll in June 1939** reported **72%** favored an embargo on war materials to Japan. But the Roosevelt Administration feared undermining Japanese moderates and provoking worse aggression.

**Joseph Grew**, American ambassador to Japan, warned of the consequences of sanctions. The Japanese would submit "to any deprivation rather than see their nation humbled by Western powers — and lose face." In autumn 1939, he met twice with Roosevelt and wrote: "I brought out clearly my view that if we once start sanctions against Japan we must see them through to the end, and the end may conceivably be war. I also said that if we cut off Japanese supplies of oil and if Japan then finds that she cannot obtain sufficient oil from other commercial sources to ensure her national security, she will in all probability send her fleets down to take the Dutch East Indies." **"Then we could easily intercept her fleet,"** the President replied.

In **September 1938**, during the Munich crisis, **George Walden** (head of Stanvac) and **Lloyd "Shorty" Elliott** (president of Stanvac's producing arm in the Dutch East Indies) listened to radio reports in The Hague. They were sure that when war came to Asia, Japan would attack the East Indies — "it was just a question of when and how." That night they began planning for that eventuality. They dismissed German, Dutch, and Japanese employees of doubtful loyalty. Plans were prepared for the **destruction of Stanvac's refinery and oil wells** — not secretly, but openly, as a deterrent. By early 1940, Walden indicated that if the U.S. placed an embargo, Stanvac "would cooperate fully" and "stop all shipments from all properties under its control all over the world."

### *Japanese Advance and American Restrictions — The First Round*

Japan sought industrial self-sufficiency and elimination of dependence on the U.S. The Japanese public was bombarded with propaganda about the **"ABCD" powers** — America, Britain, China, and the Dutch — conspiring to deny resources. After the **fall of France and the Low Countries** in May-June 1940, the colonial powers in the Far East looked vulnerable. Japan demanded oil from the Dutch East Indies. Washington made a fateful decision: it transferred the American fleet from Southern California to **Pearl Harbor** on Oahu — as a deterrent to Tokyo and a stiffening of British resolve.

Summer 1940 was a major turning point. Japan demanded a military mission to French Indochina, war materials from the East Indies, and the closing of the Burma supply route into China. Roosevelt brought **Henry Stimson** (a longtime Japan critic) into the Cabinet as Secretary of War. On **July 2, 1940**, Roosevelt signed the **National Defense Act**, whose **Section VI** gave the President power to control exports — the lever to regulate oil supplies to Japan.

In Tokyo, leaders favoring accommodation were losing ground. A secret police plot to kill those favoring settlement with the U.S. and Britain — including the Prime Minister — was aborted in July. The Cabinet was reconstructed under **Prince Konoye**; the militant **General Hideki Tojo** — known as ***"Kamisori,"*** the "Razor" — became War Minister.

On **July 19, 1940**, Roosevelt told senior advisers he had concluded "the only way out of the difficulties of the world" was "by cutting off supplies to the aggressor countries, particularly in regard to their supply of fuel to carry on the war." But on Japan specifically, there was "no consensus about whether that would make things better or worse." Treasury Secretary **Morgenthau** and War Secretary **Stimson** pushed for a complete embargo. But the State Department, fearful of provoking rupture, succeeded in limiting the ban to **aviation gasoline of 87 octane or higher**, plus some iron ore and steel scrap. American planes used 100-octane fuel; Japanese planes could operate on fuels below 87 octane. Japan bought **550% more** 86-octane gasoline from the U.S. in the five months after the proclamation than before. Despite appearances, an embargo had not gone into effect — only a licensing system.

On **September 26, 1940**, Washington banned export of all iron and steel scrap — but not oil. The next day, Japan signed the **Tripartite Pact** with Hitler and Mussolini. Roosevelt, committed to a "Europe first" strategy, husbanded resources for Europe and resisted a full oil embargo — the Presidential election was a month away. Meanwhile, Japan was buying up all petroleum supplies, drilling equipment, and storage tanks it could obtain. Britain now wanted to halt the oil flow, fearing Japanese stockpiling would make sanctions immune. Still, Roosevelt and Hull resisted.

### *Quiet Conversations — Hull and Nomura*

**Secretary of State Cordell Hull** — a tall, silver-haired backwoodsman turned statesman from a Tennessee log cabin — began secret nighttime talks with the new Japanese ambassador, **Admiral Kichisaburo Nomura**, usually at Hull's apartment at the Wardman Park Hotel. Hull had devoted himself since 1933 to breaking down trade barriers for world peace. Now he could see those labors going for naught.

Admiral Nomura was a political moderate, widely respected in Japanese circles. At six feet, he stood out. He had lost an eye in a bomb attack by a Korean nationalist in Shanghai in 1932, which also left him with a limp and more than a hundred metal fragments in his body. He had served as naval attache in Washington during WWI and known the young FDR. Upon his return in February 1941, Roosevelt greeted him as "friend" and insisted on calling him "Admiral" rather than "Ambassador." Nomura told the U.S. Chief of Naval Operations that "his lips and his heart" were "at variance." Later, trying to explain the tense days, he said simply: **"When a big house falls, one pillar can not stop it."**

Beginning March 1941, Hull and Nomura met perhaps **forty or fifty times**, plowing through discouraging soil. Hull had a startling advantage: thanks to the code-breaking operation **"Magic,"** the U.S. and Britain had cracked **"Purple,"** the top-secret Japanese diplomatic code. Hull could read, before meetings, Tokyo's instructions to Nomura. He played his part adroitly, never revealing his knowledge. In early May 1941, the Germans informed Tokyo that the Americans had broken their codes — **the Japanese simply did not believe it**, convinced Americans were not capable of such a feat.

Yet despite "Magic," Washington did not know of the Japanese Navy's concern that the American fleet at Pearl Harbor, if left unattended during an invasion of the East Indies, could launch a dangerous flank attack. The Japanese Navy had begun planning a high-risk project — a **surprise attack on Pearl Harbor**.

### *Yamamoto's Gamble — "Doubtless I Will Die"*

As early as spring 1940, **Admiral Isoroku Yamamoto**, Commander in Chief of Japan's Combined Fleets, had begun to outline this wild gamble. He was the most daring, original, and controversial of the Japanese admirals — short, broadly constructed, widely respected for physical courage. He had spent more than four years in the U.S. (Harvard, then naval representative and attache), read four or five biographies of Lincoln, regularly received *Life* magazine, and recognized that America was rich in resources while Japan was poor, with productive capacity far outstripping Japan's.

Even while developing the Pearl Harbor plan, Yamamoto continued to challenge the whole idea of war with the United States — "at best, very risky and, most probably, a losing proposition." He scoffed at ultranationalists with their "armchair arguments about war and their mystical fantasies." He called complaints about "America's economic pressure" the "aimless action of a schoolboy."

The oil factor weighed heavily on Yamamoto. He grew up in the **Niigata district**, one of Japan's small domestic oil-producing regions; his hometown of Nagaoka was populated by hundreds of tiny factories producing oil for lamps. His time in America convinced him the industrial world was moving from coal to oil and that air power was the future. Acutely conscious of Japan's oil vulnerability, he restricted the Navy (the world's third-largest) to training only in waters immediately off Japan — **to conserve petroleum**. He even sponsored experiments by a "scientist" who claimed he could change water into oil.

Yet Yamamoto was a fervent nationalist devoted to Emperor and country. "It's out of the question!" he exclaimed. "To fight the United States is like fighting the whole world. But it has been decided. So I will do my best. Doubtless I will die." If Japan had to go to war, he believed in the **"decisive blow"** — knock the United States off balance, incapacitate it, while Japan secured Southeast Asia. "The lesson which impressed me most deeply when I studied the Russo-Japanese War was the fact that our Navy launched a night assault against Port Arthur at the very beginning. This was the most excellent strategical initiative ever envisaged during the war." His plan was decided in late 1940 and early 1941 — "to decide the fate of the war on the very first day" by knocking out the U.S. fleet, and also to destroy the morale of the American people.

"Operation Hawaii" required secrecy, first-rate intelligence, superb coordination, high technical skills, new aerial torpedoes, new refueling-at-sea techniques, absolute devotion, and the cooperation of weather and waves. Early in 1941, Ambassador Grew heard a rumor about the planned attack from Peru's minister to Tokyo. Grew reported it to Washington, where it was immediately discounted. **American officials simply could not believe — then or in the months following — that such an audacious assault was even possible.**

### *Embargo*

From April through June 1941, arguments raged in the U.S. government over whether to cut off oil exports to Japan and freeze Japanese funds. The Axis powers and America were moving closer to direct confrontation. On **May 27, 1941**, Roosevelt declared an **"unlimited national emergency"** — aiming to "scare the daylights out of everyone" about Axis dangers. **Harold Ickes**, just appointed Petroleum Coordinator, immediately prohibited oil shipments to Japan from the East Coast — but the order did not cover the Gulf or West Coasts. Ickes was pushing for a general embargo on all oil exports to Japan.

An angry Roosevelt **countermanded Ickes's order**, leading to a bitter exchange. **"There will never be so good a time to stop the shipment of oil to Japan as we now have,"** Ickes argued, noting Japan was preoccupied with Russia and Siberia. Roosevelt shot back sarcastically: "Please let me know if this would continue to be your judgment if this were to tip the delicate scales and cause Japan to decide either to attack Russia or to attack the Dutch East Indies." He told Ickes the question of Japanese exports was "a matter not of oil conservation, but of foreign policy."

Ickes, as was his wont, proffered his resignation — though not as Interior Secretary. Roosevelt refused to accept it: **"There you go again!"** He explained: "the Japs are having a real drag-down and knock-out fight among themselves... trying to figure out which way they are going to jump." He added: **"As you know, it is terribly important for the control of the Atlantic for us to help to keep peace in the Pacific. I simply have not got enough Navy to go around."**

The "knock-out fight" Roosevelt referred to had been precipitated by **Germany's surprise attack on the Soviet Union in June 1941**, which forced a major strategic choice for Tokyo: continue southward or join the attack on Russia from the east. Between **June 25 and July 2, 1941**, senior officials in Tokyo grappled with and argued fervently over the choices. They made the **fateful decision**: put off doing anything about the Soviet Union, concentrate on the **southern strategy** — secure control of all of Indochina, deemed necessary to go for the East Indies. They did so knowing the occupation of southern Indochina could provoke an all-out American oil embargo, which would be **"a matter of life or death to the empire,"** in the words of the Navy General Staff. But Japan would not be deterred by the threat of war with Britain and the United States.

Through **"Magic" intercepts**, Washington knew of the momentous debate and its outcome. "After the occupation of French Indochina," said one intercepted message, **"next on our schedule is... the Netherlands East Indies."** At Roosevelt's Cabinet meeting on July 18, it was reported that the Japanese were virtually certain to advance into southern Indochina within days.

**"What are you going to do on the economic front against Japan if she makes this move?"** Treasury Secretary **Morgenthau** asked the President. **"If we stopped all oil,"** Roosevelt replied, **"it would simply drive the Japanese down to the Dutch East Indies, and it would mean war in the Pacific."** But he indicated he would support freezing Japanese financial assets in the United States.

Roosevelt himself did not want a full embargo. He wanted to tighten controls but keep them **"day to day," a flexible tool** that could be adjusted. He thought he could use oil as an instrument for diplomacy, not as the trigger for war. Undersecretary of State **Sumner Welles** proposed a program holding petroleum exports to the 1935-36 level but prohibiting export of any grades convertible into aviation gasoline. Export licenses would be required for *all* oil exports.

On the evening of **July 25**, the U.S. government ordered all Japanese financial assets in the United States frozen. Licenses — government approval — would be required for each use of frozen funds, including oil purchases. On **July 28**, Japan began its anticipated invasion of southern Indochina.

### *Dean Acheson's De Facto Embargo*

The new American policy was not meant to cut off oil entirely, at least not explicitly, but **a virtually total embargo was the actual result**. A key role was played by **Dean Acheson**, Assistant Secretary of State for Economic Affairs, one of the few senior State Department officials to favor an out-and-out embargo. He turned the July 25 order into an embargo, in consultation with the Treasury Department, by completely preventing the release of frozen funds necessary for the Japanese to buy oil. **"Whether or not we had a policy, we had a state of affairs,"** Acheson later said. **"Until further notice it would continue."** From the beginning of August, no more oil was exported to Japan. Two Japanese tankers were left sitting empty in the harbor at San Pedro, near Los Angeles.

Britain followed with its own freeze and embargo, cutting off supplies from Borneo, as did the Dutch East Indies.

By the end of July 1941, Japan had secured its occupation of southern Indochina. **"Today I knew from the hard looks on their faces that they meant business,"** Ambassador Nomura reported to the Foreign Ministry in Tokyo on July 31. He urged appeasement measures. The Foreign Ministry scathingly dismissed his concerns. With the Japanese thrust into Indochina and the consequent American freeze on Japanese funds — which in practice meant an embargo on oil — **the countdown had begun**.

The embargo did not create the impending confrontation — it was virtually the only way left for the U.S., Britain, and the Dutch to respond to Japanese aggression short of military action. For the Japanese, however, it was the final link in their **"encirclement"** by hostile powers. Tokyo refused to recognize it was creating a self-fulfilling prophecy. The embargo was the result of four years of Japanese military aggression in Asia. The only oil securely available to Japan was what it held in its own inventories. If it were to maintain its capability to wage war, it would inevitably have to risk — or make — war.

### *"We Cannot Endure It"*

The leaders of the Japanese Navy — previously more cautious than the Army about confrontation with the United States — were no longer restrained in the light of the complete embargo. **"If there were no supply of oil, battleships and any other warships would be nothing more than scarecrows,"** a leading Japanese admiral said. **Admiral Osami Nagano**, chief of the Naval General Staff, stressed to the Emperor that Japan's petroleum reserves would, without replenishment, **last no more than two years**.

The new Japanese foreign minister, **Teijiro Toyoda**, expressed the paranoia in secret messages to ambassadors in Berlin and Washington: **"Commercial and economic relations between Japan and third countries, led by England and the United States, are gradually becoming so horribly strained that we cannot endure it much longer."** He wrote on July 31, 1941: "Consequently, our Empire, to save its very life, must take measures to secure the raw materials of the South Seas... break asunder this ever-strengthening chain of encirclement."

How different it looked to **Cordell Hull**, ill and exhausted at White Sulphur Springs: "The Japanese are seeking to dominate militarily practically one-half the world. ... Nothing will stop them except force." Still he sought to postpone the inevitable: "The point is how long we can maneuver the situation until the military matter in Europe is brought to a conclusion."

Ambassador **Grew** in Tokyo saw the situation clearly: **"The vicious circle of reprisals and counter reprisals is one,"** he wrote in his diary. ***"Facilis descensus Averno est."*** By that time, power shovels were already digging shelters around the Imperial Palace in Tokyo.

### *Last-Ditch Diplomacy — Konoye's Gamble*

There were last-minute diplomatic efforts. **Prince Konoye**, the Prime Minister, with some support from the Navy, raised the possibility of a summit meeting with Roosevelt. He was even willing to jettison the Axis alliance with Hitler to reach an understanding with the Americans. Palace officials endorsed Konoye's idea. **"The whole problem facing Japan had been reduced to a very simple factor, and that was oil,"** **Koichi Kido**, the Lord Privy Seal, told the premier in private, adding, "Japan could not possibly fight a war of certain victory against the United States."

The Emperor himself gave Konoye's idea his blessing, noting intelligence from the Navy about the general oil embargo. Konoye suggested Roosevelt meet in, of all places, **Honolulu**. The President was initially quite interested — taken enough to counter-propose Juneau, Alaska. But **Hull and the State Department strenuously opposed** this breach in diplomatic due process. They did not understand this was Konoye's last gamble. Nor did they trust Japan. Moreover, Roosevelt did not want "Juneau" to join the vocabulary along with "Munich." Magic intercepts indicated the Japanese were intent on further conquest. So Roosevelt, with his talent for ambiguity, neither agreed to nor rejected such a meeting.

### *"Dwindling Day by Day"*

In Tokyo, on **September 5 and 6**, the most senior Japanese officials met with the Emperor to assume a war posture, even while diplomatic alternatives were explored. Oil was their central concern: **"At present oil is the weak point of our Empire's national strength and fighting power... As time passes, our capacity to carry on war will decline, and our Empire will become powerless militarily."** The Navy Chief of Staff told the Emperor: **"Vital military supplies, including oil, are dwindling day by day."**

The Emperor asked how long hostilities would last. "Operations in the South Pacific could be disposed of in about three months," the Army Chief of Staff answered. The Emperor retorted sharply: the General had been Minister of War at the outbreak of the China Incident and had then promised it would be disposed of in about one month — **"Despite the General's assurance, the incident was not yet concluded after four long years of fighting."** "If the Chinese hinterland was extensive," the Emperor shot back, raising his voice, **"the Pacific was boundless."** How could the general be certain of his three-month calculation? The Chief of Staff hung his head, with no reply.

**Admiral Nagano** stepped in: "Japan was like a patient suffering from a serious illness. A quick decision had to be made one way or the other." The Emperor tried to ascertain whether the senior advisers favored diplomacy or war first — he could not get a clear answer. The next day, when the question was raised again, the Chiefs of Army and Navy General Staff remained silent. The Emperor expressed regret, drew a paper from his robe, and read a poem by his grandfather, **Emperor Meiji**: *"Since all are brothers in this world, Why is there such constant turmoil?"* The hall was silent. "Everyone present was struck with awe." Admiral Nagano rose and said military force would be used only when all else had failed. The meeting adjourned **"in an atmosphere of unprecedented tenseness."**
