After the conference, the Cabinet took up whether **synthetic oil production** could be greatly increased. Konoye said it was better to spend vast sums on such a program than on war. But the head of the Planning Board said it would require up to four years, many billions of yen, vast amounts of steel, pipes, machinery, and upward of **four hundred thousand coal miners**. Konoye's proposal was put aside. In late September, four men armed with daggers and short swords attacked Konoye's car — they were repulsed, but the Prime Minister was badly shaken.

On **October 2**, the United States officially rejected a meeting between Konoye and Roosevelt. Shortly after, Konoye fell from office. He was replaced on **October 18** by **Hideki Tojo**, the bellicose war minister nicknamed **"the Razor"**, who had consistently dismissed diplomacy as useless. Ambassador Nomura futilely described himself as **"the bones of a dead horse."** Roosevelt pleaded with Nomura that between their two countries, there be "no last words."

The two Japanese tankers that had sat in the harbor near Los Angeles since midsummer, waiting for contracted oil, finally weighed anchor in the first part of November and sailed away with no oil aboard. Now no one could doubt the absoluteness of the oil embargo. With winter almost at hand in Tokyo, the Japanese retaliated by cutting off all supplies of heating oil to the American and British embassies.

Through October and November, Japan's military and political leaders debated the final commitment to war in small rooms in the Imperial Palace. Again and again, the discussion came back to oil. Japanese oil imports had fallen drastically in 1941. Inventories were declining. **"From the records available it is clear that this time-oil factor hovered over the conference table like a demon,"** one historian wrote. **"A decision for war was considered the most readily available means of exorcising it."**

### *The Imperial Conference — November 5*

On **November 5**, an Imperial Conference of the most senior leaders convened before the Emperor. **Tojo** summarized the majority position: "The United States has from the beginning believed that Japan would give up because of economic pressure... If we enter into a protracted war, there will be difficulties... We have some uneasiness about a protracted war. But how can we let the United States continue as she pleases, even though there is some uneasiness? **Two years from now we will have no petroleum for military use. Ships will stop moving.** When I think about the strengthening of American defenses in the Southwest Pacific, the expansion of the American fleet, the unfinished China Incident, and so on, I see no end to difficulties. ... **I fear that we would become a third-class nation after two or three years if we just sat tight.**"

The proposal called for stiff last-ditch demands to the United States. If rejected, Japan would go to war. Tojo asked for objections; hearing none, the proposal was approved.

A Japanese diplomat arrived in Washington the third week of November to present the demands to Hull — they read like an ultimatum. There was also an intercepted "Magic" message of **November 22**, informing Nomura that American agreement to Tokyo's proposals had to be received by **November 29** at the very latest, for **"reasons beyond your ability to guess."** For, **"after that, things are automatically going to happen."**

On **November 25**, Roosevelt warned his senior military advisers that war could come very soon, even within a week. On November 26, Hull presented a note proposing Japanese withdrawal from Indochina and China in exchange for resumed trade. Tokyo regarded this as an American ultimatum. On **that same day, November 26**, a Japanese naval task force that had gathered in the **Kurile Islands** was ordered to set sail, under radio silence. **Its destination was Hawaii.**

Secretary of War **Stimson** brought Roosevelt an intelligence report showing a large Japanese expeditionary force moving south from Shanghai toward Southeast Asia. "He fairly blew up, jumped into the air, so to speak, and said he hadn't seen it," commented Stimson. That changed the whole situation — evidence of bad faith while negotiating for a truce. On **November 27**, Hull told Stimson he had completely given up on negotiations: **"I have washed my hands of it."** Washington sent off "a final alert" to American commanders in the Pacific, including **Admiral Husband Kimmel**, commander of the Pacific Fleet in Hawaii. The message began: **"This dispatch is to be considered a war warning."**

Up to the very end, there were those in Tokyo who saw nothing but disaster ahead. On **November 29**, the Senior Statesmen met with the Cabinet and the Emperor to plead that Japan seek a diplomatic solution. Tojo railed that broken economic relations would mean progressive weakening of Japan. The Japanese leaders, in all their studies, had recognized that a long war would increasingly favor the United States because of its resources, capabilities, and endurance — **but so strongly were the militarists gripped in the trance of their own making that those committed to war simply waved that consideration aside.** War was on a speeding track.

### *Pearl Harbor*

On **December 1**, the special Japanese task force, still undetected, crossed the international dateline. "Everything is decided," a flight commander wrote on December 2. "There is neither here nor there, neither sorrow nor rejoicing." Tokyo gave orders to its embassies and consulates to destroy codes. An American military officer, sent to reconnoiter the Japanese embassy in Washington, found that **papers were being burned in the backyard**.

On **Saturday, December 6**, Roosevelt decided to send a personal note directly to the Emperor, seeking to dispel "the dark clouds." The message did not go off until nine o'clock that evening. Shortly after sending it, Roosevelt told some visitors, **"This son of man has just sent his final message to the Son of God."**

At **12:30 in the afternoon of December 7**, Washington time, Roosevelt received the Chinese ambassador and said he expected "foul play" in Asia. He had a feeling the Japanese might do something "nasty" within forty-eight hours. At **1:00 p.m. Washington time** — it was **3:00 a.m., December 8, in Tokyo** — Roosevelt's message was finally delivered to the Emperor. In the middle of the Pacific, it was the early morning hours of December 7, and the Japanese fleet was coming upon the Hawaiian Islands. Aloft above the flagship was the flag that had flown on a Japanese battleship in 1905, when the fleet had destroyed the Russian Navy in the Tsushima Strait. Planes were leaving the decks of the aircraft carriers. Their crews had been told they were going to **destroy the ability of the United States to cheat Japan out of its deserved place on earth**.

**The bombs began to fall on the American fleet in Pearl Harbor at 7:55 a.m., Hawaiian time.**

An hour after the attack began, Ambassador Nomura arrived at the State Department. Hull took an urgent call from the President: **"There's a report that the Japanese have attacked Pearl Harbor."** "Has the report been confirmed?" Hull asked. "No," the President replied. Hull made a pretense of reading Tokyo's justification. He could not control his rage: **"In all my fifty years of public service I have never seen such a document more crowded with falsehoods and distortions — infamous falsehoods and distortions on a scale so huge that I never imagined until today that any Government on this planet was capable of uttering them."** To Hull, the backwoodsman turned statesman, the two diplomats looked to him **"like a pair of sheep-killing dogs."**

Neither Japanese offered any further comment. The meeting ended, but no one came forward to open the door for them, for they were now enemies. They opened the door themselves and rode down in an empty elevator, and let themselves out to the street.

All that day, the reports flowed into Washington from Pearl Harbor — disjointed, fragmentary, and finally, dismal. "The news coming from Hawaii is very bad," Stimson noted. **"It has been staggering to see our people there, who have been warned long ago and were standing on the alert, should have been so caught by surprise."** Senior American officials had fully expected a Japanese attack — but in Southeast Asia. Virtually no one seriously considered that Japan could launch a surprise assault against the American fleet at its home base. They believed Pearl Harbor was "the strongest fortress in the world." Most American officials seemed to have forgotten that Japan's great victory in the Russo-Japanese War had begun with a surprise attack on the Russian fleet at Port Arthur.

Each side had fundamentally underestimated the other. Just as the Japanese did not think the Americans were technically capable of cracking their most secret codes, the Americans could not conceive that the Japanese would be able to mount so technically complex an operation. The Japanese counted on Pearl Harbor to shatter American morale — instead, the attack **revivified national morale and swiftly united the country**. That was a much greater error.

After the fact, Japanese intentions could be clearly discerned in the mass of information available. But in those tense months, the clear signals were lost in "the noise" — the maze of complex, confusing, contradictory, competing, and ambiguous pieces of information. The dissemination of "Magic" itself was sometimes bungled, in critical ways. The breakdown of critical communication among key actors on the American side may have been the **second most important cause** of the tragedy at Pearl Harbor, following only the failure to believe that such an attack could take place at all.

### *The One Mistake*

Pearl Harbor was not the main Japanese target — Hawaii was but one piece of a massive, far-flung military onslaught. In the same hours, the Japanese were bombing and blockading Hong Kong, bombing Singapore, bombing the Philippines, bombarding Wake and Guam, taking over Thailand, invading Malaya — **and preparing to invade the East Indies**. The operation against Pearl Harbor was meant to protect the flank — to safeguard the Japanese invasion of the Indies and the rest of Southeast Asia by incapacitating the American fleet, and to protect the tanker routes from Sumatra and Borneo to the home islands. **The primary target of this huge campaign remained the oil fields of the East Indies.**

A critical element of success — **luck** — had been with the Japanese attackers right up to the last moment. They far exceeded their own ambitions. Two waves of Japanese aircraft succeeded in sinking, capsizing, or severely damaging **eight battleships**, three light cruisers, three destroyers, and four auxiliary craft. Hundreds of American planes were destroyed or damaged. **2,335 American servicemen and 68 civilians were killed.** The American aircraft carriers survived only because they happened to be out on missions at sea. The Japanese lost only **twenty-nine planes**. Admiral Yamamoto's gamble had paid off, handsomely.

Yet the commander of the Hawaiian task force, **Chuichi Nagumo**, was far more cautious — indeed, he had actually opposed the entire operation. Despite the entreaties of his emboldened officers, he did not want to send planes back to Hawaii for **a third wave** to attack the repair facilities and the oil tanks at Pearl. His luck had been so enormous that he did not want to take more risks. And that, along with the sparing of the aircraft carriers, was **America's only piece of good fortune on that day of devastation**.

Yamamoto had observed that the great mistake in Japan's surprise attack against the Russians at Port Arthur in 1904 was in not being **"thoroughgoing"** enough. The same mistake was made once again at Pearl Harbor. Oil had been central to Japan's decision to go to war — **yet the Japanese forgot about oil when it came to planning Operation Hawaii**.

Yamamoto and his colleagues, who had endlessly reviewed America's preponderant position in oil, all failed to grasp the significance of the oil supplies on Oahu. An assault on those supplies was not included in their plans.

It was a **strategic error with momentous reverberations**. Every barrel of oil in Hawaii had been transported from the mainland. If the Japanese planes had knocked out the Pacific Fleet's fuel reserves and the tanks in which they were stored, they would have immobilized every ship of the American Pacific Fleet, and not just those they actually destroyed. New petroleum supplies would only have been available from California, thousands of miles away. **"All of the oil for the Fleet was in surface tanks at the time of Pearl Harbor,"** **Admiral Chester Nimitz**, who became Commander in Chief of the Pacific Fleet, was later to say. **"We had about 4.5 million barrels of oil out there and all of it was vulnerable to .50 caliber bullets. Had the Japanese destroyed the oil, it would have prolonged the war another two years."**

---

## Chapter 17: Germany's Formula for War

### *I.G. Farben Meets Hitler*

One afternoon in **June 1932**, an open car appeared at a Munich hotel to pick up two officials of **I.G. Farben**, the huge German chemical combine — one a chemist, the other a public relations man — and drive them to the private apartment of **Adolf Hitler** at *Prinzregentenplatz*. Hitler had not yet come to power as Chancellor, but he was the leader of the National Socialist party, which held almost 20 percent of the seats in the Reichstag and looked likely to increase significantly in the upcoming election.

The men from I.G. Farben had sought out the would-be Fuhrer to try to end the continuing Nazi press campaign against their company. The Nazis railed against I.G. Farben as an exploitative tool of **"international financial lords"** and **"money-mighty Jews,"** and attacked the company for the fact that Jews occupied some senior positions. They even caricatured the company as **"Isadore G. Farben."** The Nazis also criticized I.G. Farben for pursuing its expensive project to manufacture liquid fuels from coal — otherwise known as **synthetic fuels** — and for the tariff protection it had obtained from the government. I.G. Farben had made a very large financial commitment to synthetic fuels, but it appeared by 1932 that the project could never be profitable without continued government tariff protection and other support. The company's main argument was that a synthetic fuels industry would cut Germany's dependence on foreign oil and thus reduce the acute pressure on foreign exchange.

Hitler himself was late for the meeting, having just returned from an electoral campaign trip. He intended to give the two I.G. Farben officials only a half hour, but he became so engrossed in the discussion that he spent **two and a half hours** with them. Mesmerized by his own visions, Hitler did much of the talking, lecturing and declaiming on his plans to motorize Germany and build new highways. But he also asked technical questions about synthetic fuels and assured the two men that such fuels fit perfectly with his overall plans for a new Germany. **"Today, an economy without oil is inconceivable in a Germany which wishes to remain politically independent. Therefore, German motor fuel must become a reality, even if this entails sacrifices. Therefore, it is urgently necessary that the hydrogenation of coal be continued."** He strongly endorsed the synthetic fuels effort and promised to halt the press campaign against I.G. Farben and to keep tariff protection in place once the Nazis came to power. For its part, I.G. Farben — then or later — promised to deliver what the Nazis wanted: **campaign contributions**. When the I.G. Farben officials reported back, the chairman of the company said, "Well, this man seems to be more reasonable than I had thought."

Hitler had good cause to appear reasonable. A successful synthetic fuels program could prove essential to his overall objectives for a resurgent and dominant Germany. One of the major obstacles was Germany's dependence on imported raw materials — and oil in particular. Domestic petroleum production was tiny; imports, correspondingly high, and much of the imported oil came from the Western Hemisphere.

Germany's remarkable economic growth over the preceding half century had been based largely on its own plentiful energy source — **coal**. In the late 1930s, coal supplied **90 percent of Germany's energy** — while oil accounted for only about 5 percent. But already in 1932, Hitler was planning for the future, and oil would be essential to his ambitions. He became Chancellor in January 1933 and over the next year and a half seized complete power. He wasted no time in launching a motorcar campaign — autobahns, limited-access highways without speed limits, were to span the country, and in 1934, planning began for a new type of vehicle called **"the people's car," the Volkswagen**.

But these were pieces of his grand plan to subordinate all of Europe to the Nazi Reich — and to himself. He quickly regimented the economy, harnessed big business to the state, and built the Nazi war machine — bombers, fighter planes, tanks, and trucks, all of which required oil. And the synthetic fuels on which I.G. Farben was working were to be of decisive importance.

### *The Chemical Solution*

Pioneering work on the extraction of synthetic fuels from coal had begun in Germany before the First World War. Germany was already acknowledged as the world's leader in chemistry. In **1913**, the German chemist **Friedrich Bergius** first succeeded in extracting a liquid from coal in a process that became known as **hydrogenation**. Large amounts of hydrogen were added to coal under high temperatures and high pressure in the presence of a catalyst. The end product was a high-grade liquid fuel. A competing German process, **Fischer-Tropsch**, was detailed a decade later, in the mid-1920s. Here, coal molecules were broken down under steam into hydrogen and carbon monoxide, which were made to react together, resulting in synthetic oil. The **Bergius hydrogenation process** was deemed the better of the two — it could produce aviation fuel, which Fischer-Tropsch could not. In addition, I.G. Farben, which acquired the patent rights in 1926 to the Bergius process, was politically more powerful than the sponsors of Fischer-Tropsch.

I.G. Farben became interested in synthetic fuels in the 1920s because of the same predictions of imminent exhaustion of conventional petroleum supplies that were stimulating exploration around the world. A pilot plant was built at I.G.'s **Leuna works**, with initial production beginning in **1927**. At the same time, I.G. Farben was busy seeking potential partners in other countries. After negotiations with a leading British chemical group fell through, I.G. Farben found a much more important potential partner — **Standard Oil of New Jersey**.

### *Standard Oil and I.G. Farben*

Standard was midway in its strategic transformation from refiner to integrated oil company. It had also been exploring alternatives to crude oil as a source of liquid fuels — as early as 1921, it had purchased twenty-two thousand acres in Colorado with the hope of finding a commercially successful method for extracting oil from shale. But Standard had been dissatisfied with the results; one barrel of synthetic oil from shale required a ton of rock, and the economics were extremely unattractive.

**Frank Howard**, the head of research at Standard, visited I.G.'s Leuna works in **1926**. He was so impressed that he fired off a telegram to Standard's president, **Walter Teagle**, in Paris: **"Based upon observations and discussions today, I think that this matter is the most important which has ever faced the company since the dissolution... This means absolutely the independence of Europe in the matter of gasoline supply."** Teagle himself, alarmed about the possibility of losing European markets, hurried to Leuna. "I had not known what research meant until I saw it," he later said. **"We were babies compared to the work I saw."**

Teagle, Howard, and other Standard executives hurriedly gathered at a hotel room in Heidelberg, ten miles from the I.G. Farben works. They concluded that the hydrogenation process might be **"more significant than any technical factor ever introduced into the oil industry up to this time."** It was a clear threat to Standard's business. "Although hydrogenation of coal probably could never compete on an economic basis with crude oil," said Howard, "the nationalistic factor would lead to hydrogenation's being made the foundation of a protected manufacturing industry in many countries willing to pay the price." Markets could be closed to imported crude oil and refined products; Standard could hardly afford not to become involved.

An initial agreement was reached with I.G. Farben, allowing Standard to build a hydrogenation plant in Louisiana. But the world oil shortage was beginning to turn into a surplus, and Standard's interest shifted — hydrogenation could be used on crude oil, to increase the gasoline yield. In **1929**, the two companies struck a broader agreement: Standard would have the patent rights to hydrogenation outside Germany; I.G. Farben received 2 percent of Standard's stock — **546,000 shares — valued at $35 million**. Each company agreed to stay out of the other's main fields. "The I.G. are going to stay out of the oil business — and we are going to stay out of the chemical business." The next step came in 1930, with the establishment of a joint company to share developments in the "oil-chemical" field.

In **1931**, German science and hydrogenation received the highest accolade: **Bergius**, the inventor, and **Carl Bosch**, the chairman of I.G. Farben, shared the **Nobel Prize in chemistry**. Yet the project at Leuna was by then producing at a rate of two thousand barrels per day but floundering badly in deep financial trouble. Development was proving more difficult and much more expensive than anticipated. The oil surplus, with new discoveries in East Texas, had turned into an overwhelming global glut. The resulting collapse in world oil prices made the synthetic fuel effort at Leuna decidedly uneconomical. The cost of producing a liter of *Leunabenzin* was **up to ten times the price per gallon** at which gasoline was put onto tankers in the Gulf of Mexico bound for Germany. Some I.G. Farben executives said the whole project should be abandoned. The sole reason to keep it going was that the costs of shutting it down would be greater than the costs of continuing.

### *Nazi Patronage and the Four-Year Plan*

The only real hope for keeping the synthetic fuels project alive, in the midst of the Great Depression, was state support or bail-out. The new Nazi regime was willing to go much further — guarantee prices and markets to I.G. Farben — so long as the company promised to increase substantially its production of synthetic fuels. Even that was not enough, for hydrogenation was still an infant technology. I.G. Farben won support from the Air Force, the **Luftwaffe**, by proving it could develop a high-quality aviation gasoline. The German Army, the **Wehrmacht**, also lobbied for an expanded commitment to a domestic synthetic fuels industry, arguing that Germany's own current supplies would be woefully inadequate for the new type of warfare it was planning.

Two further developments demonstrated to Hitler the dangers of depending upon foreign oil. First, in **October 1935**, Italy invaded **Ethiopia** (Abyssinia). The **League of Nations** condemned the invasion and considered placing an embargo on oil exports to Italy. **Mussolini** well knew that a shut-off of petroleum supplies would paralyze the Italian military. He resorted to every form of bluff and bluster. A leading proponent of oil sanctions was **Anthony Eden**. But Mussolini found a willing ally in French Prime Minister **Pierre Laval**, who cleverly subverted the oil sanctions movement at the moment when it was closest to success.

By spring 1936, Mussolini's forces had conquered Ethiopia, and the sanctions movement had collapsed. The oil embargo was never tested, never applied. **Mussolini himself later confided to Hitler: "If the League of Nations had followed Eden's advice on the Abyssinian dispute, and had extended economic sanctions to oil, I would have had to withdraw from Abyssinia within a week. That would have been an incalculable disaster for me!"** Hitler took the lesson of dependence most seriously.

The second lesson: the Nazi regime was committed to "recovering" the domestic German market from Standard Oil, Shell, and other foreign companies. The hated Bolsheviks owned a large chain of gasoline stations through which they sold oil products. The Nazi government pushed a German gasoline marketer to acquire the Soviet chain in **1935**. But in **February 1936**, the Soviets abruptly stopped deliveries due to "difficulties with foreign payments." The deliveries did not resume — another warning to Hitler about the dangers of dependence.

Just at this time — February 1936 — as the League still debated oil sanctions, the annual German motor show in Berlin was opened by Hitler. He took the occasion to announce that Germany **"had effectively solved the problem of producing synthetic gasoline."** This achievement, he pointedly declared, "possessed political significance." The question of foreign supplies and sanctions was very much on Hitler's mind. The next month, **March 1936**, he boldly remilitarized the **Rhineland** in violation of treaty agreements — his gravest risk, **"the most nerve-racking in my life."** The Western powers did nothing.

Later in **1936**, Hitler took decisive steps to gird the German state for war by the target date of **1940**. He inaugurated his **Four-Year Plan**, aimed to reduce dependence on foreign oil through new technology and chemistry. **"German fuel production must now be developed with the utmost speed,"** he said. **"This task must be handled and executed with the same determination as the waging of a war, since on its solution depends the future conduct of the war."** He added that "the production cost of these raw materials" was of **"no importance."**

The synthetic fuels industry was supposed to expand output almost **sixfold**. The program received major financial support, and vast amounts of steel and labor were requisitioned. Each plant was a huge engineering undertaking, spread over acres. I.G. Farben led the way, adapting itself to Nazi ideology. By **1937-38**, it was no longer an independent company but rather an industrial arm of the German state, fully Nazified. All Jewish officials had been removed, including a third of the supervisory board who were Jews. The anti-Nazi chairman of the managing board, **Carl Bosch**, the man who had made the deal with Standard Oil, was pushed aside, while most other managing board members who did not already belong to the Nazi party fell all over each other in their rush to sign up.

By **September 1, 1939**, when Germany invaded Poland, **fourteen hydrogenation plants** were in full operation, with six more under construction. By **1940**, synthetic fuel output had increased drastically — **72,000 barrels per day**, accounting for **46 percent of total oil supply**. But the synthetic fuels were even more significant when viewed in terms of military needs. Hydrogenation, the Bergius process, provided some **95 percent of Germany's total aviation gasoline**. Without those synthetic fuels, the Luftwaffe could not have taken to the air.

### *Blitzkrieg and the Russian Campaign*

Despite the strength of his military machine and a growing supply of synthetic fuels, oil was never far from Hitler's mind. That concern had helped shape his basic strategic approach to war — the ***blitzkrieg***, or "lightning war" — fierce but short battles with concentrated mechanized forces that would lead to decisive victory before petroleum supply problems could develop. Initially, the strategy worked astonishingly well — in Poland in 1939, and also in spring 1940, as Hitler's forces overran Norway, the Low Countries, and France with surprising ease. The campaign in the West actually improved Germany's oil position, for German troops captured oil stocks considerably in excess of the fuel they had expended in the invasions.

### *The Russian Campaign: "My Generals Know Nothing About the Economic Aspects of War"*

Many factors shaped Germany's decision to go to war with the Soviet Union: Hitler's deep-seated hatred of Bolshevism ("its eradication, he said, was his 'life's mission'"); his personal enmity for Stalin; his contempt for the Slavs, whom he regarded as "little worms"; his desire to dominate completely the Eurasian land mass; and his drive for glory. He saw ***lebensraum*** ("living space") for the Thousand-Year Reich. Moreover, despite Stalin's pathetic eagerness to live up to the Nazi-Soviet Pact, Hitler suspected a secret deal between Britain and the Soviet Union. Amid all else, there was also the issue of oil.

From the very start, the **capture of Baku** and the other Caucasian oil fields was central to Hitler's concept of his Russian campaign. **"In the economic field,"** one historian has written, **"Hitler's obsession was oil."** To Hitler, it was *the* vital commodity of the industrial age. He read about it, talked about it, knew the history of the world's oil fields. If the oil of the Caucasus — along with the "black earth" farmlands of the Ukraine — could be brought into the German empire, then Hitler's New Order would have the resources to make it invulnerable. **Albert Speer**, the German Minister for Armaments and War Production, said at his interrogation in May 1945, **"the need for oil certainly was a prime motive" in the decision to invade Russia**.

Hitler also saw the Soviet Union as a permanent threat to the **Ploesti oil fields** of **Rumania**, Europe's largest source of petroleum production outside the Soviet Union. Rumania was a German ally, and Germany had become heavily dependent upon Ploesti, which provided **58 percent of Germany's total imports in 1940**. Oil shipments from the Soviet Union had resumed with the signing of the Nazi-Soviet Pact, and in 1940 they accounted for another third of Germany's oil imports. In **June 1940**, the Soviet Union used the terms of the Nazi-Soviet Pact to seize a significant part of northeastern Rumania, putting Russian troops all too close to the Ploesti oil fields. **"The life of the Axis depends on those oilfields,"** Hitler told Mussolini. An attack on Russia would guarantee Ploesti's security — and make available the far grander prize of the Caucasus oil.

In **December 1940**, Hitler issued **Directive Number 21 — Operation Barbarossa** — ordering preparations for an invasion of the Soviet Union. The Germans engaged in an elaborate charade of deception to lull Stalin. Warnings came from many sources — Americans, British, his own spies — but **Stalin resolutely refused to believe them**. Scarcely hours before the invasion, a dedicated German communist defected with word of what was about to happen. Stalin suspected a trick and ordered the man shot.

In the early morning hours of **June 22, 1941**, Russian freight trains were still lumbering westward carrying oil and other raw materials for Germany. Just after 3:00 a.m., the German Army, **three million men strong**, with **600,000 motor vehicles and 625,000 horses**, struck along a wide front. The onslaught caught the Soviet Union completely off guard and put Stalin into a nervous collapse. The Germans thought the attack would be over **in six or eight weeks, or ten at the most**.

Hitler's boast — **"we'll kick the door in and the house will fall down"** — seemed amply borne out at first. The Germans moved even faster than expected. Yet there were soon preliminary signs that they were stretching themselves. They had seriously miscalculated their supply needs, including the need for fuel. On the bad Russian roads and difficult terrain, vehicles burned considerably more fuel than anticipated, sometimes twice as much. The larger vehicles sank into unsurfaced roads and had to be replaced with small Russian wagons drawn by horses.

In **August**, German generals sought Hitler's permission to make Moscow the prime target. Hitler refused. **"The most important aim to be achieved before the onset of winter is not to capture Moscow,"** said his directive of August 21, **"but to seize the Crimea and the industrial and coal region on the Donets, and to cut off the Russian oil supply from the Caucasus area."** The Wehrmacht had to reach Baku. To the arguments of his generals, he responded with one of his favorite maxims: **"My generals know nothing about the economic aspects of war."** Intoxicated by conquest, Hitler was already dreaming aloud about the vast autobahn from Trondheim, Norway, to the Crimea, which would become Germany's Riviera. And, he said, **"the Volga will be our Mississippi."**

Later, Hitler changed his mind and put Moscow back at the top of his objectives. But critical time had been lost. The Germans reached the outskirts of Moscow, just twenty miles from the Kremlin, only at the end of autumn 1941. There they bogged down in mud and snow of fast-approaching winter. The shortages of oil and other supplies finally caught up with them. **"We have reached the end of our resources of personnel and material,"** the Quartermaster General said on November 27. Then on **December 5 and 6**, General **Yuri Zhukov** launched the first successful Soviet counterattack, tying the Germans down for the winter.

The six to eight weeks had turned into months. The Germans were stalemated by winter. They had vastly underestimated the space over which their supply lines would stretch and the reserves of Soviet manpower — **six to eight million Soviet soldiers were killed or captured in the first year of war**, and still new men were thrown into battle. Japan's decision to attack Pearl Harbor rather than the Soviet Union allowed Stalin to transfer his crack **Siberian divisions** westward to the German front.

### *Operation Blau*

In early **1942**, Berlin was making plans for another great offensive — **Operation Blau**. The oil of the Caucasus was its main objective, and from there, on to the oil fields in Iran and Iraq and then on to India. Hitler's economic experts had told him that Germany could not continue the war effort without access to Russian oil, and Hitler fully agreed. At the same time, he wanted to strike at the heart of the Russian war economy. Deprived of fuel, Russia would not be able to stay in the war. Germany assembled a **Technical Oil Brigade**, eventually **fifteen thousand men** strong, with the charge of rehabilitating and running the Russian oil industry. The only thing standing in the way was the requirement to capture it.

By late July 1942, the German armies seemed well on the way — with the conquest of the city of **Rostov** and the cutting of the oil pipeline from the Caucasus. On **August 9**, they reached **Maikop**, the most westerly of the Caucasian oil centers — but a small one, with an output equivalent to only a tenth of Baku. Moreover, before withdrawing, the Russians had so thoroughly destroyed the oil fields and equipment, right down to the small tools in the workshops, that by January 1943 the Germans were able to eke out **no more than seventy barrels per day**.

In mid-August, German mountain troops planted the swastika at the summit of **Mount Elbrus**, the highest point in the Caucasus and in Europe. But the German war machine was stopped before it could reach its objectives — blocked by mountain passes that could be defended, and stymied by **their own shortage of fuel**. The irony of Operation Blau was that the Germans **ran short of oil in their quest for oil**.

The Germans had captured Russian oil supplies, but to no avail — Russian tanks ran on diesel, which was useless to German panzer units, which ran on gasoline. Panzer divisions were sometimes at a standstill for several days in the Caucasus while they waited for fresh supplies. Trucks carrying oil could not catch up because they too had run short of fuel. In desperation, the Germans took to **transporting oil supplies on the backs of camels**. By November 1942, the last German effort to break through the mountain passes toward Grozny and Baku had definitively been repulsed.

### *Stalingrad*

The city of **Stalingrad**, to the northwest of the Caucasus, was meant to be a sideshow, a secondary German objective. But from the very beginning, its name made its fate pregnant with symbolism for both sides. It became the scene of a titanic, decisive struggle fought in the winter of **1942-43**. Again and again, the German Army was crippled by supply shortages, of which fuel loomed largest. General **Heinz Guderian**, the legendary panzer commander, wrote from the Stalingrad front: **"The icy cold, the lack of shelter, the shortage of clothing, the heavy losses of men and equipment, the wretched state of our fuel supplies, all this makes the duties of a commander a misery."**

After more than eighteen months, the tide of battle turned, and the Germans were finally on the defensive in Russia. **Field Marshal Erich von Manstein** begged Hitler in a midnight phone call to transfer the German forces in the Caucasus to help the embattled Sixth Army at Stalingrad. Hitler refused. **"It's a question of the possession of Baku, Field Marshal,"** the dictator said. **"Unless we get the Baku oil, the war is lost."** He proceeded to deliver a lesson on the central importance oil had assumed in warfare, repeating himself over and over. **"If I can no longer get you the oil for your operations, Field Marshal, you will be unable to do anything."**

Manstein tried to argue about the immediate strategic issue — the survival of the Sixth Army. Hitler would not listen. Instead he described how German armies would meet up in the Middle East. **"Then we shall march with our assembled forces to India, where we shall seal our final victory over England. Goodnight, Heil, Field Marshal!"** "Heil, mein Fuhrer!" was all Manstein could say.

Despite Hitler's rapture, the order to retreat was given in January 1943. But it was too late for the Sixth Army at Stalingrad. Surrounded by Soviet forces, it was trapped, unable to risk a breakout. Its tanks only had enough fuel to move **twenty miles**, but to escape they would have had to bridge a gap of thirty miles. It simply could not be done. At the end of January and beginning of February 1943, the encircled German forces at Stalingrad — **ground down and incapacitated, frozen, hungry, and with their essential element of mobility gone** — surrendered.

Stalingrad was Germany's first major defeat in Europe. It sent Hitler into an uncontrollable rage. The ***blitzkrieg*** phase was over. Instead of lightning attacks, the critical factors from here on would be military manpower and economic resources — including oil. The Soviets would press relentlessly forward, pushing the Germans out of all captured Russian territory and moving inexorably toward Berlin itself.

### *Rommel and the Revenge of the Quartermaster*

North Africa was, in the words of General **Erwin Rommel**, the one theater of World War II where the military struggle took place almost totally on the new **"principle of complete mobility."** That mobility was provided by Germany's Panzer Army in North Africa and its most important element, the **Afrika Korps**, both the creation of Rommel. He was a brilliant, innovative, imaginative master of tank warfare and the mobile campaign, as well as a consummate risk taker. Small, silent, and cold, Rommel had established his reputation as a battlefield leader in the First World War. Hitler was impressed by a book he wrote on infantry tactics and in 1938 appointed him, though not a Nazi party member, to head the battalion responsible for the personal safety of the Fuhrer. In 1940, he commanded a panzer division that moved with astonishing speed across France — **"We never imagined war in the West would be like this,"** he wrote to his wife. The campaign had "turned into a lightning tour of France."

In **February 1941**, Rommel was dispatched to North Africa to shore up an Italian Army on the brink of being defeated by British forces. The battle zone would stretch a thousand miles in length, from Tripoli in Libya to **El Alamein** in Egypt, though only seventy miles in width. Rommel was committed to a war of movement and boldness. He was scathing about the commander who halted at the urging of the quartermaster: **"It has become the habit for quartermaster staffs to complain at every difficulty... When, after a great victory... the pursuit is abandoned on the quartermaster's advice, history almost invariably finds the decision to be wrong."**

At first, Rommel won stunning victories against British forces, often with only slender resources and captured booty. At one point, **85 percent of his transport** was provided by captured British and American vehicles. He also ordered "dummy tanks" built at workshops in Tripoli, mounted on Volkswagens. But there was one thing even he could not fake — mobile warfare was absolutely dependent on ample supplies of fuel, delivered along very long lines. Oil proved to be one of Rommel's most persistent problems. As early as June 1941 he wrote: **"Unfortunately, our petrol stocks were badly depleted, and it was with some anxiety that we contemplated the coming British attack, for we knew that our moves would be decided more by the petrol gauge than by tactical requirements."**

In late May 1942, Rommel launched a major assault against the British. It went very well. In one week, his forces traveled three hundred miles. Rather than stop at the frontier between Libya and Egypt as planned, Rommel pushed on until his advance was finally halted, late in June, near **El Alamein** — now less than sixty miles from Alexandria; Cairo and the Suez Canal were not far beyond.

The Axis powers thought they were on the verge of famous victory. **Mussolini flew over to North Africa**, accompanied in another airplane by a white charger, on which he planned to make a triumphant entry into Cairo. Rommel's goals were far more expansive: Cairo would only be a way station for a campaign through Palestine, Iraq, and Iran whose final objective would be **Baku and its oil fields**. Their capture, in concert with the German forces then battling in the Caucasus, would **"shatter the Russian colossus."** Hitler wrote to Mussolini: **"Destiny has offered us a chance which will never occur twice in the same theater of war."**

Both Rommel and Hitler spoke too soon. While the Soviets held on in the Caucasus, the Allies had succeeded in retaining the Mediterranean island of **Malta**, off the coast of Libya, which gave them a base from which to attack the Axis shipping that supplied Rommel's forces. The Allies were further aided by their intercepts of German and Italian codes. The supply aircraft of the Luftwaffe were running short of fuel. Italian supply ships were no longer getting through to North Africa. And Rommel's very success — the incredible distance the Afrika Korps had traveled — created a dangerous vulnerability. His own supply lines were very long, and the fuel trucks from Tripoli used more gasoline to get to the front and back than they were able to carry. By driving so far and so fast, Rommel had called up **the quartermaster's nightmare**. On June 28, 1942, he wrote his wife to plan on a July vacation together in Italy. **"Get passports!"**

On the other side of the line, panic was building in Cairo. The British were burning documents, Allied personnel were being squeezed into cattle trains for a hasty evacuation, and Cairo merchants were hurriedly replacing photographs of Churchill and Roosevelt in their shop windows with those of Hitler and Mussolini. But the British did not give way in late June and July 1942, and Rommel was too short of gasoline to regain his momentum. The two exhausted armies had fought to a stalemate — the **First Battle of El Alamein**. And there in the desert they waited.

### *Montgomery*

In mid-August, Rommel gained a new and formidable adversary — **General Bernard Montgomery**, austere, ascetic, self-righteous, sometimes insubordinate, but invariably patient. A cousin of **H. St. John B. Philby**, the sorcerer's apprentice of Saudi oil, Montgomery had been the best man when Philby was married in India. Early on in life, Montgomery had learned to depend on his own resources, and not much else. After the death of his wife, he seemed to have been left with virtually no emotional attachments. "Everything I possessed had been destroyed by enemy bombing in Portsmouth in January 1941," he later wrote. **"I was now going to be given the opportunity to get my own back on the Germans."** In his first address to officers of the Eighth Army at Ruweisat Ridge near El Alamein, he felt the need to say: "I assure you I am quite sane. I understand there are people who often think I am slightly mad; so often that I now regard it as rather a compliment."

Montgomery was also a methodical, pedagogic, and deeply analytical military strategist. He would sometimes spend hours a day by himself — in his own **"mental oases"** — thinking through problems, searching for key principles. He hung a drawing of Rommel in his desert trailer, to help him think how Rommel would think. He knew that in facing Rommel, he was taking on a modern legend. He had one clear aim: turn the tables on the master of mobile warfare and decisively defeat Rommel. For, said Montgomery, Rommel **"had never been beaten before though he had often had to 'nip back to get more petrol.'"** He was "the only Field-Marshal in this war who won all his battles."

Montgomery sought to work out a strategy using the Eighth Army, now equipped with Sherman tanks, as a unified whole, capitalizing on the fact that its own supply line was short and Rommel's very long — and highly vulnerable.

### *The Battle of Alam Haifa and El Alamein*

Rommel himself was of two minds — acutely conscious of fuel shortages but also wanting to get on with the drive to Cairo and beyond. He was convinced time was running short and that the spirit of the Afrika Korps would win the day. He gave the order to attack. This round, still in the environs of El Alamein, became known as the **Battle of Alam Haifa**.

Again and again, during that week-long battle, the Afrika Korps was hampered by its fuel shortage. On **August 31**: "Due to the heavy going, the Afrika Korps' petrol stocks were soon badly depleted and at 16:00 hours we called off the attack on Hill 132." On **September 1**, "the promised petrol had still not arrived in Africa." It never came. Most of the fuel due by ship had either been sunk or was still waiting to be loaded in Italy. A small railway that might have transported gasoline had been flooded. By **September 7, 1942**, the battle of Alam Haifa was over. Rommel's latest offensive had been stopped dead in its tracks, and **a legend of invincibility was toppling**.

In the weeks that followed, Rommel begged for more supplies, including fuel sufficient for two thousand miles per vehicle. On **September 23**, Rommel left North Africa to see Mussolini in Rome, then Hitler at his headquarters on the Russian front. Again he pleaded for supplies; instead, he received the baton of **Field Marshal**, personally bestowed upon him by his Fuhrer. Hitler made generous promises, which he would not keep.

On **October 23**, after weeks of careful preparation and resupply, Montgomery opened his counterattack — the **Second Battle of El Alamein** — with a powerful artillery barrage. The Germans were stunned. On the first day, General Georg Stumme, Rommel's replacement, fell out of his car under British aerial bombardment and died of a heart attack. Hitler telephoned Rommel, who was in the Austrian Alps on sick leave, and ordered him to return immediately. By the evening of October 25, he was back in Egypt — this time commanding the beginning of a long retreat.

German hopes for new supplies were pinned on aircraft and ships that were being methodically destroyed by the Royal Navy and the RAF. When Rommel learned that four ships carrying desperately needed petroleum had been sunk by the RAF at Tobruk, he lay awake all night, eyes wide open, unable to sleep. He knew what the sinkings meant: **"In attacking our petrol transport, the British were able to hit us in a part of our machine on whose proper functioning the whole of the rest depended."**

During the ensuing weeks, all Rommel could do was fall back. At times, he believed he could have turned around and delivered devastating blows, but **he simply did not have the fuel to dare it**. To Hitler he repeatedly described the fuel situation as "catastrophic." An even greater catastrophe loomed when Allied forces invaded Morocco and Algeria — in the path of his retreat. On **Christmas Eve, 1942**, Rommel joined his headquarters Christmas party. That day, he had shot a gazelle from his car, which he contributed to the dinner table. In turn, he received a present: a couple of pounds of captured coffee, presented in a **miniature oil barrel**. "Thus proper homage," he said, "was paid to our most serious problem even on that day."

Soon all that Rommel's forces had left was a toehold between the forces advancing from both east and west. The legend had fallen. In **March 1943**, Rommel, now regarded by Hitler as defeatist, was removed from command of the Afrika Korps. By May, the last German and Italian troops in North Africa had surrendered.

### *Rommel's Death and Epitaph*

Rommel was called to serve the Fuhrer again, first in Italy, and then in France, where he was badly wounded shortly after the Normandy invasion. Three days later, a group of army officers tried to assassinate Hitler but failed. Rommel was suspected of involvement in the conspiracy. In **October 1944**, two SS generals appeared at his home with an ultimatum: commit a suicide that could be disguised as a natural death, or his entire family would be in danger. Rommel, holding the Field Marshal's baton, drove off with the two SS men. A few hundred yards from the house, the car pulled over into the woods — the area had been sealed off by the Gestapo. Rommel was given a poison pill; he swallowed it and fell forward in the seat, dropping the Field Marshal's baton. A state funeral was organized, and Hitler sent his condolences. Rommel's **"heart,"** said the official funeral oration, **"belonged to the Fuhrer."**

In Rommel's papers, he left a hard-earned epitaph for the role of supply and of oil in the age of mobile warfare. **"The battle is fought and decided by the Quartermasters before the shooting begins,"** he wrote, looking back on El Alamein. He himself had derisively rejected such a thought a few years earlier. But he had learned a bitter lesson: **"The bravest men can do nothing without guns, the guns nothing without plenty of ammunition, and neither guns nor ammunition are of much use in mobile warfare unless there are vehicles with sufficient petrol to haul them around."** Two weeks after the Second Battle of El Alamein, retreating before Montgomery's forces, Rommel wrote to his wife: **"Shortage of petrol! It's enough to make one weep."**

### *Autarchy and Catastrophe*

By mid-1943, the Axis had been defeated both in Russia and in North Africa, and the dream of German armies converging at Baku or on the oil fields of the Middle East had been relegated to fantasy. Germany had to turn back to its own resources. Synthetic fuels would be at the heart of the frantic effort to sustain the machines of war — and in that effort, Hitler's Reich would display its technological ingenuity as well as its total moral bankruptcy.

The man in charge was **Albert Speer**, Hitler's personal architect, intensely ambitious and one of Hitler's favorites. He had caught Hitler's attention with his designs for grand panoramas of flags, hundred-foot-high eagles, and spotlights for the 1933 Nazi party rally in Nuremberg. In **1942**, the Fuhrer appointed Speer Minister of Armaments and War Production. In early 1943, as the magnitude of the failures in Russia and North Africa became clear, Speer was given sweeping powers over the entire German economy. The architect proved remarkably adept at industrial mobilization. Over two and a half years, Speer drove a more than **threefold increase** in the production of aircraft, weapons, and ammunition, and a nearly **sixfold increase in tanks** — even while the Allies carried out an extensive strategic bombing campaign against German targets. German industrial production registered its highest level of the entire war in **June 1944**. **"Oil, which was Germany's weakest point,"** wrote the British military historian **Basil Liddell Hart**, **"was scarcely touched."**

The synthetic fuels industry was headed on the same upward trend. Between 1940 and 1943, synthetic fuel production almost doubled, from 72,000 to **124,000 barrels per day**. In the first quarter of 1944, synthetic fuels provided **57 percent of total supply** — and **92 percent of aviation gasoline**. Altogether, during the Second World War, synthetic fuels would account for half of Germany's total oil production.

### *Slave Labor at Auschwitz*

It could not have happened without immense effort and all the normal tools and techniques of the Nazi war economy, including **slave labor**. The concentration camps were the mechanisms for achieving the **"Final Solution,"** decided upon at the **Wannsee Conference in January 1942**. Until the "Final Solution" could be completed, those Jews who were fit — along with Slavs and other prisoners — were to be put to work to further the objectives of the Reich. A continuing supply of concentration camp prisoners was drafted into I.G. Farben's hydrogenation plants, as well as into its synthetic rubber plants. The company was building synthetic fuel and rubber plants **adjacent to the Auschwitz concentration camp** in Poland. Auschwitz was the largest of the Nazi mass murder factories; upwards of **two million people**, mostly Jews, were put to death there with gas manufactured by an I.G. Farben subsidiary. The I.G. Farben officials described the Auschwitz site, with its ample supplies of coal and labor, as **"very favorably located."** The synthetic fuels plant was under the directorship of the same chemist who had met with Hitler in Munich in June 1932.

I.G. Farben paid a per diem for each slave laborer — **three or four marks for an adult**, depending upon skills, and half price for children. The money went into the coffers of the SS. The slave laborers subsisted on a thousand calories a day and slept on wooden racks. They would work for a few months, then die from the horrible conditions or be killed in the death camp, and be replaced by others from newly arrived trains packed like cattle cars.

One of those was **Prisoner Number 174,517**, a young Italian named **Primo Levi**, who managed to survive only because he could recall enough organic chemistry to be put to work in a lab. "This huge entanglement of iron, concrete, mud and smoke is the negation of beauty," he said of I.G.'s industrial complex. **"Within its bounds not a blade of grass grows, and the soil is impregnated with the poisonous saps of coal and petroleum, and the only thing alive are machines and slaves — and the former are more alive than the latter."** Monowitz — the company's own private "branch" concentration camp — was a death factory. The records indicate that **three hundred thousand prisoners** passed through I.G. Farben's portals at Auschwitz. The plants were so large they used **more electricity than the entire city of Berlin**.

By 1944, according to one estimate, **a third of the total work force** in the German synthetic fuels industry throughout the Reich was slave labor.

### *"The Primary Strategic Aim"*

Following the haphazard Allied strategic bombing campaign against Germany, General **Carl Spaatz**, commander of the United States Strategic Air Force in Europe, decided a change was needed. On **March 5, 1944**, he proposed to General **Dwight Eisenhower** that a new priority target be set — **the German synthetic fuels industry**. Its output, he promised, could be cut by half within six months. The British opposed Spaatz's plan, insisting on targeting the French railway system. But Spaatz received a tacit go-ahead from Eisenhower.

On **May 12, 1944**, a combat wing involving **935 bombers** plus fighter escorts bombed a number of synthetic fuels factories, including the giant **I.G. Farben plant at Leuna**. As soon as Speer realized what had happened, he rushed by plane to Leuna to see the damage for himself. **"I shall never forget the date May 12,"** he later wrote. **"On that day the technological war was decided."** The results of the attack, and the broken, twisted pipe systems, made real **"what had been a nightmare to us for more than two years."** A week after the attack, Speer flew to report personally to Hitler: **"The enemy has struck us at one of our weakest points. If they persist at it this time, we will soon no longer have any fuel production worth mentioning."** He added: **"Our one hope is that the other side has an Air Force General Staff as scatterbrained as ours!"**

Yet this initial attack was not so troublesome as it first appeared. The Germans had seized Italy's oil stocks, providing a cushion. And feverish repair activity returned damaged plants to production within weeks. But on **May 28-29**, the Allies hit the oil facilities again. Other Allied bombers attacked **Ploesti** in Rumania. On **June 6**, D-Day, the Allies invaded Normandy. On **June 8**, General Spaatz gave the formal directive: **"the 'primary strategic aim' of the United States Strategic Air Forces is now to deny oil to enemy armed forces."** Regular bombing attacks on the synthetic fuels industry followed.

Speer ordered synthetic fuels plants be rebuilt quickly, or dispersed into smaller, better-protected, hidden sites — some in quarries, some underground. Even **breweries were converted to making fuel**. Upward of **350,000 workers** — many of them slave laborers — were engaged in this frantic undertaking. But as time went on, the plants became more fragile and vulnerable to further air attacks, and more difficult to get back into operation. Before the attacks began in May 1944, synthetic fuel production by hydrogenation was averaging **92,000 barrels per day**; by September, output had fallen to **5,000 barrels per day**. Aviation gasoline production that month was just **3,000 barrels per day** — only **6 percent** of the average production in the first four months of 1944. Meanwhile, the Russians had captured the **Ploesti oil fields**, depriving Hitler of his major source of crude oil.

The output of German planes was still at peak level — but they sat on the ground without fuel. **Jet fighters**, a new German innovation that would have given the Luftwaffe a significant advantage, were being introduced into operational squadrons in autumn 1944. But **there was no fuel to train the pilots**, or even to get the planes into the air. The Luftwaffe was operating on just **one-tenth of the minimum** required gasoline. In-air training for new pilots was cut to **only an hour a week**. **"This was actually the fatal blow for the Luftwaffe!"** General **Adolph Galland**, commander of German fighter forces, said after the war. **"From September on, the shortage of fuel was unbearable. Air operations were thereby made virtually impossible."**

In the autumn of 1944, bad weather temporarily curtailed the Allied attacks, and in November, the Germans managed to increase synthetic fuel output. But production fell again in December. **"We must realize that the men on the enemy side who are directing the economic air raids know something about German economic life,"** Speer told an armaments conference. **"Fortunately for us the enemy began following this strategy only in the last half or three-quarters of a year. ... Before that he was, at least from his standpoint, committing absurdities."**

### *The Battle of the Bulge: Europe's Biggest Gas Station*

By the fall of 1944, the D-Day invasion had widened to drive the Germans out of France. Soviet forces were pushing in from the east. But to Hitler, the war could not be coming to an end. On **December 16**, he launched a huge counteroffensive in the hilly, wooded **Ardennes forest** in eastern Belgium and Luxembourg — the **Battle of the Bulge**, Germany's last great concentrated attack, the final *blitzkrieg*. The plan was Hitler's own, and everything was thrown into it, including every bit of fuel that could be scrounged from other units. The objective was to throw back the Allies, isolate their armies, and buy time for the development of new, more devastating weapons.

The Germans had the advantage of surprise, but they had undertaken the assault with vastly insufficient resources. The reserves of manpower that might have been decisive **could not reach the front lines**. "They could not be moved," a German commander later said. **"They were at a standstill for lack of petrol — stranded over a stretch of a hundred miles — just when they were needed."**

The German fuel plan depended on capturing Allied fuel dumps. Near **Stavelot** in eastern Belgium was the Allies' largest fuel dump — the biggest filling station in Europe. There the Allies had stored **2.5 million gallons of fuel** for their troops, along with two million road maps of Europe. The roads were lined with hundreds of thousands of five-gallon jerry cans.

On **December 17**, the second day, a panzer unit led by the butcherous colonel **Jochem Peiper** overran a small fuel dump nearby. Peiper forced fifty captured American soldiers to fill his vehicles and then coldly ordered them killed. A large number of American prisoners were also gunned down in the **Malmedy Massacre**. By that evening, Peiper's forces were within a thousand feet of the forward edge of the **Stavelot dump** — fifty times larger than the one he had captured. The Allied defenses were light and disorganized. Peiper's forces moved into Stavelot. In a desperate effort to improvise, a small group of Allied defenders poured the contents of jerry cans into a round ditch and set it ablaze, creating **a wall of fire**. Peiper examined his maps carefully, but they were out of date and failed to show the correct location or magnitude of the dump. **He did not know about the bounty that lay at hand.** Instead of sending his forces through the thin wall of fire, he ordered them to go back across the bridge and head west. Ironically, Peiper's unit soon ran out of fuel; his tanks got only half a mile to the gallon. Resupply efforts by the Luftwaffe failed, and the unit was captured.

Peiper's U-turn was one of those small battle incidents with momentous consequences. **The Stavelot fuel supply was equivalent to the requirements of the first ten days of Germany's entire Ardennes offensive**; its capture would have given the Germans the fuel to push on to Antwerp and the English Channel. Even so, it was not until **Christmas Day 1944**, ten days after the Germans began, that they were finally stopped and pushed back.

### *"Twilight of the Gods"*

With the failure of the Ardennes offensive, Germany's war effort was over from a strategic point of view. In **February 1945**, German production of aviation gasoline amounted to just **a thousand tons** — one-half of one percent of the level of the first four months of 1944. None was produced thereafter. But the delusions of victory lived on. Those around Hitler, Speer would recall, "would listen to him in silence when, in the long since hopeless situation, he continued to commit nonexistent divisions or to order units supplied by planes that could no longer fly for lack of fuel."

Still the months of bloody fighting went on. Hitler and his immediate entourage retreated ever more into fantasy, with the Fuhrer calling for a scorched earth policy and issuing "the last crazy orders." Even as the end approached, he remained in the grip of his insane, violent visions, for which at least **35 million people** paid with their lives. He listened to Wagner's ***Gotterdammerung*** — "Twilight of the Gods" — on the gramophone, and avidly read horoscopes that promised a sudden improvement. Only when Russian soldiers were almost directly above his underground bunker, on the doorstep of the now-ruined Chancellery that Speer had designed for him, did Hitler commit suicide. **He left orders that his body be doused in gasoline and burned** so that it would not fall into the hands of the hated Slavs. There was enough gasoline at hand to carry out that final order.

On a night journey to the struggling remnants of Germany's Tenth Army in Italy, Albert Speer had seen a clear vision of why the Reich that was to last for a thousand years had, in fact, only weeks to go. He had encountered **150 German Army trucks**. To each were hitched **four oxen**, which were dragging the trucks forward. **It was the only way the vehicles could move. They had no fuel.**

**German Oil Supply, 1938-1945 (barrels per day):**
| Year | Synthetic | Other | Total |
|------|-----------|-------|-------|
| 1939 | 47,574 | 121,973 | 169,547 |
| 1941 | 89,007 | 119,614 | 208,621 |
| 1943 | 124,299 | 112,865 | 237,164 |
| 1944 | 330,499 | 244,344 | 574,843 |
| 1945 (Q1) | 5,437 | 17,726 | 23,163 |

---

## Chapter 18: Japan's Achilles' Heel

### *Balikpapan — The Prize at Stake*

In the first week of **December 1941**, an American naval squadron was on a courtesy call in the huge, splendid harbor at **Balikpapan**, in Borneo, in the British East Indies. At the turn of the century, **Marcus Samuel** had ordered his nephew to carve a refinery complex out of the jungle. Over the four decades that followed, what had seemed Samuel's foolish and reckless dream had turned into a great oil-refining center for the island's production, and one of the grand jewels of the **Royal Dutch/Shell Group** and a major landmark in the world oil industry.

Now, in December 1941, the refinery management had just hosted a party for the visiting American sailors. The junior officers, along with crates of liquor, were assembling at the local club for a return party when a senior officer suddenly appeared and ordered them back to the ship immediately. On board, fueling began at once, and by midnight, the American ships were steaming out of the harbor. That was how the English and Dutch oil men at Balikpapan learned about the attack on Pearl Harbor. The war for which they had been waiting and preparing had, at last, begun.

A year earlier, in **1940**, when a Shell manager named **H. C. Jansen** arrived at Balikpapan, he had found air raid shelters already built and evacuation plans developed. In the subsequent months, the entrance to the harbor was mined, and **120 men practiced destruction exercises**. They all knew that Balikpapan and the surrounding oil fields were one of the great prizes for which the Japanese would go to war. The oil men's job would be to **deny the Japanese that prize**.

### *The Destruction of Balikpapan*

In the days right after Pearl Harbor, the wives and children of the oil men were evacuated from Balikpapan. In the nights that followed, Jansen and his colleagues — now all bachelors — would sit in rattan chairs in his garden, looking down in the darkness at the refinery and the ocean beyond, talking about the depressing radio reports of Japanese advances. During the days, they worked themselves to exhaustion, seeking to turn out as much refined product as possible for the Allied war effort.

In mid-January 1942, with the Japanese closing in, crews in the outlying oil fields began to destroy the wells. They pulled out the tubing, cut it up, and jammed it down again into the wells, along with pumps, rods, bolts, nuts, drilling bits — and **a tin of TNT for each well**. The wells were blown up, starting with the least productive, until finally all were destroyed.

Meanwhile, the first steps toward demolishing the Balikpapan refinery complex were underway. The stills and steam boilers were turned on and allowed to boil dry until they collapsed. Then on **January 20**, the men at the refinery received word: a Japanese fleet was at sea only twenty-four hours' steaming time away. The Japanese sent, via two captured Dutchmen, an ultimatum: **surrender at once, or all would be bayoneted**. A military officer gave the order to begin the demolition.

Jansen and the other men blew up the mines store first; the blast shattered all window panes in the area. Next came the wharves, which had been thoroughly doused with gasoline or a mixture of kerosene and lubricating oil. By noon, the docks were aflame. From then on, the huge complex was rocked by one explosion after another. The flames leaped **150 feet in the sky** as the salt water station, the tin plant, the refinery installations, the power plant, and more and more buildings joined the conflagration.

The men, covered with sweat and soot, ran about amidst the flames, following plans they had rehearsed many times. They moved on to the tank farm, where oil was stored. To each tank, **fifteen sticks of TNT** had been affixed. But some of it had deteriorated in the humid weather and would not ignite. Finally, the tanks on higher ground were opened, and the oil poured down to the tanks below. Electrical ignition was used to detonate four or five tanks. Instantly, a great ball of fire rose up, followed by a terrific explosion, and then a huge hurricane of wind. As the flaming sea of oil poured down the hill, **the tank farm became a hellish conflagration**.

The oil men, their throats parched, exhausted to the bone, climbed into native boats called *proas*. The sea around them was red from the reflection of the great columns of fire. They had done their work well — **four decades of industrial creation had been destroyed in less than a day**. They entered the mouth of the Riko River, headed upriver toward an evacuation camp deep in the jungle. They spent interminable hours listening for the sound of the airplane that was to be sent to their rescue. It did not come.

The next night, Jansen and a small group sailed back down the tributary, hoping for rescue. One man, asleep on the hard seat, fell overboard; the others pulled him back in, making a great din to scare the crocodiles away. The only way to keep mosquitoes at bay was by smoking pipes and cigarettes. Eventually, two seaplanes appeared and evacuated many of them. Jansen was on the second plane, packed in so tightly he could hardly breathe.

When the evacuees arrived at Surabaya, on Java, they were greeted: **"It is no longer possible to send planes to Balikpapan; the Japs are there."** Seventy-five people were left stranded by the bay, still awaiting rescue. The Japanese had landed on the south side of the bay. In what became known as the **Battle of Balikpapan**, four blacked-out American destroyers slipped up on a dozen Japanese troop transports, silhouetted against the red fires of the still-burning refinery, and sank four of them plus a patrol boat — the first American sea battle with the Japanese since Admiral Dewey's victory at Manila in 1898.

It hardly slowed the Japanese. The stranded oil men had no choice but to retreat into the jungle. They broke into small groups, making their way by foot or in *proas*, beset by hunger, exhaustion, malaria, dysentery, and fear, their parties growing smaller as the ill and dead dropped away. Of the seventy-five people left behind, **only thirty-five survived** the jungle, the Japanese firing squads, and the Japanese prisons.

### *"Victory Drunk"*

Preemptive destruction of oil facilities, similar to Balikpapan, was carried out elsewhere in the East Indies. But this seemed a minor inconvenience to the Japanese wave sweeping across Southeast Asia and the Pacific. By **mid-March 1942**, Japan's control of the East Indies was complete. In just three months, Japan had won possession of all the rich resources of Southeast Asia — and the oil, in particular, for which it had gone to war. In Tokyo, Premier **Tojo** bragged that Hong Kong had fallen in eighteen days, Manila in twenty-six, and Singapore in seventy. A **"victory fever"** gripped the country; the stunning military successes spawned such a runaway stock market boom that the government had to intervene. Some said the country had become **"victory drunk."** Only a few warned of the inevitable morning after.

The Japanese elation was matched by American shock and despair. On **Christmas Day 1941**, Admiral Chester Nimitz arrived by seaplane at Pearl Harbor to begin picking up the pieces. As he was ferried across the harbor, he passed small boats searching for bodies; two and a half weeks after the attack, corpses were still floating to the surface. That grim scene in Hawaii was only part of the larger, dismal outlook facing the United States — war in both hemispheres, a truly global conflict.

Who would be in charge of America's Pacific War? Each service was loath to commit its entire force to an officer from the other. Two commands and two theaters were set up. General **Douglas MacArthur**, although a strategist of great shrewdness, was also egoistic, bombastic, and imperious. After three hours of listening to MacArthur, FDR told an aide, **"Give me an aspirin... In fact, give me another aspirin to take in the morning. In all my life nobody has ever talked to me the way MacArthur did."** Admiral **Chester Nimitz** was soft-spoken, unassuming, a team player. "It simply was not in him to make sweeping statements or to give colorful interviews."

From the beginning, Nimitz had no question in his own mind — the primary objectives of Allied armed forces were to safeguard their own supply lines and then drive westward to capture bases from which **Japan's indispensable 'oil line' might be blocked**.

### *"The Adults' Hour"*

While the Americans mobilized, the Japanese gloried in their victories and pondered next moves. So self-confident had they become that some military leaders considered thrusting westward through the Indian Ocean to link with German forces in the Middle East or Russia and help sever the Allies' supply of oil from Baku and Iran. In **April 1942**, Admiral Yamamoto, the architect of Pearl Harbor, wrote to his favorite geisha: **"The 'first stage of operations' has been a kind of children's hour, and will soon be over; now comes the adults' hour, so perhaps I'd better stop dozing and bestir myself."**

Yamamoto continued to share the deep belief in the **"decisive battle"** that would knock the enemy out of the war. A quick victory was essential — he knew from his years in the United States that America's oil and industrial might made a long war unwinnable. The Japanese decided to mount a major attack on **Midway Island**, just eleven hundred miles west of Hawaii, to extend their defense perimeter and draw out the American fleet.

The **Battle of Midway**, in early **June 1942**, turned out to be decisive — but not in the way the Japanese expected. It was the "adults' hour" that Yamamoto had feared. The U.S. Navy, having made a remarkable recovery from Pearl Harbor and with the additional advantage of being able to read the enemy's code, dealt a resounding defeat to the complacent Japanese, **sending four of the Imperial Fleet's aircraft carriers to the bottom** while losing just one of its own.

Midway was the **true turning point** in the Pacific War, the end of the Japanese offensive. The counterattack began two months later, when American troops landed on **Guadalcanal**, off New Guinea. Six months of brutal fighting followed, but finally the island was captured — the first American offensive of the war. The aura of invincibility that surrounded the Japanese Army had been pierced.

### *Rehabilitating the Indies Oil*

The initial attempts to deny Japan the oil of the Indies did not prove much of an impediment. The Japanese had anticipated the demolition. Despite Shell's efforts at Balikpapan and Stanvac's on Sumatra, the destruction was neither as severe nor as widespread as expected. The Japanese immediately set about rehabilitating the oil industry. Drilling teams, refinery crews, and equipment were rushed in — some **four thousand oil field workers**, 70 percent of the total in the Home Islands, were shipped south.

The result was astonishing. Before the war, the Japanese military had planned on getting sufficient oil within two years from the **Southern Zone**. That goal was exceeded. Oil production in the Southern Zone had been **65.1 million barrels in 1940**. In 1942, the Japanese were able to produce just 25.9 million barrels, but by **1943**, they had gotten it back to **49.6 million barrels — 75 percent of the 1940 level**. In the first three months of 1943, Japanese oil imports had risen to 80 percent of the amount imported in the same period of 1941, just before the embargo. The Japanese fleet could refuel locally at will.

The Japanese also took advantage of **Caltex** exploration work. Just before the war, Caltex had identified a very promising field — the **Minas structure** in central Sumatra — and had moved in drilling equipment. The Japanese took over and, using Caltex's rig, drilled a discovery well — their only wildcat in all of World War II. They struck **a super giant field**, the largest between California and the Middle East. In 1943, Premier Tojo announced that the oil problem — which had triggered Japan's aggression — was solved. But Tojo had spoken too soon.

### *The Battle of the Marus: The War of Attrition*

In devising their military strategy, the Japanese had assumed that the rich resources of the Southern Zone could be welded securely to the economy of the barren Home Islands, giving Japan the staying power to erect a "Pacific wall." Japan had entered the war with oil inventories sufficient to last two years — or so Japanese planners thought. Beyond that, Japan would have to call upon the oil of the East Indies. And this dependence, in the words of the U.S. Strategic Bombing Survey, **"proved to be a fatal weakness."** Or, as one history of Japanese military operations put it, **"The shortage of liquid fuel was Japan's Achilles' heel."**

The specific weakness was the vulnerability of Japanese shipping to **submarines**. Military planners had given surprisingly little thought to that risk. They underestimated both American submarines and the men who would sail on them. The Japanese thought Americans would be **too soft and luxury-loving** to stand up to the rigors of undersea warfare. In fact, America's submarines were the best in the war; and once equipped with improved torpedoes, they were a deadly weapon that weakened and then ruptured the critical shipping links between the Southern Zone and the Home Islands. The long, drawn-out confrontation became known as the **Battle of the Marus**, after the term the Japanese used to describe all merchantmen. Only in late 1943 did the Japanese begin to give serious attention to protection of shipping. **"When we requested air cover,"** one convoy commander said ruefully, **"only American planes showed up."**
