Moreover, convoys created their own problems — the radio signals required to coordinate convoys announced exact "noon positions." The Americans, who had cracked the Japanese codes, used these intercepts to direct the submariners. Overall, the consequences were devastating. Of Japan's total wartime steel merchant shipping, some **86 percent was sunk** during the conflict and another 9 percent so seriously damaged as to be out of action. Less than 2 percent of American naval personnel — the submariners — were responsible for **55 percent of the total loss**. The ever-tightening blockade was later described by Japanese economists as **"a death blow to the war economy of Japan."**

Oil tankers were among the favorite targets. By **1944**, sinkings were far outrunning new tanker construction. Oil imports into Japan had reached their peak in the first quarter of fiscal year 1943. A year later, imports were less than half the 1943 figure. By the first quarter of 1945, imports had disappeared altogether. **"Towards the end the situation was reached,"** a Japanese captain said, **"that we were fairly certain a tanker would be sunk shortly after departing from port. There wasn't much doubt in our minds that a tanker would not get to Japan."**

As imports dried up, the Japanese tried many expedients and improvisations. Oil was put into drums and fiber containers loaded onto decks of freighters. They filled large rubber bags with three to five hundred barrels of oil to be towed by tugs to Japan. The concept failed — gasoline attacked the rubber, filling was difficult, and the bags made tugs easy targets. In desperation, the Japanese even tried to transport oil in their own submarines and sought to force German subs to deliver oil in exchange for repair facilities.

At home, civilian gasoline consumption in 1944 was down to just **257,000 barrels — a mere 4 percent of the 1940 figure**. Vehicles deemed essential were re-equipped with charcoal or wood burners. Industrial oils were made from soy beans, peanuts, coconuts, and castor beans. Civilian stocks of potatoes, sugar, rice wine — even bottles of sake from retail stores — were requisitioned for conversion to alcohol, to be used as fuel.

### *Japan's Synthetic Fuel Failure*

In **1937**, the Japanese had made an ambitious commitment to synthetic fuels, and some in Tokyo had championed them as an alternative to war. But the wartime effort failed miserably, crippled by shortages of steel and equipment, and a never-ending series of technical problems. In 1943, Japan's synthetic fuels production amounted to a total of **one million barrels — only 8 percent** of the 14-million-barrel target. Over half of the capacity was in Manchuria, where it was rendered useless in late 1944 and 1945 by blockade. **"The synthetic fuel industry in Japan, in terms of its absorption of materials and manpower and its meager product, was more of a liability than an asset during the war."**

### *"No Sense in Saving the Fleet"*

The growing shortage of oil increasingly constrained Japanese military capabilities and directly affected the course of many battles. The pinch had been felt as early as June 1942 at Midway. Following Midway, Allied forces went on the offensive — **island-hopping westward** — Tarawa and Makin in the Gilberts, Kwajalein and Eniwetok in the Marshalls, Saipan and Guam in the Marianas. For both sides, every yard on every beach seemed to be measured in hundreds of lives.

The Americans even exacted their revenge for Pearl Harbor in **April 1943**, when cryptanalysts learned that Admiral Yamamoto was due to visit Bougainville, near New Guinea. American fighters, waiting in ambush, came out of the clouds and **sent the admiral down in flames** to his death in the jungle below.

By early 1944, the submarine campaign finally began to make the Imperial Navy feel the fuel shortage "very keenly." Vanishing oil inventories started to influence strategic decisions, with increasingly devastating consequences. In the **Marianas campaign in June 1944**, the Japanese battle fleet did not join the action because its fuel was low. The carrier force approached the Americans directly rather than circuitously, **in order to conserve oil**. "It would take too much fuel to take the longer route," the Japanese commander was later to say. The direct approach was a costly one, for the result was the **"Great Marianas Turkey Shoot,"** in which the Japanese lost **273 planes to the Americans' 29**.

In the aftermath, the sundering of the oil line to the Home Islands meant the Japanese could not base both battle groups of the Imperial Fleet together. Part of the fleet, with the carriers, was based in Japan, where it would drain the last fuel oil inventories. The heavy battleships were stationed close to Singapore, near the East Indies supply. But there, once committed to action, they could not refuel and be ready for action again for about a month. **The overall consequence of the oil shortage was to divide naval strength** when the Japanese needed a truly combined fleet.

Japanese air operations were also severely curtailed. Pilot training in 1944 was cut to **thirty hours**, half of what was considered necessary. As the shortage worsened in 1945, navigation training was eliminated altogether — pilots were simply to follow their leaders to targets. Few were expected to return. Aviation gasoline was made from the only available source — **wood turpentine** — mixed with larger and larger proportions of alcohol. The combination of inferior fuels, poorly trained pilots, and inadequately tested planes was deadly. The Japanese lost up to **40 percent of their aircraft on ferrying operations alone**.

To stretch oil supplies, many Japanese ships burned **unrefined Borneo crude**, which Marcus Samuel had claimed many years earlier was indeed good for fuel. However, it was also highly flammable, and thus a threat to the ships it powered. Under duress, the Japanese even reversed the historic trend in naval propulsion and **rebuilt ships in service to burn coal** where available. Ships under construction were converted to coal before launching — achieving security of supply but at a loss in speed and flexibility.

### *The Battle of Leyte Gulf*

It was the primacy of fuel that finally led the Imperial Navy to throw all its weight into the **Battle of Leyte Gulf**, off the Philippines, in **October 1944**. The noose was growing very tight. The recapture of Guam in August brought cities on the Home Islands into range of B-29 bombers. To the south, MacArthur had landed on Morotai, only three hundred miles from the Philippines.

Admiral **Soemu Toyoda**, Chief of the Naval General Staff, gave the order for what would become the greatest naval battle in history. **"Should we lose in the Philippines operations,"** he later said, **"even though the fleet should be left, the shipping lane to the south would be completely cut off so that the fleet, if it should come back to Japanese waters, could not obtain its fuel supply... There would be no sense in saving the fleet at the expense of the loss of the Philippines. That was my reason for my order."**

The shortage of fuel handicapped the Japanese again and again. Because of the split in basing, their Navy had to try to concentrate forces from widely separate directions. Two Japanese battleships never even made it to the battle because of the lack of oil — they proceeded to Singapore, refueled, and went home. Other ships arrived a few critical hours too late because they were slow-speeding to conserve fuel. On **October 25, 1944**, Admiral **Takeo Kurita**, commander of the Second Fleet, was in position to enter Leyte Gulf, which would have made it possible to annihilate General MacArthur's lightly defended invasion troops and change the course of battle. But, just forty miles away from the invasion beach, **Kurita abruptly pulled off and sailed away**. After the war, one of the Japanese admirals was asked why. **"Because,"** he replied, **"of shortage of fuel."**

The three-day battle of Leyte Gulf was a devastating defeat for the Japanese — three battleships, all four aircraft carriers, ten cruisers, and thirteen destroyers lost. It was out of desperation in this battle that the Japanese explicitly introduced ***kamikazes*** — the suicide pilots. The word meant **"divine wind,"** after a typhoon that had shattered Kublai Khan's invasion fleet in the thirteenth century. The suicide pilots were meant to be the ultimate embodiment of the Japanese spirit — but they also served a very practical purpose for a country extremely short of oil, planes, and skilled pilots. The Japanese calculated that whereas eight bombers and sixteen fighters were required to sink an American aircraft carrier or battleship, the same effect could be achieved by **one to three suicide planes**. Not only would the pilot cause more damage — since he was not going to return — **his fuel requirement was cut in half**.

### *The End of the Imperial Navy*

The Japanese could do little to interrupt the ever more abundant flow of fuel to the American forces. The Americans developed huge **floating bases** — composed of fuel barges, repair ships, tenders, tugs, floating docks — that gave the U.S. Navy "long legs" across the vast Pacific. Roving fueling task forces took up stations at designated areas, large rectangles twenty-five miles wide by seventy-five miles long. When Guam became the major American base for bombing Japan, **120,000 barrels of aviation gasoline** were supplied there daily. At that same time, Japan's entire air forces on all fronts were consuming just **21,000 barrels per day** — one-sixth of what was available just at Guam.

By early **1945**, the Americans had recaptured Manila and Iwo Jima — the latter at appalling cost: 6,800 Americans and 21,000 Japanese dead. The Japanese abandoned Balikpapan and the other chief oil port in the East Indies. In **March 1945**, the last Japanese oil tanker convoy left Singapore. **It never got to Japan. It was sunk.**

At home, oil had virtually disappeared from the domestic economy. Gas, electricity, coal, and charcoal were all in incredibly short supply. People called the experience **"washing potatoes in a tub"** — with the heat coming from scrap wood gathered in the streets. Many Japanese took to burning their libraries for fuel. Tokyo's fuel distribution for the 1944-45 winter did not commence until **May 21, 1945**. Food intake was down to less than **1,800 calories a day**, well below the minimum requirement of 2,160.

### *The Yamato's Suicide Mission*

The fuel situation had become so severe that the Navy decided on a dramatic variant of the *kamikaze* raid — to sacrifice the ***Yamato***, the world's largest battleship and the pride of the Japanese fleet. It was to break through to American ships supporting the invasion of Okinawa, do as much damage as possible, then beach itself and use its huge eighteen-inch guns in defense of the island. **"Any large-scale operation requiring heavy supplies of fuel became almost out of the question,"** said Admiral Toyoda. Even in getting together just **2,500 tons** of fuel oil for the mission, they had a very difficult time. Even if there was not a fifty-fifty chance, "nothing was to be gained by letting those ships lie idle in home waters... it would have been contrary to the tradition of the Japanese Navy."

It was clearly a suicide mission; the *Yamato* carried only enough fuel for a **one-way trip**. On **April 7**, at midday, three hundred American planes came out of the low-hanging overcast and began their barrage. By mid-afternoon, the *Yamato* and most of the other ships had been sunk. To many, the sinking of the *Yamato*, which had been destroyed even before it could commit suicide, marked **"the end of the Imperial Navy."**

### *A Fight to the Finish?*

Japan's position worsened steadily. Shortages of fuel were preventing its planes from flying more than two hours a month. Desperate for fuel, the Navy launched its fantastic **pine root campaign**. Guided by the slogan **"two hundred pine roots will keep a plane in the air for an hour,"** people all over the Home Islands began to dig up pine roots. Children were dispatched to the countryside to scour for the roots. **Thirty-four thousand kettles, stills, and small distillation units** were put in place, each aimed at producing three or four gallons of oil per day. The futility of the effort was revealed by the labor requirements: each gallon produced required **2.5 man-days of work**. To meet the official target of twelve thousand barrels per day would have required **1.25 million persons per day**!

By June 1945, production of pine root oil had reached seventy thousand barrels per month, but the refining difficulties had not been solved. By war's end, only three thousand barrels of gasoline intended for planes had been produced from pine root oil, and **there was no evidence that any of it had ever actually been tried in a plane**.

The Soviet Union renounced its neutrality pact with Japan on **April 5, 1945**. Senior officers in the Japanese Navy conceived yet another desperate idea — approach the Soviet Union directly, ask it to mediate between Tokyo and Washington and London, and trade Soviet oil for resources from the Southern Zone. **Koki Hirota**, a former premier, was charged with opening the dialogue. But the Japanese did not know that at Yalta, Stalin had secretly promised Roosevelt and Churchill to bring the Soviet Union into the war against Japan ninety days after the end of the war in Europe. The Soviet ambassador dismissed all of Hirota's proposals.

Premier **Kantaro Suzuki** ordered a survey of Japan's fighting capabilities. The study came back in mid-June 1945 with a picture of a war economy **almost immobilized because of lack of fuel** and American aerial attacks. Fuel oil inventories had been **29.6 million barrels in April 1937**; by **July 1, 1945, they were just 0.8 million barrels**. Below a million barrels, the Navy could not operate. **For all practical purposes, it was out of oil.** To some in the Japanese government, "the utter hopelessness of our situation" was clear. But the government was still pushing the slogan **"100 million people united and ready to die for the Nation."**

The American invasion of **Okinawa** in April 1945 was fierce and fanatic, not ending in its organized form until June 21, 1945. The Americans experienced a **35 percent casualty rate**. Assuming a similar ratio would hold in an invasion of the Home Islands, American commanders anticipated a minimum of **268,000 dead and wounded** on their side, with up to **a million American military casualties** total.

The bloodiness of Okinawa contributed strongly to the American decision to use the **atomic bomb**. American leaders knew Japan's fighting capability was crumbling, but they saw no sign that its fighting spirit was fading — the whole island nation was being mobilized for a suicidal battle to the death; even young schoolchildren were ordered to start sharpening bamboo shoots with which to kill Americans. Intercepted messages indicated the Japanese government was not prepared to ask for peace — because it was not.

Despite the ever-worsening position, the Japanese government remained ambiguous and tentative in its signals about surrender, reflecting the fact that the war party still had the upper hand. Tokyo spitefully rejected the Allies' **Potsdam Declaration**, which would have enabled Japan to quit the war on reasonable grounds, including the retention of the Emperor. To the Allies, there was little indication from Tokyo save a determination to fight to the finish.

### *Hiroshima, Nagasaki, and Surrender*

The first atomic bomb was dropped on **Hiroshima on August 6, 1945**. On August 8, the Soviet Union declared war on Japan and sent troops pouring into Manchuria, a week earlier than planned, to ensure the war did not end before it could get in. On **August 9**, the second atomic bomb fell on **Nagasaki**. Yet even as late as the Nagasaki explosion, the Army Chief of Staff insisted that Japanese soldiers and sailors were not permitted to surrender under any circumstances; suicide was the only acceptable way out. On August 13, four days after Nagasaki, Vice-Admiral **Takijiro Onishi**, the creator of the *kamikaze* missions, was still advocating that the government reject surrender — instead, he said, the Japanese people should fight to the bitter end, and **20 million of them should sacrifice themselves** in suicide attacks against the invading troops.

Yet so appalling were Japan's circumstances, and so great the shock of the atomic bombs and the new Soviet threat, that those seeking to end the war finally prevailed over the intense opposition from the military. On the night of **August 14**, the Emperor made a phonograph recording bearing the surrender message. It was to be broadcast the next day. Even then, that same night, insurgent soldiers assassinated the head of the Imperial Guard and broke into the Imperial Palace, trying to capture the record and prevent its broadcast, and at the same time kill Premier Suzuki. They were repulsed. The next day, the Japanese people heard over their radios a faint voice, fading in and out because of the uncertain electricity, that most had never heard before. It was the voice of their Emperor, calling on them to surrender. **The war in the Pacific was over.**

Still, not everyone was willing to heed the call. War Minister **Korechika Arami** committed hara-kiri; Admiral **Onishi** did the same the next day. After the surrender and the establishment of the U.S. Occupation, American authorities found a total of **316,000 barrels of oil** secreted away by the Imperial Army and Navy in remote caves and numerous hiding spots, to be used solely for suicide flights against the invaders. Some stores of the pine root gasoline — one of Japan's last hopes for resistance — were also discovered. It was tested in American military jeeps. **It turned out to be a terrible fuel, gumming up the engines beyond use.**

### *The Ambulance*

From the first moments of the Occupation, the fuel shortage continued to make its impact felt in Japan. On **August 30**, General Douglas MacArthur landed in Japan at the Atsugi airfield. The propellers had been removed from the Japanese planes on the field so they could not be used for *kamikaze* attacks. The general immediately set off in a haphazard motorcade, led by a red fire engine that resembled the Toonerville Trolley. His destination was Yokohama and the battleship *Missouri*, on which, three days later, the instruments of surrender were to be signed. The motorcade route was lined with Japanese soldiers, their backs turned toward the passing MacArthur — the same sign of obeisance previously reserved for the Emperor. Though the distance was only twenty miles, the motorcade took two hours — the battered vehicles, the best the Japanese could provide, were **powered not by gasoline, because there was no gasoline, but by charcoal. They repeatedly broke down.**

Twelve days later, on **September 11, 1945**, American officials in Tokyo arrived outside a modest one-story house belonging to **General Hideki Tojo** — the Razor, the wartime premier. Tojo appeared at an open window, was told he was under arrest, and agreed to go with the Americans. He shut the window. A shot rang out. The Americans burst into the house and found Tojo sitting in an oversized chair, bleeding from a self-inflicted bullet wound just below his heart.

Four years earlier, in 1941, as War Minister and then Premier, Tojo had forced Japan's decision to go to war against the United States, arguing that the fate of Imperial Japan hung in the balance because of its shortage of oil. The cost of what Tojo and his collaborators had launched was enormous. **The Pacific War in its entirety claimed upwards of 20 million lives**, including about two-and-a-half million Japanese. Now, in 1945, Tojo's own life hung in the balance — not because his self-inflicted wound was inevitably fatal, but because of the difficulty in locating a suitable doctor and then in finding an **ambulance that had any gasoline in its tank**. So widespread was the fuel shortage that it proved easier to find an American doctor than an ambulance with gasoline. But finally a vehicle with sufficient fuel was located, and it arrived at Tojo's house two hours after he had shot himself. Tojo was carried off to the hospital and nursed back to health. The next year he went on trial as a war criminal, was found guilty, and was in due course executed.

---

## Chapter 19: The Allies' War

### *Churchill and Britain's Oil Preparations*

**Winston Churchill** had spent the decade of the 1930s in the political wilderness, his warnings about Nazi intentions unheeded. But in **September 1939**, at the age of sixty-six, he was abruptly recalled to the post he had held more than a quarter century earlier — **First Lord of the Admiralty**. This time, his charge was not to prepare for a war; it was too late for that. War had already come a few days earlier with Germany's invasion of Poland on September 1, 1939. There ensued a lull — a phoney war lasting half a year — until the spring of 1940, when Hitler sent his troops smashing across Western Europe. The appeasers fell in London, and Churchill became **Prime Minister of Great Britain**.

The outlook was grim. Norway and Denmark were in German hands, France would surrender the following month, and Britain would stand alone through its **"darkest hour."** No one was better suited than Churchill to lead his country, and no one better understood the critical role that oil would play, first in Britain's very survival, and then in the prolonged conflict ahead.

Well before the outbreak of fighting, the British government had initiated a serious evaluation of its oil position. In late **1937**, a special committee examined the possibility of Britain's adopting an "oil from coal" synthetic fuels strategy along the lines of Germany's. The strategy was rejected — it would have been very costly, while Britain had access to cheaper conventional oil and was home to two major international companies, Shell and Anglo-Iranian. Also, a system based on importation through many ports would be **less vulnerable to air attack** than a few large, easily identifiable hydrogenation plants.

In its planning for war, the British government anticipated very tight cooperation with the oil industry. In the UK, **85 percent** of domestic refining and marketing was in the hands of just three companies — Shell, Anglo-Iranian, and Jersey's British subsidiary. At the time of Munich in **1938**, the government decided that in the event of war, all the "paraphernalia of competition" would be eliminated, and the entire British oil industry would be run as **one giant combine**, under the aegis of the government.

### *Deterding's Fall and the Nazi Threat to Shell*

The government also had to cope with the future of the Royal Dutch/Shell Group — and the risk that it could pass under Nazi sway. The heart of the problem was **Henri Deterding**, the grand master of the company, who had continued to dominate the Group through the 1920s. But by the 1930s, Deterding's grip was slipping, and he was becoming an embarrassment. His behavior was increasingly **erratic, disruptive, megalomaniacal**.

In the mid-1930s, Deterding had developed two infatuations — one for his secretary, a young German woman, and the other for **Adolf Hitler**. The determined Dutchman, entranced with the Nazis, was now in his old age urging cooperation with them. On his own, he initiated discussions in 1935 with the German government about Shell providing a year's supply of oil — in effect, a military reserve — to Germany on credit. Rumors of these talks so alarmed the Shell management in London that one of the senior directors, **Andrew Agnew**, asked the British government to investigate.

Finally, retiring from Shell at the end of 1936, Deterding acted on both infatuations. He divorced his second wife, married his German secretary, and went to live on an estate in Germany. He also took to urging other European nations to cooperate with the Nazis. By 1937, the Prime Minister of the Netherlands said his activities were **"infantile and leaving no doubt as to his feelings."** Deterding's last years, not surprisingly, undermined what would otherwise have been a considerable reputation as an "international oil man."

Deterding died in Germany in early **1939**, six months before the war began. Strange and deeply disturbing rumors immediately reached London — the Nazis were not only making much of his funeral, but were also trying to take advantage of his death to gain control of the Royal Dutch/Shell Group. That would have been a disaster for Great Britain — Shell had been virtually Britain's quartermaster general for oil during World War I. But it was discovered that the key "preference shares," which embodied control, could only be held by directors, and Deterding's shares had been swiftly distributed to the other directors. At best, the Germans could only get their hands on a tiny fraction of the common shares.

### *Britain's Wartime Oil Regime*

As soon as the war began, the British oil companies merged their downstream activities into the **Petroleum Board**, in effect creating a national monopoly. Petrol pumps were painted dark green and the product was sold under the single brand name **"Pool."** The industry people continued to operate the business under national control. Britain's oil war was thereafter run out of **Shell-Mex House** on the Strand in London. Overall government direction was eventually lodged in the **Petroleum Department**.

Britain's issues were global. It had to assume that Germany, as signatory of a pact with the Soviet Union, would have abundant Russian oil, while British supplies from the Far East would be curtailed if the Japanese invaded Southeast Asia. Closer to home, Rumania's rich resources went to Germany. The British and French had even jointly offered to pay Rumania **$60 million** to destroy its oil fields and prevent the Germans from taking the output — but the deal was never struck.

In Britain itself, **rationing was imposed almost at once**. The "basic ration" for motorists was first set at eighteen hundred miles a year, then progressively tightened, and then eliminated altogether. There was a great boom in bicycling. In case of invasion, plans were made at Shell-Mex House for the immediate destruction of British oil stocks. Some **seventeen thousand gasoline-selling establishments** in eastern and southeastern England were shut down, and sales concentrated in two thousand stations that could be better defended — or set ablaze to deny them to the enemy.

### *The Oil Czar: The Mobilization of American Supply*

For the British, the overarching concern was how to supply their war. The only place to look was the **United States**, responsible for almost two-thirds of total world production. In December 1940, Roosevelt proclaimed the United States the **"arsenal of democracy."** In **March 1941**, **Lend Lease** was instituted, which removed "the silly, foolish old dollar sign" as a constraint on American supply to Britain. Among those things to be "lent" was American oil.

In the spring of 1941, when oil supplies began to plummet in the United Kingdom, **fifty American oil tankers** were switched from supplying the American East Coast to carrying oil for transshipment to England. The U.S. had a surplus, unused oil production capacity of about **one million barrels per day** — equivalent to about 30 percent of the 3.7 million barrels per day production that year. The extra capacity, a result of the federal-state prorationing system set up in the 1930s, turned out to be an invaluable security margin, a strategic resource of immense significance. **Without it, the course of World War II might well have been different.**

In **May 1941**, Roosevelt appointed Interior Secretary **Harold Ickes** to the additional post of **Petroleum Coordinator for National Defense**. The Old Curmudgeon once again became the nation's top oil man — its **Oil Czar**. Ickes's first task was to reshape the relationship between the Roosevelt Administration and the oil industry. By the latter 1930s, the government had become increasingly critical of "monopoly" in the oil industry — in 1940, the Justice Department had launched an antitrust case against the American Petroleum Institute and twenty-two major oil companies, plus 345 smaller ones. But another shift came with the national emergency. As Roosevelt was later to explain, **"Old Dr. New Deal had to call in his partner 'Dr. Win-the-War.'"** And what Dr. New Deal had found unpalatable about Big Oil — its size, its integrated operations, its self-reliance — was exactly what Dr. Win-the-War would prescribe as the urgent medicine for wartime mobilization.

Ickes also had to take the lead in turning around an industry geared to coping with surplus to one that would maximize output and avert shortage. Harold Ickes started with a huge liability: he was **widely detested by the industry**. Upon his appointment, the *Oil Weekly* rushed out a supplement warning against "antagonistic management and probably vindictive tampering of a man who has no qualities nor specific traits of ability to handle the job." But Ickes would prove otherwise, demonstrating a willingness to work closely and pragmatically with the industry. He chose as his deputy an experienced oil man, **Ralph Davies**, a marketing executive from Standard of California. The Oil Czar succeeded in disarming the hostility and cooperating effectively to mobilize this critical industry.

### *Trial by Sea: The Battle of the Atlantic*

The most vulnerable link in the supply chain being forged between America and beleaguered Britain was the wide expanse of the Atlantic that tankers and freighters had to cross. Here lay the German opportunity to strangle the military capabilities of the British and, later, of the American forces. **"The more ruthlessly economic warfare is waged,"** Admiral **Erich Raeder**, Commander in Chief of the German Navy, declared, **"the earlier it will show results and the sooner the war will end."** The weapon was the **U-boat**, and its ability to disorganize shipping was quickly felt. Early in 1941, the U-boats, now operating in **"wolf packs,"** stepped up the campaign. Oil tankers were their favored targets.

The battle frustrated Churchill to his core. **"How willingly,"** he said, **"would I have exchanged a full-scale invasion for this shapeless, measureless peril, expressed in charts, curves, and statistics!"** In March 1941, he described the onslaughts on shipping as **"the blackest cloud which we had to face."** He had no doubt how much was at stake in the silent and remote battle in the waters of the Atlantic, and he knew he could not win it without American help.

**"Nothing less than shocking ... grave in the extreme."** That was how **Ralph Davies** reported to Ickes on England's perilous oil supply situation in July 1941. There were only five weeks of motor gasoline stocks and only **two months of fuel for the Royal Navy**, whereas seven months' inventory was considered the rock-bottom minimum. Ickes was convinced that everything that could be done to help the British should be done. He mobilized the nation's railway tank cars, launched a voluntary conservation campaign including windshield stickers saying **"I'm using one-third less gas,"** and sought to reinstate the "gasolineless Sundays" of World War I. But the voluntary conservation program was a flop, and Ickes turned to the companies and pressed them to cut deliveries to gasoline stations by 10 to 15 percent.

The one thing Ickes could not do was explain the real reasons for conservation: the dismal effects of the German U-boat campaign and the deplorable state of British oil supplies. He feared giving valuable intelligence to the Nazis. Thus the whole conservation program aroused a storm of protest. The New Jersey state legislature denounced the effort because it threatened the state's fishing and summer resorts. Major elements of the press labeled the situation a **"phony shortage."**

To counter the U-boat menace, the United States increased patrols and established bases in Newfoundland, Greenland, Iceland, and Bermuda. The British cracked German naval codes, enabling evasive convoy routings. **"It was only by the narrowest of margins,"** in the words of the official history of British intelligence, that **"the U-boat campaign failed to be decisive during 1941."**

By autumn 1941, the supply situation on the U.S. East Coast had improved, while the British temporarily handed back their fifty tankers. That seemed to prove there had been no shortage, and Ickes found himself pilloried both in the press and in Congress. A special Congressional investigating committee claimed the Secretary had invented the shortage — it was nothing more, said the committee, than **"a shortage of surplus."** Gasoline stations were putting up signs urging motorists to "fill it up." Ickes resolved never again to go too far out on oil issues.

### *U-Boats in American Waters*

Yet the threat of shortage loomed again when Germany declared war on the United States on **December 11, 1941**, four days after Pearl Harbor. U-boats immediately began to operate in American coastal waters with devastating results. Their priority targets were oil tankers, easily recognizable by their distinctive profile. In the first three months of **1942**, the number of tankers sunk was almost **four times the number built**. As one U-boat headed home from American waters with eight sinkings to its credit, the captain exultantly wrote in his war diary: **"It is a pity that there were not twenty U-boats last night, instead of one. I am sure all would have found ample targets."**

**"The situation is desperate,"** Ickes wrote to Roosevelt in late April 1942. Yet the initial American response was feeble — tankers were urged to hug the coast and use the Delaware-Chesapeake canals. The U.S. had **neglected antisubmarine warfare** and was unprepared. Onshore, American cities were making it even easier for the U-boats. They were brightly illuminated at night, providing perfect silhouettes of the tanker targets. **Miami** was the worst offender — six miles of its beachfront were lit up by neon lights. Hotel owners and the chamber of commerce insisted the lights could not be doused; it was still tourist season. Elsewhere along the coast, at Atlantic City, crowds would watch from the shore as the dark horizon at sea was suddenly illuminated — another tanker had been hit. Finally, outdoor lighting was doused on the eastern seaboard.

### *Big Inch and Little Inch*

Other steps were taken to counter the U-boat menace. Convoys were instituted along the East Coast. But an even better option was to minimize the amount of oil that had to travel by tanker. The construction of a pipeline — **Big Inch** — on a scale never before attempted, stretching from Texas to the East Coast, was put forward. Initially turned down in autumn 1941 on grounds it would use too much steel, the pipeline project was hastily resuscitated after Pearl Harbor and tanker sinkings in American waters.

Construction finally began in **August 1942**, and what followed was one of the extraordinary feats of engineering in World War II. The oil transportation and construction industries were mobilized to build a pipeline that would carry **five times as much oil** as a conventional one, stretching halfway across the country. Within a year and a half, by the end of 1943, **Big Inch**, **1,254 miles long**, was carrying one-half of all the crude oil moving to the East Coast. Meanwhile, **Little Inch** — an even longer line, **1,475 miles** — was built between April 1943 and March 1944 to carry gasoline and other refined products from the Southwest to the East Coast. At the beginning of 1942, just **4 percent** of total supplies had arrived on the East Coast by pipeline; by end of 1944, with both pipelines completed, pipelines were carrying **42 percent of all oil**.

### *Doenitz and the Climax of the U-Boat War*

In the spring of 1942, however, Big Inch had not yet begun. The Allies faced a very determined opponent in Admiral **Karl Doenitz**, commander of the German submarine force. **"Rescue no one and take no one with you,"** he ordered his crews. The main objective was **"to destroy more enemy tonnage than can be replaced by all Germany's enemies put together."** The Germans gained two other significant advantages: they changed their code procedures (so the British lost the ability to read U-boat signals) and simultaneously broke the ciphers governing Allied convoys. The resulting destruction of Allied shipping was appalling.

The Battle of the Atlantic became even more dangerous in the second half of 1942. Improved and larger U-boats joined the fleet. Admiral Doenitz introduced ***Milchkuhs*** ("milk cows"), large underwater supply ships that could deliver diesel fuel and fresh food to the U-boats. Month by month, Britain's supply picture grew worse. The United States lost **a quarter of its total tanker tonnage** in 1942. Petroleum stocks were well below required safety levels in Britain. Stalin, too, was making increasingly insistent demands for more oil.

In mid-December, Churchill was told there remained only about **two months' supply** of fuel oil for ships, aside from a last-gasp emergency reserve. **"This does not look at all good,"** was his bleak comment. In January, Churchill went to **Casablanca**, where he and the British Chiefs of Staff met Roosevelt and the American Chiefs. The main topic was an invasion of the European mainland. But all were agreed on one point, summed up by General **Alan Brooke**, Chief of the Imperial General Staff: **"The shortage of shipping was a stranglehold on all offensive operations, and unless we could effectively combat the U-boat menace we might not be able to win the war."**

By the spring of 1943, British oil stocks were at their lowest levels yet. In **March**, the U-boats sank **108 ships**, again operating with virtual impunity. The Atlantic was so infested with enemy submarines that evasion seemed impossible. **"The Germans never came so near to disrupting communication between the New World and the Old as in the first twenty days of March, 1943."**

But in the last days of March, the scales tipped dramatically. The Allies thoroughly broke the new U-boat codes while closing off their own convoy ciphers to the Germans. The British and Americans added a new coordinated counteroffensive capability including support groups designed to attack U-boats. The Allies further improved their radar and introduced newly developed **long-range aircraft** that could cover the previously inaccessible parts of the Atlantic. The tables were abruptly and finally turned. In **May 1943** alone, **30 percent of the U-boats at sea were lost**. A chastened Admiral Doenitz was forced to report to Hitler: **"We are facing the greatest crisis in submarine warfare, since the enemy, by means of new location devices, ... makes fighting impossible, and is causing us heavy losses."** On **May 24**, Doenitz ordered the U-boats to withdraw to safer areas — he was calling off the submarine campaign in the North Atlantic. Allied convoys could now cross the Atlantic in reasonable safety.

A combination of technical innovation, intelligence, organization, new tactics — and persistence — finally assured an abundant flow of oil from America to Britain and on to Europe and the Soviet Union. The path was now clear for a two-front assault on Hitler's Fortress Europe. After **forty-five months** of deadly warfare and mounting peril, the Battle of the Atlantic was over.

### *Domestic Push*

While the safety of oil transport had been the focal point of the war at sea, **Harold Ickes** was also strenuously trying to promote higher oil production in the United States. He was promoted from Petroleum Coordinator to **Petroleum Administrator for War (PAW)**. Since he was also still Secretary of the Interior, the Old Curmudgeon now wielded unprecedented power. Yet some forty or so other Federal agencies had a say in one part or another of the oil industry, and Ickes's PAW was in a constant struggle with them — particularly the War Production Board (which allocated steel), the Office of Price Administration (which set prices), and the War Shipping Administration (which controlled tankers).

Ickes was further constrained by the unwillingness of the American military to share its projected needs with the PAW in any detail — the military did not trust civilians to keep secrets. The PAW gradually molded an effective government-industry combine. It sought an antitrust exemption from the Justice Department so that oil companies could coordinate and pool supplies. About three-quarters of the PAW's executive and technical management actually came out of the oil industry. The PAW was "flanked" by national and regional committees organized by function and drawn from oil industry people — a two-way system of communication through which industry activities were directed and monitored.

At one point in the middle of winter, in **February 1944**, New York was down to just **two days of fuel oil supply**. But each time, outright shortage was averted through adept emergency coordination. Key to the system's success was the availability of crude oil. While the U.S. entered the war with a large shut-in spare production capacity, no one could be sure how high military demand would rise or how long the war would last. The PAW strove to increase and maintain capacity, fighting to allow deduction of drilling costs as intangible expenses from taxes, and using its power to allocate drilling supplies. Its biggest battle on the production side was to get the **Office of Price Administration** to raise prices to stimulate exploration. The OPA, fearful of inflation, rebuffed Ickes's efforts for an across-the-board **thirty-five cent increase** above the official **$1.19 ceiling**. One oil-industry spokesman dismissed the OPA as a **"commie outfit."**

Whatever the complaints, America's overall production record was very good: from **3.7 million barrels per day in 1940** to **4.7 million barrels per day in 1945** — a **30 percent increase**. With the estimated surplus capacity of 1 million barrels in 1940, the United States essentially called on its full reserve. Altogether, between December 1941 and August 1945, the United States and its allies consumed almost **7 billion barrels of oil**, of which **6 billion came from the United States**. Its wartime output was equivalent to more than a quarter of all oil produced in the United States from the time of Colonel Drake's well right down to 1941.

### *Rationing — Through the Side Door*

The other side of the oil equation was consumption, and here occurred the biggest political battles. Efforts were made to get industrial users to switch from oil to coal. Homeowners who heated with oil were asked to keep their houses at **sixty-five degrees during the day and fifty-five at night**. Roosevelt himself took a strong interest in the potential of natural gas.

Gasoline was the focus of contention. In the spring of 1942, a complete ban was imposed on gasoline for auto racing. Then, in May, **rationing was imposed on the East Coast** — initially through cards punched at service stations, succeeded by coupons. It stirred a great outcry from all sides. People on the East Coast "knew for sure" that there were full tanks elsewhere in the country. The Roosevelt Administration was reluctant to blanket the whole country with rationing — in the wide open spaces of the West, there were not many easy alternatives to the automobile.

The Administration finally found a way into nationwide rationing through a side door — **rubber**. The Japanese capture of the East Indies and Malaya had cut off **90 percent of the natural rubber** exported to the United States. By rationing gasoline and thus restricting driving, civilian demand for tires could be cut, freeing available rubber supplies for the armed forces. Roosevelt appointed an august committee to sell the idea — chaired by the venerable **Bernard Baruch**, the Wall Street millionaire, great industrial mobilizer of World War I, and semi-official Elder Statesman. In **September 1942**, Baruch's committee vigorously recommended nationwide gasoline rationing to conserve rubber. The actual plan was not implemented until after the 1942 Congressional elections. As it was, one hundred Congressmen from the West staged a protest.

The rationing system provided five grades of allocation. The alphabetical stickers displayed on automobile windshields became a status symbol — the most fortunate were those with an **X** (doctors, clergymen, some repairmen, and government officials) who had unlimited rights to purchase gasoline. The "basic" **A** ration, which was what most people got, provided anywhere between one and a half and four gallons a week. Not surprisingly, a black market in coupons flourished, particularly in the big cities on the East Coast. Still, civilian gasoline use was substantially reduced — **30 percent less per passenger vehicle in 1943 than in 1941**. Rationing was supplemented by a **thirty-five-mile-per-hour speed limit** and a ban on "nonessential driving" (abandoned a few months later since no one could define it). After all, as was said so often, **"There's a war on."**

### *The International Oil System*

The organization of production and consumption of oil in the United States was only part of the larger international system, jointly improvised and managed by the United States and Britain. It was a system that took crude oil from the American Southwest, refined it, moved it to the Northeast by ship or tank or pipeline, transported it across the Atlantic, and then saw that it was delivered where needed — whether to air bases in Britain, into the five-gallon cans that went to the Allied fighting men at the front, or into railroad tank cars at Murmansk and Archangel, the Soviet Union's ports on the Barents Sea. No less urgent were the needs of the Pacific theater. The Americans and British ran this system through a number of formal and informal arrangements, working on the principle that in each theater of war, one or the other would have complete responsibility for supplying the troops and air forces of both countries. In the United Kingdom and the Middle East, the British filled the Americans' gas tanks; in the Pacific and in North Africa (after the Allied invasion in late 1942), the United States had responsibility for fueling all forces.

The problems of coordination were immense — supplies had to be allocated among hotly contending priorities: Europe, North Africa, the Pacific, and the domestic American economy. Transport and supplies had to be matched, and there were constant and costly mix-ups. Yet, as difficult and contentious as all these matters were, the system that evolved served the Allies very well. **"At no time did the Services lack for oil in the proper quantities, in the proper kinds and at the proper places,"** the Army-Navy Petroleum Board pronounced proudly after the war. **"Not a single operation was delayed or impeded because of a lack of petroleum products."** Though that judgment was for the most part true, there was an exception — one shuddering moment when the system failed.

### *Innovation*

Before World War II, the American military had not anticipated that petroleum supplies might pose any particular problem. The Army did not even have records of how much oil it used. There was incomplete comprehension of how fundamental would be the difference between the First World War and the Second — the former was a static war, the latter **a war of motion**. It was Stalin who had offered a toast at a banquet in Churchill's honor in the darkest days of the war: **"This is a war of engines and octanes. I drink to the American auto industry and the American oil industry."** At the peaks, the American forces in Europe used **one hundred times more gasoline in World War II than in World War I**. The typical American division in World War I used 4,000 horsepower; in World War II, **187,000 horsepower**.

It was only the planning for the 1942 invasion of North Africa that opened the Army's eyes to the full significance of petroleum. About half the total tonnage shipped from the United States during the war was oil. The Quartermaster Corps calculated that when an American soldier went overseas to fight, to support him in his job he required **sixty-seven pounds of supplies and equipment, of which half was petroleum products**.

The Army's new oil supply organization pushed a number of innovations — standardized products, a special narrow portable pipeline system developed by Shell, and most importantly, the eventually ever-present **five-gallon gasoline can**. The Army discovered that its ten-gallon can was unwieldy and too heavy for one man. The Germans used a five-gallon can. In the quest for a more manageable receptacle, the Americans, joined by the British, based their design on captured German cans. Ironic respect was paid to the German original in the nicknames: the "blitz can" and, more commonly, the **"jerrycan."** But the Americans made an important innovation — a built-in pouring spout, which kept the dirt out.

One of the biggest technical disappointments of the war was **PLUTO** — the acronym for "Pipeline Under the Ocean." This underwater pipeline was designed to link the British side of the English Channel to the French, to supply fully half of the fuel needs for Allied forces advancing through France and into Germany. The pipeline was built, but serious technical problems meant that PLUTO's flow during the critical months after D-Day was no more than a trickle. On average, from D-Day in June 1944 to October 1944, just **150 barrels per day** moved through PLUTO — a minuscule one-sixth of one percent of what Allied forces in Western Europe consumed.

### *100-Octane Aviation Gasoline*

Perhaps the most daunting challenge of all in the Allied fuel chain was the supply of **100-octane aviation gasoline**. Developed in the early and mid-1930s, primarily by **Shell researchers** in Holland and the United States, 100-octane fuel made possible higher aircraft performance — greater bursts of speed, more power, quicker takeoffs, longer range, greater maneuverability — a **15 to 30 percent increase in power** over existing fuels. But before the actual outbreak of war, there was no significant market for the much more expensive fuel. Some companies, notably Shell, followed by Jersey, took major risks in making large investments in 100-octane research and capacity. Shell put much of the 100-octane gasoline it produced into storage.

The eruption of war suddenly meant there was a market — and an important one. The advantages of 100-octane gasoline were proved in the **Battle of Britain in 1940**, when the 100-octane-powered British **Spitfires** outperformed the **Messerschmitt 109s**, fueled on 87-octane gasoline. Some attributed Britain's critical edge and victory in that life-and-death air battle to the 100-octane gasoline. But special expensive refining facilities were required, and only small amounts were actually available. Production targets were set and then raised again and again.

Almost all of the Allies' needs for 100-octane fuel had to be met by American production — almost **90 percent of the total by 1944**. But production could not keep up with demand. The Americans responded with a huge construction and engineering program. A new refining technology — **catalytic cracking** — was under development, principally by a Frenchman named **Eugene Houdry** and by Sun Oil. The first significant advance over the thermal cracking technique developed by William Burton three decades earlier, catalytic cracking facilitated the production of large amounts of 100-octane gasoline. Without that technology, the United States simply could never have met the demand for aviation fuel. Yet when the U.S. entered the war, limited catalytic cracking operations were only just beginning. The requirements were huge — the units would be up to fifteen stories tall and vastly more expensive than traditional refining installations. Yet a multitude of catalytic cracking units were built across the country in double time.

Altogether, as part of the 100-octane campaign, dozens and dozens of plants and special facilities were built, and many existing ones were converted. All the aviation fuel plants had to be melded together, to be run as one giant integrated combine, with various components shifted around the country among different companies. By 1945, demand was **seven times higher** than had been projected at the beginning of the war. Yet the requirement was met. By 1945, the United States — which had total production capability for less than 40,000 barrels per day in 1940 — was producing **514,000 barrels per day** of 100-octane fuel. As one general explained, government and industry **"squeezed it out of a hat."**

### *"The Unforgiving Minute" — Patton, Eisenhower, and the Gasoline Crisis*

By the spring of 1944, the pendulum was clearly swinging in the Allies' favor. American and British troops had landed in Italy. The Russians were driving in on the eastern front. Then on **June 6, 1944**, D-Day, Allied forces hit the beaches of Normandy. But immediately, the long and carefully wrought plans went awry. The invading armies were pinned in Normandy much longer than expected. The Germans, though very much taken by surprise, managed to hold the invading forces for a time, even though shortages of fuel greatly constrained their ability to move reinforcements up. Field Marshal **Gerd von Rundstedt** was forced to issue the order: **"Move your equipment with men and horses — don't use gasoline except in battle."**

Then, on **July 25, 1944**, the Allied armies finally burst out of the German ring, and the Germans, disorganized and undersupplied, fell back. Now it was the Allies who were surprised — by the ease and speed with which they could rush forward in hot pursuit.

No force moved more hotly in pursuit than the **Third Army** under **General George Patton, Jr.**, who led the breakout. Driven, dynamic, impulsive, and volcanic in his anger, Patton had been barely able to contain himself in the face of what he regarded as timid Allied strategy since the June 6 landing. Patton was as much a master of mobile warfare as Rommel and he fretted while waiting for his chance for glory. He did prove Eisenhower right about his special talents — revolvers on his hips, he led the breakout from Normandy at astonishing speed; he covered a vast territory in a month — almost five hundred miles from Brest to Verdun, liberating most of France north of the Loire.

Like Rommel, Patton was contemptuous of the quartermasters. His forces pursued any unconventional methods they could think of to ensure their fuel supplies, which became increasingly short as the Third Army's lines were extended. Some of Patton's troops impersonated members of different units to get supplies; **others hijacked trains and truck convoys** or commandeered fuel that supply trucks needed for the return journey. Patton even sent spy planes flying back to the rear to spot gasoline supplies that could be seized.

Toward the end of August 1944, however, fuel was becoming a very serious constraint on the Allied advance. There was no physical shortage of gasoline in France — the supplies were simply in the wrong place, back in Normandy, far behind the lines, and there was an immense logistical problem in getting fuel to the front. In the parlance of supply, the Allies had moved **"260 logistical planning days" in just twenty-one days**. Railways would have been the efficient way to move fuel, but there were no appropriate lines. The endless convoys of fuel trucks, routed in a special one-way system right across France, could not keep up; as the supply lines lengthened, the trucks had to use proportionately more of their own fuel supply. The fast-moving Allied armies simply **outran their gasoline supplies**. The same thing had happened to Rommel when his forces had raced across North Africa in 1942.

Patton fumed. **"At the present time, my chief difficulty is not the Germans but gasoline. If they would give me enough gas, I could go anywhere I want."** The next day he noted in his diary: "I found that, for unknown reasons, we had not been given our share of gas — 140,000 gallons short. This may be an attempt to stop me in a backhand manner, but I doubt it."

Despite Patton's suspicion, the other units were also short of fuel. Eisenhower, as overall commander, faced a critical decision: whether to direct the bulk of available supplies to Patton's Third Army or give the fuel to the United States First Army, to the north, in support of the British Twenty-first Army Group under General Montgomery. Was this the moment to forsake his own "broad front" strategy and instead go for broke — let Patton and the Third Army try to punch through the **Siegfried Line** into Germany itself? Or was it more prudent to let Montgomery first capture Antwerp as a first-class supply port?

As Eisenhower struggled with his decision, Patton was raring to go. **"We have, at this time, the greatest chance to win the war ever presented,"** he wrote. **"If they will let me move on... we can be in Germany in ten days."** But Eisenhower, attuned to the larger requirements of coalition warfare and the tense relationship with the brittle Montgomery, decided on a compromise — a splitting of forces — and one that sent the critically needed gasoline to the First Army, in support of Montgomery, and not to Patton's Third Army.

Down to a half-day supply of gasoline, Patton was furious. He appeared, **"bellowing like an angry bull,"** at the headquarters of General **Omar Bradley**, commander of the American forces. **"We'll win your goddam war if you'll keep Third Army going,"** he roared. **"Dammit, Brad, just give me 400,000 gallons of gasoline, and I'll put you inside Germany in two days."**

Patton would not easily accept the limitation on his supplies. **"No one realizes the terrible value of the 'unforgiving minute' except me,"** he wrote in his diary. **"We got no gas because, to suit Monty, the First Army must get most of it."** He ordered his units to drive on until they ran out of fuel "and then get out and walk." He wrote to his wife: "I have to battle for every yard but it is not the enemy who is trying to stop me, it is 'They'. ... **Look at the map! If I could only steal some gas, I could win this war.**"

On **August 30**, the supply to the Third Army was reduced to less than a tenth of its normal level. Nor would it get any more until September 3. The next day, **August 31**, Patton's forces reached the **Meuse River**. The Third Army could go no further. **Its gas tanks were empty.** "My men can eat their belts," Patton told Eisenhower, **"but my tanks have gotta have gas."**

Montgomery's forces captured **Antwerp on September 4**. "I now deem it important," Eisenhower noted the next day, "to get Patton moving once again." And thereafter, Patton did get more fuel. But **the minute passed was to prove most unforgiving**; the few days that had elapsed had given the Germans critical time to regroup. At the very beginning of September, Hitler had at last modified his "no retreat" order so that German units could fall back, reform, and establish a defensive line. Patton's forces pushed beyond the Meuse but were stalled at the Moselle River — now no longer by lack of gasoline but by the much stiffened resistance of the Germans. **Nine months of bitter and costly warfare would follow.** And when the Germans mounted one massive last-ditch counterattack, **the Russians, not the Americans, took Berlin**.

In December 1945, eight months after the end of the fighting, the master of mobile warfare came to an unglorious end when his chauffeured limousine collided with a U.S. Army truck on a German road.

### *The Great Debate: Did the Allies Miss Their Chance?*

Did the Allies let slip the critical opportunity to bring the war to a swift conclusion? The question was bitterly debated. Of the million casualties the Allied forces suffered in liberating Western Europe, fully **three-quarters occurred after the September check on Patton's advance**. Many millions more died in the last eight months of the war in military action and in the German concentration camps. Moreover, if the Allies had broken through into Germany from the West earlier, the postwar map of Europe would have been drawn quite differently — Soviet power would not have projected so far into the heart of Europe.

For Eisenhower, it had been an immensely difficult decision, one made in fleeting time, with poor information, in the face of high uncertainty and high risks. The cost of acceding to Patton could have been very great — threatening the Allied coalition at a critical moment, leaving the entire Allied Army in a poorly supplied position, and putting the Third Army into a highly exposed position. Eisenhower stood by the judgment he had made: **"Such an attempt would have played into the hands of the enemy"** and the result, for the Allies, **"would have been inescapable defeat."**

Others came to a different conclusion — the mistake was in splitting the armies, instead of concentrating all Allied forces under Montgomery and relentlessly pushing through to the Ruhr and on to Berlin. **Basil Liddell Hart**, the eminent British military strategist and historian, shortly before his death in 1970, gave long thought to the entire question. He agreed with Patton; those days at the end of August 1944 had been **the "unforgiving minute."** The Germans were still in shock, unprepared; not one bridge on the Rhine had yet been readied for demolition. A powerful punch by Patton — "shooting the works," in the words of Patton's poem — could well have caused the disintegration and defeat of the defending German armies. **"The best chance of a quick finish,"** Liddell Hart concluded, **"was probably lost when the 'gas' was turned off from Patton's tanks in the last week of August, when they were 100 miles nearer to the Rhine, and its bridges, than the British."**
